# 南华真经章句音义

**Preface to the *Nanhua Zhenjing Zhangju Yinyi* (Exegetical Notes and Phonetic Glosses on the *True Scripture of Southern Florescence*)**
By Bixuzi

The Grand Historian states: Zhuangzi once served as the warden of Meng’s Lacquer Garden and composed a work of over one hundred thousand characters. The *Han Shu: Yiwen Zhi* records fifty-two chapters of the *Zhuangzi*. The *Sui Shu: Jingji Zhi* notes Xiang Xiu’s commentary in twenty fascicles, Guo Xiang’s in thirty-three fascicles, and further that Liang Qian compiled the *Nanhua Lun* in twenty-five fascicles. Tao Hongjing’s preface to the *Zhen’gao* states: Zhuangzi received esoteric teachings from Master Changsang and composed the seven *Inner Chapters*, each titled with three characters—modeled after the revolving jade rings of the celestial sphere and the radiant brilliance of the Three Luminaries. Thus, when Hongjing composed the *Zhen’gao*, he arranged it into seven sections, likewise using three-character headings. His writings evidently followed this tradition.

During the Tianbao era of the Tang, an imperial edict decreed that Zhuangzi’s work should retain its former title, *True Scripture of Southern Florescence*, from which we know the significance of *Nanhua* has endured since antiquity. From my youth, I have delighted in reciting this scripture. When not occupied with worldly affairs, I would unroll the scroll and read—such is the vocation of Daoists, who seek longevity and enduring vision, forgetting both praise and blame while trusting in the Dao. How can this be compared to those who rely on external conditions?

Now, I have compiled exegetical notes, dividing the text into seven fascicles. This involves parsing chapters and distinguishing sentences, tracing the intricate branches of its discourse. The seven *Inner Chapters*—*Free and Easy Wandering*, *On the Equality of Things*, *The Secret of Caring for Life*, *The Human World*, *Signs of Full Virtue*, *The Great and Venerable Teacher*, and *Fit for Emperors and Kings*—are the very ones named by the Lacquer Garden master. The remaining chapters take their titles from the first two or three characters of each, comprising fifteen *Outer Chapters* and eleven *Miscellaneous Chapters*. Some claim the *Outer* and *Miscellaneous Chapters* were edited by Guo Xiang. However, Tao Hongjing states that Zhuangzi authored the *Inner* and *Outer Chapters* without mentioning the *Miscellaneous*. Examining earlier commentaries, I find them often cursory and difficult to verify. Here, I have selected twenty-six chapters, using two-character headings for those that form complete sections—*Webbed Toes*, *Horses’ Hooves*, *Opening Trunks*, *Engraving Intent*, *Mending Nature*, *Discourse on Swords*, and *The Old Fisherman*—to pair with the *Inner Chapters* as the *Outer Chapters*. The remaining nineteen—*Yielding the Throne*, *Robber Zhi*, *In the Realm of*, *Heaven and Earth*, *The Way of Heaven*, *The Revolution of Heaven*, *Autumn Floods*, *Perfect Happiness*, *Mastering Life*, *The Mountain Tree*, *Master Tian*, *Knowledge Wanders North*, *Gengsang Chu*, *Xu Wugui*, *Zeyang*, *External Things*, *Parables*, *Lie Yukou*, and *The World*—alternate like odd and even numbers, their titles drawn from figures or events, their principles aligning with the numerology of yin and yang, their meanings resonating with the subtleties of speech and silence. Thus, they are called the *Miscellaneous Chapters*.

Within these thirty-three chapters, I have divided the text into 250 sections, assigning titles based on their themes, designating them as *Zhangju* (Exegetical Notes). Beneath each section, I provide phonetic glosses, exegetical explanations, and clarifications of allusions, labeling them as *Zhangyi* (Sectional Meanings). Upon completion, I tallied the characters in the main text, totaling 65,923—four-tenths fewer than Sima Qian’s record. I further collated the *Baowen Tonglu* from the Central Taiyi Palace, which contained several versions of the *Zhuangzi*, along with hand-copied manuscripts from my satchel. Comparing these with the 1004 edition printed by the Directorate of Education, I found 349 discrepancies. Additionally, I consulted other commentaries to address lacunae and errors in one fascicle, resolving doubts and including three chapters by Gongsun Long for reference.

Alas! Later scholars are unfortunate not to witness the complete bamboo slips of the Lacquer Garden or grasp the grand purport of its chapters—its profound insights have gradually been fragmented by various schools.

Composed on the Lantern Festival of the Jiazi year of the Yuanfeng era.

**Nanhua Zhenjing Zhangju Yinyi, Fascicle One**

Zhuangzi’s surname was Zhuang, his given name Zhou, and he was also styled Zixiu. A native of Mengcheng in Song, he lived contemporaneously with King Hui of Liang and King Wei of Qi. He once served as the Keeper of the Lacquer Garden. His teacher was Gongsun Long, from whom he received esoteric teachings. He composed a work of over one hundred thousand characters, which he titled *Nanhua*. His scholarship was rooted in the teachings of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi, and he also studied under the Chu master Juan Yuan. Later, he secluded himself on Mount Baodu, where he ingested the Northern Nurturing Fire Elixir, ascended to heaven in broad daylight, and was appointed as a Compiling Gentleman in the Pavilion of the Supreme Ultimate. On the fourth day of the fifth month in the nineteenth year of the Kaiyuan era of the Tang, the Minister of the Court Pei Guangting requested the canonization of the Four Masters. On the twentieth day of the second month in the first year of the Tianbao era, an imperial edict decreed that Zhuangzi’s work retain its original title, *Nanhua Zhenjing* (*True Scripture of Nanhua*); Wenzi’s work be titled *Tongxuan Zhenjing* (*True Scripture of Pervading Mystery*); Liezi’s work be titled *Chongxu Zhenjing* (*True Scripture of Soaring Emptiness*); and Gengsangzi’s work be retitled *Dongling Zhenjing* (*True Scripture of Penetrating Spirit*). The title *Nanhua* signifies the radiance of separation, the flourishing essence that reveals the subtleties of the Dao.

**Inner Chapters: "Free and Easy Wandering" (1)**
The seven titles of the Inner Chapters were bestowed by the Lacquer Garden. Only when one is free of self can merit and fame dissolve, and only then may one speak of "free and easy wandering." The *Free and Easy Wandering* chapter is placed first because, though it strides beyond the mundane, it still harbors pride and boasting, and thus is followed by *The Equality of Things*. "Equality" means forgetting things to achieve self-equality; those who have not yet attained equality still engage in debates of self and other. When self and other revolve in cycles, entering the emptiness within the cycle brings equality. Those who equalize through emptiness have yet to recognize the master of life and death, so this is followed by *The Secret of Caring for Life*. The "master" is the true lord of spirit and bone. The body is like firewood, and the master is like fire. One who preserves fire relies on firewood, but when the firewood is exhausted, the fire dies. Only the adept in nurturing remains unaware of its end. Yet there are those who shine alone and cannot join the multitude, so this is followed by *The World of Men*. One who dwells in filth without contamination excels in harmonizing light and dust, and by harmonizing with dust, one attains virtue, hence the next chapter, *The Sign of Fullness of Virtue*. When virtue takes form, things are forgotten. Only those who remain obscure preserve their talents in full, and with talents complete, one may become a teacher, hence *The Great and Venerable Teacher*. The true teacher is none other than the perfected person. How could the perfected person possess a heart-mind? Without a heart-mind, one may assist in the transformations of creation, hence *Responding to Emperors and Kings*. Emperors and kings are the source of the great Dao, the masters of teaching and transformation, occupying one of the Four Greatnesses and presiding as the most honored over the myriad things. Vast and profound, their appearance cannot be fathomed by physiognomists. The chapters from *Webbed Toes* onward lack distinct thematic significance and are arranged to elaborate and amplify the subtleties of the seven Inner Chapters.

**Annotations on "Free and Easy Wandering"**
*Beiming* (Northern Darkness) is also written as *溟* (*míng*), pronounced like *觅经* (*mì jīng*). Xi Kang states it signifies "boundless and vast." Emperor Jianwen of Liang says *窅冥* (*yǎo míng*), meaning "without limit." *Kun* (鯤) is pronounced *gǔn* (公浑切). The *Erya* defines it as "fish roe." The name *Kun* refers to the initial transformation of fish eggs, before they differentiate into whale or loach, embodying the meaning of primordial unity, hence "hún ěr" (混尔). When the *Kun* grows large, it spans thousands of *li* without measure.

*Peng* (鵬) was originally written as *朋* (*péng*), implying the meaning of "companionship," hence its sound does not differ from other birds. It signifies "a flock of companions." When the *Peng* takes flight, it spans thousands of *li* without measure.

*Nanming* (Southern Darkness) refers to the Southern Sea, analogous to *Beiming* (Northern Darkness).

*Qi Xie* (齐谐) is pronounced *hù jiē* (户皆切). Sima Biao says it is a person’s name; Emperor Jianwen says it is a book’s title.

*Bo* (搏) is pronounced *tú duān* (徒端切), referring to a bird flying through the air, using its claws to strike the wind and propel itself upward.

*Fuyao* (扶摇): The *Erya* defines it as a whirlwind.

*Xie* (邪) is pronounced *yé* (也嗟切), an auxiliary particle indicating uncertainty, as in subsequent instances.

*Shi er yi yi* (是而已矣) follows the *Wen Ruhai* edition; older versions write *则* (*zé*).

*Aotang* (坳堂) is pronounced *yú jiāo* (於交切). Cheng Wuying says it refers to a sunken area in a courtyard.

*Jie* (芥) is pronounced *jí mài* (吉迈切). Li Yi says it means "small grass."

*Ze jiao* (则胶) is pronounced *gǔ yáo* (古肴切), or alternatively as *jiāo* (胶). Li [Yi] says it means "to stick."

*Feng si* (风斯) is pronounced *xiāng zhī* (相支切), meaning "to split."

*Pei* (培) is pronounced *bó huí* (薄回切), meaning "to increase." The *Liji* states, "Grave mounds are not *peí* (培)." *Peí* means "to cultivate."

*Yao* (夭) is pronounced *yú biǎo* (於表切). Sima [Biao] says it means "to break."

*Guan* (关) is pronounced *yú gé* (於葛切). Sima [Biao] says it means "to stop"; Li [Yi] says it means "to block."

*Tiao* (蜩) is pronounced *tián liáo* (田聊切). Sima [Biao] says it is a cicada.

*Xuejiu* (鸴鸠) is pronounced *yú jiǎo* (於角切). It is also written as *学* (*xué*) or *鸴* (*xué*), pronounced *yù* (预). Sima [Biao] says it is a small turtledove. The *Maoshi Caomu Shu* states that *hú jiū* (鹘鸠) is a turtledove.

*Wo jue* (我决) is pronounced *xǐ quē hū* (喜缺乎), *jué* (决) as *xué* (穴二切). Li [Yi] says it means "swift."

*Qiang* (抢) is pronounced *qī liáng* (七良切). Sima [Biao] says it means "to gather"; Cui [Yun] says "to attach"; Zhi Dun says "to collide."

*Yu* (榆) is pronounced *yú* (踰), the name of a tree.

*Fang er zhi* (枋而止) is pronounced *fāng* (方), with *fang* (枋) as *fāng* (方). Li [Yi] says it is a sandalwood tree. The *Wen Ruhai* and the ancient Jiangnan manuscript read *抢榆枋而止* (*qiāng yú fāng ér zhǐ*).

*Kong* (控) is pronounced *kǔ gòng* (苦贡切). Sima [Biao] says it means "to cast" or "to draw."

Cui [Yun] says it means "to knock."

*Mǎng* (莽) is pronounced *mò làng* (莫浪) or *mò lǎng* (莫朗二切). *Cāng* (苍) is pronounced *qī dàng* (七荡切), or as the character itself. It describes the vast expanse of the wilderness, where distant views appear indistinct.

*Sān cān* (三餐) is pronounced *qī dān* (七丹切). The *Wenben* reads *kē* (颗), meaning "full" or "sated." *Chōng* (舂) is pronounced *shù róng* (束容切). *Liáng* (粮) is pronounced *liáng* (良). "Those two creatures—what do they know?" In the *Wenben*, this originally read *zhī èr chóng yòu hé zhī* (之二虫又何知).

*Cháo jūn* (朝菌) is pronounced *qí yǔn* (其陨切). Sima [Biao] says it is "dog's ear fungus," growing on dung in overcast weather and perishing at sunrise. Also called *bái jí* (白及), it knows neither the beginning nor the end of a month. Cui [Yun] says it is a dung-born fungus, sprouting at dawn and dying by dusk—those that appear at dusk do not live to see the next dawn, and those that appear at dawn do not live to see dusk. Zhi Dun says it is the morning glory, blooming at dawn and withering by dusk. Pan Ni says it is the "platform of ten thousand trees." The *Wen* text calls it a "suddenly sprouting fungus." *Huì* (晦) means "darkness," and *shuò* (朔) means "dawn."

*Huì* (蟪) is pronounced *huì* (惠). *Gū* (蛄) is pronounced *gū* (姑). Sima [Biao] says *huì gū* (蟪蛄) is the winter cicada, also called *dì liáo* (蝭蟧). Those born in spring die in summer, and those born in summer die in autumn. Cui [Yun] calls it *diāo liáo* (蛁蟧). Some say it is the mountain cicada—those that sing in spring do not live to autumn, and those that sing in autumn do not live to winter. The *Chu Ci* calls it the "cold cicada," while others say it is the summer cicada, born in wheat stalks (also called *mài jié* [麦节]), living from summer to autumn. Thus, it knows neither spring nor autumn.

*Míng líng* (冥灵) was originally written as *míng* (榠). Li [Yi] says it is the name of a tree growing in Jiangnan, where the sprouting of leaves marks spring and their falling marks autumn. This tree counts two thousand years as one year. *Dà chūn* (大椿) is pronounced *chǒu lún* (丑伦切). Li [Yi] says it grows in Jiangnan, while others say it grows in the north. This tree counts thirty-two thousand years as one year. "This is a great span of years"—this line was originally missing in the *Chengben*.

Peng Zu, as recorded by Cheng [Gong], was surnamed *Jiān* (籛) and named *Kēng* (铿), a distant descendant of Emperor Ku. Skilled in nurturing life and regulating the cauldron, he presented pheasant soup to Yao. Yao, recognizing his mastery of flavors and thus his ability to cultivate life, believed one who could nurture life could also nurture others, and enfeoffed him at Pengcheng. His Way was worthy of being ancestral, hence the name Peng Zu. He lived through the Xia and Shang dynasties into the Zhou, reaching eight hundred years of age.

*Tè wén* (特闻) in Cui’s version reads *dài wén* (待闻). *Jí biàn xiāo yáo* (极变逍遥):

The *Tang Shi Ji* states that in the system of posthumous names, "Tang" means "to eliminate cruelty and remove tyranny." A descendant of Emperor Ku and scion of Xie, [Tang] was surnamed *Zi* (子) and named *Tiān Yǐ* (天乙). Possessing sagely virtue, he banished Jie to Nanchao.

*Jí* (棘) was a worthy of the Tang era, a man of vast knowledge. *Qióng fà* (穷发): Cui [Yun] says it is the barren northern land without vegetation, where mountains take grass and trees as their "hair."

*Yáng jiǎo* (羊角): Sima [Biao] says it refers to wind curling upward like a ram’s horns. *Chì* (斥) is pronounced *chāng shí* (昌石切), meaning "to point" or "to name"; some say it means "great." *Yàn* (鴳) is also written as *yàn* (鴳), pronounced *yú liàn* (於练切), a type of sparrow.

*Áo xiáng* (翱翔) is pronounced *wǔ dāo* (五刀切), meaning "to wheel in flight." *Xíng* (行) is pronounced *xià mèng* (下孟切). *Bǐ* (比) is pronounced *pí zhì* (毗至) or *fú zhì* (扶至二切). Li [Yi] says it means "to join." In the *Yupian*, it is pronounced *néng* (能), *nú dēng* (奴登切). The *Shuowen* says it is a kind of bear, with deer-like feet and a sturdy body, hence called "capable" (*néng*). Some read it as the character itself, meaning "then" or "peace," as in the *Yi*: "It is fitting to establish a lord, yet not at ease." Zheng’s reading is not followed here.

*Zhēng* (征) is read as the character itself; Sima [Biao] says it means "trust," while Cui [Yun] says it means "to complete."

Song Rongzi: Sima [Biao] says he was a worthy man of Song. *Yóu rán xiào zhī* (犹然笑之): Li [Yi] says it means "to regard as laughable." *Jiā jǔ* (加沮) is pronounced *cí lǚ* (慈吕切), meaning "to defeat."

"Shuòshuò" is pronounced *shuò* (朔), with the same pronunciation below. Sima [Biao] says it means "like *jíjí* (汲汲, hurriedly)"; Cui [Yun] says it conveys a sense of urgency. [Emperor] Jianwen reads it as *suǒ* (所渝切), meaning "to calculate."

Liezi: Li [Yi] says he was a man of Zheng, named Yǔkòu (圄寇), or sometimes written as Yùkòu (御寇). He attained the art of riding the wind and traveling upon it, and was a contemporary of Duke Mu of Zheng. Lu Ji’s *Yaolan* states that Liezi rode the wind regularly on the day of *Lìchūn* (立春), journeying to the Eight Wastes. *Língrán* is pronounced *líng* (零). Cheng [Xuanying] says it describes a light and soaring appearance.

The Six Qi: wind, cold, heat, dampness, dryness, and fire are called the Six Qi. Sima [Biao] says they are yin, yang, wind, rain, darkness, and brightness. *Zhī biàn* is pronounced *píng miǎn* (平免切). *Biàn* means "to transform." Guo Xiang’s commentary states that "riding the transformations of the Six Qi" refers to traversing the paths of change. *Wū hū* is pronounced *wū* (乌). Cheng [Xuanying] says it means "in what" or "where." *Wú jǐ* is pronounced *jǐ* (纪). "Free and unfettered without self."

Yao: In the *Shǐ Lǒng Xū Shì Fǎ* (*Book of Posthumous Names*), "assisting virtue and transmitting sagacity" is called *Yáo* (尧). He was the son of Emperor Ku, surnamed Yī Qí (伊祁), styled Fàngxūn (放勋), and possessed sagely virtue. At fifteen, he was enfeoffed as the Marquis of Tang; at twenty-one, he ascended the imperial throne and established his capital at Pingyang. After seventy years of rule, he found Shun, and after twenty more years, he retired. Shun then administered the Son of Heaven’s governance, recommending Yao to Heaven. Yao abdicated for a total of twenty-eight years. He lived to one hundred and eighteen, ruled for ninety-eight years, and upon his death was buried in Yangcheng. In the *Book of Songs*, he is associated with the state of Tang.

Xu You: The *Gāoshì Zhuàn* (*Biographies of Lofty Scholars*) states his courtesy name was Zhòngwǔ (仲武), and he was a native of Huaili in Yangcheng. He fled to cultivate the land north of the Ying River, beneath Mount Ji, and was posthumously named Duke Ji. *Juéhuǒ* is pronounced *jué* (爵), or alternatively *zǐ zhào* (子召切). The *Zìlín* says it means "torch fire," or alternatively *guàn huǒ* (爟火), referring to a small fire.

Timely Rain: Cheng [Xuanying] says that in the time of Shennong, rain fell once every fifteen days, which was called "timely rain." *Jìn* is pronounced *zhèn* (鸩切). *Guàn* is pronounced *gǔ luàn* (古乱切).

The realm is well-governed when officials are upright and exacting. "Well-governed" (*zhì*) means the attainment of order and success. *Zhí* is pronounced *zhí* (直基切), meaning "principle" (*lǐ*); this term will not appear again below, so seek its meaning through righteousness.

*Jiāo* is pronounced *yáo* (遥切). *Liáo* is pronounced *liáo* (辽). Guo Pu states it is the peach sparrow. Cheng says it is the wren, also called the "skillful wife bird," the "craftsman sparrow," or the "female artisan."

The *yǎn-shǔ* is pronounced as written. The *Shuōwén* says it is the *fén* (mole). Li [Yun] says it is the *xī-shǔ* (dormouse). Cheng says the *yǎn-shǔ* is the size of an ox, with a reddish-black color like a river deer, its ears resembling three overlapping shells, a white-tipped tail, and a habit of entering rivers to drink. The *Jīnlóuzǐ* states that in the borders of Jìnníng County, large rats appear, resembling oxen, which the locals call *yǎn-shǔ*. When disaster is imminent, these rats emerge from the mountains to roam the fields, shedding their fur, which transforms entirely into small rats, consuming all five grains.

The *páo-rén* is pronounced *bāo jiāo* (鲍交切), referring to the kitchen steward. The *Rites of Zhou* includes the office of *páo-rén*.

*Shī-zhù* is pronounced *liù* (六切). The *shī* is the spirit tablet in the grand ancestral temple; the *zhù* is the grand invocator. It refers to holding the sacrificial tablet and invoking before the *shī*. Alternatively, it means transmitting the words of ghosts and spirits, called *zhù*.

*Zūn* is pronounced *cún* (存切). Originally written as 尊, it is a wine vessel.

*Zǔ* is pronounced *zǐ lǚ* (侧吕切), a vessel for meat.

**Wúgōng Xiāoyáo** – Jiān Wú: Li [Yun] says he is a worthy man. Lián Shū: Li [Yun] says he is a man who embraces the Dao.

Jiē Yú is pronounced *yú* (余). The *Gāoshì Zhuàn* (*Biographies of Lofty Scholars*) states his surname was Lù, given name Tōng, courtesy name Jiē Yú. A worthy recluse of Chǔ, he lived contemporaneously with Confucius but feigned madness and refused office, often tilling the land himself. The King of Chǔ sent an envoy with a hundred *yì* of gold and two teams of four horses to summon him, but he did not respond. Thereupon, he carried his wife on his back and wandered among mountains and seas, their end unknown.

Unworthy is pronounced *dīng làng* (丁浪切). Sīmǎ [Biāo] says it means speech grand but without proper focus.

Frightened terror is pronounced *pǔ bù* (普布切), meaning fear.

*Jìng* is pronounced *gǔ dìng* (古定切). *Tíng* is pronounced *chì dìng* (敕定切). Lǐ [Yún] says *jìng tíng* means excessively provocative. Chéng [Xuányīng] says it means overstepping, also describing a manner of proceeding straight ahead without regard.

*Miǎo* is pronounced *mào* (邈), also *mí zhǎo* (弥沼切). [Emperor] Jiǎnwén says it means distant.

Gūyè is pronounced *yè* (液), Lǐ [Yún] reads it *shí yì* (食亦切), the name of a mountain in the Northern Sea.

Graceful weakness is pronounced *chāo yuē* (昌略切). Lǐ [Yún] says it describes a supple and delicate appearance.

A virgin, as the text says, means one who preserves feminine stillness and is not harmed by external things.

*Cī* is pronounced *zī sī* (在斯切), meaning illness. Sīmǎ [Biāo] says it means slander.

*Pestilence* is pronounced *lì* (厉), Lǐ [Yún] reads it *lài* (赖), referring to severe illness.

*Madness* is pronounced *liáng* (梁). Lǐ [Yún] says it means foolishness.

*Blindness* is pronounced *gǔ* (古), describing eyes without pupils, like drumskin.

*Yǔ hū* is pronounced *yù* (豫), meaning "and" or "with," the same below.

*Observation* is pronounced *gǔ luàn* (古乱切).

*Deafness* is pronounced *lù gōng* (鹿工切), meaning ears that do not hear.

*Blindness!* Seeing the Tiāntái Mountain text, *Fāng Yíng Guān* (方瀛观), the old edition reads *máng* (盲).

A timely maiden, as Sīmǎ [Biāo] says, is like a virgin. Xiàng [Xiū] says a timely maiden is empty, still, gentle, and compliant, harmonious yet not assertive, never seeking others but sought by them.

*Vast* is pronounced *gāng* (刚), also written as *磅*.

"Brick" is pronounced *bó* (博切). Sīmǎ [Biāo] says *páng bó* (旁礴) means "blending as one."

*All things as one*—this phrase concludes here.

*Shì qí* is pronounced *shì qí* (世蕲, 音祈), meaning "to seek."

*Hu* (乎) in the original text was written as *luàn* (乱), an ancient form of the character *zhì* (治); later copyists altered it to *luàn* (乱).

*Bì bì* is pronounced *fú shì* (扶世切). Jiǎn Wén [Xiào] says it describes the appearance of constant striving.

*Jìn* is pronounced *zì zhèn* (子鸩切).

*Qǐ* is pronounced *jī* (鸡), meaning "to reach."

*Drowned* is pronounced *nú lì* (奴历切).

*Dust and grime* is pronounced *gǔ kǒu* (古口切), meaning "to be stained or defiled."

*□* is pronounced *bēi yǐ* (悲矣切).

*Chaff* is pronounced *kāng* (康), meaning "trivial and fragmented."

*Mold* is pronounced *tú dāo* (徒刀切), referring to shaping clay.

*Cast* is pronounced *zhū shù* (朱庶切), meaning "to smelt metal."

*Yáo and Shùn*: Yáo was explained in the previous chapter. The *Records of the Grand Historian* states that Shùn is a posthumous title, with *shùn* (舜) signifying "benevolent, sagely, flourishing, and enlightened." He was the sixth-generation descendant of Emperor Zhuān Xū. His father was called Gǔ Sǒu, and his mother, Wò Dēng, conceived him after perceiving a great rainbow. He was born at Yáo Xū and thus took the surname Yáo. Born by the Guǐ River, he was also called the Guǐ clan. His eyes bore double pupils, so he was styled Chóng Huá. His filial piety and benevolence were renowned in his village. Yáo, hearing of his virtue, wed him to his two daughters and enfeoffed him at Yú. At twenty, his filial piety was widely known; at thirty, he oversaw all government affairs; at fifty, he acted as regent for the Son of Heaven; at fifty-eight, Yáo passed away; and at sixty-one, he succeeded Yáo as emperor. His capital was at Pú Jī—some say Píng Yáng, others Pān Pān (now in Shàng Gǔ). After thirty-nine years on the throne, he died while on a southern inspection tour at Cāng Wú’s wilds and was buried south of the Jiǔ Yí Mountains, in what is now Líng Líng.

*Nameless* and *carefree*: The people of Sòng—modern Suī Yáng County in Liáng Guó—were descendants of Wēi Zǐ, the last ruler of the Shāng Dynasty, enfeoffed there.

*Zī zhāng fǔ*: Lǐ [Yún] says *zī* (资) means "to trade," and *zhāng fǔ* (章甫) refers to the ceremonial cap of the Shāng Dynasty—here, using caps as merchandise.

*Yuè* refers to the region of Kuài Jī in Shān Yīn County.

The term *duàn dīng* (断丁) is pronounced with *tú* (徒) as the initial and *duǎn* (短) as the final; Sīmǎ [Biāo] glosses it as *dūn dūn duàn* (敦敦断), meaning "to sever decisively."

The *Four Masters*, according to Sīmǎ [Biāo], are Wáng Ní, Niè Quē, Bèi Yī, and Xǔ Yóu.

*Fén shuǐ zhī yáng* (汾水之阳): *Fén* (汾) is pronounced with *fú* (扶) as the initial and *yún* (云) as the final. Chéng [Xuányīng] notes: "The Fén River rises in Tài Yuán and flows westward into the Yellow River. The northern bank of a river is called *yáng* (阳). Present-day Píng Yáng County in Jìn Zhōu lies north of the Fén River—this was once Yáo’s capital."

*Yǎo rán* (窅然) is pronounced with *wū* (乌) as the initial and *liǎo* (了) as the final. Lǐ [Yún] explains it as akin to *chàng rán* (怅然), while Chéng [Xuányīng] defines it as "a look of profound depth."

*Sàng qí* (丧其) is pronounced with *sàng* (丧) as the initial and *xī* (息) as the final, meaning "to lose one’s [true nature] in external things" or "to wander carefree."

**Huìzǐ’s Free Roaming**
Huìzǐ, according to Sīmǎ [Biāo], was surnamed Huì and named Shī, a native of Sòng. He served as prime minister to King Huì of Liáng. Chéng [Xuányīng] notes: "King Huì of Liáng was originally King Huì of Wèi. Formerly based in Ān Yì, his state was called Wèi. Later, pressed by the powerful state of Qín, he relocated to Dà Liáng and renamed his state Liáng, adopting the title of king."

*Yí* (贻) is pronounced *yí* (怡) and means "to bestow."

*Hù* (瓠) is pronounced *hù* (护).

*Zhǒng* (种) is pronounced with *zhāng* (章) as the initial and *yǒng* (勇) as the final.

*Ér shí wǔ shí* (而实五石): Chéng [Xuányīng] explains, "It could hold five *dàn* in capacity."

*Shèng* (盛) is pronounced *chéng* (成).

*Pōu* (剖) is pronounced with *pǔ* (普) as the initial and *kǒu* (口) as the final, meaning "to split open." Chéng [Xuányīng] adds, "To divide or cut apart." *Piáo* (瓢) is pronounced with *pí* (毗) as the initial and *yáo* (遥) as the final, meaning "a ladle."

*Zé hù* (则瓠) is pronounced *hù* (户) with *guō* (郭) as the final, or alternatively *hù* (护), as in the following instances. Originally written as *huò* (), pronounced *huò* (镬).

*Luò* (落): Jiǎn Wén [Xiào Dào Chéng] explains *hù luò* (瓠落) as akin to *kuò luò* (廓落), meaning "spacious and open." Sīmǎ [Biāo] states: "*Hù* (瓠) refers to a cloth cover, while *luò* (落) means 'to scatter'—its shape is flat and shallow, so when filled with water, it spills and cannot retain it."

*Xiāo rán* (呺然) is pronounced with *xǔ* (许) as the initial and *qiáo* (桥) as the final. Lǐ [Yún] defines *xiāo* (呺) as "a look of vast emptiness." Cuī [Yuán] writes it as *hào* ().

*Wú wéi* (吾为) is pronounced with *wú* (吾) as the initial and *wěi* (伪) as the final.

*Pǒu zhī* (掊之) is pronounced with *fāng* (方) as the initial and *gòu* (垢) as the final. Sīmǎ [Biāo] explains it as "to strike and break."

*Guī shǒu* (龟手) is pronounced with *guī* (龟) as the initial and *bēi* (悲) as the final. *Guī shǒu* refers to hands chapped and darkened like a tortoise’s shell.

*Píng* (洴) is pronounced with *fú* (扶) as the initial and *jīng* (经) as the final, meaning "to float."

"Pì" (澼) is pronounced with *pǔ* (普) as the initial and *lì* (历) as the final. Lǐ [Yún] defines it as "the sound of washing." Chéng [Xuányīng] explains it as "to manage" or "to prepare."

"Kuàng" (絖) refers to silk floss. It means to prepare silk floss in water. "Kuàng" is pronounced with *kuàng* (旷) as the final. The *Xiǎo Ěryǎ* states, "Fine silk floss is called *kuàng*." Lǐ [Yún] says, "*Píng cí tǒng* (洴辞统) means to float silk floss upon the water. *Kuàng* is silk floss."

"With a hundred gold pieces"—according to an ancient southern manuscript, the previous text was missing. Lǐ [Yún] explains that a *jīn* (金) is a square *cùn* (寸) of gold weighing one *jīn* (斤); thus, a hundred gold pieces means a hundred *jīn*.

"Yù" (鬻) is pronounced with *yù* (育) as the final, meaning "to sell."

"Jì" (技) is pronounced with *bǐ* (彼) as the final, meaning "skill" or "art."

"Yǐ shuì" (以说) is pronounced with *shuì* (始锐) as the final, or may be read as written.

"Yǒu nàn" (有难) is pronounced with *nǎi* (乃旦) as the final.

"Zhī jiàng" (之将) is pronounced with *zǐ* (子匠) as the final.

"Dà bài" (大败) is pronounced with *bì* (必迈) as the final.

"Not considering it a great vessel"—Sīmǎ [Biāo] explains that a *zūn* (樽) resembles a wine vessel; when bound to the body and floated upon rivers or lakes, it can be used to cross, known as a "waist boat." *Lǜ* (虑) means "to plan" or "to ponder."

"The heart of the *péng*"—Guō [Xiàng] says the *péng* does not grow straight. Xiàng [Xiù] explains that the *péng* is short and unyielding, referring to a narrow-minded scholar.

"Carefree and at ease."

"Chū" (樗) is pronounced with *chì* (敕鱼) as the final, the name of a tree. Chéng [Xuányīng] says it is a type of *kǎo* (栲) or *qī* (漆), emitting a foul odor, a worthless tree. Some say it resembles the *chūn* (椿).

"Yōng zhǒng" (拥肿) is pronounced with *zhāng* (章勇) as the final. Lǐ [Yún] explains it as resembling a swollen tumor.

"Not *zhòng*" (中) is pronounced with *dīng* (丁仲) as the final, with the same pronunciation below.

"Juǎn qū" (卷曲) is pronounced with *quán* (权), and Chéng [Xuányīng] explains it as "not straight."

"Tóng qù" (同去) is read as written, while Lǐ [Yún] pronounces it with *qiāng* (羌吕) as the final.

"Lí lì" (狸力) is pronounced with the same initial and final.

"Shēng" (狌) is a surname with two pronunciations. Sīmǎ [Biāo] explains it as *mǐn* (). Chéng [Xuányīng] says it is a wildcat.

"Áo" (敖) is pronounced with *wǔ* (五到) as the final. Zhī [Dùn] says it means to watch for another’s carelessness, taking advantage of their negligence. Sīmǎ [Biāo] pronounces it as *áo* (遨), meaning to watch for wandering creatures to eat, such as chickens or rats.

"Tiào" (跳) is pronounced with *tiáo* (条).

"Not *bì*" (辟) is pronounced with *bì* (避), and the same applies below.

"Jī bì" (机辟) is pronounced with *pí* (毗亦) as the final. Chéng [Xuányīng] explains it as the mechanism of traps, such as overturned carts.

"Wǎng gǔ" (罔罟) is pronounced with *gǔ* (古). Chéng [Xuányīng] explains it as nets and snares.

"Máo niú" (牦牛) is pronounced with *lǚ* (吕之) as the final. Lǐ [Yún] pronounces it with *lái* (来). Chéng [Xuányīng] says the *máo niú* is like the *máo niú* (旄牛), found in the southwestern barbarian regions; its form is immense, and from afar in the mountains, it resembles clouds at the edge of the sky.

"The land of nothingness, the wilds of vastness"—Chéng [Xuányīng] explains *mò* (莫) as "nothing," referring to a broad and desolate place without people, where no matter what is sought, nothing exists; hence, it is called the "land of nothingness."

"Páng" (彷) is pronounced with *gāng* (刚).

"Huáng" (徨) is pronounced with *huáng* (皇).

"Pánghuáng" (彷徨) means soaring freely. An alternate version writes it as *fāngyáng* (方羊). The *Guǎngyǎ* states: *pángyáng* (彷徉) means wandering and lingering. Chéng [Xuányīng] explains it as the name for unrestrained roaming. How could there be any distress or hardship?

*Here concludes the first fascicle of the "Pronunciations and Meanings of the Southern Florescence True Scripture: Chapter Annotations."*
*Here begins the second fascicle of the "Pronunciations and Meanings of the Southern Florescence True Scripture: Chapter Annotations."*
*Composed by Bìxūzǐ*

**Inner Chapters: "On the Equality of Things" (Part Two)**

"Qí wǒ" (齐我): Nánguō Zǐqí (南郭子綦) is pronounced *qí* (其). Luán was the younger half-brother of King Chǔ Zhāo, the Minister of War under King Zhuāng, named Dìng, styled Zǐqí. A man of the Dao, he resided in the southern outskirts, hence his sobriquet.

"Yǐn" (隐) is pronounced *qí* (蕲). It means to lean upon.

"Jī" (几) is pronounced *jǐ* (纪).

"Xū" (嘘) is pronounced *xū* (虚), meaning to exhale.

"Tà yān" (嗒焉) is pronounced *tǔdá* (吐答). It describes a state of bodily dissolution.

"Sì sàng" (似丧) is pronounced *xī làng* (息浪), with the same pronunciation below.

"Qí ǒu" (其偶) is pronounced *wǔ kǒu* (五口). It means a match or counterpart. Sīmǎ [Biāo] explains *ǒu* as the self, where the self and spirit form a pair. Chéng [Xuányīng] says the self and spirit are matched, and things and the self are counterparts. The text states that "losing one’s counterpart" refers to forgetting both form and spirit.

Yánchéng Zǐyóu: Lǐ [Yún] says he was a disciple of Zǐqí, surnamed Yán, named Yǎn, posthumously titled Chéng, styled Zǐyóu.

"Hé jū" (何居) is pronounced *jīn yú* (斤於). It means "where" or "in what state."

"Gǎo mù" (稿木) is pronounced *gǔlǎo* (古老).

"Rén lài" (人籁) is pronounced *lìdài* (力带), referring to the sound of panpipes. *Fēngsú Tōng* states that Shun invented the *xiāo* (箫), its shape uneven like a phoenix’s wings, with ten pipes, each two *chǐ* long. Guō Pú says the *xiāo* pipes are one *chǐ* and two *cùn* long, and it is also called *lài* (籁). Now, examining its meaning, *lài* refers to the multitude of sounds, hence there are "earthly *lài*" and "heavenly *lài*," not merely the *lài* of panpipes.

"Earthly *lài*" are the sounds of the myriad apertures.

"Heavenly *lài*" are the sounds of human *lài* and earthly *lài* converging naturally, thus called "heavenly *lài*." Sound arises from the great *kuài*.

"Dà kuài" (大块) is pronounced *kǔguài* (苦怪) or *kǔduì* (苦对), meaning primordial *qi*, the undifferentiated whole. Sīmǎ [Biāo] says it describes the appearance of great simplicity.

"Yī" (噫) is pronounced *yǐjiè* (乙戒). The text says it means to exhale.

"Wàn qiào" (万窍) is pronounced *kǔdiào* (苦吊).

"Nù xiāo" (怒呺) is pronounced *húdāo* (胡刀). Chéng [Xuányīng] says it means roaring like a furious gale. The text says it is the sound of expulsion.

"Liùliù" (翏翏) is pronounced *liángjiù* (良救), the sound of a long wind. Lǐ [Yún] writes it as *liú* (飂), interchangeable with *liù* (翏).

"Wèi yú guǐ" (畏於鬼) is pronounced *wūzuì* (乌罪).

"Jiā zuì" (佳醉) is pronounced *guǐ* (癸) or *zǐwéi* (子唯). Lǐ [Yún] says *wèijiā* describes the appearance of hills and mounds. Chéng [Xuányīng] says it describes the motion of fluttering.

"The apertures" (之窍) in Cuī’s edition is written as *kuǎn* (窾).

"Resembling a nose or mouth" (似鼻似口): Sīmǎ [Biāo] says when wind blows through apertures, their movements may resemble a human nose or mouth.

"Resembling a *jī*" (似枅) is pronounced *jī* (鸡), a square timber atop a pillar, like the present-day *dǒuliào* (斗). Jiǎn Wén says it refers to *bólu* (欂栌).

"Resembling a pen" (似圈) is pronounced *qǐquán* (起权) or *qíyuàn* (其院), meaning a pen for livestock. Xú Miào says it resembles the enclosures for sheep and pigs.

"Resembling a mortar" (似臼) is pronounced *qíjiǔ* (其九).

"Hollow ones" (洼者) is pronounced *wūxié* (乌携) or *wūwā* (乌蛙). Sīmǎ [Biāo] says it resembles a hanging curve.

"Sunken ones" (污者) is pronounced *wū* (乌). Sīmǎ [Biāo] says it resembles a low, sunken place.

"Jī" (激) is pronounced *jīnglì* (经历), like water surging.

"Gǔ" (古) is pronounced *diào* (吊). Sīmǎ [Biāo] says the sound resembles a sharp cry.

"Xiāo" (謞) is pronounced *xiào* (孝) or *xūjiāo* (虚交) in Lǐ’s edition. Jiǎn Wén says it resembles the sound of an arrow in flight. Sīmǎ [Biāo] says it resembles the sound of *huānxiāo* (讙謞).

"Chì" (叱) is pronounced *chāngshí* (昌实). Sīmǎ [Biāo] says it resembles a sharp rebuke.

"Xī" (吸) is pronounced *xǔjí* (许及). Sīmǎ [Biāo] says it resembles the sound of inhalation.

"Jiào" (叫) is pronounced *gǔdiào* (古吊). Sīmǎ [Biāo] says it resembles a loud cry.

"Háo" (号) is pronounced *háo* (豪). Sīmǎ [Biāo] says it resembles the sound of wailing.

"Yǎo" (宎) is pronounced *yúyáo* (於尧). Another pronunciation is *yǎo* (杳). Sīmǎ [Biāo] says it means deep, like the sound of profound stillness.

"Yǎo" (咬) is pronounced *yújiāo* (於交) or *jiǎo* (狡). Sīmǎ [Biāo] says it is a mournful sound, *yǎo yǎo rán* (咬咬然).

"Chàng" (唱) is read as written.

"Chàng yóng" (唱喁) is pronounced *wǔgōng* (五恭) or *yú* (愚). Lǐ [Yún] says it describes harmonious sounds responding to one another.

"Líng fēng" (泠风) is pronounced *líng* (零). Lǐ [Yún] says it is a gentle, cool breeze.

"Xiǎo hé" (小和) is pronounced *húwò* (胡卧), the same below.

"Piāo fēng" (飘风) is pronounced *bíyáo* (鼻遥). Sīmǎ [Biāo] says it is a swift wind. *Ěryǎ* says a whirlwind is called *piāo*.

"Lì fēng" (厉风) is pronounced as written. Sīmǎ [Biāo] says it is a great wind. *Xiàngxiàng* says it is a fierce wind.

"Jì" (济) is pronounced *zǐxì* (子细). Xiàng says it means to cease.

"Diào" (调) is pronounced *tiáo* (条).

"Diāo" (刁) is pronounced *dūyáo* (都尧). Xiàng says *diàodiāo* (调调) and *diāodiāo* (刁刁) both describe a swaying motion.

"Bǐ zhú" (比竹) is pronounced *pízhì* (毗志) or *bìlǚ* (必履). "Self-attainment" (自取) is explained by Chéng [Xuányīng] as self-contentment.

"Equal wisdom" (齐智) means great wisdom.

"Great wisdom is *xiánxián*" (大智闲闲) is pronounced *héjiān* (何间), meaning tranquil.

"Lesser wisdom is *xiánxián*" (小智闲闲) is pronounced *gǔxiàn* (古苋), meaning separation.

"Yányán" (炎炎) is pronounced *yúlián* (于廉). Chéng [Xuányīng] says it means fierce and intense. Jiǎn Wén says it describes a flourishing appearance.

"Zhānzhān" (詹詹) is pronounced *zhān* (占). Lǐ [Yún] says it describes eloquence. Cuī writes it as *yányán* (阎阎).

"Hún jiāo" (魂交): The text says in sleep, the *hún* and spirit commingle, which is the closing of *yīn* (阴阖).

"Its awakening" (其觉) is pronounced *gǔxiào* (古孝). When awake, the form and consciousness open, which is the opening of *yáng* (阳辟).

"Engaging in construction" (与接为构) means that in the human world, interactions and perceptions weave bonds of affection.

"Màn" (缦) is pronounced *mòdàn* (末旦), meaning relaxed and unhurried.

"Jiào" (窖) is pronounced *gǔxiào* (古孝), meaning deeply rooted.

"Mì" (密) means hidden and concealed.

"Minor fear" (小恐) is pronounced *qūyǒng* (曲勇).

"Zhuìzhuì" (惴惴) is pronounced *zhìruì* (之瑞), meaning the heart is unsettled.

"Mànmàn" (缦缦) is pronounced *màn* (), meaning forgetting one’s nature.

"Jī kuò" (机栝) is pronounced *gǔhuó* (古活), referring to the trigger and arrow-nock of a crossbow.

"Zǔ" (诅) is pronounced *zéjù* (侧据), meaning a curse.

"Méng" (盟) is pronounced *míng* (明), meaning an oath.

"Its decline" (其杀) is pronounced *sèjiè* (色界), meaning decay.

"Its drowning" (其溺) is pronounced *núdí* (奴狄).

"Its suffocation" (其厌) is pronounced *yūyè* (於叶), meaning submersion.

"Like binding" (如缄) is pronounced *gǔxián* (古咸), meaning a rope.

"Lǎo xù" (老洫) is pronounced *yìzhí* (弋质), meaning deep. The meaning is taken from deep drowning.

In the *Jiāngnán* edition, *xù* (洫) is written as *yì* (溢).

"Returning to *yáng*" (复阳) means returning to life.

"Sorrow and joy" (哀乐) is pronounced *yàoluò* (音洛).

"Rè" (热) is pronounced *zhīshè* (之涉).

Sima [Biao] states: an unmoving appearance. Yao [Yuanming] reads it as *yáo* (遥), meaning a light and floating appearance. Another interpretation: long. *Yì* (佚) is read as *yì* (逸), meaning a dissolute and unrestrained appearance. Another interpretation: excess.

*Qǐ jié* (启诘) is pronounced *yǐ qiè* (以切), meaning an open and expansive appearance. *Tài* (态) is pronounced *chì dài* (敕代切), meaning a haughty and indulgent appearance. Another interpretation: intent.

*Zhēng* (蒸) is pronounced *zhī yīng* (之膺切). *Chéng jūn* (成菌) is pronounced *qí yǔn* (其陨切), referring to mushrooms and fungi. Attaining this Dao of forgetting words is also the constant Dao. As it has no forced name, it is thus called this.

The true master is the lord of nurturing life. *Qí zhèn* (其眹) is pronounced *chú rěn* (除忍切), meaning a sign. *Bǎi hái* (百骸) is pronounced *hù jiē* (户皆切), referring to the myriad bones and joints of the body.

The *Jiāngnán* edition of the ancient repository writes *liù zàng* (六藏) as *wǔ zàng

Master Jianwen states it means "to encompass." Its alternate pronunciation is *dì*. The True Lord is the Supreme Unity.

*Niérán* is pronounced *nǎi jié qiè*, as Master Jianwen explains: the appearance of fatigue, illness, and exhaustion.

*Máng hū* is pronounced *mò gāng qiè*, meaning "dim and obscure." The "formed heart" refers to the deluded heart that fabricates right and wrong.

*Yǔ* is read as the character itself, meaning "to align with" or "to associate with."

The *Records of the Divine Yu* states that in posthumous naming conventions, "Yu" signifies "the source of a spring flowing freely" or "receiving the abdication and achieving success." His given name was *Wénmìng*; he was the son of Gun and the father of Qi. The "Divine Yu" refers to the great Yu, who regulated the waters with divine efficacy, hence the epithet.

He conducted an eastern inspection tour and perished at Mount Kuaiji at the age of one hundred.

*Chuī* is read as the character itself, or alternately as *chì ruì qiè*, meaning "to cry out" or "to wail."

*Kòu* is pronounced *kǔ dòu qiè*. Sima Biao explains: when a fledgling is about to emerge, it cries from within the egg, hence the term. Another interpretation: the act of a chick breaking the shell to hatch.

"To equalize right and wrong"—*dào wū hū yǐn* is pronounced *wū*; the same applies below.

"Truth and falsehood" is sometimes written as *zhēn guǐ*. The Cui edition reads *zhēn rán*.

The Confucians revere the Duke of Zhou and King Wen as their founders, while the Mohists revere Yu of Xia.

*Dào shū* is pronounced *chǐ zhū qiè*, akin to the meaning of a door pivot: hollow within yet able to turn things.

The analogy of "pointing to a finger to show that a finger is not the finger" and "using a horse to show that a horse is not the horse"—from Sima Biao, Xiang Xiu, and Guo Xiang to the renowned scholars of the Tang, all have held that this is a parable crafted by Zhuangzi of Qiyuan. Their interpretations and explications diverge in intent.

Now, upon examining the *Six Discourses* of Gongsun Long, which include the *White Horse* and *Pointing to Things* discourses, it becomes clear that Zhuangzi of Qiyuan drew upon and expanded these ideas, which had long existed. Thus, both discourses are appended here after the chapter and verse annotations, for the edification of future generations.

*Gù wéi* is pronounced *yú wěi qiè*. Below, seek meaning through principle.

*Tíng* is pronounced *tíng*.

Sima [Biao] states: *wū* refers to the roof beam.

*Yíng* is pronounced *yíng*.

Sima [Biao] states: *yíng* refers to the house pillar.

*Lì* is read as the standard character, meaning "evil."

*Lài* is pronounced *lài*, referring to leprosy.

Xī Shī: Sima [Biao] states she was Xià Jī. According to the text, she was the beauty sent by Gōu Jiàn to the King of Wú.

*Huī* is pronounced *kuīhuí*. It means "great."

The *Jiǎnwén* edition reads *diào* ("mourning").

*Guǐ* is pronounced *jiǔwěi*.

Chéng [Gōngyuán] states: it means "strange transformations."

*Juéguài* is pronounced *juéguài*.

Lǐ [Yí] states: *jué* means "perverse," *guài* means "anomalous."

*Guài* means "strange."

*Qí fèn* is read as the standard character.

*Fù* is also pronounced *fúyòu*.

Below, seek meaning through principle.

*Jǐ yǐ* is pronounced *jī*.

It means "exhausted."

The phrase *wèi zhī Dào* is punctuated as a complete clause.

Cuī [Xiāng] reads it as *wèi zhī Dào* ("calling it the Dao").

Láo [Zī] states: the merit of the Dao lies in following nature.

*Jūgōng* is pronounced *qīxú*.

Sima [Biao] states: *Jūgōng* was the official in charge of monkeys.

Cuī [Xiāng] states: he was one who raised apes and monkeys.

The *Guǎngyǎ* states: *jū* refers to macaques.

*Fù yù* is pronounced *xù*. It is the same as *zhù* ("acorn").

*Fù* here means "to distribute."

*Zhāo sān mù sì*: Sima [Biao] states: three measures in the morning, four in the evening.

*Tiānjūn*: Cuī [Xiāng]’s edition reads *jūn* ("potter’s wheel"), meaning the shaping of the Dao.

*Zhāo Wén zhī gǔ qín*: Sima [Biao] states: he was an ancient lover of the qín.

*Shī Kuàng*: Chéng [Gōngyuán] states: his courtesy name was Zǐyě, and he was the music master of Duke Píng of Jìn. Some say he was a worthy minister. A native of Nánhé in Jìzhōu, he was born blind but excelled in music, able to summon spirits.

*Zhī zhī zhī cè*: Sima [Biao] states: *zhī* means "pillar," and *cè* means "staff." Cuī [Xiāng] states: it refers to striking the rhythm with a staff.

*Huìzǐ zhī jù wú*: Chéng [Gōngyuán] states: this means Huì Shī used the *wú* tree as a desk to lean upon while discoursing, like one who reclines against a desk. Some say *wú* refers to a qín, but this is incorrect.

Thus, these accounts are recorded at the end of their lives: because their early passions were never abandoned, they are written down for posterity.

Regarding the obscurities of *jiān-bái* ("hardness and whiteness") and the convergence of *tóng-yì* ("same and different"), earlier scholars have also debated them without consensus. Here, the *Discourse on Hardness and Whiteness* by Gōngsūn Lóng is appended after the *Discourse on Pointing to Things* and the *White Horse Discourse*, to supplement uncertainties.

*Ér qí zǐ yòu yǐ Wén zhī lún zhōng*: *lún* is pronounced *lún*, meaning "thread" or "continuity." Cuī [Xiāng] states: it refers to qín strings.

*"Though I have not achieved it, it may yet be called achievement"*—this follows the ancient manuscript from the southern archives. The old edition reads: *"Though I too have achieved it."*

*Gǔyí* is pronounced *gǔmò*. It means "chaos."

*Suǒ tú tú zhě*: *tú* here means "to delineate," like drawing boundaries. It refers to being confined within one’s domain and thus entangled without application.

*Qiūháo*: Sima [Biao] states: rabbit fur forms in autumn and becomes fine and sharp by that season, hence it is used as a metaphor for smallness.

*Shāngzǐ* refers to those who die young. Some say it means those under nineteen years of age.

*Qiǎo lì* means "skilled in calendrical calculations."

*Qí Dào*: *yǒu fēng*—*fēng* means "boundary."

Here the *zhěn* is pronounced *rěn*. *Zhěn* means "boundary" or "border." Li [Shān] reads it as *jù*, referring to the demarcations of domains and paths.

There is left and right—left is yang, right is yin.
There is order and there is principle. Cuī [Xiāng]’s edition reads: "There is discourse and there is deliberation."
There is *fēn*, read as in the original character.
There is distinction—*biàn* means "to differentiate."
There is competition and there is contention: pursuing the same goal is *jìng*, debating face-to-face is *zhēng*; it may also refer to the *zhēng* of strife and conflict.

*bù chèn* is pronounced *zhèng*.
*bù qiān* is pronounced *qīn diàn*. It means "fullness." It also refers to the place where apes store their food. *Bù qiān* thus implies having nothing stored away. Xú Miào reads it as *qiān*, meaning "not greedy and thus without humility."
"Virtue is constant yet not all-encompassing"—this follows the ancient manuscript from the southern archives. The old edition reads: "is complete."

*bù zhì* is pronounced *chǐ*. It means "to oppose" or "to harm."
*yuán* is pronounced *wán*. It means "round."
*jǐ* is pronounced *qí yī*. The celestial treasury holds all without exception.
Concealed radiance obscures all without exception.

*Qí* and *zhì*—both are explained in the *Xiāoyáo* chapter.
*zōng* is pronounced *kuài guài*.
*xū* is pronounced *xī xú*.
*áo* is pronounced *wǔ gāo*. These are the names of three states. Cuī [Xiāng] states: the first is *zōng*, the second *kuài*, the third *xū áo*. Another version reads: *zōng kuài*, *cóng zhī*, *xū áo*, likening them to *péng* and *ài* to describe their flourishing yet base and small states.
The ten suns emerge together—*Hóng liè* explains that in the time of Yáo, ten suns appeared simultaneously, referring to the rebellious southern barbarians. Yì shot them, striking nine suns; the crows fell and died, meaning the nine suns withdrew in hiding.

*Qí wù*—*niè* is pronounced *wǔ jié*.

Fissure: pronounced *qiū yuè*.
Wáng Ní: pronounced *wǔ jī*.
*Gāoshì Zhuàn* states: Wáng Ní and Niè Quē were both worthies of Yáo’s time.

*Wū hū*: pronounced *wū*; same below.
*Yōng jù*: pronounced *qí jù*.
*Zì Lín* reads: "unknown term."
*Yōng* means "ordinary"; *jù* means "how."

Partial death: Sīmǎ explains as "dying from hemiplegia."
Loach: pronounced *qiū*, a kind of fish.
Trembling: pronounced *zhuì ruì*.
Shivering: pronounced *lì*.
Quaking: pronounced *xún*.
All describe fear, dread, and shuddering.

Gibbon: pronounced *yuán hóu*.
Fodder: pronounced *chú jū*.
*Xiǎo Ěryǎ* states: "straw is called *chú*."
Domesticated animals: pronounced *huàn*.
Sīmǎ explains: "oxen and sheep are called *chú*; dogs and pigs are called *huàn*, named after their feed."

Milu: pronounced *méi*.
Chéng explains: "resembles a deer but with a long tail."
Fine grass: pronounced *jiàn liàn*.
Sīmǎ explains: "fine grass."
*Sāncāng* states: "fodder for the six livestock is called *jiàn*."

Centipede: pronounced *jí*.
Maggot: pronounced *zǐ yú*.
*Wú gōng* (centipede).
*Ěryǎ* states: "*Jílí láng qū* resembles a locust, with a large belly and long horns, able to eat a snake’s brain."
Cui explains: "snake."
"*Láng qū* prefers to eat a snake’s eyes."

Owl: pronounced *chǐ yí*, a kite.
Crow: pronounced *wū jiā*, a raven.
Craving: pronounced *shì zhì*.
*Biān*: pronounced *pǐ xiàn*.

The *jū* is pronounced *qī xú rèn*.
Sīmǎ explains: "*Biān jū* is also called *gē zāng*, resembling a gibbon but with a dog’s head. The male delights in mating with gibbons—an unnatural union of male and female across species."
*Gē* is pronounced *gě*; *zāng* is pronounced *zāng*.
Some mistakenly split *biān jū* into two names or *gē qún* into two names—both are incorrect.
*Wéi* (female) is pronounced as written or also *qī*.

*Máo qiáng* is pronounced *zài liáng qiè*.
A beautiful concubine of the King of Yuè.
*Lì jī* is pronounced *lì zhī qiè*.
A favored consort of Duke Xiàn of Jìn, later made his lady.

*Jué* is pronounced *xǐ quē*.
*Zòu* is pronounced *sì qiè*.
*Jué zòu* denotes swiftness.
Cuī explains: "to run recklessly without regard is called *jué zòu*."
*Fán rán* is pronounced *fán*.
*Fán rán* means tangled or chaotic.
*Yáo luàn* is pronounced *hé jiāo qiè*.
Refers to the disorder of dice and counters.

*Hù* is pronounced *hù gù qiè*.
Xiàng explains: "frozen."
Cuī explains: "congealed or dried up."

*Piāo fēng zhèn hǎi* is pronounced *pí xiāo qiè*.
Found in the edition of the Lǐ family of Jiāngnán; previously omitted.

Achievement—thus again demoted—was the mother of Xuānyuán, the Yellow Emperor, who conceived after dreaming of lightning and a celestial being. Born in Shòu...

*Qí sǐ shēng*
*Qú què* is pronounced *zǐ jū qiè*.

The *Dao* of the scholar is termed thus, much like the *Héguànzǐ*.

Chángwúzǐ’s given name was Lì. Lǐ explains: "He dwelled beneath the long catalpa, hence his sobriquet." Jiǎnwén states: "Chángwú was a border warden." The Master (Fūzǐ) explains: "He was the teacher of Qúquè." *Mènglàng* is read as written. Xiàng explains: "*Mènglàng* is pronounced *mànlán*, denoting aimlessness or lack of direction." Lǐ explains: "It means rough or approximate." Cuī explains: "It describes a lack of precision." Some say it means careless or cursory.

*Zhī xíng* is pronounced *zhì mèng*.

The Yellow Emperor is the sagely ancestor and celestial honored one of our dynasty. According to the *Record of the Descent of the Nine Heavens’ Controller of Life and Preserver of Life, the Celestial Honored One*, composed by Emperor Zhēnzōng on the twenty-fourth day of the tenth month in the fifth year of Dàzhōng Xiángfú, "We were nine sovereigns of humanity; the central one among them is the progenitor of the Zhào lineage. In secular records, it is said of Us that We spoke at birth and later attained the Dao to ascend to heaven."

*Tīng chì* is pronounced *dìng qiè*. *Yíng* is pronounced *yú jiǒng qiè*. To acknowledge the Dao as wondrous and eternal is like perceiving jade’s hue by listening with the ear.

*Shíyè* refers to the rooster’s ability to mark the hours of the night. *Jiàn* is pronounced *tú dàn qiè*. *Xiāo* is pronounced *yú jiāo qiè*.

The *Máo Shī Cǎo Mù Shū* states: "It is large or small like a turtledove, green in color, and its flesh is most savory—well suited for roasting." The *Běn Cǎo Shí Yí* by Chén Cángqì notes: "The *xiāo* was prized by the ancients for its roasted meat, which was surely rich and fine. It is also called *xiāo*, *xiū*, or by the people of Wú as *□hún*—an ill-omened bird of harsh cry." Jiǎ Yí writes: "The *péng* resembles the *xiāo*, though they are in fact the same creature." Another name is *yú hú*, an inauspicious bird.

*Zhī xī* is pronounced *xī*, meaning "what." *Shěn* means "to discern what sound." *Bó* is pronounced *bó* or *bó* (as in *bó zàng*, "meager burial," or *bó guāng*, "dim light"). *Yī* means "clothing." *Xié* is pronounced *dié*. Cuī’s text reads *fú*. *Yǔ zhòu* is pronounced *zhì jiù*. The *Shī Zǐ* states: "The four directions of heaven and earth are called *yǔ*; past and future are called *zhòu*." *Wěn* is pronounced *zhěn*. Lǐ notes: "It describes a boundless expanse." *Xiàng* is pronounced *chún*, like the meeting of two breasts. *Gǔ* is pronounced *gǔ*—meaning "chaos." The original text reads *mì*. *Mǐn* is pronounced *hūn*. *Gǔ hùn* means "turmoil and confusion, an unsettled state." *Yú ān* is pronounced *tú bēn*—"dull and ignorant, moving blindly forward." Liú’s text reads *mào* (pronounced *mò bào*), with a note: "Like a fool in a stupor." The *Shuō Wén* defines *mào* as "grasses spreading and trailing." *Xiāng yùn* is pronounced *yū*—meaning "to accumulate." *Yú wù* is pronounced *wū* (identical to "where" in *wū hū*). *Wù sǐ* is pronounced *wū lù*. *Ruò sàng* is pronounced *xī làng*. The text states: "The people of Qín, as children, moved their households to Chǔ; longing for Chǔ, they forgot Qín—hence *ruò sàng*." *Lì Jī* has been explained earlier. *Ài* refers to the land of Ài in the state of Líróng, where the daughter of a border guard resided. *Wáng suǒ* is noted by Cuī as "Duke Xiàn, borrowing the title of king." *Qí* is pronounced *qí*—meaning "to seek." *Jué* is pronounced *jiào* (the same below).

Silent and self-serving—such is the manner of private gain, like a thief’s hoarding for himself. *Diào* is read as written: to mourn the living is *yàn*; to mourn the dead, *diào*. *Guǐ* is pronounced *jiǔ wěi*; its gloss reads *xiè*. This refers to encounters at dawn or dusk. The master of the Dao, viewing ten thousand generations as but a dawn or dusk, mirrors the *Shíyíjì* of Wáng Zǐnián: "The fusang tree withers once in ten thousand years—mortals see it as fleeting as dawn or dusk." Such is its kind.

To equalize the same and the different—*nán àn* is pronounced *àn*, meaning obscure and unclear, as Lǐ explains. *Wū néng* is pronounced *wū* (identical below). *Tiān ní* has two pronunciations, *cuī* and *ní*—the natural division. "Is *this* different from *not-this*? Then there is no dispute." (From the ancient southern repository; the old text reads "also no dispute.") "If it is truly *so*, is *so* different from *not-so*? Then there is no dispute." (From the ancient southern repository; the old text omits the character *yǐ*.) The clamor of voices debating right and wrong is but transformation itself.

*Màn* is pronounced *wàn*. *Yǎn* is pronounced *yǐ zhàn*. The text states: "the manner of ceaseless flow." Sīmǎ explains: "the manner of boundlessness." *Wǎn* means to expand or to halt; to lack limit or end, to exhaust. Cuī writes *jìng* (boundary). To equalize by following—*wǎng liǎng*: Guō explains it as "the faint yin beyond the shadow." Xiàng says: "the shadow’s shadow." Cuī’s original text reads *wǎng làng*, glossed as "the state of being and non-being." *Nǎng* is pronounced *nǎi dàng*; Lǐ explains it as "formerly." *Wú tè* means "not alone"; some write it as *chí* (to hold). *Cāo* and *yǔ* share the same pronunciation. *Shé fù* is pronounced *fù*; Chéng explains: "*shé fù* is the shedding of a snake’s skin." *Tiáo yì*: Chéng explains that the wings of the *tiáo* are not true wings but refer to the cicada’s molted shell, akin to *tiáo jiǎ* in the "Yùyù" chapter. The text means: unlike the *shé fù* or *tiáo yì*, which depend on external conditions, my solitary transformation arises from the natural—what does it depend upon?

To equalize through transformation—*hú dié* is pronounced *tú xié* (butterfly). *Jiá dié* is the same. *Xǔ xǔ* is pronounced *kuàng fǔ*; the text states: "the manner of attuning to one’s nature." Chéng explains: "the manner of joyful ease." Xú says: "the manner of delight." Cuī’s original text reads *piān piān* (fluttering). *Zì yù*: Lǐ explains it as "pleasure"; Chéng, as "awakening." *Zhì* and *yǔ* share the same pronunciation (identical below). *Jué* is pronounced *gǔ xiào*. *Qú qú* is pronounced *qú*; Lǐ explains it as "having form." The text states: "the manner of astonishment." Cuī’s original text reads *jù jù*, citing the *Dàzōngshī*: "*jù rán* awakens." Modern texts also write it as *qú*. (From the "Nourishing Life" chapter of the *Inner Chapters*, part three.)

To nourish one’s nature and destiny—*yá* is pronounced *yú jiā* (limit). It means division; some write it as *yái* (boundary). "Follow the *dū* as the constant"—*yuán* means "to follow"; *dū* means "correct"; *jīng* means "constant." *Yǐ* is pronounced *yǎng shàng*. To attain the principle of life—*páo dīng* is pronounced *bái jiāo*. *Páo* refers to the kitchen; *dīng* is his name. Chéng explains: "a servant in charge of the kitchen." The *Guǎnzǐ* mentions *Túniú Tǎn*, who could dismember nine oxen in a morning, his blade fine enough to shave hair. *Wén Huì Jūn*: Sīmǎ explains it as King Huì of Liáng. *Suǒ yǐ* is pronounced *yú qǐ* (to lean upon). *Suǒ yǐ* is pronounced *yǐ* (to gore, to stab). *Huō rán* is pronounced *hū jué*—Sīmǎ explains it as the sound of skin and bone separating. *Xiǎng rán* has two pronunciations, *xǔ wén* and *xǔ liàng*—it means to advance. *Huō rán* is pronounced *hū huò*; Cuī explains it as "a sound greater than *shē*." *Zhòng* is pronounced *dīng zhòng* (identical below). *Sāng lín*: Sīmǎ explains it as the name of Tāng’s music; Cuī says it is the name of a dance from Sòng, citing the *Zuǒzhuàn* (Duke Xiāng, tenth year): "The Duke of Sòng entertained the Marquis of Jìn at Chǔqiū, requesting to perform the *Sāng lín*." *Sāng lín* is the name of the Son of Heaven Tāng’s music. *Jīng shǒu*: Sīmǎ explains it as the name of a chapter in the *Xiánchí* music, which is Yáo’s music; some say it is the name of Qín’s music. *Xī* is pronounced *xī*—an exclamation. *Jì* is pronounced *jǔ qǐ* (skill; identical below). *Suǒ hào* is pronounced *hū bào*.

The minister engages with the spirit—*xiàng* here means to meet in darkness and align with the principle, hence "spiritual encounter." Not with the eyes does he see, but through the Dao’s lingering illumination. The senses halt at their posts—this is the method. The method lies in knowing when to stop, yet the principle cannot be paused, thus in a single morning he unravels the nine oxen. Yet the spirit desires to traverse the empty hollows, having forgotten itself.

*Pī* is pronounced *bèi jié* or *pū mí*—it means to strike. *Dà què* is pronounced *qù nì*—it refers to the spaces where joints intersect. *Dà kuǎn* is pronounced *kǔ guǎn*—it means hollow. *Kěn* is pronounced *kǔ děng*—it denotes where flesh adheres to bone. One says it is bone without flesh. Xǔ Shūzhòng explains it as "bone and flesh clinging tightly." *Qìng* is pronounced *kǔ tǐng*—Sīmǎ says it is where sinews and bones converge. The text reads *kěn yíng*, meaning the points of intersection.

*Dà gū* is pronounced *gū*—Guō explains it as the large, twisted bones. Cuī says it refers to the coiled, knotted bones. *Liáng páo*: *liáng* means good, skilled. To cut—Sīmǎ explains it as using a knife to sever flesh, hence the blade is renewed yearly. Cuī says the knife is replaced once a year, yet still suffices for cutting. *Zú páo*: Sīmǎ explains *zú* as mixed, numerous. *Xíng* is pronounced *xíng*—a whetstone. *Wéi* is pronounced *jiè yú wěi*—identical below. *Huò rán* is pronounced *huà bǎi*—Chéng explains it as the sound of bone and flesh parting.

Already unraveled—*yǐ jiě* is pronounced *xiè*—identical below. The ox knows not its death, as seen in the text like the sea. (Liu’s alternate edition; the old version was missing.) *Tí dāo* is pronounced *tú jī*. *Chóu* is pronounced *zhí liú*. *Chú* is pronounced *zhí yú*—an appearance of leisurely ease. *Shàn dāo ér cáng zhī*: *shàn* means to skillfully, carefully. To sheathe the blade is not to treat it roughly. *Yí xíng lěi fù*: Wén Xuān—Sīmǎ explains he was surnamed Gōngwén, named Xuān, a man of Sòng. *Yòushī* is an official title, a man of Sòng. *Wū hū* is pronounced *wū*.

"Jiè" is pronounced *jiè*. Cuī’s edition writes it as *wù*, or alternatively as *yuè*—amputation of the foot. Guō explains *jiè* as partial amputation. "Tiān yǔ qí rén yǔ" are both read as written, or alternatively both pronounced *yú*. Sīmǎ explains it as "by Heaven’s mandate, by human affairs." "Shǐ dú" is explained by Sīmǎ as "one foot" meaning *dú*, or *dú* meaning *jiè*. "Yǒu yǔ" is read as written. Guō explains it as "two feet walking together" meaning *yǒu yǔ*.

Far from the cage—
The marsh pheasant is a pheasant of the marsh. *Yī zhuó* is pronounced *zhì jué*. *Bù qí* is pronounced *qí*—to seek. *Fán zhōng* is pronounced *fán*; Lǐ explains it as *fān*—that which cages the pheasant. Though it be a king—pronounced *kuàng*. Its spirit and qi are full and at ease, or alternatively, abundant.

Liberation from the bond—
Lǎo Zhí is pronounced *tǔ lán*. *Guǎng shèng yì* states his name was Bóyáng, or alternatively Dān, born in Kǔ County of Chǔ, in Jī Xiāng. He stood nine *chǐ* tall, with a stride of five *bǎ* and ten, fine brows and a square mouth, double pillars and three hollows, a sun on his forehead and a moon in his eyes—possessing the marks of a great sage. He dwelt on the south side of Mount Qí, where the Earl of the West summoned him to be Archivist. In the time of King Kāng, he served as the Historian Beneath the Pillar; in the time of King Zhāo, he crossed west through the Hánguān Pass, having lived in Zhōu for over two hundred years. Qín Shī—alternatively written as *xiá*—is read according to the character. A friend of Lǎo Jūn.

The Three Titles—pronounced *hù gāo*.
That man—alternatively, the Perfected One. The lesser—pronounced *shào*. *Dùn tiān*—pronounced *tú xùn*—to flee, to escape the principles of Heaven. *Bèi qíng*—pronounced *bù duì*—to add. To add to the common emotions. Sorrow and joy—pronounced *luò*; identical below. Alternate edition writes it as "care and joy." *Xiàn jiě*—Cuī explains life as bondage, death as liberation. *Huǒ chuán*—pronounced *zhí zhuān*. *Chuán* means transmission, continuation.

The Second Scroll of the *Nánhuá Zhēnjīng Zhāngjù Yīnyì* Concludes
The Third Scroll of the *Nánhuá Zhēnjīng Zhāngjù Yīnyì*
Composed by Bìxūzǐ

**Inner Chapters: "The Human World" (Part Four: "Transformation and Guidance")**

Yán Huí—*Shǐjì* records his courtesy name as Zǐyuān, a native of Lǔ. At twenty-nine, his hair turned entirely white, and he died young.
Zhòngní—*Shǐjì* records his surname as Kǒng, given name Qiū, courtesy name Zhòngní, a native of Lǔ and descendant of Tāng. Born in an age of decline, he possessed the virtue of a great sage and was Huí’s teacher.
The Lord of Wèi—*Sīmǎ* states he was Wèi Zhuānggōng, named Méikuì. According to the *Zuǒzhuàn*, Wèi Zhuānggōng first entered the state in the winter of the fifteenth year of Lǔ Āigōng’s reign, by which time Yán Huí had already died and could not have served him. This must refer to Chū, the Duke Chū.

*Xíng*—pronounced *xià mèng*.
Cui alone explains it as "self-reliance."
*Guó liàng*—pronounced *liàng*.
*Ruò jiāo*—pronounced *sì yáo*. *Jiāo* is an insubstantial grass, signifying that virtue and grace are but hollow beauty.
*Yī yú qí*—pronounced *yú qí*. To reflect on one’s conduct may yet bring the state close to order.
*Liào hū*—pronounced *chǒu yóu*. Improvement.
The passage *"suǒ xíng"*—the Jiāngnán Lǐ edition was formerly missing.
*Xī*—pronounced *xī*, an exclamation of sighing.
*"Ruò wǎng ér dài xíng ěr"*—seen in Zhāng Jūnfáng’s edition; formerly *"ruò dài wǎng ér xíng ěr."*
*Suǒ wéi*—pronounced *zǐ wěi*.
*Xiāng zhá*—pronounced *yú diǎn*. To crush or trample, as a cart’s wheels do.
*Xìn qiàng*—pronounced *kǔ jiāng*. Jiǎnwén explains it as the appearance of sincerity.
*Qiǎng*—pronounced *qiáng liǎng*.
*Xuàn yíng*—pronounced *juàn*. To sell.
Seen in the ancient Jiāngnán repository edition; formerly written as *"shù."*
*Rén wù*—pronounced *wū lù*. Below, *"wù"* (evil) and *"bùxiào"* (unworthy) are the same. To possess one’s beauty.
Cui’s edition writes *"rén wù,"* and Yù explains it as "to sell," which aligns with the meaning of *"xuàn mài"* above.
*Zāi*—pronounced *zāi*, identical below.
*Zāi fū*—pronounced *jué*.
*Wù yòng*—pronounced *wū*. *"Ruò wéi wú zhào"* forms a complete phrase—*"zhào"* means to instruct, to speak.
*Qí jié*—pronounced *zài jiē*.
*Yíng zhī*—pronounced *hù biǎn*.
Jié—*Shǐjì* records that in posthumous titles, "oppressing the people and excessive killing" is called *Jié*.
Guān Lóngféng—Chéng explains he was a worthy minister of Jié, executed for his utmost sincerity.
Zhòu—*Shǐjì* records that in posthumous titles, "violating righteousness and harming goodness" is called *Zhòu*.
Prince Bǐgān—Chéng explains he was Zhòu’s uncle, disemboweled for his loyal remonstrance.
*Fǔ*—pronounced *fǔ*. Lǐ explains *"yǔ fǔ"* as an expression of affection.
Below, *fú*—pronounced *fú fèi*. To oppose and defy.
*Yǐ jǐ*—pronounced *zǐ jì*. To push aside.
*Shì hào*—pronounced *hū bào*.
*Cóng zhī*—pronounced *cái gōng*.
*Xū áo*—explained in the *"Equalizing Things"* chapter.
Yǒu Hù—pronounced *hù*. Sīmǎ states it was a state name, located in Shǐpíng Prefecture. According to records, it is present-day Hù County in Jīngzhào.
*Xū lì*—read as written. Also pronounced *xū*. A dwelling without people is called *xū*; dying without descendants is called *lì*.
*Yǔ wǒ*—pronounced *yú jù*, identical below—means to inform.

"Wù'è"—pronounced *wū*. An inarticulate sound of disapproval. Identical below.
"Róngyǔ"—Chéng explains as indulgence. The text states "to give free rein to one's emotions."
"Bù zǐ"—pronounced *zǐ*, to slander.
"Qíhū"—pronounced *qí*.
"Qíng"—pronounced *jīng*. To cup the hands in respect.
"Jì"—pronounced *jǐ*. To kneel upright.
"Qíng" means to clasp the hands; "jì" means to kneel on the heels.
"Qūquán"—pronounced *quán*. Ritual deportment.
"Wú cī"—pronounced *cái sī*, illness.
"Zhé"—pronounced *zhé*, punishment.
"Bù móu"—pronounced *tú xié*. In military contexts, a double agent is called a *dié*.
"Shī xīn"—self-reliance, one’s fixed mindset. To act with intention—is it easy?
"Xīn yī zhī"—as seen in Zhāng Jūnfáng’s edition, originally written: "to act with intention—is it easy?"
"Hàotiān"—pronounced *hú lǎo*. "Hào" means brightness. It is not *shuōwén*; "yí" means what is fitting.
"Bù rú"—pronounced *rǔ*, to eat.
"Hūn"—pronounced *xūn*, pungent vegetables.
The phrase "wèi shǐ dé shǐ" should be read with a pause after "shǐ"—meaning one has never attained the state of being employed with an empty mind to possess this body.
"Its boundaries and divisions."
"Quē"—pronounced *kū xuè*, emptiness.
"Shēng bái"—"bái" refers to yang brightness.
"Jíxiáng zhǐ zhǐ"—"zhǐ zhǐ" is the Way of auspiciousness.
When emptiness and stillness extinguish thought, the Dao gathers.
"Zuò chí"—to sit in body while the mind wanders.
"Xùn cí"—pronounced *jùn*. Lǐ explains as "to employ."
"Suǒ niǔ"—pronounced *nǚ jiǔ*, to bind.
Chéng explains as "guiding cords."
"Fúxī"—pronounced *xū yí*, the same as Tàihào, the first of the Three Sovereigns.
"Jī qú"—pronounced *jī jū*. Xiàng explains as an ancient emperor.
"Sàn yān"—pronounced *xī dàn*. Lǐ explains as "to release."
Cuī explains that when virtue does not reach the sage-kings, it is called "scattered."
"Mìng shǐ"—pronounced *shè* according to the Duke of Shè.

The text states: His surname was Shěn, given name Zhūliáng, courtesy name Zǐgāo. He was a distant descendant of King Zhuāng of Chǔ and the son of Shěn Yǐnshù. Serving as a grandee of Chǔ, he was enfeoffed at Yè and presumptuously styled himself "Duke." At the time, the northern Di tribes invaded Chǔ, and King Zhāo of Chǔ, filled with dread, dispatched Zǐgāo to seek aid from Qí. *Jiàng shǐ*—pronounced *suǒ lì*, the envoy. Below, *shǐ* is the same. *Jiàng*—one who is commanded. *Wáng shǐ*—pronounced *shǐ yì*, to dispatch. *Lì zhī*—*lì* is pronounced *lì*; Lǐ explains it as "fear." *Cháng yǔ*—pronounced *yú jù*. Below, the same. *Guǎ bù dào yǐ huān*—in the ancient Jiāngnán manuscript, it reads *guǎ yǒu bù dào yǐ chéng huān*. *Zhí cū*—pronounced *cū*, glutinous. *Bù zāng*—pronounced *láng*, good. *Cuàn*—pronounced *qī luàn*. *Yù qīng*—pronounced *qī xìng*. The character should follow *qīng*; *cóng qīng* is a loan usage. *Qīng*—cool. *Rè yǔ*—pronounced *yú*. Below, *shèn* is the same. *Yǐ rèn*—pronounced *ér lín*. *Āi lè*—pronounced *luò*. Below, the same. *Bù yì*—read as written, "unchanging"; also pronounced *yǐ*, meaning "to alter." Also pronounced *yǐ chǐ*, meaning "frivolous." *Ér wù*—pronounced *wū lù*. Below, the same. *Hū liàng*—sincerity and trustworthiness. *Sāng*—pronounced *xī làng*. Below, the same. *Yì yǐ*—pronounced *yǐ chǐ*. Below, the same. *Piān cí*—pronounced *piān*; Cuī’s version writes *piǎn*, pronounced *biàn*. *Qì xī fú rán*—pronounced *fú fú*. Cuī pronounces it *bó*. *Xīn lì*—read as written; Lǐ pronounces it *lài*. *Kè hé*—pronounced *xìng gé*. The *Book of Hàn*, in the "Biography of Emperor Xuān," states: "to investigate and verify names and realities." The *Scripture of Great Peace* contains a chapter titled "On Verification," as the ancients used *hé* interchangeably with *hé* (impeachment). *Shī fù*—Yán Hé, pronounced *hú là*.

Cui’s version writes *Lú*. A worthy man of Lǔ, a recluse. He first served Duke Dìng of Lǔ, then went to Wèi to become the master and transmitter for the crown prince of Duke Líng. The *Zuǒzhuàn* on Duke Líng states his given name was *Yuán*; by the rules of posthumous naming, "disorderly yet unharmed" is called *Líng*. The Grand Marshal of the Crown Prince states this was *Kuǎi Kuì*, who later became Duke Zhuāng. *Qú*—pronounced *qū*. Bóyù was his courtesy name, his given name *Ài*; a worthy grandee of Wèi. *Tiān shā*—natural inclination to kill as virtue. *Wú fāng*—Lǐ states *fāng* means the Dao. *Wéi jué*—pronounced *juē*; to fail. *Wéi xiān*—pronounced *yāo*; earth’s inversion of things is called *xiān*. *Wéi niè*—pronounced *yè*; calamity. Alternatively written *niè*. *Yīng’ér*—Cui states this means "to indulge in arrogance and wandering." *Wú tǐng*—pronounced *dǐng*. *Qí*—pronounced *guī*. Lǐ states *tǐng qí* means "boundaries and ridges." *Wú wēi yí mào*—lacking dignified bearing. *Wú yá*—Wén states this means "without ritual propriety." *Wú cī*—pronounced *yí*; illness. *Tángláng*—pronounced *táng láng*; the axe-wielding insect. *Shèng rèn*—pronounced with level tone. *Jǐ yǐ*—Wén’s version writes *jǐ dài yǐ*. *Wéi qí*—pronounced *yú*; below, the same.

Mèi yǎng chéng yún: to comply brings joy; flattery can tame and familiarize.

Shèng—pronounced *chéng*; below, the same.

Shǐ—alternatively written *shǐ*, identical in pronunciation.

Yǐ niǎn—pronounced *shěn*.

Gé lèi.

Nì—pronounced *nào*.

Liú dé—alternatively written *niào*, composed of *wěi* (tail) and *shuǐ* (water); *wén*—pronounced *wén*.

Mèng—pronounced *gēng*.

Pū—pronounced *pǔ*.

Xiàng yún: *pū pū rán*, the appearance of mosquitoes swarming and clustering.

Cuī—pronounced as written.

Yún: *pū* means to serve.

Ér fǔ—pronounced *fǔ*.

Bù cái è míng: a bad reputation for uselessness.

Jiàng shí—*jiàng* is the general term for craftsmen; *shí* is the given name.

Qū yuán—pronounced *yuán*; *qū yuán* is the name of a road.

Lì—pronounced *lì*; a useless tree.

Shè—an altar of earth is called *shè*.

Tǔ—pronounced *tǔ*; earth.

Yún: able to bring forth the myriad things, hence called *shè*.

Qí dà—pronounced *bì*.

Several thousand oxen—see Zhāng Jūnfáng, Chéng Xuányīng, and Wén Rúhǎi’s editions; previously omitted.

Xù—pronounced *xiè*.

*Jí yùn* states: *xù* means to bind and measure its size.

Yán Shīgǔ’s commentary on the *Hànshū* states: *xù* means to wind around.

Bǎi wéi—Lǐ states: a *wéi* is a span of one *chǐ*; roughly ten *zhàng*.

Ten *rèn* equal seven *chǐ*.

Guān zhě—pronounced *guān*; below, the same.

Jiàng bó—*bó* means elder; the chief among the many craftsmen.

Bù chuò—pronounced *dì*.

Yàn—pronounced *yàn*.

Sàn mù—pronounced *sàn*; a tree is called *sàn* for its uselessness.

Sù fǔ—pronounced *fǔ*; to rot.

Làn—pronounced *làn*.

Yè—pronounced *yì*.

Mén—pronounced *mán* or *mò qiān*.

Sīmǎ states: *yè* means bodily fluids, referring to resin flowing *mén mén rán*.

Cuī states: black liquid exudes.

Dù—pronounced *dù*.

Jiàn—pronounced *hù*; *mèng*—pronounced *jiàn*.

Wū hū—pronounced *wū*; below, the same.

Wén mù—refers to the beauty of its timber.

Lú—pronounced *zhā*.

Jú—pronounced *jūn*.

Yòu—pronounced *yòu*.

Guǒ luǒ—pronounced *lǐ guǒ*; tree fruits are called *guǒ*; herb fruits are called *luǒ*.

Xiè—pronounced *liè*; to wither and fall to the ground.

Wěi—Cuī’s version writes *kū*.

Pǒu—pronounced *pǒu*; to strike.

The "scattered man" nearly perished—here the sentence ends.

How could they know that the scattered tree (craftsmen and Shi) is called "scattered" for its uselessness, while the oak of the altar is deemed useless despite its utility? Thus, the craftsman Shi is called the "scattered man."

*Jué*—pronounced *gǔ xiào*.

*Zhěn*—pronounced *zhí xìn*.

To interpret dreams.

*Gòu*—pronounced *hū dòu*.

*Lì*—pronounced as written; to revile and humiliate.

*Lì*—illness.

*Jī*—pronounced *jī*; near.

*Jiǎn*—pronounced *qiǎn*; to fell.

The spirit does not boast of its ability—Nanbo Ziqi is the same as Nan Guozi in the "Equalizing Things" chapter.

*Bó*—elder.

Shangqiu—Sima states: this is present-day Suiyang County in Liang State.

A thousand chariots, each drawn by four horses, are called a *shèng*.

"About to retreat, he shelters in the sounds of his flute"—as seen in Zhang Junfang’s edition; the old version reads: "retiring, he shelters in the sounds of his flute."

*Cái fū*—pronounced *fú*.

*Quán qū*—pronounced *quán*; crooked.

*Zhóu*—pronounced *zhú*; straight.

*Jiě*—like the straight unraveling of a garment’s spindle.

*Shì*—pronounced *zhǐ*; to eat.

*Xiù*—pronounced *xǔ jiù*.

*Kuáng chéng*—pronounced *chéng*; the madness of drunkenness.

Illness from wine is called *xǐng*.

Material attainment invites misfortune.

Jing Shi—Sima states: this is a place name; *Yi Dan* is a village name, and the state of Song possessed the lands of Jing Shi.

*Yi qiu bai sang*—these three are fine timber trees.

*Gǒng*—pronounced *gōng yǒng*.

*Bǎ*—pronounced *bǎi yǎ*.

Sima states: two hands together are called *gǒng*; one hand is called *bǎ*.

*Jū*—pronounced *qī yú*.

*Hóu*—pronounced *hóu*; the macaque monkey.

*Zhī yì*—pronounced *yǐ zhí*.

Cheng states: a stake or peg, used to tether the macaque monkey.

Sima’s original text reads *pá*, pronounced *bā*, stating: intended to provide a perch for the macaque to play upon.

Cui’s edition reads *bá*, pronounced *bá*, stating: a cangue.

Three *wéi* in circumference, one *chǐ* in diameter—this is called *wéi*.

*Zhī lì*—pronounced as written; the eaves of a house.

*Qiú shàn*—pronounced *shàn*.

*Páng bó*—pronounced *bó gāng*.

Sima states: the complete side of a coffin is called *shàn páng*.

*Gǔ jiě*—pronounced *gǔ mài* or *jiā mǎi*.

*Fú*—exorcism; a sacrifice to ghosts.

*Sǎng*—pronounced *xī dǎng*; the forehead.

*Praise.*

*Kàng bí*—pronounced *kǔ zàng*.

Sima states: high.

*È*—a broken nose that is both high and upturned.

*Zhì*—pronounced *zhí lǐ*.

*Shì Hé*—Sima states: to drown a person in the river as a sacrifice.

When Ximen Bao served as magistrate of Ye, he put an end to this practice.

Spreading virtue to nourish the body.

*Zhī lí shū chéng*—"The Four Limbs Disjointed" states: the four limbs are split apart, the hundred parts of the body are loose and unconstrained—hence the name.

*Yí*—pronounced *zhī zhī*.

*Wū dǐng*—Sima states: the spine is curved and the neck is retracted.

*The Master of Huainan* states: the spinal column rises higher than the crown of the head.

*Huì*—pronounced *gǔ wài* or *gǔ huó*.

*Cuō*—pronounced *zǐ wài* or *zǐ huó*.

*Zhǐ tiān*—Sima states: a topknot that points to the heavens. In ancient times, the topknot was at the center of the nape; with the spine curved and the neck bent, the topknot thus pointed upward.

*Wǔ guǎn zài shàng*—Li states: *guǎn* refers to the *shù* points. The *shù* points of the five viscera are all located above.

*Liǎng bì*—pronounced *bì*; Cui states: a hunchback’s abdomen is tucked within the thighs.

*Wéi xié*—pronounced *xǔ jié*.

*Cuò*—pronounced *cùn wò*; to crush or break.

*Zhēn*—pronounced *zhí jīn*; Sima states: a crushed needle used for sewing clothes.

*Zhì xiè*—pronounced *xiè*; to mend old clothes. Refers to patching the sleeves of worn garments.

*Hú kǒu*—pronounced *hú*; to sustain oneself with food.

*Gǔ jiá*—pronounced *chū gé*; Cui states: to cast yarrow stalks for divination.

*Bō jīng*—Cui states: to divine hexagrams and interpret omens.

*Yǐ shí*—pronounced *sì*.

*Ráng*—pronounced *rú yáng*.

*Sān zhōng*—Sima states: six *hú* and four *dǒu* are called a *zhōng*.

**Those who bring about calamity through their actions**

When Confucius traveled to Chu, *The Records of the Grand Historian* states: Confucius was besieged between Chen and Cai. King Zhao of Chu raised an army to welcome Confucius, and Confucius proceeded to Chu.

*Jǐn*—pronounced *jìn*; talent or ability.

*Huà dì*—pronounced *huò*; to retreat. Refers to withdrawing the body and bending it to preserve wholeness.

*Qū què qū què*—pronounced *qǐ yuē*; to retreat. The Zhang Junfang edition reads *qū què qū què*; the old text reads *wú xíng yù qū*. This is an error in transcription. *Què* is pronounced *qǐ nì*, but this reading is not adopted here.

**Inner Chapters: "Virtue’s Full Manifestation" V**

**Mirroring the Dao**

*Wù zhě*—pronounced *wù hū*; one who has undergone foot amputation is called *wù*.

*Wáng Tái*—pronounced *tái dài*.

A man of Lu, though no records of him survive.

*Cóng*—pronounced as written; Li reads it as *cái*. The same applies below.

*Cháng Jì*—a disciple of Confucius.

Kong Qiu—*zhí* pronounced *hòu*—had yet to journey forth, for *zhí* refers to being still in the womb’s aftermath. Thus, he had not yet gone to study under a master.

*Wáng*—pronounced *kuàng*—means flourishing or surpassing.
