How vastly he differs from the mediocre! Confucius himself humbled himself beneath the common, was this not excessive?

*Dì fù*—pronounced *fāng fú*—to fall.

*Zhuì*—pronounced *zhí lèi*.

Yet he guarded his lineage. [From the Jiangnan manuscript collection; omitted in older editions.]

*Gān dǎn*—pronounced *dīng lǎn*.

*Suǒ sàng*—pronounced *xī làng*. The same below.

*Zuì zhī*—pronounced *cú huì*—to gather.

*Jiàn*—pronounced *gǔ zhàn*—flowing water. The Cui edition reads *mò shuǐ*.

Receiving its mandate from earth, only the pine and cypress stand alone, ever verdant through winter and summer. Receiving its mandate from heaven, only Yao and Shun stand alone, foremost among the ten thousand things. [From the Zhang Junfang edition; omitted in older editions.]

*Bǎo shǐ zhī zhēng*—Li explains *zhēng* as completion. The beginning and end may be preserved in completion.

*Nine Armies*—Li notes: the Son of Heaven has six armies, the feudal lords three, together making nine armies. Emperor Jianwen states: the military texts speak of attacking the nine heavens and seizing the nine earths, hence the term *nine armies*.

*Zì yāo*—pronounced *yī yáo*—the six limbs: the body, head, and four limbs.

*Jiǎ rén*—pronounced *gǔ yǎ*—to borrow or employ another.

*Yóu nèi*—Shēntú Jiā, as Cheng explains, was a worthy man of Zheng. His surname was Shēn, given name Jiā, and he was a man without feet. Zǐchǎn’s surname was Gōngsūn, given name Qiáo, styled Zǐchǎn, and he was a worthy minister of Zheng. *Wú rén* refers to Bóhūn Màorén, an ancient man of the Dao. Lièzǐ once studied under him.

The text in the Zhang Junfang edition omits the character *bù*: "Is it not sufficient to reflect upon oneself?"

Yì—pronounced *yì*—was a skilled archer in the time of Yao. *Gòu*—pronounced *gòu*—means to draw a bow. *Zhòng*—Guō explains that the range of a bow and arrow is called *gòu zhòng*. *Zhòng dì*—pronounced *dīng zhòng*—to hit the ground. The following *bù zhòng* is the same. *Fúrán*—pronounced *fú fú*—an appearance of sudden anger.

The Zhang Junfang edition includes, while older editions omit: "Did I not know that you would cleanse me with goodness, or was it my own awakening?"

*Zǐ suǒ*—pronounced *sè bǎi*—to show disdain. *Cùrán*—pronounced *zǐ liù*—an appearance of shock and shame. *Nǎi chēng*—pronounced as written, meaning to raise or cite.

*Wù quán*—Shūshān Wúzǐ—pronounced *zhǐ*. Li explains that Shūshān was his courtesy name, and he had no toes. *Zhǒng*—pronounced *zhū yǒng*. Xiàng explains it means the chin. Cuī explains that Wúzǐ walked on his heels. *Jiàn xián*—pronounced *biàn*—to see the worthy. The phrase "Zǐ bù jǐn" ends here. *Qián xíng*—pronounced *xià mèng*—to advance. *Yǔ*—pronounced *yú jù*—to speak.

The Zhang Junfang edition omits the character *qí*: "In Confucius’s relation to the perfected man, was he not [lacking]?"

*Bīn bīn*—an appearance of being without a master, neither within nor without. *Qí*—pronounced *qí*—to seek. *Chù*—pronounced *chǐ shū*—and *guǐ*—pronounced *jiǔ wěi*—*chù guǐ* means strange and extraordinary. *Huàn*—pronounced *gǔ bàn*—deceitful. *Zhì*—pronounced *zhī shí*—wooden shackles for the feet. *Gù*—pronounced *gǔ dú*—wooden shackles for the hands.

*Dé píng*—virtue is balanced.

Duke Ai of Lu stabilized the Duke’s son, named Jiang. *È rén*—ugly appearance. *Āi tái tái*—pronounced *dài* or *tāi*. *Tā*—pronounced *tú hé*. Li explains: *Āi tái* means ugly in appearance; *tā* was his given name, a knight of Wei. *Cháng*—pronounced *hù wò*. The same below. *È hài*—pronounced *hú kǎi*. *Cí xióng*—Li explains: belonging to birds and beasts. *Jī nián*—pronounced *jī*; a full cycle of the four seasons is called *jī*. *Zhuàn guó*—pronounced *dà zhuān*. *Mèn rán*—pronounced *mén*; Li explains: an appearance of unawareness. *Fàn*—pronounced *fú jiàn*—unbound. *Chǒu hū*—how ugly! Naturally detestable. Cui’s edition writes *kuì*. *Wú jǐ*—pronounced *jū qǐ*—a brief moment. *Yǔ lè*—pronounced *luò*. *Cháng shǐ*—pronounced *suǒ lì*. One edition writes *yóu*. *□ zǐ*—pronounced *tú mén*—same as *tún*. *Shí*—pronounced *yìn*; Cheng explains: meaning to drink its milk. *Shǎo xǔ*—pronounced *zhào*—a short while. *Shùn*—originally also written *shùn*, pronounced *shùn*; Cui explains: the movement of the eyes. It refers to the dead mother’s eyes moving as if for a brief moment. *Shà*—pronounced *zī suǒ jiǎ*.

The *Cheng* commentary states: military adornments, crafted by King Wu, shaped like square fans. The *shà* is made of wood, clothed in white cloth, and painted with cloud-like *qi*, resembling today’s ceremonial screens. They are placed on either side of the war chariot. When generals lead troops into battle and perish in the fray, on the day of their burial, the *shà* is not used. *Zī* means "to accompany in death." *Zhī jù*—pronounced *jù*; to repeat. *Qǔ qī*—pronounced *qī*; to take a wife. *Yǐ huá*—pronounced *gǔ*; to deceive. *Wū duì*—pronounced *tú wài*; joy. *Wú què*—pronounced *qù nì*; separation. *Néng lí*—pronounced *lì zhì*; the *Shiji* of Minzi states: his name was Sun, styled Ziqian, a man of Lu, disciple of Confucius, ranked among the Four Sciences. He declined office as a grandee and refused to accept the emoluments of corrupt rulers, renowned for his filial piety.

*Dé wàng*—*yīn*—pronounced *yīn*; *qǐ*—pronounced *qǐ*. *Zhī Lí Wú Shèn*—*shèn* pronounced *zhěn*. *Yīn* means "bent," *qǐ* means "crooked." *Yīn qǐ zhī lí* describes legs that are perpetually bent in motion, the body misaligned and contracted. *Wú shèn* is a name. Alternatively, *yīn qǐ yǎn* refers to one who is bent and crooked in gait. *Qǐ* means "to walk," *shèn* means "the buttocks." *Shuō*—pronounced *ruì*; same below.

The *Wei Ling Gong* chapter on "Departure from the Human World" concludes here.

*Dòu*—pronounced *dòu*; the neck.
*Jiān jiān*—pronounced *hú tián*. Li [Yun] says: a frail and diminutive appearance.
*Wèng*—pronounced *wū sòng*.
*Āng*—pronounced *wū zàng*. Li [Yun] says: *wèng āng* describes the appearance of a large goiter.
*Dà yǐng*—pronounced *yī lǐng*; a tumor.
The *Shiji* of Duke Huan of Qi states: he was the son of Duke Xiang by a concubine, named Xiaobai. The rules of posthumous naming state: one who expands the state and unifies the realm is called *Huan*.
*Wéi niè*—pronounced *yú liè*; to be a source of evil.
*Wū yòng*—pronounced *wū*; same below.
*Bù zhuó*—pronounced *zhì jué*. *Diāo* means "to carve"; *zhuó* means "to hew."
*Wú sàng*—pronounced *xī làng*.
*Tiān yù*—pronounced *yù*; to nourish.
*Tiān shí*—pronounced *sì*, or read as written.
*Shòu shí*—read as written, or pronounced *sì*.
*Wú qíng*—without emotion.
*Miǎo*—pronounced *wáng xiǎo*. *Miǎo hū*: an appearance of humble smallness.
*Áo*—pronounced *niú dāo*. *Jǐng hū*: an appearance of boastful grandeur.
*Wū dé*—pronounced *wū*; same below.
*Hào wù*—pronounced *hū bào* or *wū lù*.
*Yǐ*—pronounced *qǐ*.
*Gǎo*—pronounced *kǔ lǎo*.
*Wú*—pronounced *wú*.
*Ér míng*—pronounced *mián*. Cheng [Xuanying] says: *gǎo wú* refers to a knee-rest table. Master Hui excelled in discoursing on names and principles; when walking, he would lean against a tree and chant; when seated, he would rest against a table and sleep.
*Tiān xuǎn*—pronounced *xuān zhuǎn* or *xī liàn*; to bestow, to grant, to entrust. Senseless speech is called *wū*.
The third fascicle of the *Nanhua Zhenjing Zhangju Yinyi* concludes here.
The fourth fascicle of the *Nanhua Zhenjing Zhangju Yinyi* begins.

Master Bixu composed *The Great and Venerable Master*, sixth chapter of the Inner Chapters.

*Yōng jù*—pronounced *qí shù*. *Yōng*: to employ. *Jù*: how.
*Bù mù*—pronounced *duàn*. To scheme.
*Bù lì*—pronounced *lì*.
*Bù rú*—pronounced *zhū*. To moisten.
*Dēng jiǎ*—pronounced *gèng xià*. To reach.
*Qí jué*—pronounced *gǔ xiào*. An appearance of deep, inward breathing.
The true person’s breathing reaches the heels—*zhǒng* pronounced *zhāng yǒng*. *Zhǒng*: the heel. Thus the kidney meridian begins at the heel; fire lies beneath water, so the breath extends everywhere without obstruction.
The breathing of the common people reaches only the throat—water and fire contend, so it does not attain tranquility.
*Qí ài*—pronounced *yì*. Guo [Xiang] says: the throat.
*Wā*—pronounced *huò guā*. Cui [Yuan] says: to bind; the qi of the throat is bound and obstructed, failing to pass through.
When the true water is shallow and turbid, the celestial light dims.
*Wù sǐ*—pronounced *wū lù*.
*Bù xīn*—pronounced *xīn*. To delight.
*Bù jù*—pronounced *jù*. Li [Yi] says: to delight in emergence is to rejoice in life; to resist entry is to abhor death.
*Xiāo rán*—pronounced *xiāo*. Cheng [Xuanying] says: an appearance of freedom from entanglement.
*Sǎng xī*—pronounced *dǎng*. To prostrate.
*Kuí*—pronounced *qú zhuī*.
*Xú*—pronounced *qù guǐ*. Guo [Xiang] says: an appearance of great simplicity.
Originally written as *□ rán*. The *Shuowen* says: to weigh.

Chill—pronounced *qī xī*.
Warm—pronounced *xuān*.
Slow—pronounced *kuàng wǎn*.
Thus joy—pronounced *luò*.
Conduct—pronounced *xià mèng*.
Hu Bu-Xie—Cheng [Xuanying] says: a man surnamed Hu, styled Bu-Xie. Some say he was a worthy of Yao’s time who refused Yao’s summons and drowned himself in a river.
Wu Guang—Cheng says: a man of the Yellow Emperor’s era, seven feet tall, with ears seven inches long. Others say he lived in the Shang dynasty, ingesting herbs to nurture life, fond of playing the zither. When Tang offered him the realm, he refused, then carried a stone and drowned himself in the Lu River.
Bo Yi and Shu Qi—*Soushen Ji* states: In the state of Gubuzhu, a woman desired a scholar who drank her ink and fathered a child. The child was abandoned in a pigpen, where the pigs licked him without killing him. A bamboo stalk grew above him, rising straight to join the forest. The family took this as divine, so they raised him. When he grew strong, the Xiahou clan enfeoffed him, and he became the Lord of Mozhai, known as the Lord of Gubuzhu.
Cheng says: the two sons of the Lord of Gubuzhu in Liaoxi, descendants of Shennong, surnamed Motai. Bo Yi was named Yun, styled Gongxin; Shu Qi was named Zhi, styled Gongda. When their father died, the brothers deferred to each other and refused the throne. Hearing that the Western Earl was virtuous, they went to observe him. When King Wen died and King Wu succeeded Zhou, Bo Yi and Shu Qi grasped his horse’s bridle to remonstrate. King Wu did not heed them, so they hid in Mount Shouyang in Hedong, gathering ferns to eat, and eventually starved to death.
Some say "Bo Yi" and "Shu Qi" were posthumous titles. The *Laws of Posthumous Titles* states: "One who teaches and upholds righteousness is called *Yi*; one who holds fast to rectitude and attains solemnity is called *Qi*."
Jizi Xuyu—Cheng says: Xuyu was Jizi’s name, as seen in the *Shizi*. Others say Jizi Xuyu smeared himself with sores, let his hair loose, and feigned madness. King Zhou’s uncle remonstrated with him in vain and was imprisoned and enslaved. Some say Xuyu was Wu Zixu, who remonstrated with the King of Wu to no avail. He was killed, his eyes gouged out, and his corpse sunk in the river. Others say the King of Wu bestowed upon Zixu the sword *Shulou*, and he took his own life.
Ji Tuo—pronounced *tú hé*.
Gan Wan—a reclusive man of Tang’s time who drowned himself in the Jia River.
Shentu Di—a man of the Shang dynasty who carried a stone and drowned himself in the river. See the chapter "Robber Zhi."
*Gū*—pronounced *gōng hū*. A vessel used in the *xiang* drinking ceremony. One says: a goblet holding three *sheng* is called a *gū*.
*Bǐng bǐng*—pronounced *bǐng*. Simplified Text says: an appearance of clarity.
*Cuī hū*—pronounced *qǔ hěn*. Xiang says: an appearance of movement. Simplified Text says: an appearance of remoteness.
*Chù hū*—pronounced *chì liù*. Simplified Text says: to gather. Also, to stagnate.
*Lì hū*—stern. Cui’s original text reads "vast," saying: to encompass broadly.
*Áo*—pronounced *wǔ gāo*. Wang says: lofty and transcending the common.
*Chuò*—pronounced *chāng lüè*. Spacious.
*Zhuó*—pronounced *zhú jiǎo*. The meaning of "solitary transformation."
Springs dry—pronounced *hù gè*. Exhausted.
Mutually moisten—pronounced *rú*, to spit foam.
With foam—pronounced *mò*.
Mutually forget—pronounced *wáng*.
Not fleeing transformation.
The great clod—pronounced *kǔ duì*. See the chapter "Equalizing Things."
Leisure—pronounced *yì*.
In the ravine—pronounced *huò gè*.
That which is fled—pronounced *dù běn*. To move, to escape.
As joy—pronounced *luò*.
Can it be exhausted—pronounced *shēng*.
Good in dying young—Xu Guang’s commentary on the *Records of the Grand Historian* says: *yāo* means youth. Zhang Junfang’s edition reads "few," pronounced *zhào*.
Attaining the Dao’s mystery.

The transmissible—pronounced *zhí zhuān*.
The Supreme Polarity before qi is manifest.
Six Ultimates—the number of earth is six, hence "Six Ultimates," the utmost of yin.
Prior to Heaven—pronounced *xī jiàn*.
Long—pronounced *zhǎng yú*.
*Xī*—Wei Shi, pronounced *xǔ qǐ*.
Sima [Qian] says: name of an ancient sovereign.
With *qiè*—pronounced *kǔ jié*.
Cheng [Xuanying] says: to govern all beings, to uplift the Two Modes.
*Fuxi*—pronounced *xī*, lord of the Three Sovereigns, who took sacrificial animals to fill the kitchen.
With *xí*—to penetrate—pronounced *rù*.
Sima [Qian] says: *xí* means "to enter."
Qi Mother—the mother of primordial qi, that is, the Dao.
Also says: Fuxi entered and merged with primordial qi, hence called "Sovereign."
*Weidou*—Li [Yi] says: the Northern Dipper is the guiding cord of the world.
Everlasting without flaw—pronounced *tā dé*.
Flaw means error.
It is said: passing through beginning and end, pointing to the handle as constant, without error or flaw.
*Kanhuai*—pronounced *fú méi*.
Also pronounced *pēi*.
Sima [Qian] says: name of a spirit, with a human face and beast’s body.
Mountain name: Kunlun, north of the Northern Sea.
*Fengyi*—Sima [Qian] says: the *Qingling Zhuan* states he was a man from the embankment head of Tong Village in Huayin, who consumed eight minerals and became a water immortal, known as the River Earl.
Great river—the Yellow River.
*Jianwu*—name of a spirit.

Mount Tai—the Eastern Marchmount.
The Yellow Emperor—already appears in the *Equalizing Things* chapter.
*Xuangong*—the High Yang Clan after the Yellow Emperor, born with numinous marvels, attained the Dao and dwelled in the True Palace. The True Palace is the northern palace.
*Yuqiang*—pronounced *yú*. The *Classic of Mountains and Seas* states: on an islet in the Northern Sea there is a spirit with a human face and crow’s body, wearing two blue-green serpents as earrings and treading two red serpents, named Yuqiang.
The Northern Culmen—a mountain name.
The Queen Mother of the West—the *Tradition of the Queen Mother of the West* states: the Queen Mother of the West, surnamed Wei, styled Wanjin, is the Ninefold Numinous and Most Wondrous Golden Mother of Tortoise Mountain. She is the supreme honor of the Western Florescence and the ultimate mystery of the Dark Yin, crowned with floral ornaments and girded with tiger insignia. The cavern of Mount Kunlun is named *Lesser Breadth*, where the Queen Mother constantly resides. Beyond birth and death, thus none know her beginning or end. Some say: disheveled hair, crowned with ornaments, tiger’s fangs, and a piercing cry—this is but the Golden Direction’s White Tiger spirit, the Queen Mother’s envoy, not her true form.
Peng Zu—explained in the *Free and Easy Wandering* chapter.
The Five Lords—Xia: Kunwu; Shang: Grand Peng and Shivei; Zhou: Qi Huan; Jin: Wen. Also pronounced *bà*.
Fu Yue—pronounced *yuè*. With—pronounced *xiàng*.
King Wuding—ruled all under heaven, riding the Eastern Cord to mount the Winnowing Basket and Tail, ranking among the constellations. Sima [Qian] says: Fu Yue was a Shang minister. Wuding was King Gaozong of Shang. He dreamed of Fu Yue and sent men to seek him throughout the world, finding him at the rammed-earth works of Fu Rock in Hebei County, Shanzhou. As minister to Wuding, the realm became suddenly clear and peaceful. Fu Yue was the essence of a star. The Eastern Cord lies between the Winnowing Basket and the Dipper, the eastern cord of the Celestial Han. The *Tradition* says: one star, above the Tail star, meaning he rode the Eastern Cord between the Winnowing Basket and Tail. The *Cui edition* adds here: born without father or mother, at death he ascended to the great transformation, and after three years his form vanished. This speaks of the spirit beyond all naming.
Talent and the Dao mutually depend—Nanbo Zikui, also said to be Nanbo Ziqi.
*Yǔ*—pronounced *yǔ*, a woman of the Dao in antiquity.
Aged—pronounced *zhàng*.
Child—pronounced *rú*, a young or weak child.
*Wū wū hū*—pronounced *wū*, an exclamation of disapproval.
Already explained in the *World of Men* chapter; not repeated hereafter.
Below: *wū hū*—pronounced the same.
Bu Liangyi—Bu Liang is the surname, Yi the given name, a man of talent and capacity. Also pronounced *chǐ*.
Three days—pronounced *sān*.

At dawn’s clarity—*Sima* explains this as the break of day. *Chè* means to penetrate the wondrous Dao. Thus, in the ancient southern repository, the text was formerly defective.

Those who kill life do not die; they slay the thief of greedy life, and their bodies endure. Those who give life to life do not live; they hasten the death of those deluded by the pursuit of life.

*Yīng níng*—pronounced *yīng*, meaning the sage moves with things, still and tranquil even when touched.

*Gāo Yòu’s* commentary on the *Lüshi Chunqiu* glosses *yīng* as "to clash."

*Fù mò*—the deputy to brush and ink, referring to canonical teachings. From this point onward, all is allegory.

*Luò sòng*—to recite with clarity.

*Zhān míng*—to see the principle is called *zhān míng*.

*Niè*—as *Xǔ Shèn* explains in the *Shuōwén*, whispered conversations, meaning secret agreements.

*Xū yì*—pronounced *xū*, meaning to wait. *Yì* means to employ, to use. *Dài shǐ* refers to being put into practice.

*Wū ōu*—pronounced *wū*. To praise the flourishing virtue.

*Xuán míng*—profound obscurity. *Míng* means vast and empty. To awaken to the Dao’s deep stillness.

*Cān*—pronounced *qī*. *Liáo*—pronounced *diāo*. *Cān* means to receive; *liáo* means emptiness. To receive and uphold the ultimate Dao.

*Yí shǐ*—none know its end; whether it has a beginning or not.

From *Fù mò* to *Yuān míng*, seven layers deep, one may ascend to the lofty *cān liáo*; reaching *Yí shǐ*, nine layers deep, one enters the unfathomable realm of the Great Vastness, the boundless境 of the Dao.

**Friends in Life and Death**
*Zǐ Sì*—the *Hóngliè* commentary renders this as *Zǐ Yǒng*, who at fifty-four was afflicted with a hunched back.

*Zǐ Yú*—pronounced *yú*.

*Zǐ Lí*—pronounced *lǐ*.

*Zǐ Lái*—these four were all recluses beyond the world.

*Wéi*—pronounced *kāo*.

*Wěi zāi*—pronounced *wéi*. *Xiàng* explains this as "admirable." *Cuī* notes that from here to "gazing into the well," all describes *Zǐ Sì’s* account of his illness.

*Jū jū*—pronounced *jū*. *Sīmǎ* explains this as a body cramped and unable to stretch.

*Qū lǚ*—pronounced *lǚ*. *Qū* refers to the waist.

*Fā bèi*—the spine protrudes from the back.

*Jù*—pronounced *jù*.

*Zhuì*—pronounced *zhuì*. *Zhǐ tiān*—*Lǐ* explains that *jù zhuì* are the cervical vertebrae. Their shape resembles growths, pointing upward toward the sky.

*Yǒu lì*—pronounced *lì*, meaning disorder.

*Pián bù*—pronounced *mì*.

*Xī*—pronounced *tián*. A gait that twists and turns, not walking straight.

*Ér jiàn*—pronounced *zhàn*.

"Alas, this is *Zǐ Yú’s* affliction!"

*Rǔ è*—pronounced *wù*. The same below.

*Yuē wáng*—pronounced as written.

*Jìn*—pronounced *zǐ*, meaning to soak, to permeate.

*Wéi tán*—pronounced *dàn*. *Xiāo*—pronounced *hù*. *Zhì*—pronounced *zhà*. The explanation lies in the *Equalizing Things* chapter.

*Āi lè*—pronounced *luò*.

*Xuán jiě*—pronounced *xiè*, the same below. Meaning unbound, without attachment.

*Chuǎn*—pronounced *chǎn*, breathing in circles.

*Chì*—pronounced *chì*, to rebuke.

*Wú dàn*—pronounced *dá*, meaning startled.

*Yǐ*—pronounced *qǐ*.

*Shǔ gān chóng bì*—to liken it to the liver of a rat or the arm of an insect, signifying the most trivial and lowly.

*Bù chì*—pronounced *zhì*, meaning no different.

*Zé hàn*—pronounced *hàn*. *Hàn* means to resist.

*Mò yé*—pronounced *mò yé*. In ancient times, the swordsmith *Gàn Jiāng* forged blades for King *Hào* of *Wú*. His wife was named *Mò Yé*, and thus the male sword was called *Gàn Jiāng* and the female *Mò Yé*. The *Shíyí Jì* of *Wáng Zǐnián* states: On *Kūnwú* Mountain, the earth was rich in cinnabar. Stones were smelted into copper, which was green in hue and could sever grass and trees. All swords made from it were sharp, and the soil itself was refined and pure. On the mountain lived a beast the size of a hare, its fur golden, feeding on the cinnabar beneath the earth. It burrowed deep into the ground for its den and also consumed copper and iron. Its gallbladder and kidneys were like iron, and the female was white as silver. In the past, the armory of that kingdom had all its weapons and iron implements eaten away, yet the seals remained intact. The king ordered the armory’s den to be searched, and a pair of hares—one white, one yellow—were hunted. When killed and opened, their bellies contained iron gallbladders and kidneys. Only then was it known that the weapons’ iron had been devoured by the hares. The king summoned his swordsmiths and ordered them to forge the gallbladders and kidneys into swords. One male, one female: the male was named *Gàn Jiāng*, the female *Mò Yé*. These swords could cut jade and cleave rhinoceros hide; the king treasured them deeply.

*Dà lú*—pronounced *lú*.

*Wū hū*—pronounced *wū*.

Thus was the merging of their souls. *Qú rán*—pronounced *qú*, meaning their forms opened. *Jué*—pronounced *xiào*.

*"Mutual Forgetting" Friends*
Zǐ Sāng Hù (Mèngzǐ Fǎn), Zǐ Qín Zhāng—both companions beyond the world. Their bond was as close as kin. *Náo*—pronounced *xiǎo*. *Tiāo*—pronounced *tiǎo* or *táo*.

*Mò rán*—meaning "none," as in silence without words. For a brief moment. *"Attending the matter"*—as seen in Zhāng Jūnfáng’s edition; formerly written as *"awaiting."* *Biān qǔ*—pronounced *lián*, referring to arranging lyrics to harmonize with the zither. Lǐ [Yún] says: *"qǔ"* means the thin silk of silkworms.

*Harmonizing*—pronounced *huà*. *"I"*—in Cuī’s edition, written as *"alone."* *"Man"*—pronounced *yī*. *"Words"*—this is *"wù"*—pronounced *wū*, same below. *"Naming"*—to assign a name. *Yóu*—pronounced *yóu*. *Jué*—pronounced *xué*. *Huàn*—pronounced *luàn*. *Kuì*—pronounced *duì*. *Duān ní*—pronounced *lì*. *Máng rán*—pronounced *gāng*, a state of being unbound. *Páng bó*—pronounced *gāng*; *huáng*—pronounced *huáng*. *"Pánghuáng"* and *"xiāoyáo"* both denote carefree ease. *Kuìkuì*—pronounced *nèi*, meaning chaos.

*"To observe"*—pronounced *luàn*, meaning to show. *"Mutual visitation"*—pronounced *zào*, meaning to call upon; same below. *"Mutual forgetting"*—pronounced *wáng*, same below. *"Dao arts"*—read as written, meaning the Dao within the city. Some pronounce it *suì*, referring to irrigation ditches, which is incorrect. *Jī rén*—pronounced *yí*, meaning one who does not pair. *"Yet equal to Heaven"*—pronounced *móu*, meaning equal or aligned. The solitary wanderer, unpaired with the俗, aligns instead with natural Heaven.

*"Without Sentiment in Death"*
Mèngsūn Cái—Lǐ [Yún] says he was a descendant of the Three Huán, named Cái, a worthy man of Lǔ. Cuī says *"Cái"* may also be written as *"Niú."* *"How could"*—pronounced *wū*, same below. *"As if awakened"*—pronounced *xiào*, same below.

"Startling Form" – Cui's edition writes *ké*, stating it has the appearance of an infant.

*"Morning-dwelling"* – Form is the abode of spirit; it speaks of the swiftness of change, like the dwelling of dawn and dusk.

*"Thus"* – In *Shuowen*, *nǎi* depicts the difficulty of qi's emergence.

*"How"* – Pronounced *qú*, same below.

*"And fiercely"* – *Lì lì*, meaning to reach.

*"Creating fitness"* – *Zào*, meaning to make.

*"Presenting laughter"* – *Xiàn*, meaning to display.

*"And pushing"* – Pronounced *pái*, meaning to thrust.

*"Vast Heaven"* – Pronounced *liāo*, meaning the sky's vastness, its height and remoteness. *"One"* means undivided.

*"Roaming the Dao's domain"*
Yì’érzǐ – Li says he was a worthy scholar.

*"Rely on you"* – *Zī* means to provide.

*"For"* – Pronounced *zhǐ*, a particle of speech, an auxiliary word meaning *"this."*

*"Branding"* – Pronounced *qíng*.

*"Mutilation"* – Pronounced *yì*. Li says: *"To ruin virtue and power in the name of benevolence and righteousness—is this not like branding? To shatter the dark unity to fabricate right and wrong—is this not like mutilation?"*

*"Distant and unbound"* – Meaning scattered and unrestrained.

*"Self-contented"* – Pronounced *suī*, describing a state of self-satisfaction.

*"Shifting without attachment"* – Describing a state of unmoored transformation.

*"Its fence"* – Pronounced *fān*, meaning boundary or domain.

*"The blind"* – One edition writes *miǎo*; the explanation of blindness and blindness lies in the *"Free and Easy Wandering"* chapter.

*"With"* – Pronounced *yù*, meaning *"and,"* same below.

"Fǔfú" – Pronounced *fǔfú*. White and black form *fǔ*, resembling an axe in shape. Black and azure form *fú*, two *jǐ* characters facing away from each other.

*"This sight"* – Refers to the chaos of antiquity.

*"Losing her beauty"* – The fault of Wu Zhuang. *"Losing his strength"* – The fault of Ju Liang. As Master Huang’s commentary states: Wu Zhuang was a beauty of old who took pride in adornment. When she met Master Gaoqiu, who instructed her in principle, she awakened and abandoned her ornamental beauty. Ju Liang was a man of great strength in antiquity, capable of lifting beams and replacing pillars, using his power to overawe others. When he met Master Xianmen, who taught him the Dao, he awakened and relinquished his aggressive pursuits.

*"Lú"* – Pronounced *lú*.

*"Chuí"* – Pronounced *shuì* or *zhuì* (two pronunciations). Li says: *"Chuí"* refers to the iron nozzle of a bellows used to blow fire. Cheng says: *"Lú"* is a furnace, *"chuí"* means to forge, referring to the act of smelting and shaping.

*"Yī"* – Pronounced *yī*, an exclamation of sighing. Cui says: It is a particle. Alternatively, it may mean *"to call upon intent by name."*

*"Wéi"* – Pronounced *wěi*.

*"Suì"* – Pronounced *xī*, meaning *"to shatter."*

*"Zhǎng"* – Pronounced *zhàng*.

*"Zuòwàng"* – Pronounced *fù* (first character), *biàn* (second character; same below).

*"Cùrán"* – Pronounced *zǐliù*, describing a change in countenance.

*"Duò"* – Pronounced *xūguī*, meaning *"to collapse."*

*"Líxíng"* – Pronounced *lìzhì*, meaning *"to depart from form."*

*"Qǐ"* – Pronounced *lǚ*.

*"Wúhào"* – Pronounced *hūbào*.

*"Pushing to the limit and entrusting fate"* – *"Línyǔ"* is pronounced *lín*, as in the *Zuǒzhuàn*: *"Rain lasting three days or more is called* lín."

*"Dài"* – Means *"danger"* or *"peril."*

*"Shí"* – Pronounced *sì*.

*"Bùrén"* – Pronounced *rén*, meaning *"unable to bear."*

*"Érqū"* – Pronounced *qīzhù*. Cui says: *"Bùrén"* means *"exhausted"* or *"weary"*; *"qū"* refers to reciting poetry without melody or tune.

*Inner Chapters, "Responding to Emperors and Kings" VII: "The Teaching Without Words"*

"Nièquē" – Pronounced *wǔjié*.

"Xià" – Pronounced *qiūyuè*.

"Wángní" – Pronounced *wǔxī*. The two men already appear in the "Equalizing Things" chapter.

"Four questions with four instances of ignorance" – Refers to repeated questioning. In the "Equalizing Things" chapter, the four questions are: Do you know wherein all things are the same? (first question); Do you know what you do not know? (second question); If things have no knowledge, then... (third question); If you do not know benefit and harm, does the Perfect Person likewise not know benefit and harm? (fourth question).

"Púyīzǐ" – As stated in the *Shizi*: At eight years old, Púyī was entrusted with the realm by Shun. He wore plain garments and was the teacher of Wángní.

"Tàishì" – Sima [Biao] says: an ancient sovereign. Also, a lord without name.

"Cáng" – Pronounced *cáigāng*. Cui [Yuan] says: to cherish a benevolent heart in order to bind others. The *Jianwen* edition reads *zāng*, meaning "to hold virtue."

"Yīyào" – Pronounced *yāoyī*.

"In the age of Tàishì" – From Liu’s edition, where the old text was missing.

"Xúxú" – Sima [Biao] says: a demeanor of ease and tranquility.

"Yúyú" – Sima [Biao] says: a state of knowing nothing. The *Jianwen* edition says: *xúxú yúyú* describes the state of waking and sleeping.

"The governance of non-action" – *Rìrén* is pronounced *shí*.

"Zhōng" – Pronounced as written, also read as *zhòng*.

"Shǐ" – Li [Yi] says: the pool of the worthy, the teacher of Jiānwú. The Cui edition omits the character *yuē* (曰).

"Yǔ" – Pronounced *yù*.

"Shùmín jiàn" – From Zhang Junfang’s edition; the old text read *shì yì dù rén*.

"Qīdé" – *Qī* means deception.

"Shè hǎi záo" – *Záo* is pronounced *zài luò*. The same below. The vastness of rivers and seas, their waves surging and impassable—if one were to dredge and channel them, it would surely yield no coherent principle.

"Quèhū" – Pronounced *kǔ xué*. A firm appearance.

"Zēng" – Pronounced *zé néng*. A net.

"Yì" – Pronounced *yì zhī*. To shoot with an arrow tied to a cord.

"Xīshǔ" – Pronounced *xī*, a small mouse.

"Shén qiū shè tán" – Sacred mounds and altars.

"Xūn" – Pronounced *xiāng*.

"The transformations of nature" – *Tiān gēn* is a metaphor for the absence of *qi*. *Wúmíng rén* is a metaphor for the Dao. Both are allegories.

"Yīnyáng" – Wen [Yun] says: a mountain name, in Weizhou. The southern side of a mountain is called *yáng*. Also said to be a place name.

"Liǎo shuǐ" – Pronounced *liǎo*, a water name.

"Bù yù" – Means "to be without leisure."

"Mǎng" – Pronounced *mò wù*.

"Miǎo" – Pronounced *miào xiǎo*.

"Beyond the six poles" – Beyond the bounds of *yīn* and *yáng*.

"Kuàng" – Pronounced *kǔ guǎng*.

"Lǎng" – Pronounced *lì dǎng*.

"Yìyì" – Pronounced *yì*, meaning "method."

"Wū dàn" – Pronounced *tú zhàn*.

"Wū mò" – Pronounced *mò*.

"The sage has no name" – *Yáng Zǐjū* – *Jū* is his name. *Zǐ* is a general term for men. *Xiàng* is read as *xǔ liàng*, *xǔ* as *liǎng* in two readings.

"Jí qiáng liáng bù róng è" – To detest the strong and unyielding, allowing no evil.

"Wù chè" – Clarity and brightness; *zǒng míng* means "comprehensive illumination."

"Bù dào" – Pronounced *qí juàn*. Toil.

"Xū" – Means "assist."

"Yì" – Pronounced *yì*.

"Jì" – Pronounced *qǐ*. Skill.

"Xì" – As written.

"Chù xīn" – Pronounced *chì lǜ*. To be lured by the heart.

"Lái tián" – Means "hunting."

"Hǔ bào" – Tigers and leopards are hunted for their patterned hides.

"Yuán" – Pronounced *yuán*.

"Jū" – Pronounced *qī yú*.

"Zhī biàn" – Pronounced *pí jiān*.

"Máo lái" – Pronounced *lí* in two readings.

"Lái jiè" – Pronounced *cí yè*. To trample; interchangeable with *jiè*.

"Cù rán" – Pronounced *zǐ liù*. To alter one’s appearance.

"Gōng gài móu" – Merit surpasses all.

"Huà dài" – Pronounced *tǔ dài*. "Mò" means "none"; "jǔ" means "to manifest." To attribute merit to others without displaying one’s own name.

"The sage has no constant heart" – The spirit-medium was named *Jì Xián*. *Jì Xián* is his full name. *Wū* is the general term for male and female mediums. To be intoxicated and deluded by the Way.

*Húzi* – Named *Lín*, a man of *Zhèng* and *Lièzǐ*’s master. "None" in *Xiàng Xiù*’s version reads as written. The ancient southern repository sometimes writes *wán* or *jì wán*, but both are incorrect.

"Dé Dào yǔ" – Pronounced *yú*.

"Shì kàng" – Pronounced *kǔ làng*.

"Bì xìn fū" – The phrase ends here.

"Xiāng rǔ" – Pronounced *xī liàng*. The same below.

"Xī" – Pronounced *xī*, an exclamation of sighing.

"Xiàng wú" – Pronounced *xǔ liàng*.

"Dì wén" – The earth’s pattern lies in its immobility.

"Bù zhèn bù zhèng" – In one version, "bù ràng bù zhǐ," meaning motion and stillness remain unaroused.

"Dù dé jī" – The mechanism of virtue lies in stilling thoughts.

"Yǒu liào" – Pronounced *chǒu liú*.

The heavens and earth—heaven takes generation as its pattern. Names and realities do not enter into slander or praise, nor do they enter the numinous inner palace.

The mechanism arises from the heels—when vital qi stirs, harmony flows below.

*Bù qí* – Pronounced *cè jiē*. The same below.
*Ní* – Pronounced *wǔ xī*.
*Huán ní* – The whale.
*Huán* – To coil, to linger.
*Zhī shěn* – Read as written. *Jiǎn Wén* says it means "to dwell." *Sīmǎ* says *shěn* should be *pán*, meaning "to gather." *Cuī* writes it as *pān*, meaning "the domain where swirling currents converge." *Zhōng* also means "to gather."
The abyss has nine names—*ní*, *zhǐ*, *liú*, *làn*, *wò*, *guǐ*, *yōng*, *qiān*, and *féi*—these are the nine abysses. The *Hóng Liè Jiě* speaks of the "abyss of nine spheres," the deepest of depths.

*Zì shī* – Read as written; *Xú* reads it as *yì*. To vanish from sight.
*Wěi* – Pronounced *wēi*.
*Shé* – Pronounced *zhī*.
The appearance of utmost compliance.
*Dì* – Read as written; the old reading is *tuí*. The appearance of yielding compliance. One says it means "the unbroken appearance of decline and integrity"; another says it means "submissive obedience."
*Bō liú* – *Bō liú*, the most manifest yet unfathomable in appearance.
*Wéi* – Pronounced *yú*.
*Qī cuàn* – Pronounced *qī pàn*.
*Shí shǐ* – Pronounced *sì*. The same below.
*Diāo zhuó* – Pronounced *zhú jiǎo*.
*Kuài rán* – Pronounced *kǔ duì*.
*Fēn rán* – Pronounced *fāng yún*. Chaos.
*Rán* – Seen in *Zhāng Jūnfáng*’s version; previously omitted.
*Fēng zāi* – Undispersed.
*Cuī*’s version writes it as *róng*, saying "scattered and disordered."
*Jì tuō* – Without trace. Pronounced *zhí rěn*.

Signs.
*Kāi duì* – *Tiáo* is pronounced *shū*; Li [Yuxi] says it means "to illustrate what has form."
*Hū* – Li [Yuxi] says it means "to illustrate what is formless."
*Hún* – Pronounced *húběn*.
*Zhuàn* – Pronounced *túběn*.
The *Bao Xuan Zhen Jing* states: "When the three qi have not yet separated, it is called *hun*; when the five phases have not yet diverged, it is called *zhuàn*."
Jianwen says: "*Tiáo* and *hū* take their names from spiritual swiftness; *hún* and *zhuàn* take their form from harmonious union." Spiritual swiftness is a metaphor for action; harmonious union, for nonaction.
*Qī qiào* – Pronounced *kǔ jiào*. Apertures.
*Hún dùn sǐ* – Cui [Yun] says: "This is to go against nature, forcibly opening the ears and eyes."

*End of the Fourth Scroll of the* Nan Hua Zhen Jing Zhang Ju Yin Yi
*Beginning of the Fifth Scroll of the* Nan Hua Zhen Jing Zhang Ju Yin Yi
*Composed by Bi Xu Zi*

**Outer Chapters: "Pian Mu" Eight**
**Nurturing the True Nature and Life**

*Pián mǔ* – Pronounced *bù tián*. To pair; to join.
*Mǔ* – Pronounced *mǔ*, referring to the big toe connected to the second toe.
*Zhī zhǐ* – Read as written; Cui [Yun] pronounces it *qí*, meaning a finger with a branch.
*Ér chǐ* – Pronounced *chāng shì*. The appearance of excess.
*Yú dé* – *Dé* here means "capacity for virtue." Robber Zhi calls physical beauty "the inherited virtue of one’s parents."
*Fù zhuì* – Pronounced *zhāng ruì*. The *Guang Ya* says it means "a wart"; the *Shi Ming* says it is "a piece of flesh growing horizontally, attached to the body." It also refers to a tumor.
*Xuán yóu* – Pronounced *yóu*. The *Shuo Wen* says it means "a growth."
*Liè yú wǔ zàng* – Pronounced *cái làng*. The same below. The text says: "The liver corresponds to wood and benevolence; the lungs to metal and righteousness; the heart to fire and propriety; the kidneys to water and wisdom; the spleen to earth and trust."
All beings endowed with consciousness, all creatures born into life, possess the nature of benevolence and righteousness. Whether little or much, this is naturally determined. Though Yan [Hui] and Confucius differ by but a fraction, the latter can never be matched—how much less those who differ further still? Now, those whose nature is rich in benevolence and righteousness inevitably become self-important and lose their nature; those whose nature is deficient in them strive after them and forfeit their authenticity. Racing along the path of self-importance and striving, drifting amid the realm of right and wrong, they vie in boasting and emulation—can this be the true Way of virtue and power?
If people abide in their nature, the feelings of "much" and "little" become one; if things keep to their proper place, the principles of benevolence and righteousness naturally align. When each office is filled according to its capacity, the world will know no disorder.
*Yín pì* – Pronounced *pǐ yì*. The same below.
*Yú rén yì zhī xíng* – Pronounced *xià mèng*. Cui [Yun] says: "Though supernumerary toes and growths are not the true nature of the body, they arise from form and cannot be removed. Though the dispositions of the five viscera are not the true Way of virtue and power, they are arrayed in nature and cannot be governed."
Now, to establish the teachings of benevolence and righteousness in order to govern the dispositions of the five viscera is like cutting off supernumerary toes and growths. It injures the principle of nature and only exacerbates the affliction.
*Ér duō fāng yú cōng míng zhī yòng* – The old text reads: "Yet they multiply methods in the use of intelligence." Zhang Junfang removed the character *fāng*, making it resemble the phrase below, "excessive in intelligence."
To pair intelligence with supernumerary toes is to be deficient, for compared to the number of the five fingers, it is called "lacking." To pair intelligence is to be excessive, for it doubles other natures and is called "redundant."
*Wén zhāng* – Green and red form *wén*; red and white form *zhāng*.
*Fǔ fú* – Pronounced *fǔ fú*; explained in the "Da Zong Shi" chapter.
*Huáng huáng* – Pronounced *huáng*, meaning "radiant." It also describes dazzling eyes.
*Fēi hū* – "Is it not so?"; to affirm.
*Lí Zhū* – That is, Li Lou. Sima [Biao] says he was a man of the Yellow Emperor’s time who could see the tip of an autumn hair at a hundred paces and the point of a needle at a thousand *li*.
*Shì yǐ* – "This is"; the same as "it is so."
*Wǔ shēng* – One version writes *yīn*.

The five notes: *gong*, *shang*, *jue*, *zhi*, and *yu*.
The six pitch-pipes: *Huangzhong*, *Dacou*, *Guxi*, *Ruibin*, *Yize*, and *Wuyi*.
Metals: bells, chimes, stone chimes, and lithophones.
Silk and bamboo: zithers, se-pipes, flutes, and pipes.
*Huangzhong* is the sovereign’s note.
*Dalü* and the six *lü*.
Shi Kuang’s explanation appears in the "Equalizing Things" chapter.

"Plucking virtue’s sound, washing it clean" – *zhuo* means "to draw out."
*Huang gu* – pronounced *huang*, referring to the reed of the *sheng*.
"Drum" – means "to move."

The *Records of Zeng and Shi* state: Zeng Shen, styled Ziyu, practiced benevolence; Shi Qiu, styled Ziyu, practiced righteousness.

*Lei lie* – pronounced *bi lie*.
*Wa* – read as written.
"Knotting cords" gathers useless words, like piling tiles or tying knots.

*Cuan* – pronounced *qi luan*.
Means "subtle" or "hidden."

*Ju* – pronounced *ji ju*.
Sima [Biao] says *cuan ju* refers to heretical doctrines, subtly concealed and forcibly interpreted phrases.

In another text, *bi* is written as *bì* – pronounced *bì*.
*Kuǐ quǎn* – pronounced *kuǐ ruǐ*.
*Bi kuǐ* describes exerting effort beyond measure yet making no progress.

Yang Zhu was a man of Qin; Mo Di was a grandee of Song.

*Fu* – pronounced *fú*.
*Jìng* – pronounced *xíng dìng*.
*He* – pronounced *hù gè*.
*He* – pronounced *hèn mò*; means "to gnaw."
*Ti* – pronounced *tí*.
*Hao mu* – pronounced *hǎo gāo*.
"Gazing through mugwort" is a metaphor for confusion, for excessive worry makes the eyes dim and hazy.

*Tao* – pronounced *tǔ dāo*; means "greedy."
*Xiao xiao* – pronounced *xǔ jiāo*.
Describes the restless longing and yearning of one who admires and emulates, bending and contorting the body in fervent devotion – that is, twisting the limbs for ritual and music.

*Yu yu* – pronounced *yú* or *yǔ*.
Refers to sighing and altering one’s countenance to assume the appearance of benevolence and righteousness.

*Mo* – pronounced *mò*; means "rope."
*Suo* – pronounced *sì gè*.

Alluring one another to life means calling and gathering together to delight in their existence.

*Xù* – pronounced *xū yǒu*.
*Lián lián* – as Sima explains, refers to the continuous pursuit of benevolence and righteousness, wandering amidst the Dao and virtue.

*Ráo* – pronounced *ér xiǎo*; means "to disturb" or "to confuse."

*Xìng yǔ* – pronounced *yú*.

What follows may be inferred by context; the same applies hereafter.

The Three Dynasties – Xia, Shang, and Zhou.

*Xùn* – pronounced *jùn*; means "to strive for" or "to seek." Also refers to dying for another, called *xùn*.

*Zàng* – pronounced *láng*. Cui says: to cherish books is called *zàng*; also means "excellent." *Fangyan* states: in the northern borders of Qi and the northern outskirts of Yan, a man married to a female slave is called *zàng*, while a woman married to a male slave is called *huò*. Zhang Yi says: the son of a man married to a female slave is called *zàng*; the son of a woman married to a male slave is called *huò*.

*Gǔ* – as in *gǔ rúzǐ* ("young child"), also means "excellent."

*Mù* – as in *mù yáng* ("to herd sheep"), means "to tend" or "to nurture."

*Xié* – pronounced *xié*.

*Cè* – pronounced *chū gé*; Li explains: bamboo slips, used in antiquity for writing, two feet and four inches in length.

*Bó sài* – pronounced *xī dài*; refers to games like *bó* and *yì* (board games).

The *Han Shu* states: Wuqiu Shouwang, skilled in the game of *gé wǔ*, referred to it as *bó sài*. Cheng explains: moving through the five paths and casting *qióng* is called *bó*; without casting *qióng*, it is called *sài*.

Boyi died for fame at Mount Shouyang—this has already been explained in the "Great and Venerable Teacher" chapter. Shouyang is the name of a mountain, located in Puban County of Hedong.

Robber Zhi died for profit at Dongling—the *Han Shu* states that Robber Zhi was a great bandit of Qin. Dongling refers to Mount Tai. There is also a mound by that name, now Dongpingling, under the jurisdiction of Jinan Commandery.

*È* is pronounced *wū*.

*Shǔ* is pronounced *shù* in the sense of "to belong." *Xì* means "to attach" or "to connect." Xu pronounces it *zhú*, meaning "to adhere." The same applies below.

Yú’ér is pronounced *yú*; he was a man of the Yellow Emperor’s time skilled in discerning flavors. Cui explains: the *Shizi* states that Yú’ér harmonized flavors with *huáng* and *guì* to prepare the ruler’s meals.

*Zhī xíng* is pronounced *xià mèng*.

From the "Horse’s Hooves" chapter of the Outer Chapters (Chapter Nine):

**Wisdom gives rise to artifice.**

The *Explanation of Names* states: *mǎ* (horse) means *wǔ* (martial), for it moves upon the earth. *Tí* is pronounced *tí* and refers to the hoof of a horse. *Yù* is pronounced *yǔ* and means "to resist" or "to oppose." *Hé* is pronounced *hèn yì* and means "to bite." *Qiào* is pronounced *qí ráo* and means "to raise." *Zú* ("foot")—Cui’s edition reads *wěi* ("tail"). *Ér* is pronounced *lù* and means "to leap." Zhang Junfang’s edition reads *ér* as "vigorous," meaning a horse’s strength. *Xī* is pronounced *xǔ yí* and means "to nourish." *Tái xī* is akin to a high terrace. *Lù qǐn* refers to the grand chamber.

Bole is pronounced *luò*. Bole’s surname was Sun, given name Yang; he was skilled in driving horses. The *Star Canon of the Shi Clan* states that Bole is the name of a star governing celestial horses, and because Sun Yang was skilled in driving, the name was thus applied.

*Shāo zhī* means to burn iron to melt it. *Tī zhī* is pronounced *cì lì*.

Shearing refers to trimming the hair. *Kè* means to pare the hooves. *Luò* means to bridle its head—ancient southern texts read *luò* as "to bind." *Jī* is pronounced *gǔ yí* and means "to rein." *Dì* is pronounced *dīng yì* and refers to hobbling the front two legs. *Biān* is pronounced *bì rán* and means "to tether." *Zào* is pronounced *cái lǎo* and refers to the stable or trough. *Cuī gōng wàn xián* is the enclosure where horses are kept. *Zhàn* is pronounced *shì bǎn* and means "rack"—the edition reads *tán* ("sandalwood"), and a rack resembles a bed, used to ward off dampness. It also refers to a wooden fence.

*Jù* is pronounced *shì jiù* and means "to harness." *Zhěng* means to adjust with the crossbar and yoke. *Qí* means to align with the bit and reins. *Jué* is pronounced *qí yuè* and refers to the bit or curb. *Shì* is pronounced *shì* and means "to adorn the bit," that is, to embellish the horse’s bridle. *Biān* is pronounced *bì rán* and refers to the whip. *Cè* is pronounced *chū gé* and means "the goad." A whip is made of leather, while a goad has no leather. *Zhuā* is pronounced *zhú guā*. *Táo dào* is pronounced *dāo qiē* and means "kiln." *Zhí shí dāo* refers to clay—pottery is made from clay. It also denotes sticky earth. *Zhòng guī* is pronounced *dīng zhòng*—same as below.

The heavens let loose—Cui’s edition reads "to herd," meaning to nurture. *Tián tián* (填填), pronounced *tián*, describes a weighty solidity. *Diān diān* (颠颠), pronounced *dīng tián*, depicts single-minded focus. *Qī* (蹊), pronounced *xī*, means a path. *Suì* (隧), pronounced *suì*, means a road. *Zhōu liáng* (舟梁) refers to boats and bridges.

Their villages are interconnected—Wang Yun notes that with no state divisions or household distinctions, their villages naturally adjoin. *Suì zhǎng* (遂长), pronounced *dīng zhàng*. *Pān* (攀), pronounced *pǔ bān*. *Yuán* (援), pronounced *yuán*, means to pull or draw. *Kuī* (窥), pronounced *qù guī*. *Wū hū* (恶乎), pronounced *wū*. *Bù lí* (不离), pronounced *lì zhì*. *Sù pǔ* (素朴), pronounced *pǔ bō*. *Bié* (蹩), pronounced *bù jié*. *Xiè* (躠), pronounced *xī jié*—Cheng explains this as a display of effort. *Dì* (踶), pronounced *zhí shì*. *Qǐ* (跂), pronounced *qiū shì*—Cheng describes this as an air of self-importance. *Chán* (澶), pronounced *tú dàn*. *Màn* (漫), pronounced *wǔ bàn*—Cheng depicts this as unrestrained abandon. *Zhāi* (摘), pronounced *jiù lì*. *Pì* (辟), pronounced *pǐ bì*—Cheng calls this a breaking of restraint. *Chún pǔ* (纯朴)—Cheng notes that "wood and metal are called *chún pǔ* (pure simplicity); undecorated is called *bù cán* (unmarred)."

*Xī zūn* (牺樽), pronounced *xī zūn*—Sima explains this as a wine vessel adorned with painted oxen; Wang Su says it is carved into the shape of an ox’s head. Alternately pronounced *xiān hé*, it is decorated with phoenix feathers, shimmering in elegance. *Guī zhāng* (珪璋), pronounced *guī zhāng*—a pointed top and square base is a *guī*; half a *guī* is a *zhāng*. *Bù lí* (不离) means "not parting." *Jiāo jǐng* (交颈)—*jǐng* means "neck." *Xiāng mǐ* (相靡) means to follow closely. *Xiāng gēn* (相跟), pronounced *dà jì* or *tú xī*, means to tread. A light tread is called *dì*. *Héng è* (衡扼), pronounced *yú gé*—the crossbar at the front of a carriage, bound to the yoke; it also refers to a horse’s neck. *Yuè tí* (月题), pronounced *tú xī*—Cui explains this as the moon-shaped plate on a horse’s forehead; it also refers to a hanging ornament shaped like the moon. *Jiè* (介), pronounced *gǔ tài*. *Ní* (倪), pronounced *wǔ guī* or *wǔ qí*—Li explains *jiè ní* as akin to *pì nì* (glancing sideways). *Yīn è* (闉扼), pronounced *yīn è*, means crooked. *Chì* (騺), pronounced *chì èr*—*màn* (曼), pronounced *wǔ bàn*—*chì* means to resist; *màn* means to charge. *Yīn è zhì màn* (闉扼鸷曼) describes stubborn resistance and sluggish delay. Sima explains this as bending the neck against the yoke to charge forward. Another interpretation says *zhì màn* means to swerve aside. *Guǐ* (诡), pronounced *jiǔ bǐ*, refers to the bit in a horse’s mouth. *Guǐ xián* (诡衔) means to spit out the bit. *Qiè pèi* (窃辔) means to gnaw the reins. Cui explains *guǐ xián qiè pèi* (诡衔窃辔) as rebelling against the bit and stealing the reins.

*Hè xū* (赫胥)—*hè*, pronounced *hū bái*—Xu Shi, the ancient lord of beasts, was an early sage-king. His virtue blazed so brightly that the people naturally gathered to him, hence the name *Hè Xū*. He is said to be the Flame Emperor. *Hán bǔ* (含哺), pronounced *bù*. *Xuán qǐ* (县跂), pronounced *qǐ*. *Dì* (踶), pronounced *zhí shì*. *Qǐ* (跂), pronounced *qiū shì*. *Hào* (好), pronounced *hū bào*.

**Outer Chapters: "My Coffer" (十)**
*Abandoning Sagacity, Discarding Cunning*
*Qū* (胠), pronounced *qǐ jū*—Sima explains this as opening from the side; another says it means to pry open. *Qiè* (箧), pronounced *kǔ qíng*. *Tàn* (探), pronounced *tǔ nán*. *Náng* (囊), pronounced *nǎi gāng*. *Kuì* (匮), pronounced *qí wèi*, means a chest. *Bì shè* (必摄) means to bind or gather; *jiān* (缄), pronounced *gǔ xián*, means to seal. *Téng* (縢)—Cui’s edition reads *dēng*, pronounced *tú dēng*—means to tie. *Jiān téng* (缄縢) both refer to cords. *Jiōng* (扃), pronounced *gǔ yíng*, means a latch. *Jué* (鐍), pronounced *gǔ xuè*, means a clasp. Cui explains this as a ring and tongue. *Jiē* (揭), pronounced *qí yè* or *jié*, means to lift, carry, or bear. *Dān* (担), pronounced *dīng gān*. *Ér qū* (而趋), pronounced *qī xū*, means to run, fearing only delay. *Xiàng* (向), also written *yì* (易), pronounced *xǔ liàng*. *Wéi dà dào* (为大盗), pronounced *yú wěi*—same below. *Wǎng gǔ* (罔罟), pronounced *gǔ*, is a general term for nets. *Lěi* (耒), pronounced *lì duì*, means a plow; another says it is the handle of a plowshare. *Nòu* (耨), pronounced *nǎi dòu*, means a hoe; some say it is the wooden handle of a hoe. *Suǒ cì* (所刺), pronounced *qī zhì*. *Hé hù* (阖户), pronounced *là*. *Shè jì* (社稷)—mounded earth is *shè*; mounded grain is *jì*. *Yì wū zhōu lǘ xiāng qū* (邑屋州闾乡曲)—thirty *mǔ* make a *wū*, 3,600 *mǔ* make an *yì*, twenty-five households make a *lǘ*, 2,500 households make a *xiāng*, and 12,500 households make a *zhōu*.

*Tián Chéngzǐ* (田成子)—the *Records of the Grand Historian* says he was a minister of Qi, also known as Tian Chang. In the fourteenth year of Duke Ai of Lu, Tian Chang assassinated Duke Jian and seized the lands from Anping east to Langye as his own fief. From Jing Zhong, who was enfeoffed in Qi, to King Wei, who became the lord of Qi, this spans twelve generations. *Hé cháng bù fǎ shèng zhì zāi* (曷尝不法圣智哉)—originally "Why did they not model themselves on the sages?" *Shàn rén bù dé shèng rén zhī dào bù lì, Zhí bù dé shèng rén zhī dào bù xíng* (善人不得圣人之道不立，跖不得圣人之道不行)—Good people cannot stand without the Way of the sage, and Zhi cannot act without the Way of the sage. Thus, the sage’s benefit to the world is slight. *Shèng rén shēng ér dà dào qǐ* (圣人生而大盗起)—When sages arise, great thieves appear. *Péi jī shèng rén, shèng rén yǐ sǐ, shèng rén bù sǐ, suī zhòng shèng rén shì nǎi shèng rén zhī guò yě* (培击圣人，圣人已死，圣人不死，虽重圣人是乃圣人之过也)—Strike down the sage, and once the sage is dead, even if sages multiply, this is the fault of the sages. Those sages are the sharpest tools of the world—from here upward, the eleven sages.

*Lóng Féng zhǎn* (龙逢斩)—beheaded by Jie. *Bǐ Gān pōu* (比干剖), pronounced *pǔ kǒu*—Bi Gan said, "As a minister, one cannot but remonstrate even unto death." He forcefully admonished Zhou, who flew into a rage: "I have heard the sage’s heart has seven apertures." He then split open Bi Gan to examine his heart. *Cháng* (苌), pronounced *zhí liáng*. *Hóng chǐ* (弘胣), pronounced *chì zhǐ*.

Chǐ means "to rend." Cháng Hóng was a worthy minister of King Ling of Zhou, a grandee of King Jing, and was killed by the people of Zhou in the sixth month of the third year of Duke Ai of Lu. It is also said that Liu Kang slandered him before the king, saying, "Cháng Hóng sent a letter to Shu Xiang, saying, 'Raise the Jin troops to attack Zhou, and Jin will kill the Liu clan.'" King Ling then had Cháng Hóng flayed and killed in Shu. It is also said that he was dismembered by a sword or that his intestines were ripped out—this is called *chǐ*.

Zǐxū, pronounced *mí róng chí*. He was torn apart and rotted in the river; the explanation is found in the "Great and Venerable Master" chapter.

Gù Zhí, pronounced *zhí shí*. Zhī’s hoard, pronounced *cáng cái làng*.

The wine of Lu was weak, and Hán Dān (pronounced *hán dān*), the capital of Zhao, was besieged. When King Xuan of Chu summoned the feudal lords to court, Duke Gong of Lu arrived late and offered thin wine. King Xuan was enraged and wished to humiliate him. Duke Gong refused to accept the reproach, saying, "I am a descendant of the Duke of Zhou, elder among the feudal lords, and perform the rites and music of the Son of Heaven. My merit is recorded in the house of Zhou. Though I have already erred in presenting the wine, to now reproach me for its thinness is excessive." He then departed without taking leave. King Xuan, furious, dispatched troops with Qi to attack Lu. King Hui of Liang had long wished to strike Zhao but feared Chu’s rescue. Since Chu was occupied with Lu, Liang was able to besiege Hán Dān. This shows how matters arise from one another—it is also a case of resonance.

King Xuan’s name was Xióng Liángfū, son of King Dao. Duke Gong’s name was Fèn, son of King Mu.

It is also said that at a gathering of the feudal lords, both Lu and Zhao presented wine to the King of Chu. The wine of Lu was thin, while that of Zhao was rich. The Chu official in charge of wine sought wine from Zhao, but Zhao refused. The official, enraged, exchanged Zhao’s rich wine for Lu’s thin wine and presented it to the King of Chu, claiming that Zhao’s wine was weak. For this reason, Hán Dān was besieged.

Pǒu, pronounced *pǔ kǒu*. Strike, pronounced *gǔ lì*.

To shatter sagely wisdom is what "abolish sagely wisdom and discard cleverness" means. *Fàngshě* is pronounced *pǒu*.

What thieves and bandits covet are gold and treasures, hence "do not prize goods hard to obtain."

Once sagely wisdom is dead, great thieves do not arise—daily renewal in the clouds’ work is ever new. The new is life, the old is death, thus "the sage is already dead."

Riding the uprightness of Heaven and Earth, steering the ceaseless transformations of the ever-new, attaining reality while diminishing its name, returning to the true while forgetting its theft—then great thievery ceases.

If sagely wisdom does not die, great thievery does not end. As the saying goes, "the sage does not die," meaning one clings to the old without daily renewal, is bound by names without creating reality.

If great thievery does not cease, is this not fitting?

Making measuring vessels like *dǒu* and *hú* to quantify—from here onward, all illustrate that without the right person, even laws are of no benefit.

*Quánhéng*: Li says *quán* is the weight, *héng* is the beam. *Chuí* is pronounced *zhíwěi*.

*Fújì*: a tally split into two, reunited as one—nowadays, bronze fish or wooden contracts.

*Xǐ*: a jade seal.

*Jiǎo* is pronounced *jūbiǎo*.

*Qiègōu*: a belt hook.

*Jiē* is pronounced *yè* or *liè*.

*Fǔyuè*: an axe.

*Néngjìn* is pronounced *jūzhǎi*.

Below, "cannot be prohibited" is the same.

*Tīyù* is pronounced *chìchì*. Cui says it means "to discard."

*Dān* is pronounced *dān*, meaning "exhausted."

*Shuò* is pronounced *shīzhuó*. Li says it means "burned through." Cui says it means "dissolved."

Below, "does not dissolve" is the same.

*Yú* is pronounced *yú*. Cheng says the *yú* resembles a *shēng*, with pipes arranged together; inside, three plus six reeds make a *yú*, nineteen reeds make a *shēng*.

*Sè* is also written as *shēng*. Cheng says the *sè* is eight *chǐ* and one *cùn* long, one *chǐ* and eight *cùn* wide, with twenty-seven strings, invented by Fuxi.

*Jiāo* is pronounced *jiāo*.

*Lì* is pronounced *lǚxì*.

Li says it means "to break." Cui says it means "to tear."

*Gōngchuí*: pronounced *chuí*, a skilled craftsman of Yao’s time.

*Zhīxíng*: pronounced *xià-mèng*.

*Qián*: pronounced *jù-yán*, meaning "to clamp" or "to shut."

*Rǎng*: pronounced *rú-yáng*, meaning "to push away."

*Bùpì*: pronounced *sì-yì*.

*Yuè*: pronounced *yào*, meaning "the flickering of firelight" or "to disperse."

The Twelve Lords from Róngchéng-shì to Shénnóng-shì were all ancient sage-kings.

*Lí*: pronounced *lì-chí*.

*Chù-shì*: In the ancient southern manuscript, it is written as *Lílián-shì*.

*Fúxī*: pronounced *xī*.

*Yuèqí*: pronounced *luò*.

*Yíng*: pronounced *yíng*, meaning "to wrap," "to carry on the back," or "to bear."

*Liáng*: pronounced *liáng*.

*Érqū*: pronounced *qī-yú*.

Above, *hào* is pronounced *hū-bào*; below, the same.

*Nǔ*: pronounced *nuǎn-wǔ*.

A *bì* is a net for catching rabbits; a *gāng* has a handle and resembles the *bì* constellation. A *jiǎo* is used in archery, while the trigger of a crossbow is called a *jī*.

*Gōu’ěr*: pronounced *rú-zhì*.

*Wǎng-gǔ-zēng*: pronounced *zēng*.

*Sǔn*: pronounced *gǒu*, a bamboo tool for catching fish. Cheng says it is a curved weir, also called a *quān*; *gōu* means "to fish with a hook," and *ěr* means "fish bait." *Gǔ* is called *wǎng*, and *zēng* is a fishing net.

*Xuēgé*: the first character pronounced *xī-yuē*, the second *gǔ-bǎi*. *Xuēgé* are tools used to set up nets. Cheng says they are made of bamboo or wood, like today’s deer-antler spears.

*Luōluò-jū*: pronounced *zǐ-xié*.

*Fú*: pronounced *fú*. Cheng says bird nets are called *luō*, rabbit nets are called *zēng*, *zhuó* is called *fú*, and *fú* is a covered trap, like today’s *qiēchē*.

*Jiàndú*: pronounced *jiāng-shù*. *Jiàn* means "to soak in poison," which seeps in imperceptibly. It also means "deep harm."

*Xié*: pronounced *hù-jié*.

*Huá*: pronounced *hū-bā*, meaning "difficult to manage" or "unpredictable." It also refers to improper speech or deceitful conduct.

*Jiě*: pronounced *kǔ-xiè*.

*Gòu*: pronounced *kǔ-dòu*. It refers to devious or crooked words, though both characters may also be read in their usual pronunciations. It means the ability to explain away filth, like resolving mockery.

*Měi*: pronounced *mǔ-zuì*. The *Shuōwén* says it means "grass growing thickly upward." Now it is used to describe great chaos, as disorderly as overgrown grass.

Above, *bèi* is pronounced *bì-nèi*, meaning "to consume sparingly." Below, *shuò* is pronounced *shī-yuē*, meaning "to melt" or "to dissolve."

*Zhōngduò*: pronounced *xǔ-guī*, meaning "to crumble," "to destroy," or "to ruin."

*Zhīshī*: pronounced *shǐ-chǐ*.

*Chuǎn*: originally written as *duān*, pronounced *chuǎn-yǎn*.

*Ruǎn*: pronounced *ěr-zhuǎn*, referring to moving insects. It also means legless insects or ground-crawling creatures.

*Xiāo*: pronounced *xiāo*.

*Qiáo*: pronounced *qí-ráo*. Flying insects are called *xiāo-qiáo*. Li says they belong to the category of *xuān-fēi*.

*Shěfū*: pronounced *shě-fú*.

*Zhǒngzhǒng*: pronounced *zhāng-yǒng*. *Zhǒngzhǒng* means maintaining one’s kind without encroachment, signifying purity and simplicity.

*Yìyì zhī nìng*: *yìyì* is like *jíjí*, meaning cunning or deceitful.

*Tián*: pronounced *tú-qiān*.

*Tán*: pronounced *tú-zàn*.

All manifestations of *hēng-hēng* purity and seclusion are uniform.

Guo comments: this describes the manner of instructing others with one’s own conduct.

*End of the Fifth Scroll of the* Nan Hua Zhen Jing Zhang Ju Yin Yi *(Pronunciations and Meanings of Chapter Sentences in the* Nan Hua True Scripture*).*

*Beginning of the Sixth Scroll of the* Nan Hua Zhen Jing Zhang Ju Yin Yi *(Pronunciations and Meanings of Chapter Sentences in the* Nan Hua True Scripture*).*
By Bi Xu Zi

**Outer Chapters: "Carving Intentions" (Chapter Eleven)**

*Shǒu chúnsù*: Guarding purity and simplicity.

*Kèyì*: *kè* means "to carve" or "to chisel," signifying the sharpening of intent to make it keen.

*Yì* refers to the function of the heart-mind.
It also denotes will or intention.

*Shàngxíng*: pronounced *xià-mèng*.

*Líshì*: pronounced *lì-zhì*.

*Gāolùn*: pronounced *lì-kùn*.

*Yuànfěi*: pronounced *fèi*.
*Fěi* is also pronounced *fēi*.
Li comments: in a world without the Dao, one resents one’s own lack of opportunity.

*Wéikàng*: pronounced *kǔ-làng*.
Li comments: to be excessively lofty is called *kàng*.

*Kūgǎo*: pronounced *kǔ-lǎo*.

*Suǒhào*: pronounced *hū-bào*.
The pronunciation is the same below.

*Cǐzhāo*: pronounced *zhí-yáo*.

*Sǒu*: pronounced *sù-kǒu*.

*Diàoyú*: pronounced *diāo-jiào*.
Same as *diào*.

*Hūxī*: pronounced *xǔ-jí*.

*Xióngjīng*: pronounced as written, or *gǔ-dìng*.

*Wūshēn*: the stretching of birds and beasts is called *shēn*.

*Dǎoyǐn*: Li comments: guiding the *qi* to harmonize it, and stretching the body to make it supple.

*Dàn*: pronounced *dà-zàn*.

*Zhīzhì*: *zhì* means "upright" or "correct."

Thus it is said: the sage rests therein—resting, they become level and easy; level and easy, they become tranquil and at ease. (See Zhang Junfang’s edition: originally, "The sage rests, resting therein, they become level and easy; level and easy, they become tranquil and at ease.")

*Xiéqì*: pronounced *jiē*. The same below.
*Qù*: pronounced *qǐ-lǚ*.
*Jué*: pronounced *gǔ-xiào*.
*Chúncuì*: pronounced *suī-suì*.
*Bùpí*: *pí* means "weary."
*Fēilè*: pronounced *luò*. The same below.
*Hàowù*: pronounced *wū-lù*.
*Wǔ*: pronounced *wǔ-gù*. Means "to oppose."
*Gān Yuè zhī jiàn*: Li comments: Gan Brook and Yue Mountain both produce fine swords. Also, Wu has a brook named Gan Brook, and Yue has a mountain named Ruo Ye, both yielding superior iron used to forge renowned swords.
*Xián*: pronounced *hù-jiǎ*. Below, *pán* is pronounced *pán*.
*Outer Chapter, "Shànxìng" (Twelve)*: "Tranquility and wisdom nurture one another"

"To refine one’s nature through worldly learning in order to return to one’s original state; to disrupt desires through worldly thought in order to attain clarity." (See Zhang Junfang’s edition: originally, "To refine one’s nature through the worldly, worldly learning in order to return to one’s original state; to disrupt desires through the worldly, thought in order to attain clarity.")

*Shàn*: pronounced *shí-zhàn*. Means "to mend."
*Huá*: pronounced *gǔ*, means "to disorder."
"Wisdom arises, yet one does not employ wisdom as wisdom." (See Zhang Junfang’s edition: originally omitted.)
*Xìnxíng*: pronounced *xià-mèng*. Below, *yǐ xíng* is the same as *xiǎoxíng*.

"Music" in the *Jiangnan* edition is written as *piàn* (partial). The *Records of the Grand Historian* states: "When joy reaches its extreme, sorrow follows; when ritual is coarse, it becomes partial." Zheng Xuan’s commentary says: "Music is what people delight in, yet its harm lies in excess and ostentation; ritual is what people diligently practice, yet its harm lies in partiality and neglect." It further states: "Only the great sage can cultivate music without sorrow and perfect ritual without partiality."

*Bù* (覆): pronounced *mò-bào*, means "to cover."
*Hùn* (混): pronounced *hú-běn*.
*Máng* (芒): pronounced *mò-gāng*. Cui [Yuan] says: "*Hùnhùn mángmáng* (混混芒芒) describes the undifferentiated state of primordial chaos."
*Dàn* (澹): pronounced *tú-zhàn*.
*Xiǎo* (小): pronounced *ér-xiǎo*.
"*Yìng jié* (應節)"—in Zhang Junfang’s edition, originally written as *dé* (得).

*Suìrén* (燧人): pronounced *suì*.
*Fúxī* (伏羲): pronounced *xī*. *Suìrén* and *Fúxī* were both ancient sage-kings.
*Yáo* (): pronounced *gǔ-yáo*.
*Chún* (淳): pronounced *chún*.
*Lí dào* (離道): pronounced *lì-zhì*.
*Xīn* (心) and *xīn shí* (心識): read as written; in an earlier edition, *zhí* (職) was used, glossed as "the hearts of self and other vie for precedence."
*Bó nì* (博溺): pronounced *nǎi-lì*.
*Shì sàng* (世喪): pronounced *xī-làng*. The same below.
*Fú jiàn* (弗見): pronounced *biàn*.

Perilously upright—Guo [Xiang] says: "a uniquely correct bearing." Sima [Biao]’s edition has *guǐ* (恑), meaning "standing alone." Cui [Yuan]’s edition has *guǐ* (垝), pronounced like the *pō* (坡) in *lěipō* (累坡, "terraced slope"), describing one who holds firm with serene stability.

"Why act at all?"—in Zhang Junfang’s edition, originally written as "Why act further?"

*Lèquán* (樂全): pronounced *luò*; the same below.

"Not inherent in one’s nature and destiny"—in Zhang Junfang’s edition, originally written as "not of nature and destiny."

*Tǎnglái* (傥來): pronounced *tǔ-dǎng*, referring to what arrives unexpectedly and suddenly. Cui [Yuan] writes *dǎng* (黨), meaning "the multitude."

*Kěwéi* (可圍): pronounced *yú-lǚ*.

*Yǔ yù* (與禦) means the same as *hàn* (扞, "to ward off").

*Bùwéi* (不為): pronounced *wěi*; the same below.

"Inverted people"—Cui [Yuan] says: "those who go against their nature and destiny and do not follow the proper order." Xiang [Xiu] says: "to exchange the external for the internal may be called inversion."

*Wàipiān* (外篇) *Shuōjiàn* (說劍), chapter thirteen.

*Shénwǔ* (神武): King Zhao of Zhao—according to Sima [Biao], this is King Huiwen, whose personal name was *Hé* (何), son of King Wuling. The *Dòngjì* (洞紀) states: "The seventeenth year of King Nan of Zhou was the first year of King Huiwen of Zhao." Another note says: "By reckoning the *Chánglì* (長歷), King Huiwen and Zhuangzi were contemporaries; however, this may also be a parable used to express an idea."

*Xǐjiàn* (喜劍): pronounced *xǔ-jì*; the same below.

At *jiāmén* (夹门), pronounced *xié*.

*hào* (好): pronounced *hū-bào*; the same below.

*bùyàn* (不厭): pronounced *yú-jiàn* or *yú-yàn*.

*Kuī* (悝): pronounced *kǔ-huì*, the personal name of the crown prince.

"Dare I presume to speak?"—*zhāngběn* (张本) originally wrote *shàng* (尚).

*mù* (募): pronounced *mù*.

*shuō* (说): to persuade the king, read as written; also pronounced *yuè*.

*yǔ* (与): pronounced *shǐ-suǒ*.

*bì* (币): pronounced *cái-yòng*.

*péngtóu* (蓬头): pronounced *bù-gōng*, referring to wearing a *dōumáo* (兜矛, helmet-spear). Hair appears disheveled like *péng* (蓬, artemisia); *Chéng* [Yuan] says: "hair tangled like artemisia."

*tūbìn* (突鬓): pronounced *bì-rèn*. The hair of the temples protrudes; also said to mean short temple hair, resembling a *jùntū* (浚突, a type of cap).

*chuíguān* (垂冠): when about to fight, the cap hangs low; one says it is a *tūqímào* (突骑帽, cavalry cap).

*mànhú* (曼胡): pronounced *mò-gān*. *Mànhú* cords—referring to coarse, unadorned tassels; *Chéng* [Yuan] says: "a cloth wrapped around the neck and forehead."

*duǎnhòu zhī yī* (短后之衣): *Chéng* [Yuan] says: "for convenience in martial affairs."

*chēnmù* (瞋目): pronounced *chì-zhēn*, meaning to open wide.

*yǔnàn* (语难): meaning difficulty in speech. A brave warrior’s pent-up fury fills his chest, so his words do not flow smoothly; also pronounced *nǎi-dàn*. Once enraged, his speech becomes intimidating.

*yǔ* (与): pronounced *xián-biàn*; below, *jiàn* (见) is the same.

*wángtuō* (王脱): pronounced *tǔ-huó*. "A thousand *lǐ* without pause—ten paces to kill one man—then for a thousand *lǐ* none dare resist."

*nǎijiào* (乃校): *Sīmǎ* [Biao] says: "to compare and select the victor"; one version writes *jiàoshì* (教士).

*dūndū* (敦都): pronounced *kūn*; *Sīmǎ* [Biao] says: "to strike decisively." To test by using swords to strike and sever.

*yùzhàng* (御杖): pronounced *zhí-liàng*, meaning to wield a staff.

*Yān* (燕): pronounced *yān*. *Xīshíchéng* (溪石城) as the tip—*Chéng* [Yuan] says: "*Yān Xī* is a place name in the state of Yan; *Shíchéng* is beyond the frontier, situated in the north, thus used for the tip." The *fēng* (锋) is the point of the sword.

*Qí Dài* (齐岱) as the *è* (锷)—*Chéng* [Yuan] says: "The state of Qi’s Mount Tai lies in the east, thus used for the *è*." The *è* is the blade of the sword; also the edge of the sword.

*Jìn Wèi* (晋卫) as the spine—*Chéng* [Yuan] says: "The states of Jin and Wei are near Zhao, thus used for the spine of the sword."

*Zhōu Sòng* (周宋) as the *tán* (镡)—*Chéng* [Yuan] says: "The states of Zhou and Song lie to the south, thus used for the *tán*." The *tán* is the sword’s ring; also the sword’s *ěr* (珥, side-ring), the nose through which the belt passes; also pronounced *xún*.

*Hán Wèi* (韩魏) as the *jiá* (铗)—*Chéng* [Yuan] says: "The states of Han and Wei lie to the west of Zhao, thus used for the *jiá*." The *jiá* is the sword’s hilt; also said that the *tán* follows the edge toward the back, while the *jiá* follows the edge toward the blade.

*guǒyǐ* (裹以): pronounced *guǒ*. *Bóhǎi* (渤海) is Cangzhou; *Chángshān* (常山) is the Northern Marchmount. The Five Phases use the Five Phases to order the realm. The *Xíngdé* (刑德) discusses the governance of all living things through punishment and virtue. *Xíng* (行) follows autumn and winter, aligning with the Dao of Heaven in action and rest. *Zhì* (制) discusses forging and employing.

*mángrán* (芒然): pronounced *mò-gāng*, meaning the four directions.

The liver and lungs, pronounced *fāngfèi*.

*Qièwéi*, pronounced *yúwěi*.

Three encirclements: upon hearing righteousness and feeling shame, one circles the feast thrice yet cannot sit to eat.

*Fúbì*, pronounced *bìshì*. Enraged by the lack of courtesy, all took their own lives.

**Outer Chapter: "The Fisherman" (Fourteen)**

*Noble Authenticity*

*Zīwéi*: Sima [Biao] says it is a black forest, a name; some write it as *wéi* (帷).

*Xìngtán*: Sima [Biao] says it is a high place in the marsh where apricot trees grow; also the name of an altar.

The Fisherman is sometimes said to be Fan Li.

*Bìnméi*: One version writes *xūméi* (须眉).

*Jiāobái*: *Jiāo* means "both"; one version writes *jiǎo* (皎).

*Yú*, pronounced *yáo*, refers to walking with hands tucked inside the sleeves.

*Mèimiàn*, pronounced *shì*.

*Jùlù*: *Jù* means "to reach."

*Shìlǐ*: One version writes *chì* (饬), pronounced *chì*. *Qímín*: *qí* means "equal"; refers to the people of equal rank, or commoners.

*Jūn yǔ*, pronounced *yú*; same below.

*Yǐ wēi*: one version writes *wěi* (伪).

*Qí fèn*: one version writes *jièlí* (介离).

*Zhàng*: *zhàng* is the same as *ráo* (桡). The *Fāngyán* says an oar is called *ráo*; sometimes written with the "boat" radical.

*Ná*, pronounced *rú*: the *Shuōwén* says "to hold"; also pronounced *nǚjū*, meaning "to pull."

*Huánxiāng*, pronounced *xiāngliàng*; one version writes *xiàng* (向).

*Xùyán*: like "prior words"; also means "remaining discourse."

*Qièdài*: one version writes *shì* (侍).

*Ké*, pronounced *kǔdài*.

*Tuò*, pronounced *tǔwò*.

*Xiāngqiū*, pronounced *xīliàng*: to assist.

*Yuē xī*, pronounced *xiāngqí*: the sound of laughter.

*Zhī hǎo*, pronounced *hūbào*; same below.

*Qiū*, pronounced *shàoshī*; same below.

*Ér jīng*: *jīng* means "to manage"; also "to order" or "to measure."

*Bù zhǔ*, pronounced *zhú*.

*Zhǎngshào*, pronounced *dīngwén*; later encounters write *zhǎng* the same.

*Bù shèng*, pronounced *shēng*.

*Xíng bù*, pronounced *xiàmèng*.

*Gōngjì*, pronounced *qíqǐ*.

*Gòngzhí*: one version writes *fù* (赋).

*Chūnqiū hòulún*: later generations’ court visits do not match the ranks of former times.

*Rǎngfá*: to eliminate and strike.

*Bù chì*, pronounced *chì*.

*Bā cī*: *cī*, pronounced *sì*, means "illness."

*Zhī zǒng*: to oversee, to supervise.

*Yǐ bài*, pronounced *mài*: to destroy others is called *bài*.

*Èrén*, pronounced *wūlù*; same below. Zhang Junfang’s version writes *dérén* (德人).

*Zhī tè*, pronounced *tādé*.

*Shànpǐ*: *pǐ*, pronounced *bēiměi*, means "evil."

*Liǎng róngyán*: to adapt one’s countenance equally to both good and evil, adjusting one’s demeanor accordingly.

*Yǐ guà*, pronounced *guà*, means "to distinguish."

*Zhī tāo*, pronounced *tǔdāo*.

*Hěn*, pronounced *húhè*.

*Qiǎorán*, pronounced *qīxiǎo*: the appearance of shame and dread.

*Yù shuò*, pronounced *shuò*.

*Bù lí*, pronounced *lìzhì*.

*Gù qiáng*, pronounced *qízhàng*; same below.

*Huānlè*, pronounced *luò*; same below.

*Lù*: pronounced as written, or *lù* (录). Refers to visible form as ritual.

*Sīwéi* says *lù* means "to record" or "to lead."

*Kě yǔ wǎng*: *wǎng* means "to advance."

*Zǎo zhàn*, pronounced *dīngnán*; same below.

Drowning.

*Qiū dé yù*: *yù*, also written as 遇, means "to incur faults."

*Ér bǐ*: pronounced as written, meaning "to witness personally and compare repeatedly."

Also pronounced *pízhì*.

*Nǎi cì*: pronounced *qīyì*.

*Bō dìng*: when a boat moves, the water’s waves recede and settle.

*Páng chē*: pronounced *bùlàng*.

*Wàn shèng*: pronounced *shéngzhèng*; same below.

*Kàng lǐ*: pronounced *kǔlàng*, meaning "opposition."

*Jù*: pronounced *jù*.

*Áo*: pronounced *wǔbào*. *Qū yāo*: pronounced *yīyáo*.

*Qìng zhé*: pronounced *zhìshè*.

*Wú yǔ*: pronounced *yújù*.

---

**Miscellaneous Chapters, "Yielding the Throne" (15)**
**Governing Within**

Yao’s yielding of the world to Xu You—this passage appears in the *Free and Easy Wandering* chapter.

*Zǐzhōu Zhīfù*: pronounced *fǔ*. Li [Yun] says *Zhīfù* is a courtesy name, referring to *Zhībó*.

*Yōu yōu zhī bìng*: *Lüshi Chunqiu* cites this passage; Gao You’s commentary states *yōu* means "hidden." The *Book of Songs* says, "As if harboring hidden sorrow, my heart is not at ease"—meaning he had no leisure to attend to governing the world.

**Conducting Oneself**

Shun’s yielding of the world to *Zǐzhōu Zhībó*—some say *Zhībó* is the same as *Zhīfù*.

**Self-Attainment**

Shun’s yielding of the world to *Shànjuàn*: pronounced *juànmiǎn*. His surname was *Shàn*, given name *Juàn*, a recluse.

*Yī pí*: pronounced *yújì*; same below.

**Dwelling in Seclusion**

*Qí Chǔ*: pronounced *chānglǜ*.

**Lofty Retreat**
Shun yielded the world to his friend *Shíhù*—also written as *Hòu*, a farmer. *Shíhù* is a place name; *nóng* means "farmer."

*Juǎnjuǎn*: pronounced *quán*. The appearance of diligent integrity.

*Bǎo lì*: *bǎo* means "to shield"; *lì* means "to exert effort."

He entered the sea—Master Sima states: "Whenever it speaks of entering the sea, it refers to dwelling upon its islands and within its secluded bounds."

**Honoring Life**

King Tai *Dǎndīng*: pronounced *dàn*. *Fù*: pronounced *fǔ*, same below. *Dǎnfù* was the grandfather of King Wen.

*Dèng*: pronounced *bǐn*. A place name.

The Di people—*Xiǎnyǔn*. He did not allow what nourishes to harm what is nourished. The land is what nourishes people; now, contending over it leads to killing—this is allowing the land to harm people. People are nourished by the land, thus he did not allow the land to harm them.

*Yīn zhàng*: pronounced *zhíliàng*. *Cè*: pronounced *chūgé*. Connected in sequence—*xiānglián* read as written, meaning to follow in unbroken succession. The old pronunciation is *lìzhǎn*; some write it as *tà*, both meaning to carry and transport goods.

Mount Qi: pronounced *qíyí*. The mountain lies northwest of Meiyang in Fufeng; to its south lies Zhou Plain, where the state’s name was first changed to Zhou.

He did not allow nourishment to injure the body—he did not permit profit to burden his form. Wang [Yi] states: "Wealth and honor provide nourishment, yet one must not allow ignorance of nourishment to injure the body; poverty and hardship bring no profit, yet one must not allow the pursuit of profit to burden the form."

**Detesting Calamity**

The people of Yue murdered their ruler for three generations—*shì* pronounced *shì*.

The text states: King Xi was the first, King Hui the second, and King Ai the third.

Prince Sou—*sùgāo* cut. The text states: Prince Sou was the name of King Ai’s son.

Cinnabar Cavern: "south-facing the sun" refers to Cinnabar Cavern, a cavern in the southern mountains; it also refers to Zhuyai County.

With mugwort—*wǔgài* cut.

Jade chariot: jade linchpin. One version reads "royal chariot."

To pull—pronounced *yuán*, meaning to draw.

The reins of a chariot: the upper ropes.

And cried out—*huǒgù* cut. Some write it as "sighed."

"O Sovereign! O Sovereign!"—a repeated lament for the difficulty of the sovereign’s position.

To relinquish—pronounced *shè*.

Not to detest—*wūlù* cut. Below, in the next chapter, "truly detest" is the same.

To know light and heavy: Master Huazi—Sima [Biao] states he was a worthy man of Wei. Marquis Zhaoxi—Sima [Biao] states he was a marquis of Han.

To seize—*jūfù* and *jūbì* cuts, meaning to take. To discard, meaning to abandon. One version states: "To seize" means to pull out a written inscription; "to discard" means to sever the right hand. "Its lightness compared to Han" is a distant [matter]—the sentence ends here.

To preserve the body: The Lord of Lu—one version reads "Marquis of Lu." Li [Xian] states he was Duke Ai.

He first sent an emissary with gifts to summon Yan He, intending to make him minister.

Coarse hemp—pronounced *cū*; it bears jute. To write it as "coarse" is incorrect.

To feed oxen—*fúwǎn* cut, meaning to tend.

To send—pronounced *suǒ*. Same in the next chapter.

House and possessions—pronounced *yú*.

"Fearing mishearing"—from Zhang Junfang’s edition; the old text reads "fearing that hearing would be mistaken."

The *Lüshi Chunqiu* cites this passage, also writing "fearing mishearing."

And to leave—*wéijì* cut. Same in the next chapter.

To return—pronounced *fú*. Same in the next chapter.

Remnants—both read as written, meaning residual.

Earth—*chìyǎ* cut. Also read as written.

Coarse refuse—*cèyǎ* cut. Sima [Biao] states: "earthly refuse" is like dung and grass. Li [Xian] states: "earthly refuse" refers to dregs and lees, all things not genuine. The *Lüshi Chunqiu* cites this passage; Gao You’s commentary states: "Earth" refers to tiles and pebbles; "refuse" refers to thistles and thorns. The earthen drum and thistle drumsticks are the music of the Lord of Qi. The *Classic of Filial Piety* states: "To pacify superiors and govern the people, nothing is better than ritual; to transform customs and alter habits, nothing is better than music." Thus, these can govern the world.

Be sure to examine the means by which one attains kingship—through the sagely person’s authenticity in maintaining the self. The remainder is for governing the state, thus every movement and action must be examined therein.

The "means by which one attains" refers to the direction in which virtue extends; the "means by which one acts" refers to how one treats others. If actions are taken here, examination is not necessary.

The Pearl of Marquis Sui: The state of Sui lay near the Han River, where genuine pearls were produced. The Marquis of Sui once saw a wounded snake and applied medicine to heal it. Later, the snake returned bearing a luminous pearl, a full inch in diameter, pure white, whose radiance illuminated a room at night—this was its repayment to the Marquis. Hence, it is called the Pearl of Marquis Sui.

*Suǒyào*—pronounced *yáo*. Far from righteousness.

Ziyang was the chief minister of Duke Miu of Zheng. *Bùhǎo*—pronounced *hū*. *Jílìng*—pronounced *lì*. *Fǔxīn*—pronounced *fǔ*. *Yìlè*—pronounced *yì*. The lord’s transgression—pronounced *guò*. Meaning to pass by. To raise a rebellion—pronounced *nàn*. Same in the next chapter. And so they killed Ziyang. Ziyang was severe and harsh; he pardoned no offender. When his retainer broke a bow, fearing Ziyang’s wrathful punishment, he incited the people of the state to pursue the "hound of Jing" and thus killed Ziyang.

Adhering to laws and standards: King Zhao of Chu lost his state. Tuyang Yue—pronounced *yuè*. King Zhao’s name was Zhen, son of King Ping. King Ping heeded the slander of Fei Wuji, executed Wu She, and Wu Yun fled to Wu. Later, Wu attacked Chu, inflicting a great defeat, and King Zhao fled to Sui. At that time, the butcher Tuyang Yue followed King Zhao into exile and later returned with him to the state. *Cóng*—pronounced *cái*. *Qiǎngzhī*—pronounced *zhàng*. *Jiànzhī*—pronounced *xiàn*.
