Same below. Entering Ying—pronounced *jǐng*. *Huǐyuē*—pronounced as written, or *xiào* in Xu’s reading. *Qíwéi*—pronounced *wěi*. The Three Standards—positions of the Three Dukes. The *Records of the Grand Scribe* reads "Three Jade Tablets," stating that the three ministers of the feudal lords all held jade tablets. *Wàngshī*—pronounced as written, or *shǐ*. Upholding integrity: Yuan Xian, styled Zisi, was a native of Lu and a disciple of Confucius. His dwelling was a single *du* (a square of ten feet) on each side, a room of ten feet. *Cí*—pronounced *jī*, meaning a thatched roof of reeds. "With living grass"—using green grass in place of thatch. A door of woven mugwort. Using mulberry branches as a pivot—pronounced *shū*. Bending mulberry twigs to serve as the door’s hinge. A window of jars—pronounced *yǒu*. Breaking jars to make windows. Two rooms—husband and wife each had their own chamber. *Hè*—pronounced *gé*. *Wèisài*—pronounced *sài*. Using coarse cloth to seal the windows. Sitting upright and singing to the zither—*kuāng* means "upright." The song appears in Zhang Junfang’s edition; it was missing in the old text. *Zhōnggàn*—pronounced *àn*. Li states: *gàn* refers to the inner garment, with plain silk as the outer layer. Alternatively, *gàn* as the inner and plain silk as the outer—this was the attire of a grandee. A flowered cap—pronounced *huà*. Made from the bark of flowering trees. Straw sandals—pronounced *xǐ*. Also written as *屣* or *躧*. Meaning to tread. Alternatively, sandals without heels are called *屣*. Or, when the body cannot lift the feet, one drags them along. A staff of goosefoot—using goosefoot as a staff. The gate that answers itself—facing the gate directly. *Xī*—pronounced *qí*. The sound of laughter. *Qūnxún*—pronounced *qūn*. To retreat or withdraw. Acting in hope of worldly recognition: Sima states: *xī* means "to gaze"; one’s conduct is always guided by the desire for worldly praise, hence "acting in hope of worldly recognition." Forming cliques—pronounced *pí*. Associating closely to form factions. *Wéirén*—pronounced *wěi*. Same below for "for oneself." Teaching for oneself: One should study for oneself and teach for others, but now the opposite is done. The wickedness of benevolence and righteousness—pronounced *tè*. Meaning evil. Sima states: this refers to those who rely on benevolence and feign righteousness to commit villainy. Cultivating the will: A padded robe—pronounced *xūfěn*.

Sima states: *yùnzǔ* refers to padded silk floss—*Lunyu* says: "wearing a tattered robe lined with silk floss." *Zhǒng*—pronounced *zhǎng*. *Kuài*—pronounced *guài*. Sima states: *zhǒngkuài* means chapped and rough. Wang states: the appearance of waxing and waning without constancy. Also, the skin cracks and swells. *Pián*—pronounced *bótián*. *Zhī*—pronounced *zhúní*. The skin hardens. The tassel snaps—the cord breaks. *Zhǒu*—pronounced *zhújiǔ*. The sleeve is torn. The heel splits—the shoe is ruined. *Shāngsòng*—praising the virtue of the Shang. *Xíngxiū* *Zhān*—pronounced *zhānrán*. Some write it as *zhān*. *Zhōu*—pronounced *zhúliù*. *Zhānzhōu* is thick congee. *Zhān* is also pronounced *gān*, referring to dry cakes. *Sīmá*—some texts write it as *nièmá*. *Zìlè*—pronounced *zìluò*. *Qiǎorán*—pronounced *qīxiǎo*. Some texts write it as *xīn*. To be burdened by excess—*xiàn* appears in the Jiangnan Li edition; the old text writes it as *lì*. *Xíngxiū*—pronounced *xíngmèng*. *Bùzuò*—pronounced *bùluò*. Shame. *Qǔgāo* Prince Mou—Sima states: a prince of Wei, enfeoffed in Zhongshan, named Mou. *Zhānzǐ*—a worthy man of Wei. *Wèiquè*—Sima states: the symbolic watchtowers at the palace gate. The gate of the ruler, the Son of Heaven’s paired watchtowers. *Chóngshēng*—pronounced *zhíyòng*. Same below.

"Self-mastery"—pronounced *shēng*. Same below. If one cannot master oneself, the sentence ends here.

The spirit harbors no aversion—seen in Zhang Junfang’s edition; the old text writes it as *hū*.

"Ten thousand chariots"—pronounced *shéngzhèng*.

Delighting in the Dao.

"Not scattered"—pronounced *sùgǎn*.

Utterly exhausted—pronounced *píbài*.

Twice exiled from Lu—*The Records of the Grand Historian* states: Confucius was poor and of low status in his youth. In adulthood, he once served as an officer under the Ji family, ensuring fair measurements. He also served as an officer in charge of livestock, which flourished under his care, and thus became Minister of Works. Later, he left Lu, was rebuffed in Qi, expelled from Song and Wei, and beset with hardship between Chen and Cai. Thereupon he returned to Lu, where he was once again treated well. Duke Ding appointed Confucius as governor of Zhongdu. Within a year, all the states followed his example. From Zhongdu, he became Minister of Works, then Minister of Justice, and then acted as regent. When the people of Qi heard of this, they grew fearful, saying: "If Confucius holds power, Lu will surely become hegemonic." Thereupon they selected female musicians and sent them to the Lord of Lu. The Lord of Lu accepted them and neglected state affairs. Confucius then departed and went to Wei—this was his second exile from Lu.

Erasing his traces in Wei—meaning to sever all ties. *The Records of the Grand Historian* states: Duke Ling of Wei was aged and negligent in governance, showing no regard for Confucius. Confucius sighed and said: "If someone were to employ me, in a single month I would set things in order, and in three years, I would achieve success." Yet in the end, he was not employed.

Felling the tree in Song—*The Records of the Grand Historian* states: When Confucius was in Song, he practiced rituals with his disciples beneath a great tree. Huan Tui, the Minister of War of Song, sought to kill Confucius. Confucius departed, and later Huan Kui, resenting the place where Confucius had sat, had the tree cut down.

Beset in Shang and Zhou—*The Records of the Grand Historian* states: Shang was the land of Yin, and Zhou was Eastern Zhou. Confucius traveled widely on diplomatic missions and was once distressed in these places.

Besieged in Chen and Cai—*The Records of the Grand Historian* states: King Zhao of Chu sent an envoy to invite Confucius. As Confucius prepared to go, the grandees of Chen and Cai conspired and dispatched laborers to surround him in the wilds. He could not proceed, and his followers fell ill, unable to rise.

"Trampled"—pronounced *xícǎo*.

Ruined.

Moreover, it means "to trample and humiliate."

*Sighing deeply*—pronounced *kuìrán*.

*Spoke of*—pronounced *yǔjù*.

*Facing peril*—pronounced *nǎidàn*.

Duke Huan obtained it in Ju; Duke Wen obtained it in Cao; King Goujian obtained it in Kuaiji—these sixteen characters appear in the ancient southern repository and *Master Lü’s Spring and Autumn Annals*; they were previously omitted.

*Narrow*—pronounced *ài*, or alternatively *àoxiè*.

*Diminished*—pronounced as written.

*The sound of plucking the zither.*

*Vigorously*—pronounced *gǔrán*. Li [Yun] says: "a manner of spirited dancing."

*Grasping shields*—*gān* means "shield."

*Music*—pronounced *yuè*. The same below.

*At ease in Yingyang*—*Guangya* states: "Yu means 'to be at peace.'" One version writes *yú* (joy), meaning "to delight in."

*Gong Bo*—pronounced *gōng*. The same below.

*Attained his ambition at Qiu Shou*—Sima [Qian] says: "Gong Bo’s given name was He. He cultivated his conduct and esteemed worthy men, and all the feudal lords regarded him as virtuous. During King Li of Zhou’s calamity, when the Son of Heaven’s line was severed, the feudal lords all requested that he be made emperor. Gong Bo refused and declined the royal throne. After fourteen years of great drought, when houses burned, divination was performed at Taiyang, and the oracle declared that King Li was the baleful influence. Then the Duke of Shao enthroned King Xuan. Gong Bo returned to his clan and roamed carefree, content at the summit of Gong Mountain. Gong is a mountain, now located west of Gong County in Henan. The ancient southern repository reads: 'Gong Bo attained his ambition at Qiu Shou.' One version says 'the summit of Gong Mountain,' or alternatively 'Qiu Shou.'"

*Shame and disgrace.*

*Fields*—pronounced *quǎn*.

The ridges of the field are called *mǔ*, and the furrows between them are called *quǎn*.

*Xíng*—pronounced *mèng*. The same applies in the following chapter.
*Mànwǔ*—pronounced *wǔpàn*. It means defilement. The same applies in the following chapter.
*Tóuguī* means to cast away and return.
*Qīnglíng*—pronounced *líng*.
*Zhīyuān* lies beneath the É Mountain in Nanyang Commandery. *Liánqīng* means to follow and approach.
*Biàn Suí* is explained by Cheng Xuanying as Biàn Suí, a recluse who embraced the Dao.
*Wù Guāng* has already been explained in the "Great and Venerable Master" chapter.
The *Records of the Grand Historian* states that Yi Yin was named Aheng. Desiring to offer his services to Tang but lacking an opportunity, he became a servant of the Youxin clan, carrying his cauldron and chopping board to expound on flavors and persuade Tang toward the kingly Way. Another account says Yi Yin was a scholar-recluse; Tang sent envoys to summon him five times before he consented to go. He then discoursed on the "Unadorned King" and the "Nine Lords," after which Tang entrusted him with governance.
*Zéi* refers to secretly assessing current affairs.
*Shuòwén*—pronounced *shuò*.
*Chóu* River—pronounced *zhíliú*. Some write it as *Tóng* River, others as *Chóu*.
Sima Biao’s version writes it as *Dòng*, stating that the Dong River is in Yingchuan. Another version says it is within Fanyang Commandery.
*Qínán*—pronounced *nǎi*.
*Wǒ* means to receive.

The *Lú* River—pronounced *lǘ*. Sima [Qian] writes it as *Lú* River, located on the western border of Liaodong. Another account says it is within Beiping Commandery.

**Avoiding the World**
The state of Gūzhú—Sima [Biao] states that Gūzhú was located within the borders of Lingzhi County in Liaoxi. Bóyí and Shūqí were the two sons of its ruler. Their names were explained in the *Great and Venerable Teacher* chapter.

**Qíyáng**
The southern slopes of Mount Qí, where King Wen established his capital. This is present-day Fufeng.

**Shū Dàn**
Zhou Gong’s name was Dàn; he was King Wu’s younger brother, hence called *Shū*. One edition writes *jí* instead of *liè*.

**Blood Sacrifice**
Using the blood of sacrificial animals to anoint the written oath of alliance, which was then buried beneath the altar.

**Xī**—pronounced *xǔqǐ*.

**Qí**—to pray for blessings.

**Jìnzhì**—pronounced *zhí lì*. Below, *zhìshì* follows the same pronunciation.

**Yángxíng**—pronounced *xià mèng*. Below, *wú xíng* and *lì xíng* follow the same pronunciation.

**Yīyāo**—pronounced *yáo*.

**The Decline of Yin’s Virtue**—seen in the ancient southern repository text; the old version writes *Zhōu* instead of *Yīn*.

**"To Smear My Body"**
Fearing that wealth and nobility would defile my person. Cheng [Xuanying] explains: "If I were to coexist with Zhou, I would fear the corruption of my conduct." Another says: In the *Yielding the Throne* chapter, many passages emphasize the value of life, yet Wù Guāng and two or three others cast themselves into the water—why? The answer: The rise of Zhuangzi’s writings lies in returning to the root; the means of returning to the root begins with relinquishing glory. Thus, the *Yielding the Throne* chapter establishes a lofty standard of defiance toward the world, its purport being to refuse submission in order to refine custom, not to indulge the self in preserving life. The occasional passages that value life are merely expressions of the intent to abandon glory. Deeply weary of the toils of worldly affairs, they saw them as corrupting. Those of a lesser resolve, though clad in coarse cloth and sipping porridge, merely preserved their bodies. Those who fully embodied the Way stood far above the vulgar, transcending worldly bonds. They would sooner cast themselves into limpid waters than bow to the burdens of the age.

This was recorded in the old collection of pronunciations; it is copied here for its meaning, though it may not be entirely apt.

*The Southern Florescence: Genuine Scripture – Chapter Sentences, Pronunciations, and Meanings, Volume Six Concluded*

*The Southern Florescence: Genuine Scripture – Chapter Sentences, Pronunciations, and Meanings, Volume Seven*
*Composed by Master Bi Xu*

**Miscellaneous Chapters: "Robber Zhi" (Sixteen: "Following Nature")**

Confucius and Liuxia Ji were friends. Liuxia Hui—surname Zhan, given name Huo, style Jiqin. Some say his style was Ziqin. He dwelt beneath the willows and extended virtue and kindness. Some say *Hui* was his posthumous title. Others say *Liuxia* was the name of his fief. According to the *Zuo Tradition*, Zhan Qin lived during the reign of Duke Xi of Lu, over eighty years before Confucius’ birth. If he lived until Zilu’s death, he would have been one hundred fifty or sixty years old—impossible to have been Confucius’ friend. Thus, this is a parable.

*Zhi* (跖): *shi* rhyme. Li Qi’s commentary to the *Book of Han* states that Zhi was a great robber of Qin. Master Huang says that Zhi was the younger brother of Ji, named Yong, a great robber. The people of Qin called robbers *zhi*, hence he was commonly known as Robber Zhi.

*Cai* (才): *yong* rhyme.
*Zun* (尊): *hu* rhyme. Same below.
*Xue shi* (穴室): *kou hu*, *ku gou* rhyme. Sima’s edition reads *shu* (枢), *chi zhu* rhyme, meaning to break a man’s door-pivot and take his goods.
*Ru bao* (入堡): One edition reads *bao* (保). Zheng’s commentary to the *Record of Rites* states that a small walled settlement is called a *bao*.
*Neng zhao Shao* (能诏韶): *zhao*, to inform. Some say it means to instruct.
*Qie wei* (窃为): *yu wei* rhyme. Below, *qing wei* (请为), *wei wo* (为我), *qie wei* (窃为), *shi wei* (使为), and *yi wei* (以为) are all the same.
*Shuo zhi* (说之): *shui* rhyme.
*Piao feng* (飘风): *bi yao* rhyme.
*Yi ru* (易辱): *chi* rhyme.
*Xiu zu* (休卒): Buried on the southern slope of Mount Tai. From a Jiangnan ancient manuscript; previously missing.
*Kuai* (脍): *gu wai* rhyme. The *Explanation of Graphs* states it means finely minced meat.
*Bu* (餔): *pu gu* rhyme. To eat. Qu Yuan said, “Feeding on its dregs.”
*Guan* (冠): *guan* rhyme. A cap adorned with branches—elaborately decorated, like a tree’s abundant limbs.
*Dai si niu zhi xie* (带死牛之胁): *xu jie* rhyme. Sima states this means making a large grass belt from oxhide.
*Jiao xing* (儌幸): *gu yao* rhyme. *Jiao xing* means to hope or wish.
*Yuan wang lü mu xia* (愿望履幕下): Sima states *mu* (幕) is written as *qi* (綦), meaning one dares not look upon Zhi’s face but only gazes at his footprints before turning away. *Qi* refers to the traces of his sandals.
*Fan zou* (反走): To retreat by walking backward.
*Chen mu* (瞋目): *chi zhen* or *yi yi* rhyme. The *Expansive Elegance* states it means to open wide.
*Ru* (乳): *ru yu* rhyme. To nurture.
*Fan tian xia ren you san de* (凡天下人有三德): From a manuscript by Zhang Junfang; previously missing.
*Shao zhang* (少长): *shi zhao* rhyme. Below, *ding zhang* (丁丈) rhyme.

Fierce and bold: *hu dan* rhyme.
*Ji dan*: *gu li* rhyme. Bright.
*Zei bei*: pearls; one version reads *han bei*.
*Yin*: *ding zhong* rhyme.
*Southern envoys*: *suo li* rhyme. The following three characters share the same rhyme.
*Gong ji*: *gong* rhyme. Their conduct.
*Zhi xing*: *xia meng* rhyme. Below the same.
*Constant people*: one version reads *obedient people*; later instances follow this.
*Facing*: *hu bao* rhyme. Below the same.
*Back*: *pei* rhyme; below the same.
*Oak*: *xiang* rhyme.
*Yang*: *yang liang* rhyme. To roast and dry.
*Ju ju yu yu*: Cheng states *ju ju* describes a composed demeanor; *yu yu*, a self-contented appearance.
*Chiyou*: During the time of the Yellow Emperor, Chiyou was tyrannical. He battled at Zhuolu’s wilds, was pursued and captured, and his body dismembered and buried separately.
*Zhuolu*: *zhuo* rhyme. Sima states it is a place name, an ancient city now located eighty *li* southwest of Shanggu Commandery.
*King Wu’s regicide*: *shi* rhyme. Below the same.
*Sewn robes*: *fu gong* rhyme. Cheng states these are robes with sewn sleeves.
*Shallow sash*: Cheng states this means a narrow belt.
*Arrogant speech*: *ji biao* rhyme.
*Removing*: *qi lv* rhyme.
*Towering cap*: Li states this means a tall cap. The *Records of the Historian* state: Zhong You, styled Zilu, was a man of lowly status, crude and fond of strength, with a forthright and unyielding nature. He wore a rooster’s crest as a cap and bore a boar’s tusk as a pendant, bullying and insulting Confucius. Confucius gradually guided Zilu with ritual, and later Zilu donned scholar’s robes, offered his allegiance, and became a disciple. Zilu served as a grandee of Wei. When he heard that Kuai Kui, steward of Kong Kui’s fief, had conspired with Kong Kui to stage a rebellion and install him as Duke Zhuang, Zilu, who was outside, rushed in to confront Kuai Kui. Kuai Kui and Kong Kui ascended a tower, and Zilu sought to burn it down. Kuai Kui, in fear, sent Shi Qi and Hu Yan to attack Zilu, striking and severing his cap’s tassel. Zilu said, “A gentleman dies with his cap intact,” and so tied his tassel before perishing. One version states his body was minced into pickled meat.
His end: *zi xu* rhyme.

His end: *zhu zhuang ju* rhyme. *Zhu* means pickled meat.

Expelled twice from Lu and his traces erased in Wei—already explained in the "Abdication and Kingship" chapter.

Destitute in Qi: The *Records of the Historian* states that when Confucius journeyed to Qi, he became a retainer in the household of Gao Zhaozhi, hoping to gain an audience with Duke Jing. He discoursed on music with the Grand Master of Qi, heard the *Shao* melody, and studied it for three months, forgetting the taste of meat. The people of Qi praised him. Later, Duke Jing received Confucius with reverence, treating him with a rank between the Ji and Meng clans. In Lu, there were three ministers: the Ji clan held the highest office, while the Meng clan, though of lower rank, wielded no power—meaning the Duke treated him between these two. When Qi’s grandees sought to harm Confucius, he learned of it. Duke Jing said, “I am old and cannot employ you.” Confucius then departed—this is what is meant by “destitute in Qi.”

Besieged in Chen and Cai—already explained in the "Abdication and Kingship" chapter.

Yao was not filial and did not cede the realm to Dan Zhu. Shun was not filial—some say he fled from his blind father; others, that he married without informing him. Yu was lamed by his labors in regulating the waters. Tang exiled his lord, banishing Jie to Mingtiao. King Wu attacked Zhou and slew him at Muye. King Wen was imprisoned in Youli. The *Records of the Historian* states that when Zhou minced the Nine Lords, Lord Chang of the West sighed in secret. Chong Houhu learned of it and reported it to Zhou. In the twentieth year of Zhou’s reign, he imprisoned Lord Chang in Youli. Youli was the name of a prison in Heyang, in Tangyin.

And *qiang* rhymes with *zhang*.

Bao Jiao: Han Ying states that Bao Jiao was a recluse of the Zhou era. His clothes were tattered, his skin exposed, and he carried a basket as he gathered vegetables. He encountered Zigong on the road, who asked, “Sir, how have you come to this?” Bao Jiao replied, “Those who abandon virtue and teaching in the world are many—why should I alone not reach this state? I have heard that one who acts without being known by the age commits reckless deeds, and one who strives without being known by superiors destroys integrity. To persist in reckless deeds and destroyed integrity, yet not desist, is to be deluded by profit.” Zigong said, “I have heard that one who does not belong to the age does not partake of its benefits, and one who defiles his lord does not tread his land. Now you defile your lord yet tread his land, do not belong to the age yet gather its vegetables—whose are these?” Bao Jiao said, “Alas! I have heard that the worthy advance with weight and retreat lightly, while the incorruptible are quick to shame and slow to death.” Thereupon, he cast aside his vegetables and stood erect, withering away by the Luo River. A gentleman hearing of this said, “How unyielding was that incorruptible man! A mountain too sharp will not rise high; waters too narrow will not run deep. One who acts singularly lacks profound virtue; one whose ambitions rival heaven and earth brings misfortune upon himself. Bao Jiao may be called inauspicious. His measure of depth and shallowness led him only to this.” Some say he stood embracing a tree and withered away.

His end: *shi* rhymes with *si*.

Shentu Di: Han Ying states that Shentu Di was a man of the Shang era. Finding the age unworthy, he resolved to cast himself into the river. Cui Jia heard of it and stopped him, saying, “I have heard that the sagely and benevolent, in their place between heaven and earth, are the parents of the people. Now, for the sake of wetting your feet, you would not save a drowning man—is this permissible?” Shentu Di said, “Not so. In ancient times, Jie slew Guan Longfeng, Zhou slew Prince Bigan, and the realm was lost. Wu slew Wu Zixu, Chen slew Xie Zhi, and their states were destroyed. Thus, the ruin of states and the destruction of families were not for lack of sagacity and wisdom, but because they were not heeded.” Thereupon, he strapped a stone to himself and sank into the river. A gentleman hearing of this said, “As for incorruptibility, benevolence, and wisdom—I have yet to see them.”

Jie Zitui: The *Records of the Historian* states that Jie Zitui was a man of Jin. When Duke Wen returned to his state and rewarded his followers, the reclusive Jie Zitui was not among them. He neither spoke of his deeds nor received any emolument. Zitui said, “Lord Xian had nine sons, and only the Duke remains. Hui and Huai had no kin, and all within and without abandoned them. Heaven did not wish to end Jin, so it must have a lord. Who but the Duke should preside over Jin’s sacrifices? Heaven opened the way for him—yet these men claim it as their own doing. Is this not deceit? To steal another’s wealth is called theft—how much worse to covet Heaven’s merit and claim it as one’s own strength? Below, they conceal their crimes; above, they reward their treachery. When superiors and inferiors deceive one another, it is difficult to dwell among them.” His mother said, “Why not seek reward as well? If you die, whom will you blame?” Zitui said, “To imitate their faults would be a greater crime. Moreover, I have spoken resentful words and will not eat their emoluments.” His mother said, “At least let them know—what then?” He replied, “Words are the ornament of the person. If I wish to hide, why adorn myself? To adorn oneself is to seek recognition.” His mother said, “Can you truly do this? Then I will hide with you, and we shall not be seen again until death.” Jie Zitui’s followers pitied him and hung a letter on the palace gate, which read: “The dragon seeks to ascend to heaven, and five serpents aid it. The dragon has risen into the clouds, and four of them have found their place. One serpent alone resents that it has no station.” Duke Wen went out, saw the letter, and said, “This is Jie Zitui.” He was then preoccupied with the royal house and had not considered his merit. He sent men to summon Zitui, but he had fled. Learning that he had entered the mountains of Mianshang, Duke Wen encircled the mountains and enfeoffed them as Jie Zitui’s fields, naming them Jieshan, to record his error and honor the virtuous. Another account states that Duke Wen entreated him, but he would not emerge. When the Duke could not find him, he resolved to burn the mountain to force him out. When the mountain was set ablaze, Zitui would not emerge and was burned to death.

His end: *shi* rhymes with *si*.

Wei Sheng: A man of Lu. Some write his name as Weisheng.

Dismembered dog: *zhe* rhymes with *ke*. To display a corpse is called *zhe*. The *Feng and Hui Records* of the *Records of the Historian* state that Duke De of Qin first instituted the summer sacrifice, dismembering dogs at the four gates of the capital to ward off pestilence.

Floating pig: Butchers boil pigs—this is called *floating*.

Wielding a ladle: *cao* rhymes with *qi*.

Gourd gourd: *bi* rhymes with *yao*.

The beggar spoke of the six above as not meeting proper ends—like pigs, dogs, beggars, and those who drift in ditches.

The beggar’s *qi* may also be written as *zou*.

*Li-ming* appears in Zhang Junfang’s edition; the old text has *li*.

Zixu drowned in the river: The *Annals of Wu and Yue* state that when Zixu remonstrated, the King of Wu would not heed him. He stamped the ground and wept, saying: “Alas! How grievous—this silence shall be our undoing. Loyal ministers are silenced, while flatterers stand at the ruler’s side. Governance and instruction lie in ruins; sycophancy knows no bounds. Perverse doctrines and false words twist right into wrong. Loyalty is abandoned and attacked; thus Wu shall be destroyed. Its ancestral temples razed, its altars forsaken, its cities turned to ruins, its halls overgrown with brambles.” The King of Wu flew into a rage and bestowed upon him the *Shulou* sword, commanding him to take his own life. Zixu thereupon fell upon his sword and died. The King of Wu then took Zixu’s corpse, placed it in a *chiyi* vessel, and cast it into the river.

Bigan was disemboweled: The *Records of the Historian* state that King Zhou’s debauchery and disorder knew no end. Viscount Weizi remonstrated repeatedly, but the king would not listen, so he plotted with the Grand Tutor and Junior Tutor and departed. Bigan said: “As a minister, if one does not remonstrate even unto death, how can the common people be without blame?” He then spoke forthrightly to admonish King Zhou. Enraged, the king said: “I have heard that the heart of a sage has seven apertures—is this true?” Thereupon he slew Prince Bigan and cut open his chest to examine his heart.

*Buguo*: *wo* rhymes with *guo*. To transcend.

Illness and emaciation: *se* rhymes with *you*.
*Qilin* and *jifu*: *qi* as in *qi*, *ji* as in *ji*.
Fleeting as a galloping shadow: *guo* rhymes with *xi*.
Swift: *ji* rhymes with *li*. Sudden. Sometimes written as *ji*.
No longer: *fu* rhymes with *you*.
Restless and unsettled in appearance.
Urgent and unsatisfied: *ji* as in *ji*.
Bewildered: *mang* rhymes with *gang*.
Leaning on the crossbar: *shi* as in *shi*. The horizontal bar at the front of a carriage, on which one may rest while seated.
To obtain nothing: *wei* as in *wei*.
To cauterize oneself: *jiu* rhymes with *you*.
To collide: *liao* as in *liao*.
Head tied: *bian* as in *bian*.
Nearly: *ji* as in *qi*, meaning "close to."

**Conduct and Righteousness**
Zizhang: The *Records of the Historian* states that his surname was Zhuan Sun, his given name Shi, and his courtesy name Zizhang. He was a disciple of Confucius, steadfast in conduct and trustworthiness.
*Man Gou De* ("Fullness from Haphazard Gains"): A parable. It illustrates those who attain fullness through careless or undeserved gains.
Why not: *he* rhymes with *la*.
To practice: *xing* as in *meng*. Same below.
An exhortation: Why not cultivate virtue and conduct?
To extend: *shen* as in *xin*. Same pronunciation.
The *Shuowen* defines it as bending and extending. In the Classics, *xin* is interchangeable with *shen*. Same below.
To seek it through righteousness.
*Zang* and *ju*: Sima Biao explains this as referring to *zanghuo*—those who steal, plunder, and amass illicitly.
Then ashamed in countenance: As seen in Zhang Junfang’s edition. Formerly written as "then there would be shame in countenance."
Prime minister: *xiang* rhymes with *liang*. Same below for *xiang*.
To enter: *sao* as in *zao*.
Cheng explains: Duke Huan, whose given name was Xiaobai, killed his elder brother’s son Jiu, took his sister-in-law into his household, and employed Guan Zhong—a worthy man—as his minister.
As a minister: Sometimes written as "as a prime minister."
Regicide: *shi* rhymes with *zhi*.
Cheng explains: Initially, when Confucius heard that Tian Chengzi had assassinated Duke Jian, he bathed and went to court, informing Duke Ai: "Chen Huan has murdered his lord; I request that you punish him." This was to humble and subordinate himself to the lowly. Later, he accepted Tian Chengzi’s gifts and received his tribute—this was a contradiction between words and deeds.
To discuss: *ze* rhymes with *dun*.
Perverse: *bei* as in *zhan*.
Also rebellious: *bo* as in *fu*. To oppose.
Elders and juniors: *zhang* as in *zhang*. Same below for "eldest son."
The Five Constants: Sima Biao explains these as the years, days, months, stars, and calendrical calculations.
The *Wen* states: Heaven orders the earth; the sun orders the stars; the ruler orders the minister; the father orders the son; the husband orders the wife.
Cheng explains: Metal, wood, water, fire, and earth. Also, benevolence, righteousness, propriety, wisdom, and trust. Also, ancestors, father, self, son, and grandson.
The Six Positions: Ruler and minister, father and son, husband and wife.
The *Wen* states: These are the six positions of the *Yi*.
To distinguish: *bie* as in *lie*. Same below.

Emperor Yao executed his eldest son—Cui Yun states this refers to Yao’s execution of his eldest son, *Kao Jianming*.
Cheng explains: He did not grant the throne to Dan Zhu, calling this an execution in the name of righteousness.
*Lüshi Chunqiu* states: Yao had ten sons; Shun had nine sons.
Shun exiled his younger half-brother—*liu* means banishment.
*Mencius* says: Shun enfeoffed Xiang at Youbi, yet while he himself was Son of Heaven, his brother remained a commoner. Could this be called familial love?

King Ji was the rightful heir—*Records of the Grand Historian* states: Gu Gong had an eldest son, Tai Bo, and a second son, Yu Zhong. Tai Jiang bore a youngest son, Ji Li, who married Tai Ren, both virtuous women. They gave birth to Chang, who bore signs of sageliness. Gu Gong said: "Our line is destined to flourish—surely it will be through Chang." The eldest sons, Tai Bo and Yu Zhong, knowing Gu Gong wished to establish Ji Li as heir to pass the throne to Chang, fled together to the Jing Man. After Gu Gong’s death, Ji Li ascended, becoming Gong Ji—thus was King Ji the rightful heir.

King Wu overthrew Zhou and enfeoffed his younger brother, the Duke of Zhou, at Qufu, calling it Lu; his brother Shu Xian at Guan; and his brother Shu Du at Cai. When King Wu died, the crown prince Song succeeded, becoming King Cheng. As King Cheng was young, the Duke of Zhou assumed regency and governed the state. Guan Shu and Cai Shu suspected the Duke of Zhou of rebellion. The Duke of Zhou, bearing King Cheng’s mandate, executed Guan Shu and exiled Cai Shu—thus was this the Duke of Zhou’s execution of his elder brother.

Confucian fabrications—*Records of the Grand Historian* states: When Confucius left Chen and passed through Pu, he encountered Gongshu Shi’s rebellion in Pu, and the people of Pu detained Confucius. Among his disciples was Gongliang Ru, who followed Confucius with five private chariots. Tall and virtuous, possessed of great strength, he said: "Formerly, I followed the Master and encountered danger at Kuang; now we face it again here. This is fate. Having twice suffered hardship with the Master, I would rather die fighting swiftly." The people of Pu, alarmed, said to Confucius: "If you do not proceed to Wei, we will release you and swear an oath." They sent Confucius out the eastern gate, and he then went to Wei. Zigong said: "Can one break an oath?" Confucius replied: "It was a coerced oath—spirits do not heed it. Moreover, you were merely feigning compliance for the sake of appearances." *Wei* is pronounced *yu wei*; below, "for profit" is the same.

*Jian* in some editions is written as *jian* (to reflect).
I once saw in Zhang Junfang’s edition the character *ri* (sun) written as *ren* (person), pronounced *shi*.
*Song* is read as written, or Xu pronounces it *yao*.
*Song* means to debate or expound.
Wu Zixu gouged out his eyes—*Records of the Grand Historian* states: Wu Zixu remonstrated, but the King of Wu did not listen and bestowed upon him the *Shu Lou* sword to die. As he was about to die, he said: "Gouge out my eyes and place them on Wu’s eastern gate to witness Yue’s destruction of Wu."
The upright Gong—*Analects* states: There was an upright Gong whose father stole a sheep, and the son testified against him.
Wei Sheng (Baozi) was explained earlier.
Shen Sheng did not defend himself—*Records of the Grand Historian* states: Crown Prince Shen Sheng of Jin was slandered by Lady Li, who secretly had someone speak ill of him and placed poison in the sacrificial meat. Weeping, he said: "How could the crown prince bear such cruelty? To plot against his own father—how much more so others? Moreover, the lord is old, a man of twilight years. Could I not wait and yet seek to usurp him?" Hearing this, he fled to Xincheng. The enraged Duke Xian executed his tutor, Du Yuan Yi. Someone told the crown prince: "The one who prepared the poison was Lady Li. Why not defend yourself and clarify the truth?" The crown prince said: "Our lord is old; without Lady Li, he cannot rest or eat in peace. To argue would only anger him further." Another said: "You could flee to another state." The crown prince replied: "Bearing such an evil name and leaving—who would take me in? I will kill myself." Shen Sheng took his own life in Xincheng.
Confucius did not see his mother; Kuang Zhang did not see his father—The text states: Confucius traveled extensively on diplomatic missions but did not see his mother after her death. Kuang Zhang: The people of Qi remonstrated with his father, who drove him away, and he never saw his father again.
*Suo zhuan* is pronounced *zhuan*. To dwell in peace.

The text reads *Wu Zu* in one version as *Wu Zhi*. *Le yi* is pronounced *luò*; the same applies below.

Thus, when one upholds the correct Way without forgetfulness—forgetfulness may also be written as delusion—it means that ruler and minister need only follow the true path without wavering, and so they do not employ wealth and nobility. Those who deviate do so because their wisdom and strength are insufficient, and thus they do not resort to deception.

The recluses who cut themselves off from the world and surpass their age say: "The human heart is easily swayed. Yet if one is born alongside the worthy, one may already consider oneself above others—how much more so if one personally attains wealth and nobility?"

*Can* is pronounced *qī*; *dan* is pronounced *hé*; *zhi* is pronounced *kǒng*; *qiong* in *qiong mei* means "to exhaust"; *jiu* in *jiu shi* means "to complete." *Xia ren* is pronounced *xié*.

Desire is pronounced *wù*; contention is pronounced *zhēng*. Huang’s commentary states that this refers to Tang and Wu. According to the *Book of Shang*: "When Ge Bo failed to perform sacrifices, Tang first attacked him. Later, when he campaigned eastward, the Western Yi complained; when he campaigned southward, the Northern Di complained, all saying, 'Why does our lord come so late?' Was this not contention on all four fronts?" *Yao ming* is pronounced *yāo*.

Here follows the translation:

---

It is also akin to the ancient Southern repository edition, which was formerly incomplete.

*Chang’e* is pronounced *è*. *Guan yue* is pronounced *yào*; in one version, *guan yue* is written as *xūn chí*, while *qiàn* is pronounced *kǎn*. *Láo lǐ* is pronounced *lì dāo*. *Gāi* is pronounced *ài*; in eating and drinking, *gāi* refers to the point of swallowing. *Fèn qì* is pronounced *fèn*, meaning "full"; the same below, referring to pent-up, obstructed *qi*. *Shang ban* appears in Zhang Junfang’s edition, formerly incomplete. *Qu wèi* is also written as *wèi*. From Zhang Junfang’s edition. *Rǔ bù shě* is pronounced *shě*, the same below. *Qī jiào* is pronounced *zài yāo*. Li [Yun] says it means "to rest." *Yí* is pronounced *xǔ yè*. In Li [Yun]’s commentary on *nèi zhōu qī*, it reads "repeated seclusion within the jade tablet." *Wǎn xuān* refers to external defensive preparations. *Cái dān* is pronounced *dān*, or sometimes written as *qí*. *Liáo* is pronounced *liǎo*, describing an entangled appearance.

*End of the Seventh Fascicle of the* Nan Hua Zhen Jing Zhang Ju Yin Yi *(Exegesis and Phonetic Glosses on the* Nan Hua Zhen Jing*).*
*Beginning of the Eighth Fascicle of the* Nan Hua Zhen Jing Zhang Ju Yin Yi*.*
*Composed by Bi Xu Zi*

**Miscellaneous Chapters: "In You" (Seventeen)**
**Acting Through Non-Action**

In the *Shuo Wen*, *chǔ* means "to abide." *Yòu* is pronounced *yòu*, meaning "lenient." To indulge one’s nature is to lose it—this is called *yín*. *Yín* also means "to linger" or "to stagnate." To deviate from one’s *dé* is to lose it—this is called *qiān*. *Rén lè* is pronounced *luò*. *Tián* is pronounced *qiān*, meaning "tranquil." *Cuì* is pronounced *zài jì*, meaning "ailing" or "distressed." *Yú* is pronounced *yù*, meaning "joyful." *Pí*, according to Sima [Biao], means "to assist" or "to join." *Qiáo* is pronounced *xiāo*. Guo [Xiang] pronounces it *jiǎo*. *Jié* is pronounced *gōng jí*. Cui [Yuan] says *qiáo jié* means "a mind that is not at peace." *Zhuó* is pronounced *chì jiǎo*. *Zhì* is pronounced *chì èr*. Cui [Yuan] says *zhuó zhì* means "conduct that is not level." *Zhī xíng* is pronounced *xià mèng*. *Jǐ* means "to be sufficient in mutual support." *Xiōng xiōng* is pronounced *xiōng*, describing the clamor of competitive clamor. *Shì bèi* is pronounced *bì nèi*, meaning "contrary." *Shì xiāng* is pronounced *xī liàng*, meaning "to assist"; the same below. *Yú jì* is pronounced *qǐ*. *Shèng* is pronounced *shì zhèng*; the *Shuo Wen* says it means "to penetrate." Cheng [Xuanying] says *shèng* refers to sagely traces. *Yì* is pronounced *ní jì*, meaning "skill" or "ability." *Yú cī* is pronounced *jí sī*. *Luán* is pronounced *lì zhuǎn*. *Juàn juàn* is pronounced *miǎn*. Sima [Biao] says *luán juàn* describes a state of being coiled and unable to stretch. *Cāng* is pronounced *cāng*; Cui [Yuan]’s version writes it as *qiāng*. The *Nang* edition writes it as *cāng náng*, meaning "difficult to bear." Cui [Yuan] says *qiāng náng* is like "tumultuous disorder." The text says *cāng náng*, describing a state of haste. *Guì* is pronounced *guǐ*. *Lì* is pronounced *lì*, meaning "to oversee."

---

Thus, one who values their person over the wealth of the world may be entrusted with the world; one who cherishes their person over the world may be entrusted with the world. These two phrases differ slightly from the currently transmitted *Dao De Jing*, yet align with Wang Bi’s version. It is suspected that during the Kaiyuan era’s collation of the classic, alterations were made based on another text. Here, the version from the *Qi Yuan* is taken as definitive.

To dwell like a corpse yet manifest like a dragon—this is the image of spirit. Without speech, reverence arises. The dragon’s manifestation is drawn from its transformative ascent. Profoundly silent, like still water. The thunder’s voice is heard throughout the world. *Cóng róng*: a state of ease. *Chuī*: pronounced *chǎng shuì*. *Lèi*: pronounced *liè wěi*. Guo [Xiang] says it resembles dust motes moving of their own accord.

The sage empties the heart-mind. *Cuī Qú*: ancient worthies. *Lǎo Dān*: pronounced *tǔ lán*. "Take care, sir, not to disturb the human heart." The *Lü Shi Chun Qiu* states: "That which gives life is Heaven; that which nurtures and completes is man. To nourish what Heaven has begotten without disturbing it—this is the role of the Son of Heaven." The Son of Heaven’s actions aim to preserve Heaven’s wholeness as their purpose. Gao You’s commentary says: "*Yīng* means to offend; *gù* means affair."

*Pái*: pronounced *pí jiē*. *Nào*: pronounced *chāng lüè*. *Lián guì*: pronounced *jū wèi*, meaning "to injure" or "to benefit." *Zhuó*: pronounced *dīng jué*. *Xuán*: a state of lofty and distant vastness. *Fèn*: pronounced *fěn jiān*, meaning "to collapse." *Jiāo fèn*: the unbridled gallop of a proud steed—an unstoppable force. It also refers to defying ritual and law, like a horse running wild.

The Yellow Emperor first used benevolence and righteousness to disturb the human heart. In high antiquity, people dwelled in nests and caves, lacked fire for cooking grains, and suffered from many illnesses due to their diet. The Yellow Emperor then introduced robes and skirts, constructed houses, invented pestles and mortars, and promoted plowing and weeding, allowing the people to enjoy the comforts of clothing, food, and shelter—all through the sagely governance of the Yellow Emperor. People benefited from this and called it *ren* (benevolence). When Chiyou disrupted order and cruelly harmed the people, the Yellow Emperor punished and executed him—again, an act of sagely governance. People found this fitting and called it *yi* (righteousness). Yet benevolence and righteousness arise naturally, their origins long preceding this. Later generations, unaware of this, merely saw their traces and claimed that benevolence and righteousness began with the Yellow Emperor.

*Bā*: pronounced *pàn mò*, meaning "white flesh." *Jìng*: pronounced *xíng dìng*, meaning "shin." Without hair—this describes how Yao and Shun followed the Yellow Emperor’s traces, exhausting their hearts and bodies, their thighs emaciated without white flesh, their shins bare of fine hair. *Huān*: pronounced *huān*. *Dōu*: pronounced *dīng hóu*.

Huan Dou was the son of Emperor Hongshi, known as Hundun. He hailed from the southern foothills of Mount Chong. In the sixtieth year of his reign, Yao banished Huan Dou to Mount Chong. The San Miao were the descendants of Jinyunshi, known as Taotie. The Three Peaks (pronounced *wēi*) were mountains in the western frontier, now under Tianshui. In the sixty-sixth year of his reign, Yao exiled the San Miao to the Three Peaks. Gong Gong was the son of Shaohao, known as Qiongqi. He came from the northern frontier of Youdu in Youzhou. In the sixty-fourth year of his reign, Yao banished Gong Gong to Youdu.

*Shī* (施): pronounced *yǐ chǐ*, meaning "to extend." *Dà hài* (大骇): "great shock." *Shàn fǒu* (善否): pronounced *pǐ*, meaning "evil." *Jīn jù* (釿锯): pronounced *jīn jù*. *Zhì yān jīn jù* (制焉釿锯): refers to the application of corporal punishment. *Shéng mò shā yān* (绳墨杀焉): Cui Yun states this means "to execute by the standard of the law," while others say that those whose crimes are already determined are bound with black cords, and those not yet determined with red cords. The binding of criminals follows a hierarchy of severity. *Zhuī* (椎): pronounced *zhí zhuī*. *Záo* (凿): pronounced *zài luò*. *Jué yān* (决焉): pronounced *gǔ xuè*, meaning "to bore holes for shackles using a mallet and chisel." *Jǐ* (脊): pronounced *jí*, also *zài yì*. *Xiāng jiàn* (相践): "to trample upon." One variant reads *xiāo* (肴), meaning "chaos." *Kān* (堪): pronounced *kǔ yán*. *Yán* (岩): pronounced *yǔ xián*. *Shū sǐ* (殊死): *shū* means "to sever"; the Han ordinance states that chieftains of the Man and Yi tribes guilty of crimes are to be executed by beheading. *Xiāng zhěn zhī* (相枕之): pronounced *zhǐ*. *Héng* (桁): pronounced *hù gāng*; Sima states this refers to long-footed shackles, while Yang and Cui say that shackles for the neck and shins are all called *héng*. *Lí* (离): pronounced *lì zhì* or *lì shì*. *Qǐ* (跂): pronounced *qiū shì*. *Rǎng bì* (攘臂): pronounced *rú yáng*. *Zhì* (桎): pronounced *zhī shí*. *Gù* (梏): pronounced *gǔ dú*. *Jiē* (椄): pronounced *xiè*. *Xí* (槢): pronounced *xí*; Sima states that *jiē xí* refers to wooden wedges for shackles, while another says they are beams for restraints. The *Honglie Jie* explains that large ones serve as pillars, and small ones as *jiē xí*. *Záo* (凿): pronounced *zài bào* or *zài luò*. *Ruì* (枘): pronounced *rén ruì*; the *San Cang* states this is the tenon at the top of a pillar, fitting into a mortise like the tenon of a pillar. *Yān zhī* (焉知): pronounced *wū yú*. *Xiāo shǐ* (嗃矢): pronounced *xǔ jiāo*, referring to arrows that whistle or are fierce. One variant includes an additional twenty-four characters here: "The mutual arising of being and non-being is extreme. Zeng and Shi, Jie and Zhi—whether they possess being or non-being, how could they not collide with one another?"

*Qīng jìng mín zhèng* (清静民正): The Yellow Emperor has already explained this in the *Qi Wu* chapter. Guangchengzi was an ancient Daoist master. Kongtong is a mountain name, said to be where the Northern Dipper culminates; another account places it thirty *li* east of Yucheng in Liangguo. The character *zhī* (之) in "of the mountain" follows Zhang Junfang’s edition, while older versions read *shàng* (上). *Zhì* (质): refers to coarse and shallow physical form. Clouds and qi do not wait to gather before raining—Sima states that *zú* (族) means "to assemble"; rain before gathering implies scant moisture. Plants and trees do not wait to yellow before falling—Sima states that *luò* (落) means "to die," indicating an excess of killing energy. *Jiǎn jiǎn* (翦翦): Sima states this describes a glib appearance; another says it means "flattering." Li explains it as "shallow and narrow," while another glosses it as "cramped." *Juān* (捐): pronounced *yuè jīn*, meaning "to discard." *Yāo zhī* (邀之): pronounced *gǔ yáo*, meaning "to encounter." *Nán shǒu* (南首): pronounced *shòu*, meaning "facing the light." *Cù* (蹙): pronounced *qī*, meaning "to startle and rise." *Wú yǔ* (吾语): pronounced *yú jù*; the same below. *Yǎo yǎo* (窈窈): pronounced *wū liǎo*. *Wú wéi* (我为): pronounced *yú wěi*; the same below. *Yǒu cáng* (有藏): pronounced *cái làng*.

At one thousand two hundred years—Li Chunfeng states that heaven has no master and things grow thin—this span of one thousand two hundred years is called the "Ten Great Measures," the first being the minor reckoning of yin and yang. Four nines make thirty-six, totaling three hundred sixty; four eights make thirty-two, totaling three hundred twenty; four sevens make twenty-eight, totaling two hundred eighty; four sixes make twenty-four, totaling two hundred forty; together these sum to one thousand two hundred.

The hundred flourishings: all things thrive. *Mín* (缗): pronounced *wǔ jīn*. *Yuǎn* (远): pronounced *wàn*. *Hūn* (昏): darkness; Sima states that *mín* and *hūn* both denote absence of intent. *Wúwéi* (无为): the people are transformed. *Yúnjiāng* (云将): pronounced *zǐ jiàng*. Li explains this as "master of clouds and leader." *Hóngméng* (鸿蒙): primordial qi. The term "wind, clouds, qi" is used because they act without intent yet benefit things. *Fǔbì* (拊髀): pronounced *bì*, meaning the thigh. *Quèyuè* (雀跃): leaping like a sparrow. *Tǎngrán* (倘然): pronounced *chǐ zhǎng*. Sima states this describes a desire to halt. *Dàngrán* (蕩然): pronounced *lì zhī*. Li explains this as an unmoving appearance. *Sǒu* (叟): pronounced *sù*, a term of respect for elders. *Bùchuò* (不辍): pronounced *dīng liè*, meaning to cease. *Yuē yū* (曰吁): pronounced *kuàng yú*.

The six qi: yin and yang, wind and rain, darkness and light. Another version lists them as wind-cold, summer-heat and dampness, dryness and fire; or wind-wood, cold-water, summer-heat-fire, dampness-earth, dryness-metal, and ministerial fire—these are called the six qi. *Diào* (掉): pronounced *tú diào*. *Yǒu Sòng* (有宋): name of a state. *Yāngzhǎng* (鞅掌): pronounced *yú zhàng*.

Multitudes. *Zhīfàng* (之放): pronounced *fāng wǎng*, meaning to imitate. *Yī* (噫): pronounced *yī*, same below. *Xiānxiān* (仙仙): pronounced *xiān*, describing a light and soaring appearance. *Duò* (堕): pronounced *xǔ guī*, meaning to destroy. *Tǔ* (吐): pronounced as written, defined in the *Shuowen* as "to write." *Xìng* (涬): pronounced *hù dǐng*. *Míng* (溟): pronounced *wáng dǐng*. *Zǐmíng* (滓溟): a state of primordial chaos. Sima explains this as the natural qi. *Húnhún* (浑浑): pronounced *hù běn*. *Dùndùn* (沌沌): pronounced *tú běn*. *Bùlí* (不离): pronounced *lì lí*, same below. To uphold, excel, bear, and embody the Way—yet detest. *Wū* (恶): pronounced *wū lù*.

Relying on the multitude to attain tranquility: accepting what the multitude has seen and heard, entrusting and yielding to it, one naturally finds peace. Inferior to the many arts: *Qǐ* (绮): pronounced *qǐ*. The multitude surpasses me in knowledge and skill; the many arts of others far exceed my own. How can one not become self-ensnared? *Cǐlǎn* (此揽): pronounced *lǎn*, same as *lǎn* (揽). This passage, "taking hold of another’s state," follows the Jiangnan ancient manuscript; the old text was incomplete.

*Yáo* (侥): pronounced *gǔ yáo*. *Xìng* (幸): pronounced *xìng*, describing an insatiable pursuit of gain. Also, *yáo* means to demand. Seeking illicit advantage from the realm of august kings for one’s own fleeting fortune—how much can one gain? *Jǐhé* (几何): pronounced *jū qǐ*. Not losing: *Xīlàng* (息浪): pronounced *xī làng*, same below.

Holding the latter yet placing it first. *Qiè* (挈): pronounced *kǔ jié*, meaning to grasp or hold. *Náo* (挠): pronounced *náo* with a falling tone. The sage, by following nature, carries and sustains the myriad things, allowing them to move in accord with their inherent tendencies. *Wūhū* (恶乎): pronounced *wū*. The Way leaves nothing undone.

Concealing—pronounced *nǜ lì*. To hide. *Bùyì* (不易): pronounced as written. *Bó* (薄): pronounced *bǎi gè*. To press or compel.

*Zhìlè* (至樂), Chapter Twenty-Two: "Supreme joy lies in non-action." *Zhìlè*: pronounced *luò*. Where the same term appears within the chapter, the pronunciation remains unchanged. *Zhì* means utmost; *lè*, joy.

*Xī* (奚): pronounced *wū lù*. *Hūnhūn* (惛惛): pronounced *hūn*; in Zhang Junfang’s edition, written as . Describing a demeanor of yielding, following, and effortless compliance. *Hēnghēng* (誙誙): pronounced *hù gēng*. Li [Yun] glosses it as the appearance of hastening toward death.

"I know not whether this is joy or not-joy—whether joy truly exists or not. I find joy in the sincerity of non-action." This passage follows the Jiangnan ancient manuscript; the old text read: "I know not this joy, nor this not-joy—whether joy truly exists or not. I find sincerity in non-action as joy."

*Huàshēng* (化生): follows the Jiangnan ancient manuscript; the old text was incomplete. *Mánghū* (芒乎): pronounced *huāng*, same below. *Wùhū* (芴乎): pronounced *hū*, same below. Vaguely, things arise; suddenly, they emerge—arising from nothingness, taking form in being.

The myriad things flourish—Li [Yun] says this describes their profusion. The *Erya* states: *zhí* (職) means "to govern," referring to each having its own ruler and distinction.

*Yí qínglèi* (遗情累): to cast off emotional burdens. *Jījù* (箕踞): pronounced *jù*. Cheng [Xuanying] explains it as letting the legs hang down like a winnowing basket. *Pén wǎ* (盆瓦): earthenware vessels. "With others, dwelling"—phrase ends here.

*Chángzǐ* (长子): pronounced *zhàng zǐ*—phrase ends here. "Now transformed and dead"—follows the Jiangnan ancient manuscript; the old text read: "now further transformed and gone to death."

*Wúgài* (无槩): pronounced *gǔ dài*. Sima [Biao] says it means "to feel"; Cheng [Xuanying] says it means "to sigh in astonishment." *Yǎnrán* (偃然): Cheng [Xuanying] says this describes a state of peaceful rest. *Jùshì* (巨室): the space between heaven and earth.

*Jiàojiào* (噭噭): pronounced *gǔ diào*. Wen [Ying] says it is the sound of weeping. *Huàkōng* (化空): transformation into emptiness.

*Zhīlí Shū Gǔ* (支离叔滑): *Gǔ* pronounced *gǔ*. *Jiè* (介): pronounced *jiè*. Some take the scattering of the *ganzhi* (stems and branches) as good; others take *gǔ* (wit) and *jiè* (solitude) as good. *Shū* means "good." *Míng Bó zhī qiū* (冥伯之丘): *Kūnlún* pronounced *lì mén*. *Zhīxū* (之虚): pronounced *xū*—a vast and desolate mound, the loftiest of wastes. *Suǒ xiū* (所休): to rest.

*Zuǒ zhǒu* (左肘): pronounced *zhú jiǔ*; one variant writes *fū* (胕), pronounced *fū*. Cheng [Xuanying] says it refers to the top of the foot. *Cùcù* (蹙蹙): pronounced *gū wèi*. Cheng [Xuanying] says it describes a startled appearance. The *Jiyun* says it means "to stiffen" or "to leap."

*Jué* (): same as *língjué* (灵蹶). *Wū* (恶): pronounced *wū lù*, same below. *Chéngòu* (尘垢): pronounced *gǒu*. *Liǎng miù* (两谬): *dú* (髑): pronounced *dú*; *lóu* (髅): pronounced *lóu*. *Xiāorán* (骁然): pronounced *kǔ yáo*—the appearance of white bones, a withered form.

*Qiào* (撽): pronounced *kǔ diào* or *gǔ de*. The *Shuowen* writes it as , meaning "to strike from the side." *Mǎ zhuī* (马捶): pronounced *zhuō ruǐ*—a horsewhip. *Kuì yí* (愧遗): pronounced *wéi jì*. *Dòng* (冻): pronounced *dīng gòng*. *Něi* (馁): pronounced *nú zuì*.

*Yuán* (援): pronounced *yuán*. *Zhěn wò* (枕卧): pronounced *zhēn zhǎi*. *Jiàn mèng* (见梦): pronounced *xián biàn*. "Xiangzi’s discourse"—follows Zhang Junfang’s edition; the old text was incomplete.

*Cóngrán* (从然): pronounced *qī róng*—describing a state of ease and rest. Zhang Junfang’s edition writes it as *fànrán* (泛然). *Fùshēng* (复生): pronounced *fú*. *Shēn pín* (深矉): pronounced *pín*. *Cù lèi* (蹙类): pronounced *zǐ liù*; originally also written as *cù* (顣), pronounced *yú gě* below. Li [Yun] says *píncù* describes a sorrowful appearance.

"Joy"—pronounced *luò*. "And again"—pronounced *fú yòu*. "Is it for the toil of the living?"—follows Zhang Junfang’s edition; the old text read: "and again for the toil of the human world?"

*Míngshí* (名实): *chǔ* (褚): pronounced *zhù*.

The *náng* (囊): to hold with tolerance and receptivity.

*Gěng* (绠): pronounced *gé měng*, a well-rope for drawing water. *Jí* (汲): pronounced *jū jí*, the act of drawing water. *Ér zhòng* (而重): pronounced *zhí yòng*.

Regarding the "sea crow"—Sima [Qian] notes in the *Guoyu*: it is called *àijū* (爱居). When it alighted outside the eastern gate of Lu for three days, Zang Wenzhong ordered the people to offer sacrifice to it—not to the Lord of Lu. Yet it was worshipped in the Lu temple, hence the text refers to the "Lord of Lu," and the *Ode* speaks of the *yuān jū* (爰居). Another name is *zá xiàn* (杂县). It stands eight *chi* tall, its form resembling the *fènghuáng* (凤皇), and its cry sounds like *yà* (讶).

*Shāng* (觞): pronounced *shāng*. "In the temple"—Sima [Qian] explains this means it was given drink within the temple.

*Jiǔ sháo* (九韶): pronounced *cháng yáo*, the name of Shun’s music.

*Xuàn* (眩): pronounced *xiàn*, meaning the eyes have no fixed focus.

*Yī luán* (一脔): pronounced *lǐ zhuǎn*.

*Tán* (坛): pronounced *dà dān*. Sima’s edition writes it as *chán* (澶), pronounced *dàn*, meaning to ford water.

*Zhōu* (洲): an islet in the water.

*Shí zhī* (食之): pronounced *sì*.

*Qiū* (鳅): pronounced *qiū tiáo*.

*Suí xíng* (随行): pronounced *hù gāng*.

*Wěi* (委): pronounced *wēi*.

*Shé* (蛇): pronounced *yǐ zhī*, describing a state of ease and unhurried self-possession.

*Náo náo* (譊譊): pronounced *nǎi jiāo*, the sound of clamor.

*Xián chí* (咸池): the Dongting [Lake]—already explained in the "Heavenly Revolutions" chapter.

*Rén zú* (人卒): pronounced *zǐ hū*, meaning the multitude; also pronounced *cùn hū*.

*Huán* (还): pronounced *huán*, meaning to encircle.

"Those [people] must differ in their likes and dislikes"—the Jiangnan ancient manuscript reads: "Those must differ in their likes and dislikes, hence their differences." The old text read: "Those must differ in their likes and dislikes, thus they are different."

*Huà jī* (化机):
*Dào cóng* (道从): Sima [Qian] explains this means beside the Way. Originally written as *tú* (徒).

*Qiān* (攓): pronounced *jū niǎn*.

To uproot.

*Péng bù* (蓬步): pronounced *dōng qiè*.

*Rǔ guǒ* (汝果): the *Yuánjiā* edition reads *guò* (过).

*Yǎng* (养): Sima [Qian]’s edition reads *mù* (暮), meaning death.

*Yú guǒ* (予果): the *Yuánjiā* edition reads *guò* (过).

*Huān* (欢): Sima [Qian]’s edition reads *huān* (嚾), meaning the cry of life.

*Zhǒng* (种): pronounced *zhāng yǒng*.

*Yǒu jǐ* (有几): pronounced *jū qǐ*.

As when the *áo* (鳌) becomes the *chún* (鹑)—seen in Liu’s alternate edition; originally omitted.

When it obtains water, it becomes *jì* (继), pronounced *jì*. Sima [Qian]’s edition reads *jì* (继), explaining: though the myriad things bear signs, only with the qi of water and earth do they succeed one another in generation. The *Shuōwén* defines *jì* (继) as continuation. Another saying: the reversal of *là* (腊) is *jué* (绝). The present text reads *xùn* (), which is the reversal of *là* (腊).

At the juncture of water and earth, it becomes *lóng* (龙), pronounced *hù wā*.

*Pín* (蠙): pronounced *bù tián*.

*Zhī yī* (之衣): Sima [Qian] explains: the roots of things lie at the water-earth juncture, spreading through the water; when viewed from above, they are unseen, but when scooped, they may be obtained—like silk floss submerged in water. The people of Chu call it the *wā pín* (鼃蠙) garment.

Cheng [Xuanying] says: green moss spreads in the water like silk floss; commonly called the frog’s garment.

It grows on mounds, *líng tún* (陵屯), pronounced *tú mén*. A hill or mound.

Then it becomes *líng xì* (陵舄), pronounced *xī*. Sima [Qian] says: things form through water, yet land-borne life arises on mounds, transforming into plantain. Its name is changed to *líng xì*. Another name is *zé xì* (泽舄), altering with dryness and moisture. Yet none know its origin—things transform without fixed form. When humans die, some may turn into grasses or trees; the essence of grasses and trees may become human.

*Líng xì* that obtains *yù qī* (郁栖) becomes *wū zú* (乌足). Li [Yun] says: *yù qī* is manure and soil; when mound crows dwell in manure and soil, they transform into *wū zú* grass. Sima [Qian] says: *yù qī* is an insect’s name, *wū zú* a grass’s name, growing by water. When *yù qī* resides among mound crows, it transforms into *wū zú*.

The root of *wū zú* becomes *qí cáo* (蛴螬), pronounced *qí cáo*, an insect’s name. Its leaves become *hú dié* (胡蝶), pronounced *dié*. Sima [Qian] says: the butterfly. Grass transforms into insects, insects into grass—without beginning or end.

*Hú dié xū yě* (胡蝶胥也): *xū* means mutual arising. Things mutually generate one another.

Transforming into an insect born beneath the stove, Sima [Qian] says: it arises from warmth and *qi*. Its form resembles a cast-off skin, pronounced *tuō tā*. That is, shedding its skin. Sima [Qian] pronounces it *yuè*, saying: newly emerged, its skin is smooth and fine. Its name is *qú duō* (鸲掇), pronounced *qú duō*. *Qú duō* is an insect’s name.

*Qú rǎng* (鸲攘) after a thousand days becomes a crow; its name is *gān* (乾), pronounced *gān*. The foam of *yú gǔ gān* (余骨乾)—*yú gǔ*’s foam, pronounced *mò*—Li [Yun] says: the juice in its mouth—becomes the *sī mí* (斯弥) insect. *Sī mí* becomes *shí xī* (食酰), pronounced *xī*. Sima [Qian] says: the midge that swarms over bitter wine.

*Shí xī* arises from *yí lù* (颐辂), *yí lù* pronounced *yí lù*, an insect’s name. It arises from *huáng xuàn* (黄軦), pronounced *kuàng*, an insect’s name. *Huáng xuàn* arises from *jiǔ yóu* (九猷), pronounced *yóu*. Li [Yun] says: *jiǔ* means long-lasting; *yóu* is an insect’s name. *Jiǔ yóu* arises from *mào ruì* (瞀芮), pronounced *mào ruì*. Small insects, fond of flying in chaotic swarms. Also a grass’s name.

*Mào ruì* arises from *fǔ quán* (腐蠸), pronounced *fǔ quán*, an insect’s name. The *Ěryǎ* says: another name is *shǒu guā* (守瓜); also called the yellow beetle in melons. Another says: the firefly. Another says: the mole rat.

*Fǔ quán* arises from *yáng xī* (羊奚), *yáng xī* pronounced *bǐ zhì*. The two characters above in red ink follow Zhang Junfang’s edition; previously omitted. It arises from *bù shī* (不), pronounced *xī yǐn*. Long bamboo gives rise to *qīng níng* (青宁). Sima [Qian] says: *yáng xī* is a grass’s name, its root resembling turnip; when it joins with long bamboo, they form a being—all arising from unlike kinds. *Qīng níng* is an insect’s name.

*Qīng níng* gives rise to *chéng* (程). Jia [Kui] says: *chéng* is called *bào* (豹) in the Central States, *mò* (貊) by the Yue people—another name for the leopard. *Chéng* gives rise to the horse; the horse gives rise to humans. The *Shǐjì*’s *Chronological Tables* say: in the twenty-first year of Duke Xiao of Qin, a horse gave birth to a human—all transformations of a single *qi*, adapting without limit.

*End of the Eighth Fascicle of the* Nan Hua Zhen Jing Zhang Ju Yin Yi
*Beginning of the Ninth Fascicle of the* Nan Hua Zhen Jing Zhang Ju Yin Yi

Master Bi Xu crafted *Miscellaneous Chapters: "Attaining Life" (23)*

"Attaining Life" (*dá shēng*): "Attaining" (*dá*) means to flow freely; "life" (*shēng*) refers to that which has life.

*Wú lí* (无离): *Lì* rhymes with *zhì* (智). Same below.
*Xiāng tiān* (相天): *Xiāng* rhymes with *liàng* (亮).
*Zhuān xī* (专熙): Guan Yin—Guan, the Prefect of the Pass, styled Gongdu, was a grandee of Zhou.
*Bù zhì* (不窒): *Zhì* rhymes with *xī* (悉).
*Dǎo huǒ* (蹈火): *Dǎo* rhymes with *bào* (报).
*Zhī liè* (之列): *Liè* pronounced as *lì* (例).
*Yú yǔ* (予语): *Yǔ* rhymes with *jù* (据).

"This is but form and color."—From the ancient southern repository; previously missing.
One who attains *this* (*shì*) grasps the Dao of formlessness and unchanging transformation.

*Zhèng yān* (正焉): In Zhang Junfang’s edition, formerly written as *zhǐ* (止).
*Wù yān* (物焉): *Yān* rhymes with *qián* (虔).
*Zhī zhuì* (之坠): *Zhuì* rhymes with *lèi* (类). Same below.
*Chéng* (乘): Also pronounced as *shéng* (绳).
*È* (遌): Pronounced as *wù* (悟). The *Erya* states: "to oppose, to resist." Guo [Pu] says: "to clash, to strike."
*Bù shè* (不慑): *Shè* rhymes with *shè* (涉). Means fear.
*Bù zhé* (不折): *Zhé* rhymes with *shé* (舌).
*Zhèn* (镇): Pronounced as *mò* (莫).
*Gān Mòxié* (干镆邪): Gan Jiang and Mo Xie, names of ancient sharp swords—explained in the *Great Ancestral* chapter.
*Zhì* (忮): *Zhì* rhymes with *chǐ* (豉). Li [Yi] says: "to harm, to injure." The *Zishu* states: "cruel, ruthless."
*Piāo wǎ* (飘瓦): *Piāo* rhymes with *yáo* (遥). Li [Yi] says: "to drift and fall."
*Bù kāi rén zhī rén* (不开人之人): Liu De’s edition reads *bù kāi rén zhī rén*; formerly written as *bù kāi rén zhī tiān* (不开人之天).
*Bù yàn* (不厌): *Yàn* rhymes with *yàn* (艳).
*Jī hū* (几乎): Pronounced as *jī* (机).

A Single Mind
*Jū* (痀): Pronounced as *yǔ* (禹). The text states: "hunchbacked, with a curved spine."
*Lǚ* (偻): Pronounced as *lǚ* (缕).
*Chéng tiáo* (承蜩): *Tiáo* rhymes with *tiáo* (条). The cicada. The text states: "*Chéng tiáo* means to catch cicadas by sticking them." The ancients used them to make minced meat.
*Yóu duō* (犹掇): *Duō* rhymes with *huó* (活). Means to pick up.
Fifth and Sixth Months: Sima [Biao] says this is the time for catching cicadas. Cheng [Xuanying] says: "Practicing with stacked pellets approaches half a year."
*Lěi wán* (累丸): *Lěi* rhymes with *bǐ* (彼). The same below. The text states: "to stack pellets on the tip of a pole."
*Zī* (锱): Pronounced as *qí* (其). The *Shuowen* states: "six *zhū*." Another saying: eight *liǎng* are called *zīzhū*. *Zhū* (铢) is pronounced as *shū* (殊). The *Shuowen* states: "a *quán* is the weight of one-tenth of a millet grain." Another saying: ten millet grains make a *lěi*, and ten *lěi* make a *zhū*.
*Jué* (橛): Pronounced as *yuè* (月).
*Zhū* (株): Pronounced as *zhū* (诛).
*Jū* (拘): Pronounced as *jū* (俱). Same pronunciation as *qú* (劬), meaning a standing piece of wood. *Jué*, *zhū*, and *jū* refer to cut wood. Zhang Junfang and the Southeast Ancient Repository, along with the *Jiyun*, all follow *jū*. The old edition also follows *jū*; the current version may be a copying error.

Grasping the arm—*zhí* means to hold. *Ruò gǎo*: pronounced as *kǔ lǎo*. *Jīn zhòng*: the abyss of *Shāngshēn*. *Chéng* states: *Shāngshēn* is the name of an abyss; its shape resembles a goblet, hence the name, located in the state of Song. *Cāo zhōu*: pronounced as *qī cáo*. *Chéng* states: to seize. *Shuò néng*: pronounced as *shuò*; the same below. *Zhī fù*: pronounced as *fāng fú*; the same below. *Wū wǎng*: pronounced as *wū*. *Wǎ zhù*: pronounced as *zhū wù*. Seen in the Jiangnan Ancient Repository edition; the old edition has *qì*. *Lǐ* states: to strike. *Lǚ Làn* quotes *Zhuāngzǐ*: "Those who shoot with earthen pellets soar; those who shoot with hooked pellets contend; those who shoot with golden pellets perish. The pellets are the same, yet some perish—this is because they must have something externally valued. When something is externally valued, it leaks, for internally it is hollowed out." *Fúzǐ* states: "King Xia sent Yi to shoot at a target the size of a square *cùn* and a bullseye the size of a *cùn* in diameter; he never missed a shot. The king said, 'Well done! If you hit the target again, I shall reward you with ten thousand gold; if you miss, I shall strip you of ten cities.' Yi’s countenance changed, his heart trembled, and he missed every shot. The king said, 'This is because joy and fear became your calamity, and ten thousand gold became your misfortune.'" This meaning is similar. *Dàn*: pronounced as *tú dān*. *Jì*: to detest, to loathe. Also means difficulty, alarm, or fear. *Hūn*: also written as *殙*, pronounced as *hūn*. *Mén miǎn*: two pronunciations; *pǔ xùn* glosses as *jīn*. *Shàn mù*: Tian Kaizhi. *Lǐ* states: *Kaizhi* is his given name. *Zhōu Wēi Gōng*: *Cuī* states: *Zhōu Wēi Gōng* Zao. *Chéng* states: a descendant of the Duke of Zhou, his name is obscure; he held fiefs in Zhou and was posthumously titled *Wēi*. *Zhù*: pronounced as *dà*. *Shèn*: pronounced as *shì zhěn*. Also written as *贤*. *Xuéshēng*: *Sīmǎ* states: to study the Dao of nourishing life. *Cāo*: pronounced as *qī cáo*. *Bá*: pronounced as *pú mò*. *Lǐ* states: a rake. *Huì*: pronounced as *yǐ suì*. A broom. *Ér biān*: as written; *Cuī*’s edition has *匿*, stating: to hide. When he saw the emaciated and weak ones lagging behind, he hid them in pens to nourish them. *匿*: pronounced as *sōu*; *Zhào sōu* means to walk without advancing. *Shàn Bào*: pronounced as *shàn*; *Lǐ* states: a recluse of Lu; *Zhāng Yì* states: a wealthy man of Lu. "Seeing tall gates and hanging curtains"—*jiàn*. Seen in *Liú*’s alternate edition.

The previous text is missing. None flee—*Lǐ* states: *zǒu* means "to go." *Sīmǎ* states: *zǒu* means "to arrive," signifying that none fail to come to the gate to serve the wealthy and noble.

*Jièwēi*: the perilous path of obstacles and dangers, to be feared.
*Zútú*: pronounced *zǐ hū*. *Zú* means "multitude"; most dreaded. Seen in the *Jiāngnán Gǔ Cáng* edition; formerly written as *qǔ*.
*Rènxí*: pronounced *shèn*. Sleeping garments; from *zhú*, meaning "sleeping mat." Also written as *rèn*.
*Qùyì*: *Zhù Zōngrén*—*Chéng* states: the six invocators of the *Tàicháng*, an official post.
*Láocè*: pronounced *chū gé*. *Lǐ* states: *láo* is a pigsty; *cè* is a wooden pen. *Shuō* reads as written; also pronounced *shǐ ruì*.
*Zhì*: pronounced *zhí lì*.
*Xīwù*: pronounced *wū lù*.
*Huàn*: pronounced *huàn*, meaning "to rear." Seen in *Zhāng Jūnfáng*’s edition; formerly written as *nì*. The rhyme dictionary does not include it; likely a transcription error. The original also writes it as *xī*.
*Yuē*: pronounced *cè zhāi*. The same in the following chapter.
*Jí*: pronounced *zài yè*.
*Kāo*: pronounced *kǔ gāo*. The buttocks.
*Diāozǔ*: pronounced *zhuāng lǚ*. *Zǔ* is a vessel for holding meat; *diāo* means "carved and adorned."
*Wéizhì*: pronounced *wěi*. *Wéi* is the same as *wèi*.

As with *sì* (food).
*Kāngzāo*: pronounced *kāng*, *zāo*.
*Cuò*: pronounced *qī gù*. To place.
*Zhuàn*: pronounced *zhí zhuǎn*.
*Shǔnshí*: pronounced *zhǔn*. *Zhuàn* is akin to "inscribed"; *shǔn* is akin to "tray."
*Jùlǚ*: pronounced *lì zhǔ*. Coffin and outer coffin.
What distinguishes it from *zhì*?—Seen in *Zhāng Qiánfū*’s edition; formerly omitted.

**Resolving Doubts**
*Zhòngfù*: pronounced *fǔ*.
*Éi*: pronounced *yú dài*. *Shuōwén* states: an expression of loathing.
*Yí*: pronounced *tǔ dài*. Sīmǎ states: a countenance of weariness. Lǐ states: *éi yí*, an appearance of lost soul.
*Shuò*: pronounced *suǒ zhǔ*.
*Huángzǐ Gào’áo*: Sīmǎ states: surnamed Huáng, styled Gào’áo. A worthy scholar of Qí.
*Guǐwù*: pronounced *wū*.
*Fènfú*: pronounced *fěn*.
*Chù*: pronounced *chì liù*. When this *qi* scatters and does not return, it becomes insufficiency. Lǐ states: *fèn* means "full"; *chù* means "congealed." When the spirit is obstructed, *yáng* binds within, and *húnpò* disperses without—hence, it is called insufficiency.
If it ascends but does not descend, it makes one prone to anger; if it descends but does not ascend, it makes one prone to forgetfulness. Lǐ states: when *yáng* scatters, *yīn* congeals—hence anger; when *yīn* rises and *yáng* lies hidden—hence forgetfulness.
If it neither ascends nor descends: pronounced *dīng zhòng*. It strikes the heart and causes illness. Lǐ states: when above and below are disharmonious, *yīn* and *yáng* contend and assail the heart; the heart governs the spirit—hence illness.
*Shěn yǒu lǚ*: Sīmǎ’s edition reads *Shěn yǒu lòu*. States: *shěn* is muddy water; *lòu* is the name of a spirit.
*Zào yǒu jì*: pronounced *jié*. Sīmǎ states: *jì* is the name of the stove spirit. She wears red robes and resembles a beautiful woman.
The vexations of the inner door and the *fánrǎng*—*léitíng* dwells there. Liú Déyī’s edition reads *fénrǎng*, stating: *fénrǎng* is the name of a thunder spirit.
*Bèi*: pronounced *péi*.
*Āguī*: pronounced *hù wō*.

The *lóng*, pronounced *lóng*: Sīmǎ states *bèi āguī lóng* is the name of a spirit, resembling a small child, one foot and four inches tall, clad in black robes with a red sash and a large crown, bearing a sword and holding a halberd.

*Yìyáng*, pronounced *yì*: Sīmǎ states it has the head of a leopard and the tail of a horse. One edition reads "dog’s head." Another states it is the name of a spirit.

*Wǎngxiàng*: Sīmǎ’s edition reads *Wúshāng*, stating it resembles a small child, with a reddish-black complexion, red claws, large ears, and long arms. Another states it is the name of a water spirit.

*Zhēn*, pronounced *suǒ zhēn*: Sīmǎ states it resembles a dog, with horns, a patterned body, and five colors.

*Kuí*, pronounced *qiú guī*: It resembles a drum yet has but one foot.

*Páng*, pronounced *páng*.
*Huáng*, pronounced *huáng*.
Sīmǎ states it resembles a snake, with two heads and a five-colored pattern.

*Wēi*, pronounced *wēi*.
*Shé*, read as written.
*Gǔ*, pronounced *gǔ lù*: *Shuōwén* states it is where the spokes converge.
*Yuán*, pronounced *yú yuán*: *Shuōwén* states it is the shaft.

*Zhūguān*: Sīmǎ’s edition reads *Yúguān*, stating it is the crown of the Yú kingdom, its form resembling a conch.

*Wù*, pronounced *wū lù*.
*Pěng*, pronounced *fāng yǒng*.
*Zhěn*, pronounced *chì yǐn*: Lǐ states it describes a great laugh.

*Bùzhēng*, pronounced *jǐ shěng suǒ jǐng*.

Personal Names.

*Wéi*, pronounced *yú wěi*: Sīmǎ states it refers to the King of Qí. The original text reads King Xuān of Zhōu.

*Xūjiāo*, pronounced *jū qiáo*: Lǐ states it describes holding the head high. Chéng explains it as empty cowardice and haughty arrogance, a self-reliant demeanor of qi.

*Xiǎng*, pronounced *xǔ wén*.
*Jǐng*, pronounced *yú lǐng*: Lǐ states it means responding to echoes and gazing at shadows.

Those who saw it turned and fled—*jiàn* follows the *Hǎi* edition; the old text was missing.

Habit becomes nature.

Lǚliáng: Sīmǎ states it is where the river cuts through stone, now in Lishi County west of the Western River; the world calls it Huángliáng. *Huáinánzǐ* records that in ancient times, before the Dragon Gate was carved, the river flowed above the Mèng Gate.

Chéng explains that in Sòng’s Péngchéng Prefecture there is a Lǚliáng where the water plunges downward—this must be the place.

The waterfall drops thirty *rèn*—a *rèn* is seven feet.
*Mò*, pronounced *mò*.
*Yuán*, pronounced *yuán*.
*Tuó*, pronounced *tú duō*.
*Biē*, pronounced *bì miè*: afflicted with bitter illness.

To save them—*zhěng* means to rescue them.

With hair unbound, pronounced *pí jì*.
Singing as they walk—Sīmǎ’s edition reads *xíngdào*, stating it is the path they always follow.

*Cháng*, pronounced *dīng wén*—same below.

And with Qi: Sīmǎ states the swirling water is like a millstone’s motion. Guō explains that water turning like a millstone’s wheel and spiraling inward is what is meant by *qí*.

People east of the Pass call a mill *wèi*.

And *gǔ*, pronounced *gǔ* (ancient *hū* cut).

Sīmǎ states it is surging waves. Guō explains that water receding and then surging forth is what is meant by *gǔ*.

Selecting timber—
Zǐ Qìng: Lǐ states he was a master carpenter of Lǔ. *Zǐ* is the office; *Qìng* is the name.

*Jù*, pronounced *jù* (alternate *jù èr*), Sīmǎ states it is a musical instrument resembling the *jiā zhōng*, carved from wood, identical to the *yú*. It is said the *fēi yú* is a celestial divine beast with a deer’s head and dragon’s body, and the base of bells and drums is often adorned with fierce beasts.

*Hào*, pronounced *hū bào*. To diminish.

The *Wén* says: when *qi* is depleted, the heart stirs; when the heart stirs, the spirit is not focused.

*Qí*, pronounced *zhāi*. Same below.

*Zhé rán*, pronounced *dīng xié*. The appearance of stillness.

*Gōng cháo*, pronounced *zhí yáo*.

*Gǔ*, pronounced *xiāo* (alternate *gǔ*), means disorder.

*Chéng jiàn*, pronounced *xián biàn*.

*Qí yóu shì yǔ*, pronounced *yú*: *yóu* is seen in an ancient southern Jiāngnán manuscript; the old text reads *qí shì yǔ*.

Excessive skill—
Dōngyě Jì: Lǐ states *Dōngyě* is the surname; *Jì* is the name. Sīmǎ states Xún Qīng wrote of *Dōngyě Bì* as a charioteer.

*Jiàn*, pronounced *xián biàn*. Same below.

Duke Zhuang—*Jiù Yīn Yì* states he was Duke Zhuang of Wèi.

*Zhòng shéng*, pronounced *dīng zhòng*. Same below.

*Wén fú guò yě*—Sīmǎ states this refers to exceeding the patterns of woven silk.

*Shǐ zhī gōu bǎi ér fǎn*—Sīmǎ states: Jì, priding himself on his skill, drove the chariot in curves like a hook, tracing the same path a hundred times without ceasing.

Yán Hé, pronounced *hù là*. A worthy man of Lǔ. The *Yuán Jiā* edition reads *Lú Cuī* instead.

*Wàng wèi*

Gōng Chuí, pronounced *chuí*. A skilled craftsman of Yáo’s time.

*Xuán ér gài guī jǔ zhǐ yǔ wù huà ér bù yǐ xīn jī*, pronounced *jī*. Thus his spirit-terrace remained unified and unobstructed.

*Xuán* means swift. Gōng Chuí applied his swift skill, yet still did not depart from the compass and square. In the space between brushstrokes, his response to the transformations of things was no different, and his mind never lingered—thus he followed the natural nature of things, so his spirit-realm congealed into oneness without obstruction.

*Zú jù*, pronounced *jiǔ zhù*.

*Wàng yào*, pronounced *yī yáo*.

*Wàng shì fēi*—Zhāng Jūnfáng writes *wàng shì fēi*. The old text reads *zhī wàng shì fēi*.

*Shěn shòu*

Sūn Xiūchéng states he was a man of Lǔ.

*Zhǒng*, pronounced *zhāng yǒng*. Sīmǎ states it means "to arrive."

*Ér chà*, pronounced *chì jià*. Sīmǎ states it means "to inform."

*Zǐ Biǎn Qìngzǐ*, pronounced *piān*. Lǐ states: *Biǎn* was his surname, *Qìngzǐ* his courtesy name. Chéng states he was a worthy man of Lǔ.

*Lín nàn*, pronounced *nǎi dàn*.

*Bīn yú*, pronounced *bì rèn*. Means "to reject."

*È yù*, pronounced *wū*. Same below.

*Máng rán*, pronounced *wǔ gāng*. The appearance of being without intent.

*Páng huáng*, pronounced *bàng huáng*. Chéng states it means "the appearance of unfettered movement."

*Zhǎng*, pronounced *dīng zhàng*.

*Míng wū*, pronounced *wū*.

*Ruò jiē*, pronounced *qí liè*. Means "to bear."

*Jiǔ qiào*, pronounced *kǔ diào*.

*Bǒ*, pronounced *bǐ wǒ*.

*Jiǎn*, pronounced *jǐ niǎn*.

*Ér bǐ*, pronounced as written.

*Wéi jù*, pronounced *yú wěi*.

*Yǐ lè*, pronounced *luò*. Same below.

*Shí zhī*, pronounced *sì*.

*Wěi yú*, pronounced *wēi*.

*Shé*, pronounced as written. Lǐ states it means a large crow swallowing a snake. Sīmǎ states *wěi shé* refers to the loach.

*Zé ān píng lù ér yǐ yǐ*—*ān* is seen in Liú Dé’s edition; the old text omits it.

*Kuǎn qǐ*—Lǐ states *kuǎn* means "hollow"; *qǐ* means "to open." Like an opening in emptiness, what is seen is small. Alternatively, *kuǎn* means "bent"; *qǐ* means "to reveal."

*Xī*, pronounced *xī*. A small mouse.

*Yàn*, pronounced *yàn*. A kind of sparrow.

*Zá Piān: Shān Mù* (Chapter 24)

*Xiǎn huì zài Dào*

In the mountains—*Shì Míng* states *shān* refers to what the mountains produce; *chǎn* means "to give birth to things." *Shuō Wén* states: *shān* means "to disperse," for it disperses *qi* and gives birth to the myriad things.

Great trees—*Shì Míng* states: *mù* means "to cover," for they cover the earth and grow. Lǐ states *mù* means "multitude," the general term for forests. *Bái Hǔ Tōng* states: *mù* means "to surge," for the Master—Chéng states the disciples called Zhuāngzǐ "the Master"—rested there. *Shě yú*—Chéng states *shě* means "to rest."

*Pēng*, pronounced *pǔ péng*. Means "to boil."

*Wú zǐ*, pronounced *zǐ*. Means "to slander."

*Wéi liàng*, pronounced *liàng*. The transmission of human relationships.

Sīmǎ states: affairs of a kind that can be transmitted and practiced.

*Zé cuò*, pronounced *zǐ wò*. Means "to thwart."

*Wén pí wéi zāi*: Disaster arises from adornment.

*Shì Nán Yí Liáo*, pronounced *liǎo xiāo*. Sīmǎ states: his surname was Xióng, given name Yí Liáo; he dwelled south of the market and thus took it as his epithet—a man of Chǔ.

*Lǔ hóu*: The *Wén* text states this refers to Duke Shuāi of Lǔ.

Chéng states: the former kings—King Jì, the forebear of King Wén, and Bó Qín of Zhōu.

*Wú xū yú lí*, pronounced *lí lì zhì*. In the *Jū* text, the sentence ends here: *lí* means "to disperse."

*Jū* means "to dwell in peace."

*Fēng hú*: Sīmǎ states *fēng* means "great."

*Xū shū*: *Xū* means "to assist"; *shū* means "sparse."

That is, to assist from afar in a scattered manner.

*Jī pì*, pronounced *bì yì*. Chéng states *jī pì* refers to hunting nets.

*Kū xíng*, pronounced *kū*: Chéng states this means "to forget the body."

*Qù pí*, pronounced *qǐ lǚ*. The *Wén* text states this means "to abandon Lǔ." Below, *qù* is the same as "to leave the lord."

*Xǐ xīn*, pronounced *xiān diǎn*.

*Chāng kuáng*: Chéng states this means "without heart."

*Wàng xíng*: Chéng states this means "to wander without bounds."

*Kě lè*, pronounced *luò*.

*Wú xíng jù*, pronounced *jù*: Sīmǎ states this means "without arrogance in one’s form."

*Wú liú jū*: Sīmǎ states this means "without clinging to one’s dwelling."

*Dà mò*: *Mò* means "none."

*Xū jǐ miǎn hài*: To empty oneself and avoid harm.

"Fāng zhōu": *Fāng*, as in the *Shuōwén*, means "joined boats," resembling two vessels lashed together.

*Biǎn xīn*, pronounced *bì shàn*: The *Ěryǎ* states this means "urgent."

*Zé*, pronounced *hū huǒ*; the same below.

*Háo*: To cry out.

*Zhāng xī*, pronounced *xǔ jí*: *Zhāng* means "to open," *xī* means "to gather."

*Yīn xún chéng huà*: To follow and become transformed.

*Běi gōng Shē*: Lǐ states this refers to a grandee of Wèi who resided in the Northern Palace, hence the epithet. *Shē* was his given name.

*Wéi*, pronounced *yú wěi*: To levy.

*Fù liǎn*, pronounced *lì yàn*: *Zhōng* is the name of a musical instrument. Some say it refers to casting twelve bells. *Wéi tán* means that before casting bells, one must first offer sacrifices, hence the construction of an altar.

*Shàng xià zhī xiàn*: Sīmǎ states that when the eight sounds are complete, it is called *xiàn*, though the tones differ in high and low. Some say it refers to an upper and lower tiered frame.
