*Wáng zǐ Qìngjì*: Lǐ states this was a member of the royal clan, *Qìngjì* being his given name. He was a grandee of Zhōu. Finding his simplicity and swiftness strange, he questioned it.

*Tóng hū*, pronounced *tǔ dōng*: An appearance of ignorance.

*Tǎng hū*, pronounced *chì dàng*: Unrestrained.

*Cuì hū*, pronounced *zài pàn*: To assemble.

*Máng hū*, pronounced *mò láng*: *Qiáng liáng*: Chéng states this refers to those who are unyielding and difficult to govern, whom one follows and employs.

*Qū fù*, pronounced *fù*: Sīmǎ states this means those who bend and attach themselves to one, whom one follows and complies with.

*Bù cuò*, pronounced *zǐ wò*: Chéng states *cuò* means "to diminish."

*Dà tú*: Chéng states this means "the great Way."

*Wú néng yuǎn huò*: Unable to distance oneself from misfortune.

*Wéi yú Chén Cài*: Already explained in *Huī Yù Piān*.

*Tài gōng Rèn*: Lǐ states *Tài gōng* is an honorific meaning "great elder," *Rèn* being his given name.

*Zǐ jī*, pronounced *jī*.

*Zǐ wù*, pronounced *wū lù*.

Below, all are the same.

*Fēnfēn*, pronounced *fēn*: *Zhìzhì*, pronounced *zhì*. Sīmǎ states *fēnfēn zhìzhì* describes a leisurely, unhurried appearance. Another interpretation: the manner of flight that does not rise high.

Forced to roost under duress: Lǐ states this means not daring to roost alone. Pressed into the flock, with only enough space to perch and rest—this is the utmost in avoiding harm.

*Qí xù*: In the *Shuōwén*, this means the tip of a silk thread. Here, it refers to the scattered remnants.

*Xíng liè*, pronounced *hù gāng*: Below, "disorderly conduct" is the same.

*Bù chì*, pronounced *chǐ*: Chéng states that the flock of birds does not single out and reject any one.

*Rén cù*, pronounced *zǐ xù*: The end.

*Míng wū*, pronounced *wū*: *Jiē qí*, pronounced *liè*.

The *dà chéng* person: Chéng states this refers to one whose virtue and power are vast and expansive, who gives life to and completes the ten thousand things—such a one is called *dà chéng*. The Old Master is such a one.

*Self-aggrandizement*: Chéng states *fá* means "to take."

*Zhě duò*, pronounced *xǔ guī*: Chéng states this means "to fail."

*Qù gōng*, pronounced *qǐ lǚ*: *Dé xíng*, read as written. The text states that when the world all attains the Way that the perfected person practices, the perfected person does not dwell in the fame.

*Yī*, pronounced *jì*: *Qiú hè*, pronounced *hù gē*: *Zhù shí*, pronounced *rǔ*, also read *xù*.

Heavenly bonds draw them together.

*Zǐ Sāng Hù*, pronounced *hù*; originally written as *Níng*, pronounced *yú*. Lǐ states this refers to one surnamed Sāng, named Níng, a recluse.

Twice driven from Lǔ, he felled trees in Sòng, erased his traces in Wèi, was impoverished in Shāng and Zhōu, and besieged in Chén and Cài—all explained above in *Huī Yù Piān*.

*He yǔ*, pronounced *yú*: Below, follow this pattern.

*Jiǎ*, pronounced *gǔ yǎ*: Lǐ states this is the name of a state; Chéng states it is a town in Jìn, which was once destroyed.

*Lín Huí*: Sīmǎ states this is the name of a refugee from Shāng.

Chéng states this is a worthy person.

*Bù* refers to wealth and property, spread among the people and thus named.

*Wú yì*, pronounced *jí*: Lǐ states this means having nothing to grasp or hold.

*Zhēn* is sometimes written as *zhí*; *líng*, pronounced *líng*.

Yǔ: Sīmǎ states *líng* means "to enlighten." This refers to awakening Yǔ with the true Way.

*Líng* is sometimes written as *mìng* or *lìng*, meaning "command" or "teaching."

*Dé yǐn*: *Yī*, pronounced *jì*.

Great cloth: Sīmǎ states this refers to coarse cloth.

*Zhèng mí*: pronounced *xián jié*. Sīmǎ states this means a belt.

*Tying shoes*: Lǐ states this refers to shoes being worn out and thus tied.

*Ér guò*: pronounced *gǔ hé*.

*King of Wèi*: Sīmǎ states this is King Huì.

*Bèi*: pronounced *pí bài*. Sīmǎ states this means illness.

*Nán*: pronounced *nán*.

*Zǐ* and *yù zhāng* are both fine, straight, and excellent woods.

*Lǎn*: originally written as *ná*, pronounced *lì gǎn*.

*Màn*: pronounced *wàn*. Chéng states *lǎn màn* means "to grasp" or "to seize."

*Wáng*: pronounced *wǎng kuàng*.

*Cháng*: pronounced *dīng liàng*. Chéng states this describes a self-contented appearance.

*Sī zhèng*: pronounced *zhí liáng*. It states that two branches are far apart.

*Yì*: pronounced *yì*.

*Péng méng*: pronounced *fú gōng*, also pronounced *péng*. Sīmǎ states Yì was an ancient master archer, and Péng Méng was his disciple.

*Pì*: pronounced *pǔ jì*.

*Nì*: pronounced *yì*. Lǐ states *pì nì* means "to gaze sorrowfully."

*Kū jí*: pronounced *zhāng yè*.

*Zhǐ*: pronounced *zhǐ*.

*Gǒu*: pronounced *jǔ*—both are thorny woods.

*Bù biàn*: pronounced *bì miàn*.

*Luàn xiāng*: pronounced *xī liàng*.

*Bǐ gān*: pronounced *xián biàn*.

*Is this not proof?*—a rhetorical question affirming verification.

*Great attainment*: On the left, leaning against withered wood, pronounced *kǔ lǎo*. It refers to a zither.

On the right, striking a withered branch—this refers to playing the zither.

*Zhòng shì*: pronounced *bì yáo*. In ancient times, *yuán* referred to emperors and kings, and also to Shénnóng.

*Lí rán*: pronounced *lì xī*. Sīmǎ states *lí rán* means "trembling" or "pure and serene in appearance."

*Yǒu dàng*: pronounced *dīng làng*.

*Huán mù*: pronounced *xuán*—to look back.

*Ér kuī*: pronounced *qǐ guī*.

*Zào*: Sīmǎ states this means "to go to" or "to reach." Another interpretation states it means "to create" or "to make."

*Sun*: pronounced *yì*—same below.

*Qióng zhì*: pronounced *zhī shí*.

*Yùn huà*: seen in the Jiāngnán ancient manuscript; originally written as *yùn wù*.

*Zhī xiè*: pronounced *xī lì*. Sīmǎ states this means "to release" or "to emit."

*Yán yǔ zhī yán* means "to speak of" or "to refer to."

*Yì ér*: pronounced *yì ér*. Chéng states this refers to swallows.

*Where the eye should not dwell*: pronounced *chāng lǚ*. It means a place where one should not stop, for the eye has already perceived it and thus abandoned it.

*Bù jǐ*: In the *Shuōwén*, *jǐ* means "to suffice mutually." It refers to places where one should not dwell, which are insufficient to hold the gaze.

*Luò qí shí*: *shí* here means "eggs."

*Ér xí*: pronounced *xí*. Chéng states this means "to enter" or "to penetrate."

*Shè jì*: Zhàng Wén states this means "nest."

*Qí shàn*: pronounced *shì zhàn*. Sīmǎ states this means "to grant" or "to bestow." Chéng states it means "to succeed" or "to replace."

*Yān zhī*: pronounced *yú qián*—same below.

*Losing illumination.*

"Diāo" is pronounced *diāo*.

"Líng zhī Fán" is pronounced *líng zhī fán*. Sīmǎ states: *Diāo Líng* is the name of a mound; *fán* means "fence." It refers to wandering within the fence of a chestnut grove. *Fán* may also be written as *yě* ("wild"), an ancient form of the character *yě*.

"Yùn cùn": Sīmǎ states this means "approximately four inches."

"Gǎn zhōu zhī sǎng" is pronounced *gǎn zhōu zhī sǎng*. It means "of the same kind." *Gǎn* means "to touch."

"Yì yīn bù shì, mù dà bù dǔ": *Yīn* means "great." *Shì* means "to move in curves." Since wings are large and movement is difficult, and eyes are large but vision is sparse, one does not see others.

"Qiān" is pronounced *qián*. Formerly written as *jiǎn*.

"Jué" is pronounced *qū bì* or *jiǔ fù*. Sīmǎ states this means "to move swiftly." The *Analects* says: "the feet move *jué*."

"Zhí dàn" is pronounced *zhí dàn*.

"Liú" is pronounced *liú*. Sīmǎ states this means "to stay, to remain in its convenience."

"Tángláng" is pronounced *táng láng*.

"Zhí yì" is pronounced *zhí yì*. *Yì* means "axe." The mantis holds the axe like a shield to conceal itself.

"Bó" is pronounced *bó*; it means "to seize."

"Wàng qí xíng": In the *Shuōwén*, *xíng* means "form, image."

"Wàng qí shēn": In the *Shuōwén*, *shēn* means "the body, the self," resembling the form of a person. The *Ěryǎ* says: "I."

"Wàng qí zhēn": *Zhēn* means "nature."

"Fǎn rù gōng": According to the *Jiāngnán Gǔ Zàng* edition, the old text was missing this. The *Ěryǎ* states: "gōng is called a chamber; a chamber is called gōng."

"Lìn" is pronounced *lì xìn*.

"Qiě" is pronounced *zǐ yú*.

Sīmǎ states that *Lìn* and *Qiě* were disciples of Zhuāngzǐ.

"Follow their customs": The *Jiāngnán Gǔ Zàng* edition writes *cóng qí jīn* ("follow the present").

"Chestnut grove": The *Yúrén Wén Rú Hǎi* edition omits the characters *lì lín* ("chestnut grove").

"To be shamed": *Lù* means "disgrace, humiliation."

"Self-regard": Yángzǐ—Sīmǎ states this refers to Yáng Zhū.

"An inn with a concubine": The *Liú Déyī* edition originally wrote *nì lǚ* ("inn").

"To depart": *Qǔ* is pronounced *lǚ qiě*.

"His conduct": *Xíng* is pronounced *xià mèng*.

*The Southern Florescence True Scripture: Chapter Sentences and Phonetic Glosses*, Scroll Nine, concludes.
*The Southern Florescence True Scripture: Chapter Sentences and Phonetic Glosses*, Scroll Ten.
By Bìxūzǐ.

**Miscellaneous Chapters: Tiánzǐfāng (Twenty-Five)**
*True Awakening*
Tiánzǐfāng—Lǐ states this was the teacher of Wèi Wénhóu, named Wúzé.

"Frequently praised": *Shù chēng* is pronounced *shuò* (here and below).

Xīgōng—Lǐ states this was a worthy man of the stream.

Dōngguō Shùnzǐ—Chéng states he dwelled east of the outer wall, hence the surname; his name was Shùnzǐ, a teacher of Yú Fāng.

"Preserving authenticity": *Bǎo zhēn* is pronounced *bǎo*.

"Unconstrained": *Tǎngrán* is pronounced *chì dàng*.

Sīmǎ states this describes a dejected appearance. *Yǔ* is pronounced *jù*.

"His conduct": *Xíng* is pronounced *xià mèng*.

"Form dissolved": *Xíng jiě* is pronounced *hù zhēn*.

"Mouth restrained": *Kǒu qián* is pronounced *qí měi*.

"Clod of earth": *Tǔ gěng* is pronounced *gēng měng*. *Tǔ* means "straw." Sīmǎ states this refers to a man of earth, who encounters both soil and dust.

*Profound Union*
Wēn Bó Xuězǐ—Lǐ states he was a worthy man of the southern lands. Chéng states he was a worthy man of Chǔ.

"Man of Lǔ": Chéng states this refers to a disciple of Confucius.

"The Central States": Chéng states this means the state of Lǔ.

"Crude": Chéng states this means "clumsy."

"To seek": *Qí* is pronounced *qí*.

"To rouse": Chéng states this means "to move."

"Composure": *Cóngróng* is pronounced *qī róng*.

The text states: "Regulations and propriety" refer to ritual and righteousness; "dragons and tigers" refer to majesty and awe.

"Its Way": The *Jiāngnán Gǔ Zàng* edition writes *dǎo* ("guide").

"*Fū rén*": Pronounced *fú rén*.

"Eyes meet and the Dao is present"—Sīmǎ states this means that when the eyes move, the spirit is already manifest. *Jī* means "to move." He also states it means the moment the eyes glance, intent is already conveyed.

*Mì yí*
*Chēng* is pronounced *chì gēng*. The *Zìlín* states this describes a fixed gaze.

*Bù bǐ*—*Bǐ* is pronounced *pǐ zhì*. This means "intimate closeness."

*Tāo hū qián*—*Tāo* is pronounced *tǔ dāo*. This refers to one lacking the capacity of a ruler, with all things amassing before them.

*Wū kě*—*Wū* is pronounced *wū*. *Chá* shares the pronunciation *yú* with *āi yú* below.

"Those with eyes and feet"—the text states this refers to beings with consciousness.

*Xūn rán*—*Xūn* is pronounced *xū yún*, describing the appearance of qi gently steaming and rising.

"Perhaps you perceive what I perceive"—*Zhuó* is pronounced *zhāng lǜ*, meaning "to see." Sīmǎ states: "What I perceive is external transformation; you likely approach this. What I alone do not transform is beyond your reach."

"This is seeking a horse in a Tang market"—Guō states that a *Táng sì* is not a place where horses linger. *Mò* also means "to halt." Sīmǎ’s edition writes *guǎng sì*, stating it means a vast courtyard, and that seeking a horse in the marketplace or a vast courtyard is not where one would find it.

"Virtue and power arise naturally"
"With hair unbound"—*pī fà* is pronounced *pí jì*.

"While *qián* originally may be written as *gān*.

*Zhírán*—pronounced *nǎi dié*—Sīmǎ states: 'the appearance of immobility.'

*Shǎo yān*—pronounced *shī zhào*.

*Jiàn rì*—pronounced *xián biàn*.

*Xuàn*—pronounced *xuán*, same as below.

*Jué*—pronounced *xū wù*.

*Gǎo mù*—pronounced *kǔ lǎo*.

*Lí rén*—pronounced *lì zhì*.

*Kǒu pì*—pronounced *bì yì*—Sīmǎ states: 'the mouth curls and does not open.' Alternatively pronounced *bì yì*. Chéng states: 'the mouth opens but does not close.'

*Cháng wéi*—pronounced *yú wěi*.

*Zhì lè*—pronounced *luò*, same as below.

*Xíng xiǎo*—pronounced *xià mèng*, or read as written.

*Xiōng cì*—Lǐ states: '*cì* means "within."'

*Néng gǔ*—pronounced *gǔ mò*.

*Dé sàng*—pronounced *xī làng*.

*Suǒ jiè*—pronounced *jiè*.

*Jiě hū*—pronounced *xiè*, or *hù shí*.

*Yú zhuó*—pronounced *zhuó*—Lǐ: 'to skim.'

*Yì*—to take.

*Xiē jī*—pronounced *xǔ xī*—Guō states: 'the midges in a jar.'

*Jiàn yán*—Lǔ Āi Gōng—Sīmǎ states: 'Zhuāngzǐ was a contemporary of King Huì of Wèi and King Wēi of Qí, one hundred and twenty years after Duke Āi.' Chéng states: 'This is a parable.'

*Guān*—pronounced *gǔ luàn.*"

"Yuán guān"—pronounced *yuán*.

*Lǚ jù*—pronounced *jǔ*; Lǐ states: 'square.'

*Jù*—pronounced *jū*.

*Huǎn*—pronounced *guǎn*. Sīmǎ’s version reads *shòu*.

*Pèi jué*—pronounced *jué*. Luán states: 'a five-colored corded rope threading jade *jué* to adorn the girdle.' Alternatively, it is also written as *huǎn*: 'dragging the corded *jué* to the matter at hand, thereby deciding it.' Some say *fū* resembles a ring with a gap, and the *jué* bestowed upon exiled ministers signifies the finality of parting. *Duàn*—pronounced *duàn*.

*Bù hào*—*hào* means 'to close.'

*Inner attainment, outer abundance*—Bǎi Lǐ Xī’s rank and emolument did not enter his heart, thus he fed cattle and the cattle grew fat. The *Records of the Grand Historian* state: 'Duke Xiàn of Jìn destroyed Yú and captured its lord along with his grandee Bǎi Lǐ Xī, who was sent to Qín as a dowry attendant for Duke Mù’s consort. Bǎi Lǐ Xī fled Qín and went to Wǎn, where a border guard of Chǔ seized him. Hearing of Bǎi Lǐ Xī’s worthiness, Duke Mù wished to ransom him heavily, but feared the Chǔ people would not consent. He then dispatched an envoy to Chǔ, saying: "My dowry attendant Bǎi Lǐ Xī is there; I request to ransom him with five black ram skins." The Chǔ people thus permitted it. At that time, Bǎi Lǐ Xī was already over seventy. Duke Mù released him from captivity and consulted him on state affairs. Bǎi Lǐ Xī declined, saying: "I am a minister of a ruined state; how am I worthy of such inquiry?" Duke Mù replied: "The lord of Yú did not employ you, hence his downfall—this was not your fault." After three days of persistent questioning, Duke Mù was greatly pleased and entrusted him with governance, styling him the Five Ram Skins Grandee. Bǎi Lǐ Xī demurred, saying: "I am not the equal of my friend Jiǎn Shū. Truly, though the world does not know him, I once wandered destitute in Qí and begged for food from a chariot driver. Jiǎn Shū took me in, and I then wished to serve Lord Wú Zhī of Qí. Jiǎn Shū dissuaded me, and I thus escaped the calamity of Qí. I then went to Zhōu, where Prince Tuí of Zhōu favored cattle. I offered my skills in cattle-rearing to gain his favor. When Prince Tuí wished to employ me, Jiǎn Shū again dissuaded me, and I departed, thereby avoiding execution. Later, when serving the lord of Yú, Jiǎn Shū once more dissuaded me. I knew the lord of Yú would not heed me, yet I remained for private gain of rank and emolument. Twice I followed his counsel and escaped disaster. Thus I know his worth." Duke Mù then sent envoys with generous gifts to invite Jiǎn Shū, appointing him as senior grandee.

*Zhì*—pronounced *zhì*, a place name; also read as *yì*, or written as *zhì* in common texts.

*Forgetting his lowly station and entrusting him with governance*—referring to forgetting his former lowly work of feeding cattle.

The Yǒu Yú clan—Shùn served his parents with utmost filial piety; matters of life and death did not enter his heart. His fame spread throughout the world, moving even rulers.

*Mastery of skill, forgetting the form*—receiving the bow and standing. Sīmǎ states: 'receiving the command, he bowed and stood.'

*Shì*—pronounced *zhǐ*.

*Chán chán*—pronounced *tǎn*; Lǐ states: 'a relaxed and unhurried appearance.'

*Pán bó*—pronounced *páng*; Sīmǎ states: 'sitting with legs splayed.'

*Léi*—pronounced *luǒ*; Sīmǎ states: 'about to paint, hence he bared his form by removing his robe.'

*Consulting the multitude, employing the worthy*—King Wén observed Zāng. Chéng states: 'Zāng is a place near the Wèi River.' The old man is a parable for the Grand Duke. Sīmǎ’s version reads: 'King Wén, in plain clothes, observed Zāng.'

*Shǔ*—pronounced *zhú*.

*Bān*—pronounced *zhān*. Chéng states: 'King Wén’s father, Jì, was dark-skinned and had many *bān*.'

*Bó mǎ*—pronounced *bó*; *jiǎo*—pronounced *jué*.

*Pian zhū tí*—Lǐ states: 'one hoof was partially red.'

*Liào hū*—pronounced *liú*; Chéng states: 'imperfect.'

*Cù rán*—pronounced *zǐ liù*.

*Former lord and king*—Sīmǎ states: 'the spiritual efficacy of the former lord and king.'

*May the king have no other intent*—Sīmǎ states: 'no disobedience.'

*Liè shì*—pronounced *guài*; same below.

"Zhí"—pronounced *zhí*. Sīmǎ states: '*Zhí* means ranks or rows.'

*Scattering the multitude*—this means not keeping a retinue of followers. Another version reads: '*Zhí*' refers to erecting a strong boundary and constructing a hall to await remonstrators.

*Cháng*—pronounced *dīng dà*; same below.

*Officials who fail to perfect virtue*—Sīmǎ states: 'unfavorable to merit and fame.'

*Yǔ hú*—pronounced *yǔ*; Lǐ states: 'six *hú* and four *dǒu* make one *yǔ*; ten *dǒu* make one *hú*.'

*Mèi rán*—pronounced *mèi*.

*Fàn rán*—pronounced *shù jiàn*.

*Fled by night*—pronounced *tú kùn*. Having accomplished merit and fulfilled affairs, one conceals brilliance and withdraws, hence "fled by night."

*Cì yān*—pronounced *qī cì*.

*Intent yet unrefined*

*Yù kòu*—pronounced *yú wěi*.

*Yíng guàn*—pronounced *gǔ luàn*. Sīmǎ states: 'an arrowhead.' Wén states: '*Yíng guàn* means beyond the arrowhead.'

*Shì shǐ*—pronounced *dīng lì*; means 'to strike.'

*Fù tà*—pronounced *fú yòu*; same below. Chéng states: '*Tà* means repetition.'

*Xiàng rén*—Chéng states: 'wooden puppets' or 'clay figurines.'

*Biān xún*—pronounced *qī xún*.

*Chéng gōng wàn què xíng*—Chéng states: 'to retreat ten thousand steps.'

*Hàn liú*—pronounced *hù dàn*.

*Huī chì*—pronounced *chǐ*; Guō states: '*Huī chì* means to let loose or indulge.'

*Chù rán*—pronounced *chì lǜ*.

*Yǒu xún*—pronounced *xún*; *Ěr yǎ* states: '*Xún lì* means trembling.' *Mù zhī zhì xún* means 'to dazzle'; desiring to dazzle others' eyes, hence 'trembling.'

*Yū zhōng*—pronounced *dīng zhòng*.

*The Dao fills yet remains unmoved."

Yan Wu*—Chéng states: 'a recluse.'*

Sun Shu'ao*—a worthy minister of Chu.*

*Xǔ xǔ rán*—pronounced *kuàng fǔ*; a self-contented appearance, also meaning joyous and carefree.

*Chóu*—pronounced *zhí liú*.

*Chú*—pronounced *zhí yú*; Chéng states: 'leisurely and self-contented.'

*Four glances*—Chéng states: 'to gaze high in all eight directions.'

*Dé jié jū yè*—pronounced *chū*; to seize by force.

*Fú Xī*—pronounced *Xī*.

*Wú jiè*—pronounced *jiè*; *Shuōwén* states: 'to draw.' Another says: 'to hear.'

*Bù bèi*—pronounced *pí bài*; to cultivate the self and preserve life.

King of Chu*—Chéng states: 'King Wen of Chu sat with the Marquis of Fan, discussing alliances and covenants. Chu harbored ambitions to annex the Yi, so he had his attendants sway him with words.'*

Lord of Fan*—Sīmǎ states: 'Fan was a state in Gong County of Jì Prefecture. According to the *Zuǒzhuàn*, Fan was a descendant of the Duke of Zhou.'*

Miscellaneous Chapters, "Knowledge Wanders North" (Chapter 26)

*Chōng mò*—*Zhī* read as written; formerly pronounced *zhì*, now not adopted.

*Wandering north upon the Dark Waters*—Sīmǎ’s version reads: 'north of the Dark Waters.'

*Yǐn fèn*—pronounced *fú yún*; Chéng states: 'Hidden, it is profound and hard to know; manifest, it is dense and visible.'

*South of the White Waters*—Chéng states: 'White is the color of purity; south is the direction of brightness.'

*Hú què*—pronounced *kǔ xuè*; Lǐ states: 'Hú is a hill name. *Hú* means doubt. *Què* means rest.'

*Yet beholds*—pronounced *dīng gǔ*.

"Kuáng" means delusion; "qū" means crookedness. Sīmǎ states: "zhī" here is the particle "zhī." *Āi*—pronounced *āi zài*; Lǐ states: it is a responsive sound. *Yǔ*—pronounced *yú jù*. *Qí*—pronounced *yì chǐ*. *Suǒ wù*—pronounced *wū lù*. "It unites the qi of Heaven and Earth as one"—seen in Liú Dé’s edition; formerly: "It unites the qi of all under Heaven as one." "Great beauty" refers to the virtue of encompassing and sustaining all things. "Unite with that"—seen in Liú Dé’s edition; formerly: "unite with this." *Piān rán*—pronounced *piān*; Chéng states: the appearance of pervading life. The original text reads *piān rán*, meaning the appearance of daily renewal. *Wèi lí*—pronounced *lì zhì*. "Within itself" means it cannot emerge through self-transformation. *Hūn rán*—pronounced *hūn*, meaning obscurity. Zhāng Jūnfáng’s edition reads *hūn*. *Yóu rán*—pronounced *yóu*, meaning nothing is withheld or begrudged. Chéng states: the appearance of being unbound. *Shén jiě*—*Bèi yī* read as written, also pronounced *pī*. *Tóng yān*—pronounced *chì hóng*, the appearance of not blinking. "Like a newly born calf"—Lǐ states: the appearance of innocence. *Ruò gǎo*—pronounced *kǔ lǎo*. *Méi méi*—pronounced *mèi*. *Huì huì*—pronounced *huì*. *Méi méi huì huì*—the appearance of being without intent; also, the appearance of obscurity and darkness. "The constant Dao."

The Four Assistants of antiquity—Left Auxiliary, Right Minister, Front Advisor, and Rear Chancellor—were official titles. Some write it as *zhēng*. Lǐ Gōngwàn was Shùn’s teacher. *Fū* is pronounced *fú*. *Wěi xíng* in the *Shuōwén* means "to yield." "Descendants" follows Zhāng Jūnfáng’s edition; the old text reads "grandson and son." *Wēi yí* is pronounced *tǔ wò*. The strong yang *qi* of Heaven and Earth—Guō states: "strong yang" means nothing more than movement. The implication is that if even Heaven and Earth are in motion, how can the living, formed by the gathering of *qi*, be grasped and detained?

*The Dao That Can Be Spoken Of*
*Yàn* is pronounced *yú jiàn*. *Shū lún* is pronounced *yào*, meaning "to soak." Chéng states: to wash and rinse. *Zǎo xuě*—Chéng states: to refine and purify. The text reads: to still the spirit. *Pǒu jī* is pronounced *pǔ kǒu*. Chéng states: to shatter. The text of Gōngwàn reads: to discard wisdom. *Yǎo rán* is pronounced *wū liǎo*. *Jiāng wéi* is pronounced *yú wěi*. "Formless" refers to the Great Beginning. The spirit is born of the Dao, meaning the constant Dao. *Jiǔ qiào* is pronounced *kǔ diào*. *Huáng huáng*—the text reads: the appearance of boundlessness. *Yāo* is pronounced *yú yáo*. Chéng states: to encounter. "This, this"—the subtle and wondrous Dao. *Xún dá* is pronounced *xū lún*; the *Shuōwén* defines it as "sincere heart." Another says: "balanced." "Heaven cannot but be high"—meaning Heaven cannot be high without this Dao. *Dào yǔ* is pronounced *yú*, the same below. "To be learned is not necessarily to know; to be eloquent is not necessarily wise"—Chéng states: one may be broadly versed in the classics yet fail to grasp the truth; one may be grandly eloquent yet lack wisdom’s illumination. *Yǐ* is pronounced *duān*. Chéng states: to discard.

Majestic—pronounced *wēi*. Chéng states: a term for lofty and vast. It sustains and measures the myriad things without omission—*yí* is pronounced *jiā*, meaning "to perish." Alternately, "to remain." From Liu’s *Déyī* edition, formerly written as *kuì*. The myriad things all turn to it for sustenance yet it does not deplete—*kuì* is pronounced *wèi*.

The Central Ultimate—*àn* is pronounced *àn*, identical to *yīn*. *Yì*—pronounced *yǐn jì*. Guō states: *ànyì*, the appearance of qi gathering. *Jǐhé*—pronounced *jū qǐ*. *Guǒluǒ*—pronounced *lì guǒ*. The text reads: the fruit of trees is called *guǒ*; the fruit of grasses is called *luǒ*. The White Colt—the text reads: "light." Chéng states: a fine steed. To pass—*què* is pronounced *qù jué*. Chéng states: *què* means "to nurse." *Zhùrán bórán*—pronounced *bù hū*. Chéng states: *zhùbó*, the aspect of arising. *Yóurán liáorán*—pronounced *liú*. Chéng states: *yóu* means "to seep"; the state of entering death.

Heaven’s concealment—*tāo* is pronounced *chì dāo*. Chéng states: *tāo* means "to wrap and store." To collapse—pronounced *xǔ guī*. Heaven’s wrapping—*zhì* is pronounced *chén bǐ*. Chéng states: "to bind," meaning to bundle together. *Fēnhū wǎnhū*—pronounced *wū yuàn*. Chéng states: *fēn lún wǎn zhuǎn*, both describing the appearance of dispersal.

The Dao is nowhere absent. Dōngguōzi—Lǐ states: he dwelled in the eastern outskirts, teacher to Tián Zǐfāng. *Wūhū*—pronounced *wū*. *Lóu*—pronounced *lì hóu*.

"Ants—*qǐ* is pronounced *qǐ*. *Tí*—*dà* is pronounced *dà xī*. *Bài*—*pú* is pronounced *pú mài*. A name for grass. Tiles—*pì* is pronounced *bù lì*. It is said: *língzhuān*, baked bricks. Excrement—*shǐ* is pronounced *shǐ zhǐ*. Urine—*nì* is pronounced *nǎi diào*.

Between the *zhèng* and the *huò*—*yú* is pronounced *yú jiān*. *Shì lǚ xī*—*xī* is pronounced *xū qǐ*. Lǐ states: *zhèng* refers to the market constable, today’s market magistrate. *Huò* is his name. *Jiānshì* is the market chief. *Xī* is a large pig. *Lǚ* means "to tread." When the market chief treads upon the fat beneath the pig’s thighs and hocks where the hooves hang, he knows the upper parts are fat. Inquiring of the Dao is likewise to observe the lowly, and thus one knows the Dao’s pervasiveness.

One says: *kuàng* means "increase." Each time he treads upon the difficult-to-fatten parts beneath the hanging hooves and sees their fatness, he knows the abundance of the upper portions. Another says: *kuàng* means "to infer by analogy." You must not insist there is nowhere for things to escape.

No single boundary—
*Zhōu* means "all-encompassing," for nothing is left unembraced;
*biàn* means "all-reaching," for nothing is left untouched;
*xián* means "all-inclusive," for nothing is left unprepared.

*Dàn*—pronounced *tú zhàn*. *Liáo*—pronounced *liáo*. *Pánghuáng*—pronounced *páng huáng*. Chéng states: the appearance of unrestrained wandering. *Píng*—pronounced *pí bīng*. *Hóng*—pronounced *hóng*. Lǐ states: *píng* and *hóng* both mean "great." Guō states: it refers to vast emptiness. *Shuāishài*—pronounced *sè jiè*. Same below.

Attaining the Dao is like an autumn hair—*háo* is pronounced *yú hé*. *Hégān*—pronounced *hé gān*. Chéng states: *háo* is the surname, *Hégān* the given name. Old Dragon Jí—Lǐ states: a man who embraced the Dao. Shénnóng—Chéng states: Shénnóng was not an emperor. Leaning on a table—*zhǎn* is pronounced *zhǎn*. Closing the door—*hé* is pronounced *hù là*. Sleeping by day—*míng* is pronounced *mián*. *Zhā*—pronounced *chǔ yě*. Also pronounced *shē*. Sīmǎ states: it means "to open wide." *Bó rán*—pronounced *bò*. Lǐ states: the sound of casting aside a staff. *Pìlòu*—pronounced *zú yì*. Chéng states: remote and rustic. *Màn*—pronounced *wǔ bàn*. Also pronounced as written. *Yí*—pronounced *tú dàn*. Chéng states: *gōng wàn*, not single-minded or attentive. *Yǐ yǐ fū*—pronounced *yǐ fú*. *Yǎn*—pronounced *yǎn*. *Gāng*—pronounced *gāng*. *Diào*—Lǐ states: his surname was *Yǎnlán*, his given name *Diào*, a man who embodied the Dao.

To be bound—it means to be sought out by things. Abyss upon abyss—*qióng* is written as *qióng* in Cuī’s edition. In Zhāng Jūnfáng’s edition, it is *yǎng*. The *Jíyùn* states *yǐn* and *yǎng* share the same pronunciation. The *Shuōwén* says it means "to raise." The *Book of Songs* reads: "Gazing up to the vast heavens." The old text had *zhōng*, likely a scribal error.

Light and shadow utterly vanish—*yǎo rán* is pronounced *wū liǎo*. *Bó zhī*—pronounced *bó*. Guarding the One—Dàmǎ states: the Grand Marshal of Chǔ. *Chuí*—*gōu* is pronounced *léi*. In the region between the three Wèi rivers of Jiāngdōng, people call forging *chuí*. *Gōu* means "belt." *Qiǎo*—pronounced *yú*. Same below. And *hào*—pronounced *hū bào*. *Yǐ zhǎng*—pronounced *dīng zhàng*. Not preceding things—*xiàn* is pronounced *biàn*. Also *wéi* is pronounced *yú wěi*.

To have no descendants yet to have grandsons—this speaks of necessity having a cause; one cannot have a lineage transmitted without reason. Thus, to have descendants, one cannot have a grandson without first having a son. Likewise, heaven and earth cannot now exist without having first existed. *Xiān*—pronounced *xīn jiàn*. Same below.

To harmonize the realm—*ān* is pronounced *ān*; it means "to dwell." *Zhī yòu*—pronounced *yòu*. *Zhī pǔ*—pronounced *bù wǔ*. *Xiāng*—pronounced *chì shí jiǔ zǐ xī*. It means "harmony." To turn from the vulgar—*shān lín* is pronounced *yú*, same below. *Gāo rǎng*—*Yǔ* states: divine marshes and hidden soils. *"With me no kinship"*—seen in the ancient southern repository; previously omitted. Yet delight—pronounced *luò*, same below. To govern—pronounced *yú lǚ*. To equal—*zhī* is pronounced *cái xì*. Also read as written. *Nánhua Zhēnjīng Zhāngjù Yīnyì*, Scroll Ten ends. *Nánhua Zhēnjīng Zhāngjù Yīnyì*, Scroll Eleven. By Bìxūzǐ.

**Miscellaneous Chapters: Gēngsāng Chǔ (Twenty-Seven)**
**Abandoning Cunning**
Lǎodān’s disciple—*Sīmǎ* states: *yì* means "service," a student or follower. Gēngsāng Chǔ—*Sīmǎ* states: a name from Chǔ; Gēngsāng is the surname. The *Records of the Grand Historian* writes it as *Kàngcāng*. *Piān dé*—pronounced *piān*. *Chéng* states: among his disciples, the most excellent, hence called *piān dé*. To fear—pronounced *wū zuì*. Originally written as **.

"Lěi"—pronounced *lì zuì*.

Hé Càn’s *Annotations on the Kāngcāngzǐ* states: Mount Wěilěi is Feather Mountain, located in Xúzhōu.

"Huàrán"—pronounced *huò*.

"Qièrán"—pronounced *kǔ jì*. "Huàrán" refers to those who scheme and embellish with cunning. "Qièrán" refers to those who bind themselves to benevolence through agreements. Both arise from deceit and falsehood, losing their natural state.

Also pronounced *kǔ jié*—to lift, also denoting benevolence and love.

"Yuǎn"—pronounced *yú wàn*.

Sīmǎ states: People treat benevolence and wisdom as servants and concubines; Gēngsāng Chǔ wholly discards benevolence and wisdom.

"Yōng"—pronounced *yǒng*.

"Zhǒng"—pronounced *zhāng yǒng*.

Guō states: *Yōngzhǒng* means uncarved simplicity.

"Yāngzhǎng"—pronounced *yú wén*.

Some say *yōngzhǒng* denotes worthlessness. Guō states: *Yāngzhǎng* means self-discontent. Xiàng states: These two lines refer to the burdens of simplicity.

Sīmǎ states: Both describe ugly appearances.

"Three years"—Chéng states: Three years make one intercalary month; this marks the minor attainment of the Way.

"Dàrǎng"—pronounced *ér zhǎng*.

The *Guǎngyǎ* states: *Rǎng* means abundance.

"Sǎrán"—pronounced *sù zhēn*.

Lǐ states: A startled appearance.

"Counting daily, one finds insufficiency"—Xiàng states: No small gains from dawn to dusk.

"Counting yearly, one finds surplus"—Xiàng states: Following heaven’s seasons brings great abundance.

"Just as autumn arrives and the myriad treasures ripen"—Heaven and earth regard the ten thousand things as treasures; by autumn, they come to fruition. The *Yuánjiā* edition reads "myriad fruits."

"Huán"—a circle. "Dǔ"—pronounced *dīng lǔ*.

Sīmǎ states: A wall of one *zhàng* is called a *dǔ*; "encircled by walls" (*huán dǔ*) means each side measures one *zhàng*, indicating narrowness and smallness.

"Whispering in secret" (*qièqiè píngzhāng*)—engaging in private speech.

*Zǔ dòu*—pronounced *zè lǚ*.

Chéng states: *Zǔ* is the stand for cutting meat; *dòu* is the vessel for holding dried meats—both are ritual implements. "The *zǔ dòu* feed me among the crowd."

*Biāo*—pronounced *dì*, or *pǐ yāo*.

Guō states: The standard measure for things. Wáng states: This is because one sets oneself as the standard for others.

Sīmǎ’s original text reads *yuē* (約), meaning "target" (晉的).

"A ditch of *xún* and *cháng*"—eight *chǐ* make one *xún*, twice a *xún* makes one *cháng*. "A ditch of *xún* and *cháng*" refers to the *Zhōu Lǐ*’s measurements for *xù* and *lún*: a *xù* is eight *chǐ* wide and deep, while a *lún* is two *xún* wide and two *rèn* deep.

"Where it returns" (*suǒ huán*)—pronounced *xuán*; meaning "to revolve."

*Ní*—pronounced *wǔ xī*.

*Qiū*—pronounced *qiū*.

"For them to bend" (*wèi zhī zhì*)—the *Guǎngyǎ* states: *Zhì* means "to break." This refers to small fish finding room to curve.

Wáng states: *Zhì* means "to monopolize"; *ní* and *qiū* dominate the small ditch.

"A mound of one *bù* and one *rèn*"—six *chǐ* make one *bù*, seven *chǐ* make one *rèn*; this refers to a width of one *bù* and a height of one *rèn*. Kǒng Ānguó states: Eight *chǐ* make one *rèn*. The *Ěryǎ* also states: Four *chǐ* make one *rèn*.

*Yǎn*—pronounced *jié*.

"The fox regards it as an omen" (*hú wèi zhī xiáng*)—Lǐ states: *Xiáng* means "strange." Foxes delight in portents and omens; each has its proper place, and when propriety is not lost, the great person enjoys abundant fortune.

Wáng states: Wild foxes rely on it to create auspicious signs. Cuī states: The fox considers a small mound auspicious. *Xiáng* means "good."

*Hán*—pronounced *hán*.

"The beast of the carriage"—Lǐ states: A beast large enough to carry a carriage.

"Alone" (*jiè ér*)—pronounced *jiè*; the *Guǎngyǎ* states: "solitary." One text reads *fēn* (分), meaning "to separate."

*Líshān*—pronounced *lì zhì*.

"A fish that could swallow a boat" (*tūn zhōu*)—pronounced *chì ēn*.

"Stranded after the waters recede" (*dàng ér shī shuǐ*)—pronounced *tú làng*. This means the waters overflow and then recede.

Cuī’s original text reads "leaving the water to dwell on land."

"Then the ants" (*zé yǐ*)—pronounced *qǐ*.

"Suffer" (*kǔ zhī*)—another text reads "exhausted" (*qióng zhī*).

"Deep and distant" (*shēn miǎo*)—pronounced *mí xiǎo*. Chéng states: *Miǎo* means "remote."

"The two masters" (*èr zǐ*)—Xiàng, Cuī, and Guō all state: These refer to Yáo and Shùn.

"Growing reeds and cattails" (*zhí péng pú*)—pronounced *kōng*.

"And combing them" (*ér zhì*)—pronounced *cè sè*, interchangeable with *zhǐ*. The *Shuōwén* states: The general term for combs and fine-toothed implements.

"Counting grains of rice" (*shǔ mǐ*)—pronounced *sè zhǔ*.

"And cooking them" (*ér chuī*)—pronounced *chāng chuí*. Xiàng states: This refers to managing profit—petty behavior.

"Whispering in secret" (*qièqiè*)—Sīmǎ states: Soft speech. Another text reads "the appearance of calculating."

*Zhá*—pronounced *yú xiá*. The *Shuōwén* states: *Zhá* means "to repay"; *bào* (報) means "to crush." "Repaying and crushing" refers to what a cart treads upon. *Bào* is pronounced *nǚ zhǎn*; *lì* is pronounced *lì dì*.

"To assassinate" (*yǒu shì*)—pronounced *shì*; the same below.

"Cavities in walls" (*xué pèi*)—pronounced *pǔ huí*. Xiàng states: *Pèi* means "wall"; this implies having no fear.

"I speak" (*wú yǔ*)—pronounced *yú*.

Nánróng Chú—pronounced *chú*, *chāng yú*. A disciple of Gēngsāngzǐ. Xiàng pronounces it *chóu*; another pronunciation is *shào jū*. Xú is pronounced *zhí jū*, or *chì jū*, or *chǔ yóu*. The *Hànshū Gǔjīn Rénbiǎo* writes it as Nánróng Chóu, or as *chóu* (儔), or as *shòu* (寿). The *Huáinánzǐ* writes it as Nánróng Dào, stating: "He walks swiftly, his steps never ceasing."

*Cùrán*—pronounced *zǐ liù*.

"Already grown" (*yǐ zhǎng*)—pronounced *dīng zhàng*.

"Will detest" (*jiāng è*)—pronounced *wū*.

"Also avoid" (*yì pì*)—pronounced *bì yì*; meaning "to open." Cuī states: Must be pronounced *bì*. "To cling to one another."

"Or in the privy" (*huò jiàn*)—the space between latrines.

"Striving to hear the Way" (*miǎn wén dào*)—Xiàng states: "Forcing oneself." Another text reads "late."

"Merely reaching the ears" (*dá ěr yǐ*)—Xiàng states: Only reaching the ears, not penetrating the heart. Another text reads "not yet reaching the ears."

"Called running bees" (*yuē bēn fēng*)—pronounced *fú gōng*. Sīmǎ states: Small bees. Another text reads "ground bees." The Jiāngnán Lǐ edition omits the character *yuē* (曰).

*Huò zhú*—pronounced *shǔ*; Sīmǎ states: A large green grub in bean stalks.

"Vietnamese chickens" (*yuè jī*)—Sīmǎ states: Small chickens. Another text reads "castrated chickens."

"Brooding" (*fú*)—pronounced *fú yòu*; a crow nurturing its young.

*Swan" (*hú*)—pronounced *hè*, or *hù wò*.

"Eggs" (*luǎn*)—pronounced *lì guǎn*.

"Lu chickens" (*lǔ jī*)—Xiàng states: Large chickens. Now known as Shu chickens.

"Carrying provisions" (*yíng liáng*)—pronounced *yíng*. According to the *Fāngyán*: *Yíng* means "to carry on the shoulder." In the regions of Qí, Chǔ, Chén, and Sòng, it is called *yíng*. Another pronunciation is *guǒ*.

"Said" (*yuē wéi*)—pronounced *wéi guǐ*.

"Startled" (*jùrán*)—pronounced *jì jù*. The text states: "Losing composure." Chéng states: The appearance of alarm. Another text reads *jué*.

*Zhū yú*—pronounced *zhōng shū*.

The *Shuowen* states: *Zhū* is a wood with a red heart, belonging to the pine and cypress family. Another text says it means "cinnabar." *Zhū yú*—the meaning is taken from "cinnabar heart" and "foolish intent." In an ancient southern repository, it was written as *zhì yú*.

*Méi jié*—pronounced *jiē*; the *Shiming* states: the hair of the eye.

*Guī guī*—Li states: the appearance of lost spirit. Another text says it means "minute" or "petty."

*Ruò sàng*—pronounced *xī làng*.

*Jiē qí liè*, *qí yè*—pronounced *jiē qí liè* and *qí yè*.

*Gān*—pronounced *gān*.

"Yet seeking it in the sea"—Xiàng states: using something short and small to measure a vast and deep expanse.

"You, a lost one!"—Cui states: a person who has lost their nature and emotions.

*Wǎng wǎng hū*—the text states: not knowing where to return.

*Suǒ hào*—pronounced *hū bào*.

*Suǒ wù*—pronounced *wū lù*.

*Xī chóu*—formerly written as *zì chóu*. Liu obtained a version reading *xī chóu*. The text, like the sea, writes *chóu xī*.

*Xǐ zhuó*—pronounced *wén jué*.

*Yù yù*—the *Lüshi Chunqiu* states: water pent up becomes foul, wood pent up becomes worm-eaten, grass pent up becomes withered.

*Jīn jīn*—the text states: unclean. Cheng states: still leaking.

Still there is malice—Li states: malicious thoughts not yet exhausted.

*Wài huò*—pronounced *huò*, meaning restraint or binding. The *San Cang* states: the leather thong of a sword hilt. Originally also written as *huò*. Xiang pronounces it *huò*, meaning abundance, or in general. *Ér zhuō*—pronounced *zé jué*. Cheng states: *zhuō* means to grasp. *Nèi jiàn*—pronounced *qí niǎn*, meaning difficulty. Xu writes *jiàn*, pronounced *qí yǎn*, meaning to close or shut. *Kě miù*—pronounced *mò hóu*. Xiang states: entangled. *Fàng dào*—read as written, or Xiang pronounces it *fāng wǎng*, meaning to rely upon. *Wèi shēng*—Li states: to guard one’s life, making it align with the Dao. *Néng shě*—pronounced *shě*, the same below. *Xiāo*—pronounced *xiāo*. The text states: an appearance of stagnation. *Tóng*—pronounced *dà dǒng*. Xiang pronounces it *chì dòng*, meaning straight and unburdened. The *San Cang* states: upright in form. The text states: an appearance of unimpeded flow. *Háo*—pronounced *hù gāo*. *Ài*—pronounced *yì*, meaning the throat or pharynx. Li’s version reads *bù ài*, pronounced *è*, meaning choking. The text’s version is the same, meaning qi does not flow. *Bù shà*—pronounced *wū mài*. Sima states: in Chu dialect, extreme weeping without sound is called *gā*, or a voice broken from strain. *Wò*—Li states: to clench the hand is called *wò*. *Bù nǐ*—pronounced *wǔ lǐ*. The *Guang Ya* states: to grasp. Cheng states: to clench the fist. The text states: weary and lax. *Gòng qí*—Cui states: *gòng* means unity. The text states: not using strength in a biased way—infants share this power. *Shùn*—pronounced *shùn*, meaning movement. *Piān*—pronounced *piān*. *Wěi*—pronounced *wēi*. *Shé*—pronounced *yǐ zhī*. *Bīng xiè*—pronounced *xiè*. *Jiāo shí*—Cui states: *jiāo* means together. Li states: shared. *Jiāo lè*—pronounced *luò*. *Xiāng yīng*—pronounced *yíng*. The *Guang Ya* states: disorder. *Ruò gǎo*—pronounced *kǔ lǎo*. *È yǒu*—pronounced *wū*. *Zì dìng*.

When the cosmos is at peace, the heart is greatly serene. When one perceives others as things, one perceives things as such—Zhang Junfang’s edition notes this was formerly missing. *Bài*—pronounced *mài*. *Yǐ huá*—pronounced *gǔ*. The *Língtái* and *Língfǔ* both refer to the heart. The true ruler dwells within, called the *fǔ*; the true master roams above, called the *tái*. Not seeing one’s own sincerity when acting means failing to reflect inwardly and rushing outward. *Měi fā ér bù dàng*—pronounced *dī làng*. The *Er Ya* states: *měi* means "though," indicating that though there is movement, it does not strike true. *Quàn nèi*—pronounced *quàn*, meaning a contract. What is internal belongs to oneself; what is external belongs to others. The inner contract bears no name; the outer contract is but merchant’s boasting. The *Shuowen* states: *quàn* means a contract. A contract is a written agreement split with a knife beside the text, hence called a contract. Guo Zhongshu’s *Pèishāng* notes: *quàn* with a slanted knife or slanted force refers to the labor contract’s *quàn*. Another meaning: to halt. *Kuí*—pronounced *juàn*.

Though delusion repeatedly arises is to lose delusory perception. Liu Deyi’s edition had a former lacuna here.

*Qī*—pronounced *fèi fāng guì*. The *Shuowen* states: *qī* means "to meet." Another meaning: "to demand."
*Fèi*—the *Shuowen* states: to disperse wealth and resources. Merchants—pronounced *gǔ*, like the traders of commerce—expend their minds on the tip of an awl.

People see their *qǐ*—still pronounced *kuí rán kǔ huí*. *Kuírán*: an unmoving appearance. Another meaning: "secure," "master," signifying that the multitude, having seen their *qǐ* seeking what lies beyond, yet remain self-assured—how deeply shameful.

Things proceed in *qiě qiě* fashion—*gǒuqiě*, meaning careless. With hearts careless, they have no leisure to shelter themselves; how then could they shelter others?
How could they—pronounced *yān néng yú qián*.

*Mò cǎn*—pronounced *qī kǎn*. The *Guang Ya* states: "pain."
*Mòyé*—pronounced *mò yē*, name of a fine sword.

*Qí fēn*—pronounced *fú wèn*. Same below.
*Suǒ wù*—pronounced *wū lù*. Same below.

Thus, to depart and not return is for the true to drift away without homecoming. To see its ghost is for the yin *qi* to come and lodge.
To depart and obtain this is to obtain death; the lost are as one with the obtained, hence called "obtaining death."

To perish yet have substance—one of the ghosts. The *Guang Ya* states: *miè* means "to annihilate," "to exhaust." Though the light of heaven is extinguished, though there remains true nature, it is no different from those things of darkness.
The *Zhen’gao* calls them "pure numina," "ghosts of good clarity."

To exit without root, to enter without aperture—pronounced *kǔ diào*. Creation has no root; the great void has no aperture.
To dwell—pronounced *hū chù zhào*. Same below.

Long—pronounced as in the common reading. The original *Piaojing* writes it as *biāo*, pronounced *fǔ xiǎo*. Cui states: "its tip." *Yǔ* means the four directions and above and below. *Zhòu* means the past and present.

To exit and enter without seeing its form—*see Zhang Junfang’s edition; the old text reads "enter and exit."*
*Yí shì*—pronounced *wū hū*.
To be—pronounced *sàng xī*.
*Yǐ fēn*—pronounced *fāng yún*.
To be—pronounced *kāo*.
One lineage—one text reads *shǒu*.
*Zhāo* and *Jǐng*—pronounced *zhāo dīng lüè*.
*Jiǎ*—pronounced *zhāo zhāng lǜ*.
To have life—*yǎn*, pronounced *yú chéng*.
Sima states: *wū diàn*.
*Yǎn* means "blemish"; those with blemishes wish to cast them off.
*Cast off*—pronounced *pī*. To disperse.
Thus it is said: *yí shì* arises from the gathering of *yǎn*, and death comes from its dispersal.
*Là*—pronounced *lì hé*.
Refers to the *pí*—pronounced *pí*; Sima states: "the cow’s omasum."
*Hǎi*—pronounced *gǔ lái*. The big toe.
Cui states: "to prepare."
Now, *là* is the great sacrifice, where offerings are prepared and dishes include *tuō hǎi*. Though this follows the rites of dispersal, all should be complete and not scattered away.
*Its yǎn sōu*—pronounced *suǒ liú*.
*Yǎn mò* means "latrine."
*Sōu*—*see the Jiangnan Li edition; the old text was missing.*
To be—pronounced *yú wěi*.
*Dead recompense*—pronounced *cháng liàng*.
The *Guang Ya* states: *cháng* means "repayment."
*Tiáo*—pronounced *tiáo*.
*Yīng*—pronounced *xué*.
*Yí shì* is not of today’s people—*see the Jiangnan ancient repository edition; the old text was missing.*
*Lǐ wěi*—pronounced *niǎn nǚ zhǎn*.

Tread—also "to step upon."
*Áo yú*—pronounced *dào qiè*.
A demeanor of arrogance and indulgence.
*Yù*—pronounced *wū yù*.
A manner of gentle voice.
The *Wen* states: "startled into losing one’s voice."
Another saying: *yù xǔ*, a look of concern and inquiry.
*Pì*—pronounced *jīn bì shuò*.
To remove.
*Xū wú zhī bó*—pronounced *bì mèi*.
Same below.
*Zhī miù*—read as written; means "error," "deception."
One alternate version writes *miù* as *móu*, pronounced *wáng hóu*.
Refers to striking and binding.
*Qù dé*—pronounced *qǐ lǚ*.
*È yù*—pronounced *wū lù*.
*Zhī lěi*—pronounced *liè wěi*.
Same below.
*Bù dàng*—pronounced *tú dǎng*.
Guo states: "to move."
*Dé xìng*—the radiance of virtue; one alternate version writes *xiān* ("prior").
*Yóu nì*—pronounced *yú jì*.
To gaze.
*Zhuō wěi*—*Yì*—pronounced *wǔ jì*.
*Zhōng wēi*—*dīng zhòng*—pronounced *dīng zhòng*.
*Ér liáng*—pronounced *liáng*; Cui states: "a worthy man."
Also pronounced *làng*.
*È tiān*—pronounced *wéi lù*.
Same below.
*Zhì lóng*—*zhī lóng*—pronounced *lì dōng*.
*Tāng yǐ páo*—*bái jiāo*—King Tang employed Yi Yin: Yi Yin carried a cauldron to seek audience with Tang, who recognized his worth and thus followed his nature, placing him in the kitchen to *lóng* him. Already explained in the *Shàn Wáng* chapter.
Duke Mu of Qin *lóng*ed Bai Li Xi with sheepskin: Bai Li Xi fled Qin for Zhou, where a border guard from Chu captured him. Duke Mu ransomed him from Chu with five sheepskins. Another account says Bai Li Xi favored robes of five-colored skins, so the duke *lóng*ed him through what he loved. Already explained in the *Tián Zǐ Fāng* chapter.
*Wàng xíng tóng tiān*—to forget form and unite with Heaven.

"Jiè"—pronounced *jiè*; Guo states: "amputation."
*Guǎng Yǎ* states: "solitary."
*Chǐ huà*—pronounced *chì zhǐ*.
To split; implements for adorning appearance.
Now lacking feet, thus no longer cherished.
Another account says *chǐ* means "to separate."
Cui states: "*Chǐ huà*—unrestrained by rules and measures."
*Fū fù*—pronounced *fú*.
The text states *fù*—to warm repeatedly.
*Zhū*—pronounced *xí*; the text states: "to indulge."
*Bù kuì*—pronounced *kuì*.
*Guǎng Yǎ* states: "to bestow."
Yet one who forgets others in repeated warmth—this is what "to warm repeatedly" means.
That is, to indulge.
When a person indulges in habit, even in small matters they reach extremes. Now, one who warms and indulges another in what they have already obtained, yet does not return to weave and bestow it—this is the height of folly, failing to gain what others cherish.
Lacking the sentiment of mutual care, thus called "forgetting others."
*Wǔ*—pronounced *wǔ*.
*Colophon to the Chapter-and-Verse Pronunciations and Meanings of the Southern Florescence True Scripture, Scroll Eleven*
*Chapter-and-Verse Pronunciations and Meanings of the Southern Florescence True Scripture, Scroll Twelve*

Master Bixu composed *Miscellaneous Chapters: "Xu Wugui" Twenty-Eight—"Transforming Virtue"*

*Xu Wugui*:
A recluse of Mount Min in Wei.

Sima’s edition reads: *Xu Wugui of Mount Min*.
*Master Nü Shang* notes: a favored minister of Wei.

*Duke Wu of Wei* (named Ji), son of *Duke Wen*, ruled from Anyi.

*Láo*:
Pronounced *lì*; to toil.

*"The toil of mountain forests"*—*láo* here reads as written; in the following chapter, all instances are pronounced *lì*.

*Yíng*:
Pronounced *shì*; to indulge desires.

(Identical below.)

*Zhǎng*:
Pronounced *zhàng*.

*Hào*:
Pronounced *hào*.

(Identical in the following chapter.)

*Wù*:
Pronounced *wù*.

(Identical in the following chapter.)

*Chù*:
Pronounced *chù*; to demote, to dismiss.

*Qiān*:
Pronounced *qiān*.
*The Erya* states: "to secure."
*Master Cui* says: "to lead away."
*Sima* says: "to pull."

*Chāorán*:
*Sima* says: "as if disconsolate."

*Shǎo yān*:
Pronounced *zhào*.

*Cháng yǔ*:
Pronounced *jù*; to speak.

*Wú xiàng*:
Pronounced *xiàng*.

(Identical below.)

*"Holding to satiety and stopping"*:
*Master Cheng* says: "clinging to the passions of the mind, only craving fullness in eating—this is the virtue of the wildcat."

*"Gazing at the sun"*:
*Sima* says: "to look far ahead."

*"As if forgetting one’s own body"*:
That is, the spirit remains unmoved, as if the body did not exist.

*Zhí*:
Pronounced *zhòng*; straight as a plumb line.

(Identical below.)

*Sima* says: "Straight" refers to the horse’s teeth; "bent" to its back; "square above" to its head; "round" to its eyes.
*Master Cheng* says: "Bent" refers to the neck.

To be "naturally sufficient in talent" means one requires no instruction or practice.

*Xù*:
Pronounced *xù*; *Wen* says: "to ail."

*Shī*:
Pronounced *yì*; *Wen* says: "to lose."

*Li* says: "to grieve and to lose."

All are startled and alert, as if taking flight.

*Ràng*:
Pronounced *sàng*.
(Identical in the following chapter.)

"That one" means to lose one’s mate.

*Chāoyì*:
Pronounced *yì*; to surpass.

*Xú*:
Pronounced *túliè*.

*Cui* says: "to penetrate."

*Cóng*:
Pronounced *zǐróng*.

*Jīnbǎn*:
Some say it refers to secret prophecies.
*Sima* says: a chapter in the *Book of Zhou*.

*Liùtāo*:
Pronounced *tǔdāo*; referring to the *Six Secret Teachings* of *Tai Gong*, namely *Wen*, *Wu*, *Tiger*, *Leopard*, *Dragon*, and *Dog*.

"Exiles of Yue":
*Sima* says: exiles of the state of Yue, those banished for crimes.
Alternatively, "to go beyond."

*Shuò*:
Pronounced *zhǔ*.

*Jīnián*:
Pronounced *jī*.

"Those who flee into emptiness":
*Sima* says: old burial grounds or abandoned dwellings become emptiness.
*Master Cheng* says: empty gardens and deserted houses.

*Lí*:
Pronounced *lìxī*.

*Cáo*:
Pronounced *túdiào*.

*Zhù*:
Pronounced *zhǔjǔ*.
*Sima* says: "to block."

*Yǔ*:
Appears in *Junfang’s* edition; originally written as *hū*.

*Shēng*:
Pronounced *shēng*.

*Yòu*:
Pronounced *yóujiù*.

"To the path":
*Sima’s* original text reads *jìng*, meaning "way."

*Liàng*:
Pronounced *liáng*; *Gēn* says: "the appearance of about to walk."
*Sima’s* original text reads *liáng*, meaning "good people," referring to those who patrol the emptiness.
"To stand in the void" means to dwell in the space between emptiness.

*Hearing the sound of footsteps*:
Pronounced *tángrán* (like the sound of footsteps striking the ground).
*Master Cheng* says: the sound of people walking.

"And rejoicing":
*Li* says: this illustrates that the *Marquis of Wu* lacked the virtue of a ruler and dwelled in a state of defense. Though he feigned severity at court, it was contrary to his true intent. When he obtained what he desired, it was like a fugitive hearing the sound of footsteps—how could he not change his expression and rejoice?

*Qǐng*:
Pronounced *kǔxiàng*.

*Kài*:
Pronounced *kǔ’ài*.
*Li* says: *qìngkài* is a metaphor for speech and laughter.
Merely hearing what one loves brings great joy—how much more so the affection of flesh and blood, the utmost in joy.

"Long indeed":
Pronounced *fú*; the same below.

*Xiūchéng*:
To eat taro, pronounced *xù*.

*Cōngjiǔ*:
Pronounced *jiǔ*; scallions and leeks.

*Bīn*:
Originally written as *bìn*, pronounced *bìrèn*.
*Sima* says: "to cast aside."
*Li* says: the *bīn* of "guest."

"Seeking to serve":
*Li* says: *gān* means "to seek."

"The blessings of the altars of soil and grain, perhaps":
*Li* says: good words and excellent plans that benefit the state.

"Ten thousand chariots":
Pronounced *shèngzhèng*.

"Not to permit oneself":
*Sima* says: "to grant or withhold."

Meaning the treachery of self-interest.

"Treacherous illness":
*Wang* says: treachery means to follow the right while inclining toward the wrong—this is called illness.

"Laying down arms":
*Yǎn* means "to rest."

"Crane formations":
*Li* says: troops arranged like cranes in flight.
*Sima* says: *hèliè* refers to bells and drums.
*Wen* says: battle formations.

*Lìqiáo*:
Originally written as *jiào*, pronounced *zàixiāo*.
*Sima* says: *Lìqiáo* is the name of a tower.
Note: it means "magnificent and towering."
*Wen* says: a watchtower.

"Foot soldiers":
*Sima* says: infantry.
*Wen* says: cavalry.

*Zītán*:
Pronounced *cèqí*; *Zītán* is the name of a platform.
*Master Cheng* says: the name of an office.
*Wen* says: a battlefield.

"How":
Pronounced *wū*.

"Do not entangle":
Pronounced *yīng* or *yíng*.

"Already stripped":
Pronounced *duó*; flesh removed from bone.

*Pronounced* *tuō*; bone separated from flesh.
This is incorrect.

"Release the heart."

The Great Weī King
*Pronounced* *zuǐ*.

*Master Cheng* says: an ancient perfected person.

Jùcí Mountain
*Pronounced* *bǎzī*.

*Sima* originally wrote *jiù*; a mountain name in Mì County, Yíngyáng, now called Tàiwěi Mountain.

Chāngyǔ
*Pronounced* *yǔ*.

Cānshèng
*Pronounced* *shéng*.

*Cānshèng* refers to the attendant on the right of a chariot.
The charioteer stands on the left.

Wèi
*Pronounced* *xí*.
Péng
*Pronounced* *péngdēng*.

*Master Cui* originally wrote *yí*, *pronounced* *shūshì*.

The lead horses:
*Sima* says: two men who guide the horses ahead.

Kūnhūn
*Pronounced* *hūn*.

Gǔ
*Pronounced* *gǔ*.

Jī
*Pronounced* *jī*.

The rear chariot:
*Sima* says: two men who follow behind the chariot.

The wilds of Xiāngchéng:
Now in Rǔzhōu, there is a Xiāngchéng County, south of Dàwěi Mountain.

The Seven Sages:
The Yellow Emperor (one), Fāngmíng (two), Chāngyǔ (three), Zhāngruò (four), Wèipéng (five), Kūnhūn (six), Gǔjī (seven).

Yú
*Pronounced* *shào*.

Mào
*Pronounced* *mòudòu*.

*Li* says: the appearance of dizziness.
*Sima* reads it as *jīng*, meaning vertigo.

The elder
*Pronounced* *zhàng*.

The chariot of the sun:
*Sima* says: taking the sun as a chariot, meaning to ride the ever-renewing sun in transformation.
A chariot is that which shifts and does not settle.

Shàoguàn
*Pronounced* *qíquán*.

*Li* says: to remove.

And again
*Pronounced* *fúyòu*.

Also to depart
*Pronounced* *qǐlǚ*.

The pleasure garden
*Bùlè* is *pronounced* *luò*.
The same below.

The discerning scholar:
*Li* says: one of keen perception.
*Master Cheng* says: a vigilant and discerning scholar.

Líng
*Li* says: to overbear and oppress.

Suì
*Pronounced* *xìn*; *Guǎngyǎ* says: to inquire.
Also *pronounced* *suìsuì*.

Refers to delighting in overbearing and humiliating, scolding others.

The phrase appears in *Zhang Junfang’s* edition; originally written as *jùn píng zhī shì*.

All are confined
*Pronounced* *yòu*; *Wen* says: stuck in one direction.

The flourishing court
*Pronounced* *zhíyáo*.

The scholar of the common people:
*Li* says: one skilled in governing the people.

Taking hardship upon oneself
*Pronounced* *nǎndàn*.

Withered and withered
*Pronounced* *kǔlǎo*.
The same in the following chapter.

The long-established:
A long-accumulated reputation.

*Wang* says: taking a withered and dried life as amusement, their lodging is but a name.

Extensive governance
*Pronounced* *zhílì*.

Valuing alliances:
Refers to matters of covenants and assemblies.

Not aligning:
*Pronounced* *bù bǐ*; *Cheng* says: aligning refers to harmony and joy. The same below.

Merchants:
*Pronounced* *gǔ*. Those who study to occupy office are called *scholars*; those who open land and cultivate grain are called *farmers*; those who craft and complete implements are called *artisans*; those who circulate wealth and sell goods are called *merchants*. Another says: sitting to sell is called *merchant*.

Marketplace:
In antiquity, markets were established near wells, hence called *marketplace*.

Then vigorous:
Vigorous is akin to diligent.

Erroneous and deluded:
*Pronounced* *ér zhòng*.

Ru, Mo, Yang, Bing:
*Cheng* says: Ru is Zheng Huan by name; Mo is named Di; Yang is Yang Lai; Bing is Gongsun Longzi.

Lu Ju:
*Pronounced* *lǔ jù*; *Cheng* says: a man of the early Zhou.

Winter hearth:
*Pronounced* *qī luàn*. *Cheng* says: in winter, one takes ten-year-dried ashes to surround the fire, and after a moment, fire emerges that can be used to heat the tripod.

Summer ice:
*Cheng* says: in the height of summer, one fills an earthen jar with old broth, boils it to extreme heat, then sinks the jar into a well, and ice forms of itself.

To fabricate:
*Pronounced* *wéi zhī yú wěi*.

Abandon:
Abandon means to set aside.

Change and adjust:
*Pronounced* *tú diào*.

Without proper measure:
*Pronounced* *wú dàng*.

To join:
To unite.

"Then with me debate":
The Jiangnan edition reads *Then with me debate*; the old edition reads *Then with me by debate*.

To oppose with words:
*Pronounced* *fú fèi*. *Wen* says: *fú* means to oppose.

To suppress with sound:
*Wen* says: *zhèn* means to press down.

The man of Qi threw his son in Song:
*Pronounced* *zhí zǐ yú Sòng*; to throw means to cast. *Sima* says: the man of Qi, hating his son, threw him in Song and had the gatekeeper guard him.

Now, though his form is incomplete, he takes himself as right.

The *xing* bell:
*Pronounced* *yán zhōng*. *Zilin* says: *xing* resembles a small bell with a long neck; another says it resembles a *yi* vessel but is larger.

Bound with restraints:
*Guo* says: for fear it might break or be damaged. Note: this speaks of valuing the humble bell and taking oneself as right.

Tang’s son:
Refers to a lost and perished son.

Abandoned kind:
Abandoned means perished; perished in its kind, therefore.

Hui Shi betrayed the Dao and loved debate, just as the man of Qi cast off his son and cherished the bell.

Never yet departed:
*Pronounced* *wèi shǐ lí lì*.

From Cen:
*Pronounced* *yú cén shì jīn*.

Cen means "bank." *Cheng* says: A man of Ying in Chu, having lodged by the riverside, threw his bell to the gatekeeper’s household. At midnight when no one was about, he would board others’ boats, yet when the boat had not yet left the bank, he would turn and fight with the boatman. Ashamed and in the wrong, he then fabricated grievances. So deluded was he, yet he took himself as right.

*Wangzhi*
*Pronounced* *cóng zhě cái yòng*.

*Yingren*
*Pronounced* *yǐ jǐng*.

The capital of Chu. The *Hanshu yinyi* writes it as "actor," referring to an ancient master of plastering. He wore broad collars and large sleeves to plaster overhead, keeping his collar and sleeves clean. Once, a speck of flying mud accidentally landed on his nose, so he had Craftsman Shi wield his axe to hew it off.

*You*
*Pronounced* *wēn*.

*E*
*Pronounced* *wū luò*.

White clay.

*Man*
*Pronounced* *mò gān*.

*Li* says: to plaster. The four characters "closing the eyes and indulging the hand" are from the Jiangnan edition of the text; the old edition mistakenly attributes them to Guo Xiang’s commentary.

*Shiwei*
*Pronounced* *yú wěi*.

*Gongju*
Some say "how could one call it..."—the Jiangnan Li edition writes *hui* (avoid), while the old edition writes *wei* (call).

*Dabing*
Great illness; *da* means "extreme."

*Wu hu*
*Pronounced* *wū hū*.

*Zhu*
*Pronounced* *zhú*.

*Yu*
Read as *yù*, or alternatively *yú qiě*.

*Gougou*
To turn back.

"Above forgets while below rebels" means that one who does not exalt himself above will not be betrayed below.

"With sageliness over others, with worthiness beneath others"—the Jiangnan edition writes: "with sageliness over others, with worthiness beneath others"; the old edition writes only "with worthiness."

*Jiejiao*
*Pronounced* *jū qī xú*.

King Wen of Wu—his name was Fuchai.

*Xunran*
Pronounced *xún rán* or *xùn rán*; Sima [Biao] says it means "sudden." Cheng [Xuanying] says it means "fearful."

*Shenzuan*
Pronounced *zè jīn* or *shì jīn*. Cheng [Xuanying] says it means "thicket."

*Wei*
Pronounced *wēi*.

*She*
Pronounced *yí zhī*. Cheng [Xuanying] says it means "leisurely."

*Jue*
Pronounced *jū fù*. The *San Cang* says it means "to seize."

*Sao*
Pronounced *sù bào*. The text says *shèng zhí*, describing swift movement.

*Jian*
Pronounced *biàn*.

*She*
Pronounced *shí yì*; the same below.

*Bo*
Pronounced *bó*.

*Jieji*
Pronounced *jí yè*; means "swift."

*Xiang*
Pronounced *xī liàng*. Sima [Biao] says it refers to the king’s hunting attendants.

*Qushe*
Pronounced *cù*; means "urgent."

*Ju zhi si*
Sima [Biao] says it means "to die when captured." *Zhi ju zhi* means "this is."

*Qi bian*
Pronounced *bì miàn*.

*Yi’ao*
Pronounced *ào*; means "arrogant."

*Ci ji*
Pronounced *jì lì*; means "to punish."

Dong Wu was a man of the Dao; he emulated his virtue to eradicate sensual indulgence.

*Yi chu*
Pronounced *shì jū*. Cheng [Xuanying] says it means "to remove."

*Qu*
Pronounced *qǐ lǚ*.

*Le*
Pronounced *luò*.

*Yu*
His name.

*Yinji*
Pronounced *yú zhǎn*.

*Xu*
Pronounced *xū*.

*Ru jian*
Pronounced *biàn*.

The mouth of the mountain cave—Li [Yi] says it refers to the caves of Mount Qi in the south. Sima [Biao]’s edition reads "within the mountain cave."

Tian He was the lord of Qi. He honored virtue, so the people of the state rejoiced in him.

*Yu zhi*
Pronounced *yáng liù*.

*Bi wu*
Pronounced *wū*; the same below.

*Zi sang*
Pronounced *xī làng*.

*Gui mo*

*Shāng*
Pronounced *shāng*; Li [Yi] says it is the general term for wine vessels.

Sunshu Ao held the *jué*-cup. According to the *Zuo Zhuan*, Sunshu Ao was the prime minister of King Zhuang of Chu. Confucius had not yet been born, and in the sixteenth year of Duke Ai of Lu, Confucius passed away. Later, when the Duke of Bai caused chaos, Yi Liao had never served in Chu. The *Zuo Zhuan* of the twelfth year of Duke Xuan also records that Chu had Xiong Yi Liao, who was a contemporary of Sunshu Ao, far removed from Confucius—this is likely a figurative reference. Cheng [Xuanying] says the *jué* is also a wine vessel, holding one *sheng*.

In this context, the phrase "words of the ancients" refers to the invocations spoken by the ancients during gatherings and alliances, at the time of libation sacrifices. *Nòng wán*: *wán* means "pearl." *Nòng wán* refers to the skill of juggling pearls. Yi Liao regarded the affairs of the world as if turning pearls in his palm, finding amusement in this alone. The *Master of Ghost Valley* has a method for turning pearls, akin to the boundless ferocity of beasts. The infinite wisdom of the sage is like the ceaseless turning of pearls, resembling the inexhaustible might of beasts—thus Yi Liao demonstrated his skill in juggling pearls.

*Liǎng jiā zhī nàn*
Pronounced *nǎi dàn*. *Jiě* pronounced *xiè*. Sima [Biao] says Yi Liao was a brave warrior of Chu, skilled in juggling pearls. When Bai Gong Sheng of Chu was about to cause chaos, he killed the prime minister Zixi and Ziqi. They said, "In the south of the market, there is Xiong Yi Liao. If we obtain him, he can match five hundred men." They went to inform him, but he refused. When they pressed him with a sword, he remained unmoved, continuing to juggle pearls as before, saying, "I will not betray you." Bai Gong then killed Zixi. Ziqi sighed over the conflict between the two families, while Yi Liao remained uninvolved in their troubles.

The text says: Bai Gong Sheng and the grandee Zixi raised armies to fight each other. The two families’ grandees said, "Yi Liao is a reclusive scholar; one man is worth five hundred." They both sent envoys to summon him. Yi Liao lay at ease, resting his head high, receiving the envoys of the two grandees. He juggled pearls with both hands without pause, then lay back down without rising. When they pressed him with a sword, he remained unmoved. The envoys of the two grandees returned and fully explained Yi Liao’s intent. The two grandees said, "His high pillow and reclining show us his non-action; his unmoving response to the sword shows that military force is not to be relied upon. The two hands represent the two families; the pearls are round objects of non-action. Juggling them without cease means both will reach exhaustion. This makes clear that if the two families continue their conflict, they will surely perish." The two grandees disbanded their troops and returned, no longer resorting to arms.

*Gān qǐn bǐng yǔ*
*Qǐn* read as written; it means covering the face with a fan. Sima [Biao]’s edition reads *dèng*, glossed as *hé* (or *chì*, "wings"), the implement held by dancers in the *yú* sacrifice. Cui’s edition reads *yì* ("wings").

The people of Ying laid down their weapons. Sima [Biao] says this means Sunshu Ao wished to sleep peacefully and rest in tranquility, cultivating his virtue in the ancestral temple while repelling enemies a thousand *li* away. The state of Ying laid down its weapons, having no need for attack or conquest.

*Huì*
Pronounced *xū huì*. *Sān chǐ*: *chǐ* means "long." Sima [Biao] says *huì* means "breath." Yi Liao juggled pearls amid mounting difficulties; Sunshu Ao removed obstacles to repel enemies. I, too, wish to sigh over my *sān chǐ*. *Sān chǐ* refers to a dagger. Cheng [Xuanying] says *huì* means "mouth." If one’s words are fitting, then silence is no different from having a mouth three *chǐ* long. Thus, I wish for this.

*Bǐ zhī wèi*
*Bǐ* refers to Yi Liao and Sunshu Ao. One says *bǐ* refers to peaceful repose.

*Cǐ zhī wèi*
*Cǐ* refers to Confucius. One says *cǐ* refers to juggling pearls.

*Zǒng*
Pronounced *zǒng*. One edition reads "mutually identical."

*Shàn fèi*
Pronounced *fèi*. Sima [Biao] says it means not distinguishing between guest and host, bringing things to a halt.

*Shàn yán*
Sima [Biao] says it means losing the root while chasing the branches, speaking without cease.

*Xún gǔ ér bù mó*
One edition reads *mó* ("to grind"). Guo [Xiang] says *mó* means "to wipe." Wang [Bi] says *mó* means "to erase." Though one constantly engages with things, one does not lose oneself. Though governing the present, one should follow the Way of the ancients. From ancient times to the present, one’s name is not erased.

*Xiāng xíng*
Jiu Fang Yin, pronounced *yīn*, was a skilled horse physiognomist; the *Huainanzi* writes this as Jiu Fang Gao.

*Wéi wǒ*
Pronounced *yú wěi*.

*Xiāng wú zǐ*
Pronounced *xī liàng*.

*Kǔn*
Pronounced *kùn*.

*Qú rán*
Pronounced *jì jū*. Sima [Biao] says it means a joyful appearance. The *Zilin* says it means "to gaze widely."

*Suǒ rán*
Pronounced *xī gè*. Sima [Biao] says it means the appearance of tears falling.

*Yù fú*
Pronounced *yú lǚ*. It means "to resist" or "to oppose."

*Zāng*
Pronounced *miè*. The *Erya* says it is a female sheep. *Yú ào*: *ào* pronounced *wū bào*, the southwest corner, the position of the *wei* earth and the sheep. One says it refers to a pig or a pen.

*Hào tián*
Pronounced *hū bào*. *Yú yǎo*: one edition reads *yǎo*, pronounced *wū diào*, the northeast corner. One says it is the southeast corner, the fire earth, where quails are born. One says it is the southeast, the *chen* earth, where *chen* is the quail-raven.

*Yóu yú tiān dì*
This line appears in the ancient southern manuscript; it was missing in earlier editions.

*Yāo*
Pronounced *gǔ yáo*, meaning "to encounter."

*Lè wěi*
*Lè* pronounced *luò*, *wěi* pronounced *yú wēi*.

*Shé*
Pronounced *yú zhī*.

*Zhī cháng*
Pronounced *chén yáng* or *shí liàng*, meaning "to return."

*Guài xíng*
Pronounced *xià mèng*.

*Wú jǐ*
Pronounced *jū qǐ*.

*Yú yān*
Pronounced *yān*.

*Yù*
Pronounced *yù*.

*Yì*
Pronounced *chǐ*.

*Yuè*
Pronounced *yuè*.

Qu Gong was a wealthy man of Qi. His household was the standard for the street. He bought *kǔn* to replace himself, eating meat all his life until death. One says Qu Gong was a butcher who, with *kǔn*, formed a lord-minister relationship, sharing meat together.

*Wài xián*
*Xù xù*: Li [Yi] says it means the appearance of practicing benevolence. Wang [Bi] says it means the appearance of compassion, encouragement, and diligence. *Qí rén yǔ rén xiāng shí*: *yǔ* pronounced *yú*. It means they will rush toward benevolence and righteousness, no longer tending to agriculture, and when hungry, will consume one another.

*Suǒ wù*
Pronounced *wū lù*, referring to their conduct.

Moreover, let us suppose that the implements of the greedy among birds and beasts—Shi Ma says: the greedy among birds and beasts kill without limit; those greedy for benevolence and righteousness harm without end. Thus, let us suppose that benevolence and righteousness serve as the implements of the greedy among birds and beasts.

*Yī mì*
Pronounced *bó jié*. *Shuowen* says: "to conceal, so as not to see one another."

*Tōu ān*
Pronounced *nuǎn xuān*. The appearance of warmth.

*Shū*
Pronounced *shū*. *Shuowen* says: "good and fine."

Again, *nuǎn shū*: the appearance of self-satisfaction.

*Rú*
Pronounced *rú*, meaning peace.

*Xū*
Pronounced *xū*.

*Rú* here refers to seeking ease in momentary comfort, a fleeting instant.

*Juǎn*
Pronounced *quán*.

*Lóu*
Pronounced *lǚ*. *Juǎn qī* means constrained and bound, like being fettered.

This speaks of those who restrain themselves in pursuit of fame: bending the self to serve things, though things may long to return, they will ultimately suffer. Like Shun ascending to office and enduring trials, his body became emaciated and withered—such harm arises from the stench of his conduct.

*Shǐ shī*
Pronounced *sè*.

*Shū liè*
Long hair; as seen in Zhang Junfang’s edition, formerly omitted.

*Kuí*
Pronounced *kuí*.

*Kuí*, according to *Shuowen*: "the space between the thighs."

*Qū xiàn*
Pronounced *wū huí*, meaning the crook of the thigh.

*Cāo yān*
Pronounced *qī cáo*.

"Mutton does not attract ants"—*yǐ* pronounced *qǐ*. It is said that as Shun aged, his heart labored without joy or delight, just as mutton does not attract ants.

*Shān*
Pronounced *shè rán*.

*Shān xíng*
Pronounced *xià mèng*.

"Three moves form a capital": in one year, a dwelling becomes a settlement; in two, a town; in three, a capital.

"To Deng’s ruins": the name of a town.

*Tóng tǔ*
"Barren earth": land without grass or trees.

*Chǐ zhǎng*
Pronounced *dīng zhàng*.

*È zhòng*
Pronounced *wū lù*.

*Bù bǐ*
Pronounced *pí wàng*, meaning "not aligned." Below, the same.

*Hé*
Harmony.

*Yáng*
Pronounced *yáng*. Harmony is the warmth of roasting.

To be roasted by the warmth of harmony.

"In the case of ants, abandoning wisdom; in the case of fish, gaining strategy; in the case of sheep, abandoning intent": ants die in water, fish live in water, and sheep sicken in water. Another version says: the true person has no stench, thus does not attract ants—this is ants abandoning wisdom. To dwell together in the great Dao of mutual forgetfulness, possessing no virtue of clinging or delay—this is the fish’s strategy. Sheep, having no stench, do not attract ants—this is sheep abandoning intent.

*Self-concealment*
Waiting for others with the heavens; as seen in Zhang Junfang’s edition, formerly written as *zhī*.

**Dǒng**
Pronounced *jǐn*. Sima [Qian] states: this is *wūtóu* (aconite), which treats wind-cold impediment.

**Jié gěng**
Pronounced *jié*, anciently *měng* cut. Sima [Qian] states: *jié gěng* (platycodon) treats blood stasis, concretions, and impediment in the heart and abdomen.

**Jī yōng**
Pronounced *yú róng*. Sima [Qian] states: this is *jītóu* (foxnut), also called *qiàn*; when ground with lotus seeds into powder and ingested, it prolongs life. The original text sometimes writes it as *yōng*.

**Shǐ líng**
Sima’s edition writes it as *shǐ náng* (pig’s sac), also called *zhū líng* (polyporus); its root resembles a pig’s testicles and can treat thirst. Note: these four are all names of medicinal herbs.

**At this time, they serve as the emperor’s [remedies]**
Sima [Qian] states: medicinal herbs have their seasons, taking turns as the "emperor," referring to their phases of dominance, decline, and rest, each finding its proper use.

**Shèng yán**
Pronounced *shēng*.

**Gōu Jiàn**
Pronounced *gōu*. The King of Yuè.

**Jiǎ dùn**
Pronounced *chún yǐn*. *Dùn*: a shield, the part of the shaft that covers the body and obscures the eyes.

**Qī yú**
Pronounced *bǐng*. Li [Yi] states: to ascend a mountain is called *qī*.

**Huì**
Pronounced *gù wài*.

**Jī**
Pronounced *jī*.

**Zhǒng Zhāngyǒng**
Pronounced *zhāng yǒng*. A minister of Yuè. The *Annals of Wu and Yue* states: his surname was *Wén*, given name *Zǐ Shǎoqín*.

**Thus it could be preserved**
One version writes *can be preserved*, meaning that though Yuè was destroyed, it could still be preserved.

**Chī**
Pronounced *chǐ yí*. *Mù chéng* states: the owl’s eyes hear by day and open by night, thus they are suited to night but not to day.

**Hè jìng**
Pronounced *xíng dìng*.

**Jiě**
Pronounced *jiā mǎi*. Sima [Qian] states: to remove. *Chéng* states: the crane’s legs, having received their allotted measure, grow long; thus they can be long but not short. Such are the joints of its limbs—when severed, it grieves. This is like Zhong [Zhangyong], who was dim in self-preservation but skilled in preserving the state.

**There is loss in this**
When forms naturally burden one another, the world can burden things, and things can burden people—thus Minister Zhong could not avoid [his fate].

**Those who rely on the source and proceed**
Water flows from its source; though it may encounter wind and sun, it cannot be harmed. When the Dao is perfected, one’s nature, though in the world, cannot be altered.

**Zhī**
Pronounced *dīng zhàng*.

**Zī**
To gather or assemble. Li [Yi] states: many.

**Relying on what they do not tread**
Pronounced *nǚ zhǎn*. Li [Yi] states: one foot never moves, thus it can travel far and wide.

**Abandoning calculation**
Pronounced *xiè*, the same below.

**Yòu**
Pronounced *jiā mǎi* cut.

**Xié**
Pronounced *xié* (lower) *jié* cut.

**Huá**
Pronounced *hū* (eighth) *bā* cut.
**Xié-huá** means disorderly and chaotic.

**Yáng-què**
Pronounced *jué*.
The *San Cang* states: *què* means opposition.
Xu Shen says: *yáng-què* refers to rough approximation of standards.
Wang [commentary] states: to scrutinize briefly and make manifest.

**Huò-jiě**
Pronounced *jiā mǎi* cut.

**Fù-yú**
Pronounced *fú*.

*The Southern Florescence: Authentic Scripture, Chapter and Verse with Pronunciations and Meanings*, Scroll Twelve—concluded.
*The Southern Florescence: Authentic Scripture, Chapter and Verse with Pronunciations and Meanings*, Scroll Thirteen.
By Master Bìxū.

**Miscellaneous Chapters: Zéyáng (Twenty-Nine)**
**Yìjìn**

**Zéyáng**
Sima [Biao] states: named Zéyáng, styled Péngyáng. Another account holds he was surnamed Péng, named Zéyáng, a man of the early Zhou.
Cheng [Xuanying] states: a man of Lu who wandered serving the feudal lords, later entered Chu intending to serve King Wen of Chu.

**Yíjié**
A minister of Chu. Wang Guo [commentary] states: a worthy man of Chu. Cheng [Xuanying] states: a grandee of Chu.

**Bù-tán**
Pronounced *tán*. Li [Yi] states: to persuade.

**Gōngquè-xiū**
Pronounced *yuè*, a recluse.

**Chuò**
Pronounced *chū jué* cut.
Sima [Biao] states: to stab.

**Shān-fán**
Pronounced *fán*. Li [Yi] states: beside.
Sima [Biao] states: secluded.
The *Guǎngyǎ* states: edge.
Cheng [Xuanying] states: dense forest.

**Yú-zhái**
Sima [Biao] states: to dwell in the mountain shade without revealing oneself.
Wen [commentary] states: to make one’s home beside the mountain.

**Diān-míng**
Pronounced *mián*.
Sima [Biao] states: *diān-míng* means delusion.
[The text] speaks of those who entangle themselves with rulers, their hearts racing after wealth and honor.

**Yē**
Pronounced *yè*.

The *Zìlín* states: injury from summer heat.

**Náo**
Pronounced *nǎi xiào* cut.
Wang [Guo] states: Only with upright virtue and the perfect Dao can one subdue them; flatterers seize it through clever eloquence, thus they can muddy and bend it.

**Transforming the lowly while dwelling in the high, taking the lowly as the root.**
An alternate reading: *or transforming the lowly among others.*

**Lè-wù**
Pronounced *luò*.

**Yǐn**
Pronounced *zhèn*.

**Chóu**
Pronounced *zhí zhōu* cut.

**Miù**
Pronounced *wáng hóu* cut.
Entangled and bound.

Wen [commentary] states: active in forming connections.

**Zhōu-jìn-yī-tǐ**
All-pervading, unified reflection—coarse and refined, thoroughly penetrating. Thus it is called *all-pervading, unified reflection*. *Unified* is Heaven.

**Fù-mìng**
Pronounced *bō*, to move; *yáo*, to stir.
All things stir and move, growing and flourishing, each according to its natural endowment—this is returning to its mandate.

**Mìng**
To name.

**Yōu-hū-zhì**
Anxious over wisdom, yet their conduct constantly lacks even brief respite.
Pronounced *jū qǐ* cut.

**Shí-qí-yǒu-zhǐ**
When it comes to rest—what then?
Wang [Guo] states: *Anxious over wisdom* refers to those who act with intention, fretting that their bodily wisdom falls short. They do not know that employing wisdom must lead to loss, and having lost, they then worry over the lack of wisdom. As for their congratulatory deeds—flawed and unavailing—thus their roots of sorrow and trouble intertwine without a moment’s pause, hence *constantly lacks even brief respite*. When it comes to rest, unable to relinquish wisdom and abandon anxiety—what is there if not sorrow?

**Shēng-ér-měi-zhě**
Those born beautiful—others reflect and approve them. If not told, they do not know their beauty surpasses others. The moment reflection arises, the beauty of things exists, yet it is bestowed by those without intent.

**Zuò-míng**
Blind reflection.
Thus people praise them. If others did not inform them, they would not know their beauty surpasses others.

**Hào**
Pronounced *hū bào* cut.
The appearance of joyful delight.

**Zhī-mín**
Pronounced *mín rěn* cut.
Guo [Pu] states: to unite.
Same as *yù*.
Sima [Biao] states: to flourish.

**Shí-jiǔ**
Nineteen—seeing ten but recognizing nine.
*Seeing* refers to what is witnessed; *hearing*, to what is heard.

Rǎnxiàng-shì, pronounced *xī liàng* cut.
Guo [Pu] states: an ancient sage-king.

Once lodged—pronounced *shè*.
All follow—pronounced *xùn jùn* cut.
Their conduct was complete yet not drowned—pronounced *kuàng bì* cut.
Deep; the sense is to be steeped and submerged.
Li [Yi] reads *yì*: overflowing.
Wang [Bi] states: ruin and decay.

Without intent, they moved in harmony—where could they not arrive? Thus it is said: all follow.
Their conduct was complete yet neither things nor self were harmed, thus there was no ruin or decay.

Mén-Yǐn Dēnghéng
Mén-Yǐn was an office; Dēnghéng, the man.
To enact—pronounced *wěi* cut. Below likewise.
To transmit—pronounced *fù*. Below likewise.
Its name flourished—pronounced *yíng*.
The Dao of attaining both—pronounced *xiàn biàn* cut.
Obtaining the Dao of following completion to govern its name, name and reality established the law—thus both were attained, like a mirror and its reflection meeting.

Róngchéng-shì
Laozi’s teacher, who composed fourteen scrolls.
*Stopping Strife*

Wèi Yíng, pronounced *yíng*.
Sima [Biao] states: King Hui of Wei.
And Marquis Tián Móu—Sima [Biao] states: King Wei of Qi, named Móu, son of Duke Huan.
(According to the *Records of the Grand Historian*, King Wei’s name was Yīn, not Móu.)
To bind—pronounced *yī què* cut. Also read as written.

Sima [Biao] states: in the twenty-sixth year of King Hui.
To turn one’s back—pronounced *pèi*.
To stab—pronounced *qī cì* cut.
