Xīshǒu was an office of Wei; Sima [Biao] states: akin to the modern "Tiger’s Fang General," Gōngsūn Yǎn held this post.
Ten thousand chariots—pronounced *shéng zhèng* cut.
A commoner—*Chéng* states: one without office, a man and woman paired as husband and wife.
*Cóng chóu*—to avenge a grievance.
To act as lord—pronounced *wěi* cut. Below, "to request to act as lord" likewise.
To seize a state—"seize" means to capture cities and take them, as one uproots grass and trees, obtaining both root and stem.
Jì fled—*Chéng* states: Tián Jì, a general of Qi, fled in fear, or some say he was besieged.
To strike—pronounced *fú chì* cut. The *Sāncāng* states: to strike; pronounced *shé* cut when meaning "to break."
Jìzǐ was a minister of Wei.
Wén was a prince of the state of Wei.
To collapse—pronounced *guài*.
Huázǐ was also a minister of Wei.
Huìzǐ was Huì Shī.
And to perceive—pronounced *xiàn biàn* cut. Below likewise.
Dài Jìnrén was a worthy of Liang; Huì Shī recommended him to the king of Wei.
Snail—pronounced *guā*; *Lǐ* states: the snail is an insect with two horns, commonly called the "snail cow." The *Sāncāng* states: a small cowrie. Another says its common name is "yellow calf."
To pursue the routed—read as written; "north" refers to the shady, secluded places, hence those who retreat in defeat and flee are called *běi*.
Said "alas"—pronounced *wū qí* cut.
Words and speech—pronounced *yú*.

Suddenly—pronounced *chǎng*.
The *Zìlín* states: "bewildered"; Chéng states: "the appearance of bitter resentment."
To mutter—pronounced *xū jiāo* cut.
The sound of a pipe.
The *Guǎngyǎ* states: "to wail."
The sword’s pommel—Sīmǎ states: "the small ring at the hilt of a sword."
To whistle—pronounced *xuè*; to whistle suddenly like the wind passing.
Far-flattering—pronounced *yǐ qǐ* cut.
Ant Hill—Lǐ states: "the name of a mountain."
The wine-seller—Lǐ states: "a household that sells wine."
Ascending the ridge—Sīmǎ states: "the ridge, the peak of a house."
To observe from without.
Another says: "the ridgepole of a house."
Gathered—pronounced *zǒng*; Lǐ states: "the appearance of a crowd."
The sage’s servitude—refers to one who embraces sagely virtue yet conceals himself among servants and bondsmen.
Sīmǎ’s text writes *pú* for *servitude*, meaning the sage’s return to simplicity.
Concealed in the fields—Wáng states: "to cultivate the work of farming, thus hidden in the borders of the fields."
Sound vanishing—pronounced *xiāo*; Sīmǎ states: "small."
The great artisan—stone silences sound and erases traces, so none recognize him.
Not petty—*xiè* also.
Not entangled in the world.
Sunken in obscurity—Sīmǎ states: "to be eminent yet remain hidden, like sinking where there is no water."
Cultivating form—
Chángwú—Féngrén: Chángwú is a place name.
Chéng states: "a place abundant in long catalpas, hence the name."
Féngrén—one who guards the borderlands.
Zǐ Láo—Sīmǎ states: "namely Qín Láo, a disciple of Confucius."
Saline land—pronounced *lǔ*.
Wilderness—pronounced *mǎng mò* cut.

"Extinction and rupture" are like cutting short the grass. Lǐ states: meaning "not thorough." Guō states: "reckless and hasty, careless and incomplete, failing to fulfill one’s proper role." Sīmǎ states: "crude and careless, meaning shallow plowing and sparse sowing; 'extinction and rupture' refers to severing the grass."

"Transforming the Qí" means altering the methods of the common people. Another reading: *cái xì qiē*, where the *Shuōwén* states: "when grain and wheat sprout ears, their tops are level."

*Yōu*—pronounced *yōu*. Sīmǎ states: "a hoe." The *Guǎngyǎ* states: "to push." The *Zìlín* states: "a tool for smoothing fields."

"Sated with meals"—pronounced *sūn*; meaning "to eat." Another reading: "rice soaked in water is called *cān*."

"Detached"—*lí*, with the same pronunciation below. "With the multitude"—read as written; Wáng states: "referring to all matters one may undertake." "Fleeing, detachment, extinction, and ruin" all resemble "acting with the multitude," where "acting with the multitude" is what is called "crude and careless." Sīmǎ’s text reads "multitude of deceptions."

"Desires"—*wù*, pronounced *wū lù*. "Their calamities"—*niè*, pronounced *yú liè*.

*Huán*—pronounced *wán*, a type of reed. *Wěi*—pronounced *yú guǐ*, meaning "reed." *Lú*—also "reed."

*Jiān*—pronounced *gǔ tián*. *Jiānjiā*—reeds and rushes. *Jiā*—pronounced *jiā*, also "reed."

"Collapsing"—pronounced *huí nèi*. "Leaking and erupting"—Lǐ states: "referring to the scattering and draining of essential *qi*, rising as collapse and descending as leakage, without regard for where it emerges."

Originally written as *piāo*, now *biāo*—pronounced *fū miào*. *Jū*—pronounced *qī yú*. "Boils and sores"—referring to festering wounds and discharging pus.

*Jiè*—pronounced *jiè*. *Yōng*—pronounced *yōng*. *Sōu*—alternatively written as *shòu*, pronounced *suǒ qiú*.

"Grease"—Sīmǎ states: "referring to the thick, white foam that forms atop the urine of those suffering from consumptive exhaustion." All these arise from being moved by profit and desire, losing one’s proper *qi*. None compare to the fullness yielded by deep plowing and thorough smoothing.

"Wading through Dust"—Bó Jǔwén states: "There was a man of virtue from Lǔ who wished to journey to Qí to propagate the Dao."

*Gū*—pronounced *gū*, meaning "criminal" or "condemned man." Lǐ states: "referring to those sentenced to death." Another says: "those executed in the marketplace and left exposed."

*Qiǎng*—pronounced *qí liáng*.

"Morning robes"—pronounced *zhí yáo*.

*Mù*—pronounced *mò*; Sīmǎ states: "to cover."

"Wailing to Heaven"—pronounced *hù dāo*.

"Great calamity"—pronounced *zāi*.

*Lí*—pronounced *lí*, meaning "to attach" or "to cling." Wén states: "to be afflicted."

*Nì*—pronounced *nǚ lì*. Wén states: "to conceal."

"Not enduring"—pronounced *shēng*.

"Following things"—*Qú*, pronounced *qí jū*.

Bóyù Chéng states: "*Qú* is the surname, Qióng the given name, and Bóyù the courtesy name of a worthy minister of Wèi."

*Shàn*—pronounced *qǐ wù*. *Guǎngyǎ* states: "to bend."

Guō—pronounced *chù*.

*Rán yǔ*—pronounced *yú*, meaning "as if" or "as though," indicating something not yet so.

"Innate stability"—*Dàtāo*, pronounced *tǔ dāo*.

Bó Chángqiān—pronounced *qǐ qián*.

*Xī*—pronounced *xū qǐ*.

Wéi Chéng states: "These three were all names of official historians."

*Zhàn*—pronounced *dīng nán*, meaning "to delight in for long." Also pronounced *shěn*.

*Lè*—pronounced *luò*.

"Among the feudal lords"—Sīmǎ states: "referring to matters of alliance and assembly."

*Làn*—pronounced *lú kàn*. *Shuōwén* states: "to overflow."

Should it be rendered as *jiàn*, pronounced *hú zhàn*—a bathing vessel.

Shǐ Qiū—pronounced *qiū*. Sīmǎ states: "referring to Shǐ Yú."

*Suǒ bó*—pronounced *bó*, meaning silk and cloth, or a bathing towel.

Sīmǎ—pronounced *bì*, stating: "to draw one’s garments to conceal oneself."

"Yet supporting one another"—Sīmǎ explains: "referring to the lord and the bathing woman aiding each other to conceal themselves."

"Thus the tomb"—alternatively written as *Great Tomb*.

Shāqiū—a place name, located north of Méngjīn’s bank.

*Jué*—pronounced *qí yuè*.

*Xǐ*—pronounced *xī lǐ*.

"Not relying"—pronounced *píng*.

"His descendants"—Sīmǎ states: "meaning his posterity were unworthy of reliance, thus allowing the lord to occupy this site as his burial mound."

"Seized and buried"—alternatively written as *seized and interred*.

*Ér*—meaning "you."

*Lǐ*—a dwelling place.

"Investigating principle"—Shàozhī Chéng states: "limited perception is called *shàozhī*."

Dàgōng Tiáo—Chéng states: "one whose virtue is vast and impartial, who also harmonizes all things, is called *dàgōng*."

*Tiáo*—likely an allegory.

"Village and hamlet"—a square *lǐ* forms a *jǐng*, while a *qiū* comprises sixteen *jǐng*.

Lǐ states: "four *jǐng* make a *yì*, four *yì* a *qiū*; five households form a *lín*, five *lín* a *lǐ*."

In ancient times, neighboring villages and hamlets had distinct customs, much as regions today retain their own local traditions, with things differing accordingly.

"Uniting ten surnames and a hundred names"—one surname comprises ten individuals, ten surnames a hundred names, thus containing both differences and similarities, which are reconciled through synthesis.

"Accumulating the lowly"—read as written; alternatively written as *kù*, pronounced *bì*.

"Converging waters"—alternatively written as *converging streams*.

"Uniting to form public land"—*jǐng*, commanderies, and counties are established as public domains.

"Heaven does not bestow"—meaning "does not grant."

"The Five Offices"—Chéng states: "in ancient times, there were officials of the Five Phases."

The text states: "referring to the five ranks of feudal lords."

*Chúnchún*—Wáng states: "the appearance of flowing movement."

The text states: "without constancy."

*Suǒ fú*—pronounced *fú fèi*, meaning "rebellion."

"The vastness of *qi*"—*guǎng* as seen in Liú’s alternate version; formerly written as *dà*.

*Ér dú*—Lǐ states: "*dú* means speech."

*Wù qǐ*—pronounced *wū*.

*Yù wù*—pronounced *wū lù*.

*Qiáo qǐ*—read as written; Wáng states: "a tall and vigorous appearance, indicating swift movement at its inception."

*Piàn hé*—pronounced *pàn*, or read as written.

"Following in order"—meaning blessings follow in sequence with proper precedence.

*Xù*—alternatively written as *hòu* or *xiǎng*.

"Alternating governance"—*qiáo yùn* refers to mutual succession and coordination, where alternation and sequence ensure orderly governance, and rotation enforces mutual restraint.

Jì Zhēn and Jiēzǐ—Chéng states: "both were worthy men of Qí."

Jì Zhēn took non-action as the Dao, while Jiēzǐ took action as the Dao; each adhered to one school, neither offering a comprehensive discourse.

*Miscellaneous Chapters: External Things, Thirty*
"No Certainty"

Wáng states: "One who forgets the self naturally has no counterpart in the world, and thus external things require no attention."

If one clings to something, it is unlikely to attain subtlety at any time.

Now, examining the constancy of external things, they refer to good and evil, loyalty and filial piety.

Self and things stand in opposition; the self is internal, things external. Because good and evil, loyalty and filial piety cannot preserve life in full, it is said: "In doing good, do not seek fame; in doing evil, do not incur punishment."

Lóngféng and Bǐgān—already explained in the *Human World* chapter.

Jīzǐ the Mad—*Records of the Grand Historian* states: "Zhòu was dissolute and excessive; Jīzǐ remonstrated but was not heeded. Someone said, 'You could leave.' Jīzǐ replied: 'As a minister, to remonstrate and not be heeded, then to leave, is to expose the ruler’s faults and seek favor with the people—I cannot bear to do so.' He then let his hair down, feigned madness, and became a slave, playing the zither in sorrow to conceal his grief. Thus the tradition speaks of *Jīzǐ’s Melody*."

Èlái’s Death—*Records of the Grand Historian* states: "Fēilián begot Èlái, who was strong; Fēilián was swift. Father and son both served Zhòu with their abilities. King Wǔ attacked Zhòu and killed Èlái; at that time, Fēilián was serving Zhòu and built a stone outer coffin in the north. His surname was Yíng, given name Èlái, a man of great strength. Zhòu’s favored minister, he was executed alongside Zhòu."

Wǔ Yuán—already explained in the *Stealing the Box* chapter.

Cháng Hóng—a loyal minister of King Líng of Zhōu, slandered by Liú Gōnggōng and exiled to Shǔ. Resentful of his loyalty being maligned, he disemboweled himself. The people of Shǔ pitied him, placed his blood in a casket, and after three years, it turned to jade—a type of stone. *Lǚ’s Spring and Autumn Annals* states: "His blood was preserved, and after three years, it transformed into jade."

Xiàojǐ’s Sorrow—Chéng states: "Xiàojǐ was the eldest son of Gāozōng. He suffered his stepmother’s persecution and died of grief."

Zēng Shēn’s Grief—Lǐ states: "Zēng Shēn was extremely filial but was hated by his father. He once went without food until evening. Chéng states: 'He was beaten by his father and nearly died, hence his weeping.'"

*Dà hài*—pronounced *hài*.

"Double entrapment"—referring to the snares of good and evil, both sinking into sorrow and suffering.

*Chén*—pronounced *chén*, or *chǔ yǔn* and *chì jìn*.

Dūn—pronounced *dūn*.
The *Wén* states: "a dejected appearance."
Also pronounced *zhù yǔn*, *chì zhuǎn*, or *yú zhǔn*.

Sīmǎ states: *chén dūn* is read as *zhòng róng*, describing the qi of fear and dread as surging and overflowing, an appearance of unease.

Wèi mǐn—pronounced *wǔ jīn*.
Wèi means "pent-up"; mǐn means "stifled."

Chén tún—pronounced *zhāng lún*.
Sīmǎ states: *chén* means "deep"; *tún* means "difficult."

Fèn rán—pronounced *tuí*.
The *Wén* states: "from the heart to the chin"—a vacant expression.

Jí nàn—*dài*, also written as *dài*, pronounced *tè*.
The *Qún Jīng Yīn Biàn* states: "to borrow from others is *dài*; to lend is *dài*."

Jiàn Hé Hóu—*Shuō Yuàn* renders this as Wèi Wén Hóu.

Jiāng dài—pronounced *tā dài*, meaning "to give."

Ér hū—pronounced *huǒ gù*, meaning "a loud cry."

Fù yú—pronounced *fù*.
The *Guǎng Yǎ* states: "the *jì* fish."
*Jì*—pronounced *jì*.

Bō chén—Sīmǎ Gōngfāng refers to "ministers adrift on the waves."

Nán yóu—seen in Zhāng Jūnfáng’s edition; omitted in older versions.

Jī—pronounced *gǔ dí*.

Xī Jiāng—Chéng states: "the Shu River."
The Shu River flows from the west, hence called the Western River.

Wú shī wǒ cháng yǔ—*yǔ* means "intimacy," referring to the constant closeness of fish and water.

Bēi suǒ—pronounced *suǒ bái*.

"Withered fish"—Lǐ states: "akin to dried fish."

Qū Yuǎn—Rén Gōngzǐ: Lǐ explains *Rén* as the name of a state.

Dà gōu—also written as *gōu jù zī*.
Sīmǎ states: "a great black fishing line."

Jiè—pronounced *gǔ mài*.
A castrated ox, referring to a hump-backed bull.

Ěr—pronounced *èr*.

Dūn—pronounced *cún*; Chéng explains: "to squat."

Huì—pronounced *gǔ wài*.

Qǐ—pronounced *gǔ xī*.
A mountain name, now an administrative region.

Qī nián—also written as *qī*, pronounced *jī*, meaning one must persist in the endeavor long enough to evoke a response.

Xiàn mò—pronounced *xiàn*.

Wù yáng—one version writes *jīng*.

Fèn jì—pronounced *qiú yí*.

Lǐ—pronounced *xū*.

Dàn—pronounced *dān wèi*.

Hè—pronounced *huǒ bǎi*.
"Thousand *lǐ*"—all within a thousand *lǐ* are struck with fear.

Ruò yú—Sī Yú states: "a great fish," *ruò* meaning "spirit."
Some say *ruò yú* simply refers to "this fish."

Ér là—pronounced *xī*.

Zhì hé—also written as *zhè*, pronounced *zhū shè*.
"River" here refers to the Yangtze; northerners call all waters "rivers."
The Zhe River marks the boundary of Wú and Kuài.
Sīmǎ states: "The Zhe River now lies in Kuaiji and Qiantang."

Cāng Wú—some say it is a mountain name in Lingnan; others say it is Mount Jiuyi.

Quán—pronounced *cái cái jīn*.
Lǐ explains: "to measure a person’s talent."
Also written as *líng líng*, meaning "small," or as *qīng*, meaning "light."

Fěng shuō—pronounced *fāng fèng*.

Jiē qí liè—*jiē* and *qì* both pronounced *qì jié*.

Gān lěi—pronounced *liè bǐ*.
"Gān lěi" refers to fishing rods of thorny bamboo and silk lines of cocoon thread.
*Lěi* means "fishing line."

Guàn dú—Sīmǎ states: "channels for irrigation."

Shǒu ní—pronounced *wǔ xī*.
Fù—pronounced *fù*; Lǐ explains: "both *ní* and *fù* are small fish."

Xiàn lìng—*xiàn* means "elevated"; *lìng* means "renown."

Jì bì—*lú*, pronounced *lì yú*.
Sū Lín’s annotation to the *Hàn Shū* states: "the transmission of words from superior to inferior is called *lú*; *lú* means 'to proceed.'"

Chuán—pronounced *zhì liàn*.
Xiàng explains: "from superior to inferior is *lú chuán*," also pronounced *zhāng liàn*, meaning "urgent."

Dōng fāng zuò yǐ—Sīmǎ states: "referring to the sun’s imminent rise."

Qún rú—pronounced *ér zhū*.

Qīng qīng zhī mài—Sīmǎ states: "this is a lost poem, satirizing the dead."

Líng bēi—pronounced *bǐ yí*.

Bù shī—pronounced *shǐ chǐ*.

To receive and uphold.
*Chéng* explains: "to gather."

*Ài*—pronounced *nǎi xié*.
The *Zì Lín* states: "*Ài*: to press with a single finger."

*Qí huì*—the original also writes *yuě*; pronounced *xǔ huì*.
Sīmǎ states: "the hair beneath the chin."
*Chéng* explains: "*Huì*: the mouth."

*Jīn zhuī*—pronounced *zhí zhuī*.
*Kòng*—pronounced *kǔ jiāng*.
*Chéng* explains: "to strike."

*Xú*—pronounced *bié bǐ liè*.
To separate.

*Jīn jīng*—Lǎo Lái Zǐ, a recluse of Chǔ, once dwelled in seclusion on Mount Méng. The King of Chǔ summoned him, but he did not respond. He fled with his wife to the lands south of the Jiāng, knowing where to go.

"Went out to gather firewood"—the *Jiāngnán* edition reads: "went out to gather firewood." Another version reads: "went out to gather kindling." Formerly written as "went out for firewood."

*Qū xià*—pronounced *cù*; Lǐ explains: "short in stature."
*Mò lǚ*—refers to the head leaning forward and the back hunched.

*Hòu ěr*—Sīmǎ states: "the ears slant backward."
"Gazing as if seeking the Four Seas"—those who labor body and mind, employing cunning to attend to worldly affairs, lose their natural state.

Thus, Yáo had his metaphor of the *kàng* dragon, Shùn his discourse on the hunched back, the Duke of Zhōu likened it to a fleeing wolf, and Kǒngzǐ compared it to a stray dog—how could this not be true?

*Qiū*—pronounced *qǐ lǚ*.
*Gōng jīn*—to take pride in one’s person through cultivating good conduct.
*Róng zhì*—to adorn wisdom for the sake of appearance.

*Cù rán*—pronounced *zǐ liù*.
"Can one advance in one’s endeavors?"—asking whether one can practice benevolence and righteousness in the world.

*Ér jīng*—pronounced *wǔ bào*.
To take pride. The same below; in some versions, *xià* is written as *wù*.

*Jù*—pronounced *qí jǔ*.
*Zhī xíng*—pronounced *xià mèng*.
"Easy to advance"—from the *Zhāng Jūnfáng* edition; formerly omitted.

"To act contrary is but harm"—to go against principle is to act perversely.
"To move is but to err"—pronounced *sì jiē*.
To act is to take pride in it.

The sage is *chóu*—pronounced *chóu*.
*Chú*—pronounced *zhí jū*.
"To initiate affairs"—each time success is achieved, for "each" means "every time there is success." *Chóu chú* means to initiate affairs with composure; though success may come, the sage does not dwell on it, yet still leaves traces of harm, poisoning future generations. How much worse, then, is one who takes pride in good conduct and records it without end?

*Intelligence in distress*—Sòng Yuán Jūn, as Lǐ explains, refers to the Duke Yuán of Sòng.

Examining *Yuán Gōng*—his given name was Zuǒ, son of Duke Píng of Sòng. The *Records of the Grand Historian* states that in the thirty-second year of Duke Yuán’s reign, he dreamed of a turtle.

*Ā mén*—Sīmǎ explains: *ā* means the curved eaves of a gate.

*Zǎi Lù*—Lǐ explains: his name was Yuān, the place where the turtle dwelled.

*Wéi*—pronounced as in the standard reading, or *yú wěi* in another pronunciation.

*Shǐ*—pronounced *hé suǒ*.

*Yú zhě*—pronounced *yú*.

*Yú*—pronounced *yù*.

*Qiě*—pronounced *zǐ yú*.

The *Records of the Grand Historian* states: his surname was Yú, his given name Qiě, and he caught the turtle at Quányáng.

*Jué*—pronounced *gǔ xiào*.

*Zhān zhī*—The *Records of the Grand Historian* states: the diviner was the academician Wèi Píng.

*Lìng*—pronounced *lì chéng*.

*Huì cháo*—pronounced *zhí yáo*. The same below.

*Kū*—pronounced *kǒu gū*.

*Qī shí èr zuān*—pronounced *zuǒ duān*.

*Ér wú yí jiá*—pronounced *chū gé*.

The turtle divination yielded seventy-two omens without a single error. The *Guān-shì Yì Zhuàn* states: thus seventy-two became the standard, forming the basis for the calendar. Five cycles of seventy-two make a year; seven hundred twenty is the foundational reckoning, seven thousand two hundred the unifying reckoning, seventy-two thousand the comprehensive reckoning. Beneath the *qì* and new moon calculations, fractions are rounded to whole rates, with seventy-two million as the grand rate, called the *Yuán Jì*.

*Tí*—pronounced *tú xī*.

*Hú*—pronounced *hú*, a waterfowl.

One name is *Táohé*. *Qù*—pronounced *xiǎo qǐ lǚ qiè*. The same below.

*Shíshī*—a stonemason’s title, meaning one who has no master. *Jiàng*—the one who instructs. One version reads *suǒ shī*; another, *shuò shī*.

*The Use of the Useless*
*Zú*—pronounced *cè*, referring to the side of the foot. *Diàn*—pronounced *dīng niàn qiè*. Cuī states: below. *Zhì*—to reach. *Huángquán*—the Yellow Springs. It means that when a person grasps the side of the foot and presses downward, one sinks to the Yellow Springs.

*Liúdùn*—*xíng* pronounced *zhī háng xià mèng qiè*. *Rèn*—pronounced *yú*. *Fùzhuì*—pronounced *zhí lèi qiè*. *Xī*—the *Wéi* clan, pronounced *xū qǐ qiè*. An ancient emperor-king. *Bō*—the appearance of unevenness. The text states: *bōdàng*, the nature of things. *Bù pì*—pronounced *pǐ yì qiè*. *Nèi tōng*—inner penetration. *Chàn*—pronounced *shū tíng qiè*, meaning to examine the scent of *qi*. *Gěng*—pronounced *gēng měng qiè*, meaning blockage. *Jiàn*—pronounced *nǚ zhǎn qiè*, meaning to tread. *Dòu*—pronounced *dòu*, a hole. *Bāo*—pronounced *bìng jiāo qiè*, the fetus in the womb. The *Shuōwén* states: the wrapping of a newborn. *Chóng*—pronounced *zhí lóng qiè*. *Làng*—pronounced *làng*; Guō states: vast and empty. *Bóxī*—pronounced *bó xī*. *Bó*—to contend. *Xī*—emptiness. Sīmǎ states: *bó* means *xī fǎn lì*—rebellion; when a room has no empty space to accommodate private desires, it leads to rebellion and mutual strife.

*Liùzáo*—pronounced *zài bào qiè*. *Xiāngrǎng*—pronounced *rú yáng qiè*. Guō states: opposition. Sīmǎ states: the six emotions plunder one another.

*The Great Forest*
A fine phrase from the mountains and hills. In humans, even the spiritual cannot surpass the beauty of lush forests forming hills—yet when the lush forest is felled, the *qi*-image is diminished. Extraordinary talent is the excellence of a scholar, but when talent is conscripted into service, the spirit grows dim.

*Xián*—pronounced *xián*; Guō states: urgency. Xiàng states: firmness and uprightness; urgency gives rise to schemes. *Chái*—per the *Shuōwén*, small scattered wood. Xú Xuàn: when the Four Masters traveled the wilds, they set up scattered wood as boundaries, calling it a *cháilí*. Guō states: to block. *Diào*—pronounced *qī yáo qiè*, meaning to pare. *Nòu*—pronounced *nǎi dòu qiè*, resembling a farming tool. *Dàozhí*—pronounced *shí lì qiè*; also pronounced *zhí*, meaning to stand. Sīmǎ states: when a hoe uproots and the plant regrows, it is called *dàozhí*. *Zì*—pronounced *zǐ sī* or *zǐ zhì*; originally also written as *jiǎn*, pronounced *zǐ qiǎn qiè*. The *Sāncāng* states: *jiǎn* means to shear. *Wēi*—originally written as *miē*, pronounced *miè*. The *Zìlín* states: to strike. The *Shǐ Chóng Cángjīng Yīnyì* states: *miē*—to pluck by hand. It can be used to *mù lǎo*—wash away old age, for when one is old, the skin at the corners of the eyes wrinkles. *Miē*—to smooth; by pressing and rubbing the wrinkles flat, one can restore a youthful appearance, meaning to polish the "red dwelling." One version reads *xiū lǎo*. *Fēi yì*—pronounced *yì*, meaning to startle. *Yǐ hài*—pronounced *hù kǎi qiè*.

Wang states: it refers to altering the people’s seeing and hearing. Xu pronounces it *jiè*, meaning the superior does not inquire of the subordinate.

*Yuǎnzhēn*—pronounced *yǎn mén sì qiǎn qiè*.
Cheng states: the name of a gate in Song city; another version says it is the name of a town in Chu.

*Guānshī*—a Chu official title.

*Dàngxìng*—*Wù Guāng* and *Jì Tā* (pronounced *tú hé qiè*)—both explained in the "Great and Venerable Master" chapter.

*Ér cún*—pronounced *cūn*, the ancient form of *cún* (to crouch).

*Kuǎnshuǐ*—pronounced *kuǎn*, the name of a river.

*Diào zhī*—Sima states: fearing he would drown himself, thus they mourned him.
*Shēntú Dí*—already explained in the "Great and Venerable Master" chapter.

*Bó*—pronounced *fù*, originally also written as *qū*.
The *Zìlín* states: to fall prostrate.
Li states: to collapse.

*Wàng quán*—*quán* (pronounced *qī quán qiè*).
Cui pronounces it *sūn*: a kind of moss used as fish bait. Another version says it refers to piling firewood in water so fish gather to feed. Yet another says it is a fish trap.

*□*—pronounced *dà xī qiè*: a rabbit snare.
Another version says it is a rabbit trap, binding its legs—hence the name *□*.

*Juàn*—pronounced *gǔ xiàn qiè*.
*Jiàng*—pronounced *jù liàng qiè*.

The pronunciation is *fú* for the phonetic component.

*The Southern Florescence True Scripture: Chapter Sentences and Phonetic Glosses*, Scroll Thirteen concludes.
*The Southern Florescence True Scripture: Chapter Sentences and Phonetic Glosses*, Scroll Fourteen
Composed by Master Bìxū

**Miscellaneous Chapters: "Parables" Thirty-One**

**The Central Way**
"Parables" (*yùyán*): nineteen instances of *yù* (寓), meaning "to lodge" or "to entrust."
Because others do not believe oneself, one attributes the words to another—thus, nine out of ten statements are accepted as true.

"Weighty words" (*zhòngyán*) refer to those uttered by persons of profound virtue.

"Goblet words" (*zhīyán*): pronounced *zhī* (日出音支), Li’s pronunciation is *qǐ yí qiè*.
The goblet vessel tilts when full, rights itself when empty, and remains level when balanced—this illustrates words of central rectitude.
As the sun, when rising, is not yet centered, slants when past its zenith, and shines brightly when at midday, so too are goblet words called "daily arising."
Their meaning lies in their central rectitude and clarity.

"Celestial boundary" (*tiānní*): pronounced *yá*; Cheng says it refers to the natural allotment.

*Jí* (藉): Guo says it means "to borrow."
Li says it means "to rely upon."

"Matchmaker" (*zǐméi*): Cheng says it means "to unite."

*Qí’ài*: pronounced *wǔ gài qiè*.

"Canon and weft" (*jīngwěi wén*): the six classics and six weft texts.
Cheng says: vertical threads are called *jīng* (canon), horizontal threads *wěi* (weft).

"Root and branch" (*běnmò wén*): the root is the Dao and virtue; the branch is benevolence and righteousness.

*Yǐqī*: Cheng says it means "to await."

"Elderly person" (*chénrén*): Cheng says it refers to one who has long endured.

*Mànyǎn*: pronounced *yǐ zhàn qiè*, meaning "unbroken" or "unceasing."
The text says: "not lingering" or "not stagnating."

*Wūhū*: pronounced *wū* (音乌), the same below.

*Jiē zhǒng*: pronounced *zhāng yǒng qiè*.

"To abdicate" (*xiāngshàn*): pronounced *shàn*; Cheng says it means "to succeed" or "to replace."

**Transformations of the Times**
*Good* (*hǎo*): pronounced *hū bào qiè*.
*Evil* (*wù*): pronounced *wū lù qiè*.

*Wù*: pronounced *wù* or *wǔ gè qiè*, meaning "to oppose" or "to go against."

*Xìlù*: (systematic record)

Three *fu*: The *Xiao Er Ya* states that six *dou* and four *sheng* make one *fu*.

*Xīn lè*: pronounced *luò* (音洛), the same below.

Three thousand *zhong*: Cheng says six *hu* and four *dou* make one *zhong*.

*Bù jì*: pronounced *qí* (其器切), meaning "to reach" or "to extend to."

*Ruò cān*: pronounced *suǒ* (所金切).

"With no place to suspend their guilt" (*wú suǒ xuán qí zuì hū*): *xuán* means "to bind" or "to implicate."

The heart transforms twice in service to emolument, yet what it preserves is kinship; though bound to emolument, it is not bound to guilt.

*Rú guān wū què*: pronounced *gǔ luàn* (古乱切).
*Wū* appears in Zhang Junfang’s edition; the old text was obscure.

*Wén méng*: pronounced *mèng gēng* (孟庚切).

When crows, sparrows, mosquitoes, and midges pass one another, they do so unnoticed in an instant. Wang says: "Crows, sparrows, mosquitoes, and midges" take their great and small disparity as an analogy for the difference between three *fu* and three thousand *zhong* in quantity.

**Narrating Learning**
*Zǐ qí*: pronounced *qí* (音其).

He urged the duke to die for his private interests—*sī* appears in Zhang Junfang’s edition; the old text was missing.

The reckoning of the heavens forms images; there is a reckoning by which they may be inferred. Humans take their place on earth to form shapes; there are humans by whom they may be known.

*Wū hū*: pronounced *wū* (音乌), the same below.

**Independent Transformation**
*Kuò cuō*: pronounced *gǔ huó* (古活切).
*Cuō* appears in Zhang Junfang’s edition; the old text was missing.

*Bèi fà*: pronounced *pí jì* (皮寄切).

*Sǒu sǒu*: the original also writes *sōu sōu* (搜搜), pronounced *sù kǒu* (素口切), or *xiāo* (音萧). Xiang says it describes a moving appearance.

*Shāo*: pronounced *shān qiǎo* (山巧) or *suǒ jiào* (所教切), meaning "gradual" or "incremental."

*Tiáo jiǎ*: pronounced *tiáo* (音条); Sima says it refers to the molting of cicada shells.

*Shé tuì*: pronounced *yuè* (音悦), or *tǔ wò* (吐卧) or *shǐ ruì* (始锐切).

*Wú tún*: pronounced *tú mén* (徒门切), meaning "to gather" or "to assemble."

Moreover, how much more so for those who are without dependence and without attachment? The text is missing in Zhang Junfang’s edition; the old text was also absent.

**Departing from Arrogance**
Yang Ziju: surnamed Yang, named Rong, styled Ziju.

*To Pèi*: pronounced *bèi* (贝), Cheng Yun says it refers to Pengcheng, now Xuzhou.
*Yāo*: pronounced *yāo* (邀), with the ancient pronunciation *yáo* (尧切), meaning "to intercept" or "to encounter." The *Yùpiān* says it means "to seek," "to intercept," or "to block."

*To Liáng*: Cheng Yun says it refers to Song territory, now Bianzhou.

*To reach the lodging*: meaning the inn’s lodging.
*Guàn*: pronounced *guǎn* (管), to wash the hands.
*To sprinkle*.
*Sòu*: pronounced *sù* (所救切), to rinse the mouth.
*To swill the mouth*.
*Jīn zhì*: pronounced *zhuāng yǐ* (庄乙切), the general term for combs and hairpins.
*Jù*: pronounced *jù* (俱遇切), meaning shoes.
*Suī suī*: pronounced *xǔ guī* (许圭切), to look upward.
*Xū xū*: pronounced *xiāng yú* (香于切), to open the eyes wide.
*Cù*: pronounced *zǐ liù* (子六切), Cheng Yun says it means "to shrink back in fear or alarm."
*The master of the house*: Li Gong says it refers to the host.
*Yáng*: pronounced *yáng shàng* (羊尚切), Xiang says it means "to cook."

**Miscellaneous Chapters: "Lie Yukou" (Chapter 32)**
**Emerging from the Extraordinary**
Bohun Maoren: pronounced *mào* (茂) or *wù* (务), a worthy and reclusive man of Chu, whom Liezi also took as his teacher. Also written as *Wúrén* (无人).

*Xī fāng*: Li Gong says *fāng* (方) means "the Dao/Way."
*I was startled*: Li Gong says it means that seeing others move him, he departs from the Dao/Way, hence his alarm.
*Wū hū*: pronounced *wū* (乌).
*Shí jiāng*: pronounced *zǐ xiáng* (子祥切).

"Ten households all sell fermented beverages." Also written as *jiāng* (). *Five households first present offerings*: *kuì* (馈) means "to bestow"; among the ten households, five first come to call. Wang says they all first present offerings to him. *Bù xiè* (不解): pronounced *xiè* (蟹). *Xíng dié* (形谍): pronounced *tú xié* (徒协切). Guo says it means "flattery." *Shuōwén* says it means "to intervene." Cheng Guang and Sima say *xíng dié yú zhōng* (形谍於衷) means "to display brilliance within." *Guì lǎo* (贵老): to esteem Liezi more than the elderly. *Ér rěn* (而): pronounced *zǐ xī* (子兮切), meaning "disorder." *Wéi shí* (为食): pronounced *sì* (嗣). *No excess profit*: *yíng* (赢) pronounced *yíng* (盈); as seen in the Jiangnan editions of Li and Zhang Junfang, the old text was missing. *Wàn shèng* (万乘): pronounced *shéng zhèng* (绳证切). *Ér xiào* (而效): pronounced as written, or as *jiào* (校), pronounced *gǔ xiào* (古孝切). *Chǔ jǐ* (处己): pronounced *jǐ* (纪); Cheng says, "You settle yourself but cannot forget me, displaying your form alone, becoming the object to which all things turn." *Bǎo rǔ* (保汝): Sima says *bǎo* (保) means "to cling to." *Wú jǐ* (无几): pronounced *jū qǐ* (居岂切). *Dūn zhàng* (敦杖): pronounced *dùn* (顿). *Cù zhī* (蹙之): pronounced *zǐ liù* (子六切). *Bīn zhě* (宾者): originally written as *bīn* (傧), pronounced *bì rèn* (必刃切), referring to those who receive guests. *Xiǎn ér* (跣而): pronounced *xiān diǎn* (先典切). *Jì qí qì* (暨其器): *jì* (暨) pronounced *qì* (器), meaning "to reach." *Fā yào* (发药): pronounced as written; Sima’s edition has *fèi yào* (废药), meaning "to set aside." *Ér yān* (而焉): pronounced *yú qián* (於虔切). *Yáo ér běn xìng* (摇而本性): one edition has *cái* (才) instead of *xìng* (性). *Also means "without cause"*: to agitate one’s innate nature or talent to seek, which is not the meaning of the Dao/virtue. *Xiǎo yán* (小言): words that do not enter the Dao/Way, hence called "petty words." *Rén dú* (人毒): because of their many afflictions, they are called "the poison of men." *Mò jué* (莫觉): no awareness; *mò wù* (莫悟): no realization. *How could they compare?*: They dare not tell you, and you do not realize it yourself—how could they ever compare? Wang says petty words are poisonous, yet no one speaks of it. *Who would be close and loving?*: Since no one speaks of it, this is the extreme of lacking closeness and love. *Fàn ruò* (泛若): pronounced *fāng jiàn* (芳剑切). *Zì fá* (自伐): to boast of oneself.

Gentle and unhurried—*Huǎn* (缓) is his name; a Confucian scholar of the state of Zheng, named Huan. At that time, the Confucian and Mohist schools were both flourishing, and Huan wished for his younger brother Di to study Mohism, hoping to receive the benefits of being a Mohist.

*Shēnyín* (呻吟), pronounced *shēn*, refers to the sound of chanting and reciting in study. Another interpretation says *lù shēnyín* (露呻吟), meaning reading aloud. *Qiú shì zhī dì* (裘氏之地): a place name; Cui says *Qiú* refers to Confucian robes, hence the name. The *dì qí* (地祇) spent three years before Huan transformed into a Confucian—*qiào* (翹), pronounced *yí*. The *Shuōwén* says *dì qí* is that which brings forth the myriad things, meaning that after a thousand days of study, his Confucian learning was complete.

*The river moistens nine li*: the text says the river comes from the *qián* position, and *qián* corresponds to the yang number nine. *Shǐ qí dì Mò* (使其弟墨): to make his younger brother Di become a Mohist. *Hé hé* (阖阖): "why not?" *Cháng shì qí liáng* (尝视其良): *liáng* (良) refers to the good man, meaning Huan, saying, "Why not go and see the fruit of the *jiūbǎi* (揪栢) that has grown on Huan’s grave?" Some write it as *yín* (垠), pronounced *làng*. *Zhǒng* (冢) means grave. *Hé cháng shì qí liáng* (阖尝视其良): In the ancient southern Jiāngnán edition, the old text reads *Hé hú cháng shì qí liáng* (阖胡尝视其良).

*Xiāng zuó* (相捽), pronounced *cái gǔ*. It speaks of the man who digs a well, claiming the merit of creating the spring for himself, while those who drink from it do not know the spring’s natural origin. This is a metaphor for Huan not recognizing Di’s innate Mohist nature and resenting him.

*Nèi xī* (内熙): Zhuangzi says, "Those who know the Way are easy"—*yǐ* (以), pronounced *chǐ*. *Gǔ zhī zhìrén* (古之至人): The Zhang Junfang edition has this; the old text omits it. *Wú yì* (无益): *Zhū píng* (朱泙), pronounced *píng*.

Màn (曼), pronounced *wèi dàn*. Sima [Biao] says: Zhu Pingman and Zhili Yi are both personal names.

Tú (屠), pronounced *tú*. Dān (单), pronounced *dān*, means "exhausted." The phrase *qiān jīn zhī jiā* (千金之家) is read as written; the original text has *gǔ* (贾), also written as *jià* (价). Another version has *chǎn* (产).

Jì chéng (技成), pronounced *qǐ*. Guāi lǐ (乖理): *shùn* (顺) in "shùn yú bīng" (顺於兵) is sometimes written as *shèn* (慎).

Bì qiǎn (敝浅): "petty man," Cheng [Xuanying] says, "ordinary man." Bù lí (不离), pronounced *lì zhì*.

Bāo zhí (苞直), pronounced *zhí yú*. Sima [Biao] says: "having a wrapping." Gān (竿), pronounced *gān*. Dú (牍), pronounced *dú*; Sima [Biao] says: "bamboo slips for writing, used to exchange inquiries and cultivate *qi*." Cheng [Xuanying] says: "*Bāo zhí* is a fragrant herb." To pluck fragrant grasses as gifts or fold bamboo slips for inquiries is not the conduct of a true man.

Bì jīng shén (敝精神), pronounced *bì shì* or *bì shì*. Jiǎn qiǎn (蹇浅): appearance of petty and shallow. Gān míng (甘瞑), pronounced *mián*. Fā xiè (发泄), pronounced *xī liè*.

Làn jìn (滥进): Cao Shang is a petty man of Song. Wéi (为), pronounced *wěi*. Sòng wáng (宋王): Sima [Biao] says, "King Yan." Shǐ Qín (使秦), pronounced *suǒ lì*. Chéng (乘), pronounced *shéng zhèng* (here and below).

Ài xiàng (阨巷), pronounced *wū xiè*: straitened alleys, distressed and constrained. Gǎo (稿), pronounced *kǔ lǎo*. Xiàng Lǐ says: "emaciated appearance." Huáng guó (黄馘), pronounced *gǔ huò*; Sima [Biao] says: "yellow face from heat."

Cóng chē (从车), pronounced *cái yòng*: to follow in attendance. Qín wáng (秦王): Sima [Biao] says, "King Hui." Cuó (痤), pronounced *zǔ hé*: boils. Shì (舐), pronounced *shǐ*: to lick. Zhì (痔), pronounced *zhì jì*: hemorrhoids.

Guān jì (观迹): There is *liào chì* (廖敕), pronounced *chì yóu*. Jī hū (圾乎), pronounced *yú jí*: perilous. Cóng shì (从事): embellished words, with *zhī* (支) as ornate and *diāo zhuó* (雕琢) as polished, *zhī màn* (支蔓) as sprawling and *zhǐ yào* (旨要) as essential.

Rǔ yǔ yú (汝与予): *yǔ* (与) and *yú* (予) are both pronounced *yǔ*. Dǎng yǔ (党与): "associates," meaning "those with whom one is allied." Yú (予), pronounced *zhuī*. Yǔ zhī (与之): "to give," meaning "to grant."

Lí shí (离实), pronounced *lì zhì*. Shī yú (施於), pronounced *shǐ*. Shāng gǔ (商贾), pronounced *gǔ*. Xiāo rén (宵人): Wang [Bian] says, "those who are not upright and bright, called 'night-walkers.'" Xùn zhī (讯之), pronounced *xìn*: to question. Chá xíng (察行): to examine conduct.

"Mào yuàn" (貌愿), pronounced *yuàn*: The *Guangya* says, "earnest and sincere." "Yǒu zhǎng" (有长), pronounced *dīng zhàng*. "Though outwardly resembling a venerable elder, inwardly he is not the same."

"Yǒu shùn" (有顺), in some versions written as *gǔn* (), an ancient form of *shùn* (顺), pronounced *shū rùn*: "to follow principle." The Wang edition, along with the Jiangnan and ancient *zàng* editions, all write it as *shèn* (慎), "to be cautious." Originally written as *xún* (循), "to follow."

"Huān" (懁), pronounced *huán*: The *San Cang* says, "urgent in the abdomen." Wang [Bian] says, "to scrutinize and discriminate." "Outwardly cautious and discerning, yet always valuing substance and restraint."

"Màn" (缦), pronounced *bàn*. Li [Xun] says, "inwardly solid and firm, outwardly like plain silk."

"Hàn" (焊), pronounced *hú dàn*. Also pronounced *gān*, meaning "urgent." Another explanation: "one’s appearance and emotions are at odds."

"Cù rán" (卒然), pronounced *cùn hū*: "its model is the standard." Some write it as *cè* (侧), "not upright." Another explanation: "refers to a drunkard who delights in tilting his cap askew."

"Jiǔ zhēng" (九征): "to seek out." To investigate a person’s nature.

"Zhēn Dé" (真德): "Zheng Kaofu, a distant descendant of Duke Min of Song and the great-grandson of Fufu He." Cheng [Xuanying] says, "thus the eighth-generation ancestor of Confucius, a grandee of Song."

"Yū" (伛), pronounced *yū jǔ*. "Lǚ" (偻), pronounced *lì jǔ*."

"Three ranks: a *shi* receives one mandate, a grandee receives two, and a village elder receives three. *Ér fū* (而夫): Guo [Xiang] says, 'an ordinary man.' *Lǚ jù* (吕巨): a haughty and unyielding appearance. *Shú xié* (孰协): 'who aligns with?' Yao of Tang and Xu You both revered humility—this asks with whom Kaofu aligns among Tang and Xu.

*Zì hào* (自好), pronounced *hū bào*. *Ér* (而), pronounced *pǐ ěr*. Guo [Xiang] says, 'to disparage.'

*Bì dá* (必达): 'Poverty has eight *jí* (极, limits/extremes),' where *jí* means 'to reach.' 'Reaching has three *bì* (必, necessities),' where *bì* means 'the utmost of one’s allotment.' 'Punishment has six *fǔ* (府, repositories),' where *fǔ* means 'to gather.'

*Měi rán* (美髯), pronounced *yán*. *Yuán* (缘): following the text, 'unable to act.' *Yǎn* (偃), pronounced *yāng à zhàng*. The text says, 'chest protruding,' or 'fearful and inferior to others'—the text says, 'timid and weak.'

'These three' all refer to those who attain thorough understanding: the text says, 'these three are of no use to others, unfit for service or labor, yet all can fully realize the principle of their lives.'

'The six' are what mutually restrain one another—seen in Liu’s alternate edition; previously omitted.

*Kuǐ* (傀), Guo [Xiang] pronounces *hū guāi*: a grand and tranquil appearance. *Zhě* (者), pronounced as written: 'to resemble.' *Qiè lù* (窃禄): there was a man who appeared before the King of Song—Cheng [Xuanying] says, 'during the time of King Xiang of Song, a mediocre and petty man ingratiated himself with the king through flattery and was granted ten chariots.' *Shí shèng* (十乘), pronounced *shéng zhèng*—same below."

"Arrogant and childish," pronounced *zhí lì*. Li [Yi] says: "Self-conceited, he dismisses *Zhuangzi*—Cheng [Xuanying] says: *shì* means 'later,' placing *Zhuangzi* behind himself, priding himself on being first among things."

*Wěi* (纬), pronounced *xiāo wěi*: to weave. *Xiāo dí* (萧荻): mugwort. Weaving mugwort into baskets to sell them. Some editions write *wěi* (苇, reeds), same pronunciation.

"Forging," pronounced *duàn luàn*. Refers to hammering and breaking.

"Nine layers," pronounced *zhí lóng*. *Lí lóng* (骊龙), pronounced *lì chí*: black dragon. The text says "poisonous dragon."

"Under the jaw," pronounced *hù gǎn*. *Fěn* (粉), pronounced *fú*. *Fū* (夫), pronounced *fú*.

"Yue Sheng"—*qí shǐ* (其使), pronounced *suǒ lì*. "Clothed with," pronounced *yǐ yú jì*. Some say: when a ruler is formally visited with a letter and gifts, it is called *pìn* (聘). "Fed with," pronounced *yǐ sì*. *Chú shū* (刍菽), pronounced *chū jū*: *chú* means grass; *shū* means soybeans.

"Regulated burial"—*zhū jī* (珠玑), two pronunciations: *qí jī*. Some say: the coffins of kings and feudal lords. The coffin is painted with cinnabar, using the colors of the four seasons: a green dragon on the left, a white tiger on the right, with gold, silver, sun, moon, jade garments, pearls, and jade disks adorning the coffin. *Shā* (沙) refers to cinnabar, applied as pigment. Huang [Shu] says: round ones are called *zhū* (pearls), irregular ones *jī* (beads).

*Lián bì* (连璧): Huan Tan’s *Xin Lun* says: "Surveying the calculations of all schools of calendrical astronomy, and examining their records, from the primordial *Tian Yuan* of high antiquity down to the midnight of the *jiǎzǐ* day in the eleventh month, when the new moon and winter solstice coincide, the sun and moon appear like *lián bì* (joined disks)."

"Fasting," pronounced *zī*.

"Crows and kites," pronounced *yǐ quán*."

Lóu is pronounced *lǚ*.
Yǐ yú is pronounced *qǐ*.
*Zhēng chéng*: to respond, to correspond.

**Miscellaneous Chapters: "The World" (33)**

*Rú dào*: Confucianism and the Dao.
*Guǒ wù* is pronounced *wū*.
*Bù lí* is pronounced *lì zhì*.
Below, *lí* (離) in "nature" and in the following chapter is the same as *lí* (離) in "to part from."
*Wéi xíng* is pronounced *xià mèng*.
Same as within this chapter.
*Xūn rán* is pronounced *xǔ yún*, meaning warm and gentle in appearance.
Cui [Zi] says: "With compassion and benevolence, one’s fragrance spreads far."
*Yǐ cāo* is pronounced *qī cáo*, meaning to grasp or hold.
*Yǐ jī* is pronounced *jī*, meaning to examine or verify.
Its numbers are one, two, three, four—these are *shù* (numbers), referring to numerological arts.

The *Yinwenzi* states: "There are four types of laws:
1. The unchanging law: the order of ruler and minister, superior and subordinate.
2. The law of equality: the same treatment of ability and baseness, similarity and difference.
3. The law for governing the masses: rewards, punishments, penalties, and executions.
4. The law of standardization: measures, weights, and balances.
These are what the ruler employs in secret, and what his subordinates must not rashly pry into."
All officials are ranked and recorded accordingly.

*Fán xī* is pronounced *fán*.
*Chún* is pronounced *shùn*.
*Liù tōng sì pì* is pronounced *bì yì*.
*Jīng cū* is pronounced *qī nú*.
Same as within this chapter.
This refers to the six *qi* being unobstructed and the four seasons unimpeded.
*Zōu* is pronounced *yóu*, the fiefdom of Confucius’ father.

The *jìnshēn* states: *jìn* means to insert, referring to the tablet. *Shēn* is the sash. *Míngfèn* is pronounced *fúwèn*, denoting the distinctions of rank—nobility and baseness.

To grasp one aspect is to master a single method. To observe with self-regard is pronounced *hūbào*, meaning to spy below. "Observe" here suggests a narrow view. *Jì* is pronounced *qǐ*, meaning incomplete or uneven, pronounced *biàn*. *Chènshén* is pronounced *chǐzhèng*, consistent in the following chapter.

Mo Di, a grand officer of Song, advocated frugality and simplicity. *Qín Gǔ* is pronounced *gǔ*, and *lí* is pronounced *lìzhī*. He was a disciple of Mo Di, rejecting the music of the Five Emperors and Three Kings as excessively lavish.

The *Dàxún* is written as *shùn* in three other versions. *Fēi Yuè* and *Jié Yòng* are two chapters of the *Mozi*: *Fēi Yuè* critiques music’s faults and opposes its use, while *Jié Yòng* urges moderation in the people’s expenditures. *Fàn* is pronounced *fāngjiàn*, meaning universal love and mutual benefit—uniting frugality with broad care.

The Yellow Emperor had the *Xiánchí*: *xián* means "all," and *chí* suggests its encompassing, nurturing influence. Yao had the *Dàzhāng*: *zhāng* means "illumination." Shun had the *Dàsháo*: *sháo* means "continuation," signifying his succession of Yao. Yu had the *Dàxià*, pronounced *hùyǎ*, meaning "greatly inheriting" the two emperors (Yao and Shun). Tang had the *Dàhù*, pronounced *hù*, signifying the salvation and protection of the people. King Wen had the *Bìyōng*, pronounced *bì*: the *Baihu Tong* states that the Son of Heaven established the *Bìyōng* to practice rites and music, promulgating virtue and transformation. *Bì* resembles the circular jade disc, modeling heaven, while *yōng* (encircled by water) symbolizes the flowing spread of education.

King Wu and the Duke of Zhou composed the *Wǔ*, declaring the pacification of the world through martial achievement.

The Son of Heaven’s coffin and outer coffin consist of seven layers, pronounced *zhílóng*. The *Liji* states that the Son of Heaven’s coffin has four layers: a water-buffalo hide coffin three inches thick, covered by one lacquered coffin, two catalpa coffins, and all four bound with three crossbars and three side panels, each bundle secured with a cypress coffin. The length is six feet, and five layers of *shà* (coffin ornaments) are buried. Zheng Xuan explains that *zhòng* refers to pillows—wooden supports and mats—totaling ten layers when combined with the coffin and outer coffin. The ranks of feudal lords and below differ in number from the *Liji*, though the specifics remain unclear. Xunzi records the Son of Heaven’s coffin and outer coffin as ten layers.

*Mòbài*: *mò* may also be written as *wèi*, and *bài* as *huǐ*. Mozi represents the orthodoxy of one school, so it cannot be deemed corrupt. Cui [Yuan] states that his Way remains unblemished.

*Fēi Gē*: "Song" should be "sing," but Mozi condemns singing as wrong. *Yuè’ér* is pronounced *luò* below as well. *Hú* is pronounced *kǔjiǎo*. Guo and Li both explain it as lacking moisture.

*Qíxíng* is pronounced *xiàmèng*, consistent within the chapter. *Néngrèn* is pronounced *rén*. How could the world bear Mozi’s demands? *Yīn* is pronounced *yīn* or *yān*, meaning to block, submerge, or channel water into the sea so it flows underground—an allusion to Yu’s frugality aligning with his own principles.

*Zhīchuān*: some versions write it as *zhīliú*. *Zìsào* is pronounced *qícáo*. *Tuó* is pronounced *tuō*, a sack. Sima [Biao] explains it as a vessel for holding earth. *Sì* is pronounced *sì*: the *Shiming* states *sì* resembles teeth cutting things. The *Sancang* says it is the iron tip of a plow. Cui [Yuan] calls it a mallet, while Sima [Biao] describes it as a water vessel. *Jiǔ* is read as the character itself or pronounced *jiū*, meaning "to gather." *Dí* appears in the Jiangnan Li edition; older versions write it as *zá*. Cui [Yuan] explains that the waters he governed were not uniform, hence "miscellaneous."

The *Shu* states: "The nine rivers are cleansed at their sources." Kong Anguo explains that the rivers of the nine provinces are dredged to prevent blockages. *Féi* is pronounced *féi*. *Wúbā* is pronounced *bùgé*.

Gěng is pronounced *xíngdìng*. *Wúmáo fēi gěng* refers to the calf; *shuàn fēi* refers to the intestines; *gěng fēi* refers to the bone before the intestines. *Bá* are the fine hairs on the thigh.

*Shuàn* is pronounced *shíyǎn*. *Shèn yǔ*: Cui’s edition writes it as *zhàn*, pronounced *yín*. *Zhì* is pronounced *cèbǐ*. *Qiú hè* is pronounced *hùgé*. *Qǐ* is pronounced *qí nì*. *Qiāo* is pronounced *jǐ lüè*. Li explains: hemp makes *juē*, wood makes *jī*. *Jī* is the same as *qǐ*, *juē* the same as *qiāo*. Another account says they are types of shoes. Another pronunciation is *jūyù*, used as padding beneath shoes.

*Wéi fú*: *fú* means "to use." *Xiāng* is pronounced *xī liàng*. *Lǐ Qín*: Sima explains he was a master of ink, surnamed Xiangli, named Qin. *Kǔ Huò Jǐ Chǐ*: Li explains these are the names of two individuals. *Ér bèi* is pronounced *pèi*. *Jué* is pronounced *jué*. *Xiāng Zǐ* is pronounced *zǐ*. *Yǐ jī* is pronounced *jī yí*. Cheng explains: to sing alone is called *chù*. The *Shuowen* states: when an ox’s horns are one lowered and one raised, it is called *jī*, an expression of discord. *Bù wǔ* is mistakenly read as *wǔ èr*.

*Jùzǐ*: Xiang explains that among the Mohists, one who has mastered their doctrine is called a *Jùzǐ*, like a *shuòrú* among the Confucians. *Zhī hào* is pronounced *hū bào*. *Kū gǎo* is pronounced *kǔ lǎo*.

"Bù shě" is pronounced *shè*, the same in the following chapter.

*Míng zhì*: *Zhì* is pronounced *zhì chǐ*, meaning "to oppose." Cui explains it means "to harm." The *Zishu* states it means "ruthless."

*Bái xīn*: Cui explains it means "to clarify one’s heart," or alternatively, "to follow one’s heart."

*Sòng Jiān* is pronounced *xíng*. A native of Song, he authored eighteen chapters and studied the teachings of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi.

*Yǐn Wén*: Cui explains he was a contemporary of King Xuan of Qi and authored two chapters. Cheng explains that both Song Jian and Yin Wen were contemporaries of King Xuan of Qi and studied together at Jixia.

*The cap of Mount Hua*: Mount Hua’s peaks are uniformly level; the cap is made in its likeness to symbolize the evenness of one’s heart.

*Yǐ bié* is pronounced *bǐ liè*.

*Yòu wéi shǐ*: *Shǐ* means "beginning." Cui explains it means "to distinguish good from evil." *Yòu* refers to those who fall short, for whom this is the starting point. Another explanation: to distinguish boundaries and domains.

*Shǐ* means "foundation." Establishing names and teachings to engage with the world and differentiate the myriad things begins with this.

*Ér*: Pronounced *ér*. Wang explains it means "harmony." Another explanation: it means "to regulate."

*To harmonize the myriad things*: When things are in accord, joy arises. Those who harmonize through the Dao bring things into accord, and when regulation aligns with intent, joy follows.

*Shàng yuè* is pronounced *yuè*.

*Xià jiào*: The "upper" refers to the ruler of the state; it means the lower delights in the upper’s teachings. Another explanation: *yuè* means "to teach," so *shàng jiào* means "to teach the lower."

*Qiǎng* is pronounced *zhàng*.

*Guō* is pronounced *gū huó*, meaning to clamor incessantly in someone’s ear.

*Jiàn yàn* is pronounced *yàn chè*.

*Wéi rén* is pronounced *yú wěi*.

*Xià zì wéi* is the same as above.

*Tú ào hū* is pronounced *wǔ bào*. *Tú ào hū* means "not to scheme arrogantly."

*Kē chá* is pronounced *hé*, with another version reading *xún*.

*Qí xíng* is pronounced *xià mèng*, or read as written.

*Lùn fǎ*: *Bù dǎng*, with another version reading *dāng*. Cui’s version reads *dǎng*, explaining it as "utter impartiality without faction."

*Yì* is pronounced *yǐ chǐ*.

* Péng Méng*: A native of Qi.

*Tián Pián* is pronounced *bó tián*. A native of Qi, he authored fifteen chapters and was skilled in rhetoric. Shenzi states his given name was Guang.

*Shèn Dào*: A native of Zhao, he authored forty-two chapters. All three studied together at Jixia.

*Bù biàn* is pronounced *biàn*.

*Líng* is pronounced *líng*.

*Tài* is pronounced *tài*. *Líng tài* means "refined and polished."

*Xī* is pronounced *hù lǐ*. The *Shuowen* states it means "shame." Another explanation: *xī gòu* refers to the anger of petty men.

*Kē* is pronounced *hù wǎ*. Guo explains *xī kē* as the appearance of deceit and impropriety. Wang explains it means "meticulous and severe."

*Wú rèn*: No application of one’s abilities. Wang explains: though meticulous in the law, one does not impose oneself on affairs; when matters are not one’s own but shared by all, there is non-action, and this is why the exaltation of worthies is ridiculed.

*Zòng tuō*: Cheng explains it means "indulgent and unrestrained, lacking virtuous conduct in benevolence and righteousness."

*Wú xíng* is pronounced *xià mèng*, the same as the conduct of others below.

*Zhuī* is pronounced *zhuī zhí*. *Pāi* is pronounced *pū bǎi*. Cheng explains these mean "flogging and beating." *Wǎn* is pronounced *wǔ guǎn*. *Duàn* is pronounced *dīng guǎn*. Cheng explains these mean "administering punishment." Wang explains: *zhuī pāi wǎn duàn* are all implements used by executioners.

*Wǎnzhuǎn*: Cheng explains this means "transformation." *Shě* is pronounced *shè*. *Wēirán* is pronounced *yú wēi*. Cheng explains this means "immovable in appearance." *Ruò piāo* is pronounced *bì yáo*. The *Erya* states this means "whirlwind." *Zhī* is pronounced *xuán* or *huán*.

*Ruò mó* is pronounced *mò zuǒ*. Stone’s *suì* is pronounced *tuì*, meaning "to revolve." Though whole and without flaw, like a grindstone’s edge, coarse or fine depends on the user; its power is complete, yet it bears no blame in the moment, for it acts without intent.

No fear of falling behind—Jiangnan edition reads *dài*; old edition reads *yuǎn*. *Bù lí* is pronounced *lì zhì*. *Fū kuài* is pronounced *kǔ duì*. *Hù* is pronounced *huō* or *mài*. Xiang explains this means "the sound of wind blowing against." *Rán* is pronounced *wū*. *Yú* is pronounced *wǔ guǎn*. *Duàn* is pronounced *dīng guǎn*. Same meaning as above: *wǎn duàn*, meaning "administering punishment." Cheng explains: though laws are established and transformations enacted, one cannot yet fully harmonize the myriad things, thus one cannot escape *yuán duàn*.

*Zhī wěi* is pronounced *yú guǐ*. This is so.

In general—pronounced *gài*. Cheng explains: "rough and unrefined." Wen says: "moderate and restrained."

Zanlao—pronounced *dànrán*. Tranquil and serene.

Guanyin—Guanyin Xi, styled Gongdu, author of nine chapters; served as magistrate of Hangu Pass during the reign of King Ping of Zhou.

Lao Dan—pronounced *tāgān*. The Old Master.

With gentleness—pronounced *rú*.

Like an echo—pronounced *xiǎng*.

Fleeting—pronounced *hū*.

Valley—pronounced *xī*.

Its filth—pronounced *gòu*.

Steadfast—pronounced *kuī*. Cheng explains: "the meaning of standing alone."

Without waste—pronounced *fèi*. To diminish.

Though not yet reaching the ultimate—old edition reads *kěwèi zhìjí*; Jiangnan ancient repository reads *suī wèi zhìjí*—this refers to verbal teachings. Verbal teachings are but the coarse words of the Old Master and the Pass Magistrate in responding to things and transforming people; they are not yet the subtlest marvel of the ultimate. How could the subtlest marvel of the ultimate be spoken?

Preface to Zhuangzi:

Death—pronounced *yǔ*, same below.

Vast—pronounced *máng*, same below.

Miuyou—meaning as if forgetting the truth of things. One says: the empty pass.

Huangtang—meaning vast and boundless without borders. One says: reckless and unrestrained.

Without carelessness—pronounced *dàng*. Refers to the *tàng* in *titàng* (unconventional); "without carelessness" means adhering to the law.

With oddness—pronounced *jī*, meaning unpaired.

Zhuang’s words—Zhuang refers to Zhuangzi.

With a goblet—pronounced *zhī*.

Mandyan—pronounced *yǎn*. Already explained in the "Parables" chapter.

Proud—pronounced *ào*.

Ni—pronounced *ní*. "Ganbao ni" describes an arrogant and haughty appearance.

Without reproach—pronounced *qiǎn*.

Xiang—pronounced *guī*.

Wei—pronounced *yú*. Extraordinary.

Lianfan—pronounced *fān*, meaning to follow things without opposition, thus causing no harm.

Can—pronounced *cān*.

Cha—pronounced *chāi*.

The text speaks of an inconstant appearance—pronounced *chù*.

Guǐ—pronounced *jiǔwěi*. The text speaks of an appearance contrary to custom.

Ér pì—pronounced *bìyì*.

Shēn hóng—pronounced *hóng*.

Bù shuì—pronounced *yuè*, or *shǐruì*. One version writes *tuō*.

**On Hui Shi:**
Hui Shi’s multifarious doctrines are not recorded in older accounts; his new principles are fully documented in the *Explanations of Chapters and Verses* below, and thus not reiterated here.

Wǔ chē—pronounced *chǐshé*, or *jū*.

Chuǎn—pronounced *chuǎnyǎn*.

Bó—pronounced *bāngjué*.

Bù zhòng—pronounced *dīngzhòng*. *Lì* may also be written as *历*.

Fāng nì—pronounced *yì*.

Sì—pronounced *fāngjiàn*.

Dà guān—pronounced *gǔluàn*.

**On "Xiǎo biàn":**
The *Zìlín* states: *biàn* means wisdom.

Lè zhī—pronounced *luò*.

Bù zhǎn—pronounced *nǚchén*.

Záo—pronounced *cáobào*.

Ruì—pronounced *rúruì*.

Zú—pronounced *zǐmù*. The arrowhead of an arrow.

Lí—pronounced *lìzhī*.

Chuí—pronounced *zhāngyào*.

Huán Tuán—pronounced *chúwán*.

Zhī yòu—pronounced *yòu*.

Qí dǐ—pronounced *diǎnlǐ*. The *Shuōwén* states: the root of a tree. Also pronounced *dīngjì*.

Yǐ rén—read as written, though some versions write *jī*, pronounced *jǐyí*. Li [Yun] says: meaning "different."

Huáng Liáo—pronounced *liǎo*. Li [Yun] says: a worthy person.

Bù zhuì—pronounced *zhílèi*.

Tíng—pronounced *tíng*.

Biàn wéi—pronounced *yúwěi*.

Ào—pronounced *wūbào*. Li [Yun] says: meaning "profound."

Wén—pronounced *wén*.

Mèng—pronounced *mēnggēng*.

Yí—pronounced *dài*.

The *Dàng Jiù* states: *dài* means "to release," as in being unrestrained and unable to restrain oneself.

Alas! The old pronunciation of *fū* is *hàowàn*. Guo Xiang’s commentary, in discussing its fundamental principles, may truly be said to have grasped the essence of Zhuangzi’s thought.

Guo, in his lifetime, lamented the sophistry of the wealthy and refined; I, too, have lately witnessed the vain discourse of the privileged and idle. This is what is meant by "different eras, same customs"—what more can be said?

Some ask: Zhuangzi and Huizi shared the bond of the Hao River’s bridge and modeled themselves after the master craftsman of Ying, yet why is it said that his works fill five carts while his words miss the mark? Could it be that their bond was like that of the craftsman of Ying, where even Xiang’s adze, when laid to rest, was met with criticism? Such words are truly excessive.

I reply: If one wishes to rescue the workshops of established teachings and illuminate their words with divine clarity, how can one fail to refine their expression and exhaust their analogies? Zhuangzi struck the resonant note of mystery across his seven chapters, arranging these writings for all generations. His profound words traverse the path of the arcane, and his endeavors unfold in the order of eloquent discourse. Though he discoursed on the primal and prized disputation, his teachings were never empty rhetoric. Yet his text is easy to peruse, while its deeper meaning is difficult to fathom. Those who approach it without full comprehension may harbor suspicions of overreaching its principles, and thus the flaws of such misapprehensions must be dispelled. This is why I have broadly addressed Huizi’s views on disputation.

*Colophon: The Fourteenth Scroll of the* Nan Hua Zhen Jing Zhang Ju Yin Yi *is complete.*
